EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A09
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A09
Cite key        : Mwakiluma2024
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management systems — MCS three-dimension model:
                  budgeting, information, training and development),
                  T2 (employee productivity/performance as DV),
                  T5 (empirical: MCS → employee performance),
                  T7 (Africa — Tanzania LGA public sector)
Title           : The effects of management control systems on local
                  government authorities' employee performance in Tanzania
Authors         : Mwakiluma, L. A.
Year            : 2024
Journal         : International Journal of Development and Management
                  Review
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 19, No. 1, pp. 121–139
DOI             : 10.4314/ijdmr.v19i1.8 ✓ (confirmed from CSV)
Open access     : Yes (AJOL)
Publisher       : African Journal Online (AJOL)
Affiliation     : Department of Management Sciences, National Institute
                  of Transport, Dar es Salaam, Tanzania

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; explanatory research design;
                  multiple regression
Aim             : To assess the effects of MCS (budgeting, information,
                  training and development systems) on employee
                  performance in Mkinga District Council, Tanzania
Setting/context : Mkinga District Council, Tanzania (LGA); 15
                  departments; n=93 employees; public sector
                  Africa-specific context
Constructs      : IVs: Management Control Systems — three dimensions:
                    (1) Budgeting Control System (BGT)
                    (2) Information Management Control System (ICS)
                    (3) Training and Development System (TD)
                  DV: Employee Performance (EP) — productivity, quality
                      of work, customer satisfaction
Theoretical lens: Control Theory (Cybernetic Theory — Ouchi, 1970s):
                  organisations as interconnected systems; budgeting,
                  information, and training systems control employee
                  behaviour toward performance goals

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Explanatory (causal); cross-sectional; quantitative
Sample          : 93 employees from 15 departments; non-proportional
                  stratified simple random sampling; Yamane (1967)
                  formula applied (N=1,330; e=0.1; 95% confidence)
Instrument      : Questionnaire (closed-ended); Likert scale implied
Reliability     : Cronbach's α = 0.895 (>0.70 ✓; <0.95 ✓)
Statistical analysis: Pearson correlation; multiple regression
                  (OLS); ANOVA; multicollinearity check (VIF<5 ✓);
                  normality tests (Kolmogorov-Smirnov + Shapiro-Wilk)
Ethics          : Not explicitly reported (AJOL publication implies
                  institutional review)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Correlation results (all significant at p<0.01):
  BGT → EP: r = 0.834
  ICS → EP: r = 0.917 (strongest relationship)
  TD → EP:  r = 0.858

Regression results (R² = 0.871; F = 200.493, p = 0.000):
  ICS → EP: β = 0.566, t = 5.820, p = 0.000 ✓ (strongest predictor)
  TD → EP:  β = 0.307, t = 4.213, p = 0.000 ✓
  BGT → EP: β = 0.178, t = 1.685, p = 0.096 (marginal — positive
            but p > 0.05 at conventional significance)

Model explains 87.1% of variance in employee performance —
  very strong explanatory power.

Key substantive conclusions:
1. Information control system is the strongest predictor of employee
   performance (β=0.566) — access to timely, accurate information
   enables informed decision-making and effective task performance
2. Training and development system significantly enhances employee
   performance through skills, knowledge, and competency development
3. Budgeting control system has a positive but only marginally
   significant effect — suggests budgetary clarity aids but is not
   the primary driver of performance in this LGA context
4. All three MCS dimensions together explain 87.1% of EP variance —
   management control systems are powerful performance predictors

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Provides operational definition of MCS (management
               control systems) as the thesis's core management systems
               construct. The three-dimension model (budgeting/
               information/training) maps directly onto MIS and OMS
               sub-constructs flagged by Dr Balogun. Control Theory
               offers alternative theoretical framing for Ch. 2.2.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Strongest empirical evidence in corpus for MCS → employee
               performance in Africa public sector (R²=0.871). Tanzania
               LGA context is directly analogous to GGHN as a public-
               sector service delivery organisation in East Africa.
               β=0.566 for information control system is the largest
               management systems → performance coefficient found so far.

Ch. 3 use    : Yamane sample size formula (n=93 from N=1,330) — usable
               as precedent for thesis sampling; Cronbach's α=0.895
               (> 0.70 ✓) instrument reliability confirmation; multiple
               regression with diagnostic tests — methodological model.

Statement of Problem: Study acknowledges "employee performance remains
               suboptimal despite management practices" in Tanzania LGA —
               directly supports thesis Statement of Problem paras 1–2.

Limitation   : Single district council (Mkinga); n=93; Tanzania (not
               Nigeria); no hybrid work component; cross-sectional;
               budgeting system marginal significance may limit
               generalisability of that sub-finding.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] R² = 0.871 — exceptional explanatory power for MCS → EP
  [+] Full diagnostic reporting: correlation, regression, ANOVA,
      VIF, normality, heteroscedasticity
  [+] Cronbach's α = 0.895 ✓ — strong instrument reliability
  [+] Africa + public sector — closest contextual match to GGHN
      yet found in Block A (Tanzania LGA ≈ Nigeria LGA/MDA)
  [+] Control Theory framework clearly operationalised
  [+] Open access (AJOL) — fully accessible
  [~] Single district council; n=93 — small, context-specific sample
  [~] BGT coefficient not significant at p<0.05 (p=0.096) —
      authors claim significance but conventional threshold not met
  [~] Ethics not explicitly reported
  [~] Single author — no independent verification

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with A02 (Bogale2021) and A03 (Marie2024):
  All three are Africa public sector studies of management systems.
  A02 (Ethiopia hospitals) — poor HMIS implementation;
  A03 (South Africa municipality) — PMS challenges;
  A09 (Tanzania LGA) — MCS positively predicts EP.
  Together: A02/A03 show the problem; A09 shows what effective MCS
  achieves. Strong empirical arc for Ch. 2.3 narrative.

Convergence with A04 (Nor2025):
  Both examine management systems in East/sub-Saharan Africa public
  sector; A04 = Somalia civil service; A09 = Tanzania LGA.
  Consistent cross-national pattern: management system effectiveness
  → employee performance in African public administration.

Strongest R² in corpus: A09's R²=0.871 surpasses all prior papers.
  The information control system coefficient (β=0.566) is the largest
  single management systems → performance effect found so far.

Emerging corpus pattern reinforced: information/MIS component of
  management systems is consistently the strongest predictor (A06:
  trust/technology; A09: information control system β=0.566).

First-mention tracker updates:
  - Management Control Systems (MCS) three-dimension model:
    budgeting / information management / training and development
  - Control Theory (Ouchi, Cybernetic): first management-specific
    control theory framework in corpus
  - Yamane (1967) sample size formula: first explicit sampling
    formula cited; precedent for thesis Ch. 3
  - Tanzania LGA / public sector: fifth Africa country/context;
    East Africa public sector empirical evidence

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Mwakiluma, L. A. (2024). The effects of management control systems on
local government authorities' employee performance in Tanzania.
International Journal of Development and Management Review, 19(1),
121–139. https://doi.org/10.4314/ijdmr.v19i1.8
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A10
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A10
Cite key        : Ishiwu2024
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management systems — PMS constructs: performance
                  planning, target setting, monitoring), T2 (employee
                  productivity/performance as DV), T5 (empirical: PMS
                  → employee performance, Nigeria), T7 (Nigeria public
                  sector — Enugu State ministries)
Title           : Performance management systems and employee performance
                  in selected ministries in Enugu State, Nigeria
Authors         : Ishiwu, M. U., Chikeleze, O. F., & Okwueze, O. F.
Year            : 2024 (received June 2024; accepted September 2024;
                  shortlist CSV records as 2022 — citing year 2024
                  per published volume; DOI confirms Vol. 16, Issue 1)
Journal         : Journal of Policy and Development Studies (JPDS)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 16, Issue 1 (2024)
ISSN            : 1597-9385 (print); 2814-1091 (online)
DOI             : 10.4314/jpds.v16i1.20 ✓ (confirmed from CSV)
Open access     : Yes (AJOL)
Publisher       : African Journal Online (AJOL)
Affiliation     : Department of Public Administration, Enugu State
                  University of Science and Technology, Enugu, Nigeria

NOTE ON YEAR: Shortlist CSV records 2022; paper states "Received
27 June 2024; Accepted 14 September 2024" — publication year is
2024. Cite as Ishiwu et al. (2024) in APA 7.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Descriptive survey; quantitative; cross-sectional
Aim             : To investigate the effects of PMS (performance
                  planning, target setting, monitoring) on employee
                  performance in selected Enugu State ministries, Nigeria
Setting/context : 4 ministries and establishments: Ministry of Finance;
                  Works and Infrastructures; Office of Survey-General;
                  Bureau of Statistics — Enugu State, Nigeria
Constructs      : IV: Performance Management System (PMS) — 3 dimensions:
                    (1) Performance Planning
                    (2) Performance Target Setting
                    (3) Performance Monitoring
                  DV: Employee Performance — punctuality, quality,
                      output, productivity, customer satisfaction
Theoretical lens: Goal-Setting Theory (Locke, 1968) — specific and
                  challenging goals enhance motivation and performance;
                  specificity, commitment, challenge, feedback

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Descriptive survey; cross-sectional; quantitative
Population      : 637 staff (entire population included — census)
Sample          : 540 returned questionnaires (85% response rate)
                  Note: study states "sampled entire population"
                  (census design)
Instrument      : Structured Likert questionnaire (5-point: SA–SD);
                  540 usable responses
Statistical analysis: Frequency tables; mean scores (decision rule:
                  grand mean ≥ 3.0 = accepted); one-sample t-test
                  (SPSS v23); test value applied to each hypothesis
Reliability     : Not explicitly reported — Cronbach's alpha absent
                  [FLAG: reliability coefficient missing]
Ethics          : Not explicitly reported

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Descriptive results:
  Performance planning → EP: Grand mean = 3.5 (accepted; range 3.2–4.0)
  Performance monitoring → EP: Grand mean = 3.8 (accepted; range 3.7–4.0)

Hypothesis tests (one-sample t-test):
H1 SUPPORTED: Performance planning → employee performance
  (t = −796.442, p = 0.000) — significant positive effect
H2 SUPPORTED: Performance monitoring → employee performance
  (t = −128.604, p = 0.000) — significant positive effect

Substantive conclusions:
1. Performance planning significantly affects employee performance —
   clear goal setting, continuous feedback, and employee development
   drive performance alignment
2. Performance monitoring significantly propels employee performance —
   clear expectations, continuous tracking, and feedback create
   accountability and motivation
3. Effective PMS is critical for high-performing Nigerian public
   sector ministries — reduces absenteeism, increases punctuality,
   quality, and hourly output

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Provides operational definition of PMS as a management
               systems construct within the Nigerian public sector.
               Three-dimension PMS model (planning/target setting/
               monitoring) maps onto the thesis's management systems IV.
               Employee performance definitions (effectiveness,
               efficiency, quality, productivity) directly adoptable
               for thesis DV operationalisation.

Ch. 2.3 use  : HIGHEST PRIORITY paper — only source in entire corpus
               to provide empirical PMS → employee performance evidence
               specifically in NIGERIA public sector. Directly
               supports the thesis research context (GGHN is a Nigeria-
               based global health NGO operating in the public sector
               space). Provides both construct definitions and
               empirical findings for Ch. 2.3.

Statement of Problem: Paper cites "out-of-fashion performance
               measuring strategies and decline in work performance
               in Enugu State ministries" — directly supports the
               thesis's problem statement for Nigeria public sector
               management systems challenges.

Goal-Setting Theory: Useful supplementary theory for Ch. 2.2 —
               complements AMO/SET/COR by providing a goal-oriented
               mechanism for management systems → performance pathway.

Limitation   : Enugu State only — not Lagos or FCT; government
               ministries not NGO/global health; no regression
               coefficients (t-test only); Cronbach's alpha not
               reported; no hybrid work component; descriptive rather
               than explanatory design despite t-test use; t-test
               interpretation has unusual test values (test value=26
               and test value=8) — methodology warrants caution.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] NIGERIA public sector — highest T7 relevance in entire corpus
  [+] Three-dimension PMS model well-articulated; Goal-Setting Theory
  [+] Large n=540 responses; 85% return rate; census design
  [+] Open access (AJOL); peer-reviewed Nigerian journal
  [~] Cronbach's alpha not reported — reliability cannot be confirmed
  [~] One-sample t-test only — no regression coefficients (β);
      explanatory power not quantified (no R²)
  [~] Unusual test values (test value=26 for H1; test value=8 for H2)
      — methodology requires user caution when citing
  [~] Negative t-values despite claimed positive effects — technical
      reporting anomaly (population mean test value choice affects
      sign; substantive direction confirmed by mean scores)
  [-] No Cronbach's alpha; no regression; no mediation analysis
  [-] Enugu State government ministries — not NGO/global health sector

[FLAG: Cronbach's alpha absent — reliability unverifiable. Use
with caution; supplement with stronger methodological sources.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with A03 (Marie2024) and A09 (Mwakiluma2024):
  All three examine PMS/MCS in Africa public sector organisations.
  A03 = South Africa municipalities; A09 = Tanzania LGA;
  A10 = Nigeria ministries. Together they form a tri-national Africa
  public sector evidence base for management systems → performance.
  A10 is the most contextually specific to GGHN's national setting.

Convergence with A04 (Nor2025) and A09 (Mwakiluma2024):
  All three test management systems → employee performance in
  developing country public sector organisations — consistent
  cross-national pattern; A10 adds Nigeria to the empirical map.

Unique contribution: Only Nigeria-context empirical study in Block A.
  This makes it an anchor citation for Ch. 2.3 — any Nigeria-specific
  claim in the literature review must reference A10.

  - Goal-Setting Theory (Locke, 1968): first explicit goal theory;

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A11
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A11
Cite key        : Achief2025
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management systems — HRM practices as management
                  systems; five-practice framework), T2 (employee
                  productivity/performance in healthcare), T4 (theoretical
                  frameworks — AMO, RBV, SET all explicitly applied),
                  T5 (empirical: HRM → employee performance),
                  T7 (Africa — Nigeria healthcare + Sub-Saharan Africa)
Title           : The impact of human resource management practices on
                  employee performance in the healthcare sector:
                  A literature review
Authors         : Achief, G. O., Ezenekwe, J. E., Ajayi, A. B.,
                  Adewara, D. E., Oluwalusi, A. O., & Abeyie, K. S.
Year            : 2025 (received July 2025; published September 2025)
                  [CSV records 2024 — actual year 2025; cite as 2025]
Journal         : African Journal of Politics and Administrative Studies
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 18, No. 3, pp. 534–561
DOI             : 10.4314/ajpas.v18i3.29
                  [Missing from CSV; confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes (AJOL)
Publisher       : AJOL / Ebonyi State University

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Systematic literature review; PRISMA; 30 studies
                  included from 78 retrieved (2014–2024 literature)
Aim             : To examine empirical/theoretical evidence on HRM
                  practices → employee performance in healthcare
Setting/context : Healthcare sector globally; Sub-Saharan Africa and
                  Nigeria given particular attention
Constructs      : IVs: Five HRM practice dimensions:
                    (1) Recruitment and Selection
                    (2) Training and Development
                    (3) Performance Appraisal
                    (4) Compensation and Rewards
                    (5) Employee Engagement
                  DV: Employee Performance (productivity, job
                      satisfaction, service quality, patient outcomes)
                  Mediators: job satisfaction, organisational
                  commitment, work engagement
                  Moderators: leadership style, regulatory environment,
                  workload, resource availability
Theoretical lens: AMO Model (Appelbaum et al., 2000) — primary;
                  RBV (Barney, 1991); SET (Blau, 1964)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Systematic literature review; PRISMA framework
Databases       : Scopus, Web of Science, PubMed, Google Scholar
Date window     : 2014–June 2024 (literature) / published Sept 2025
Final sample    : 30 studies from 78 retrieved
Geography       : Global — Nigeria (5 studies), Ghana, Kenya, UK, Saudi
                  Arabia, Pakistan, India, Iran, Jordan, South Korea,
                  China, USA, Sudan, Oman, UAE, Malaysia, Bangladesh

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. All five HRM practice dimensions positively affect employee
   performance but effectiveness is context-dependent

2. Training and Development: most consistently impactful — simulation-
   based training reduced medical errors; patient safety improved by
   18% in one longitudinal study (Jain et al., 2022)

3. Performance Appraisal: positive in high-resource settings; limited
   in low-resource environments — consistent with A03 and A05

4. Compensation + Employee Engagement: competitive pay + recognition
   reduce burnout; participatory decision-making improves quality

5. Mediator structure confirmed: HRM → job satisfaction/engagement/
   commitment → performance (not direct — operates through attitudes)

6. Nigeria-specific findings (5 studies):
   - Chigwe & Emmanuel (2020): incentives improved patient care/punctuality
   - Eneh & Eneh (2021): training improved clinical accuracy
   - Nwankwo et al. (2024): HR analytics improved decision-making
   - Okechukwu & Nwosu (2018): rewards boosted productivity
   - Oladipo et al. (2019): training improved surgical teamwork

7. Moderators: transformational leadership strongest enabler;
   inadequate funding/infrastructure undermine HRM in Africa

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : HIGHEST PRIORITY for Ch. 2.2 — AMO, RBV, and SET all
               explicitly operationalised in healthcare context:
               AMO: Ability (recruitment/training) + Motivation
               (compensation/appraisal) + Opportunity (engagement)
               RBV: human capital as strategic asset in healthcare
               SET: reciprocal exchange → commitment → performance
               This is the definitive Ch. 2.2 theoretical source.

Ch. 2.1 use  : Five-dimension HRM practices model; employee performance
               definitions in healthcare; mediator architecture.

Ch. 2.3 use  : 30-study synthesis of HRM → performance evidence for
               healthcare in developing countries; 5 Nigeria-specific
               studies cited. Confirms mediation through attitudinal
               outcomes — directly supports thesis mediation model.

Statement of Problem: Resource-constrained healthcare systems in Africa
               show diminished HRM effectiveness — supports GGHN context.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] PRISMA framework; transparent 4-stage selection process
  [+] AMO + RBV + SET all explicitly operationalised — thesis T4
      coverage complete in one paper
  [+] Nigeria + Africa healthcare context — highest combined T4/T7
      relevance of any paper in corpus
  [+] 30 studies synthesised; global + Africa coverage
  [+] Six-author team across 5 Nigerian + 1 Ghanaian institutions
  [~] SLR — no primary data; quality appraisal not formally reported
  [~] Some cited studies predate 2021 window (embedded in SLR)
  [~] DOI missing from CSV; confirmed from paper

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with A01: Healthcare-specific counterpart to A01's generic
  HRIS → PM SLR. Together: comprehensive management systems review
  (global generic + healthcare-specific) for Ch. 2.1 and 2.3.

Convergence with A04, A08: Mediator structure (HRM → attitudes →
  performance) confirmed theoretically (A11 SLR) and empirically
  (A04, A08). Strongest combined evidence for thesis mediation model.

KEY: Only paper in corpus to explicitly operationalise all three
  thesis theoretical frameworks simultaneously.

First-mention tracker updates:
  - AMO Model (Appelbaum et al., 2000): first explicit AMO in corpus;
    Ability + Motivation + Opportunity → performance
  - Social Exchange Theory / SET (Blau, 1964): first explicit SET;
    reciprocal exchange mechanism
  - Healthcare HRM SLR (PRISMA; 30 studies): first healthcare-
    specific systematic review in corpus
  - Nigeria healthcare empirical evidence: 5 studies cited

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Achief, G. O., Ezenekwe, J. E., Ajayi, A. B., Adewara, D. E.,
Oluwalusi, A. O., & Abeyie, K. S. (2025). The impact of human
resource management practices on employee performance in the
healthcare sector: A literature review. African Journal of Politics
and Administrative Studies, 18(3), 534–561.
https://doi.org/10.4314/ajpas.v18i3.29

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A12
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A12
Cite key        : Ihejirika2025
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management systems — HR policies: staff
                  orientation, employee welfare, work-life balance),
                  T2 (employee performance/productivity as DV),
                  T3 (work-life balance as flexibility construct —
                  partial T3 coverage), T5 (empirical: HR policies
                  → employee performance), T7 (Nigeria — Enugu State
                  Teaching Hospital)
Title           : Impact of human resource policies on employee
                  performance: Evidence from Enugu State Teaching
                  Hospital
Authors         : Ihejirika, N. O., Agbo, I. C., & Nnabuchi, H. C.
Year            : 2025 (received September 2025; published November 2025)
                  [CSV records 2024 — actual year 2025; cite as 2025]
Journal         : Direct Research Journal of Public Health and
                  Environmental Technology
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 10, No. 3, pp. 88–100
DOI             : 10.26765/DRJPHET17471914I
                  [Missing from CSV; confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Publisher       : Direct Research Publisher / AJOL
Affiliation     : Department of Public Administration & Local Government,
                  University of Nigeria, Nsukka, Enugu State, Nigeria

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; survey; cross-sectional
Aim             : To examine the impact of HR policies (staff orientation,
                  employee welfare, work-life balance) on employee
                  performance at Enugu State Teaching Hospital
Setting/context : Enugu State University Teaching Hospital (ESUTH),
                  Parklane, Enugu State, Nigeria — healthcare sector
Constructs      : IVs: Three HR policy dimensions:
                    (1) Staff Orientation Policies (SOP)
                    (2) Employee Welfare Policies (EWP)
                    (3) Work-Life Balance Policies (WLB)
                  DVs:
                    (1) Employee Punctuality
                    (2) Quality of Work
                    (3) Employee Task Performance
Theoretical lens: Human Capital Theory (Becker, 1964; Schultz, 1961);
                  Equity Theory (Adams, 1965);
                  Expectancy Theory (Vroom, 1964);
                  Goal-Setting Theory (Locke, 1968)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Survey research design; cross-sectional; quantitative
Population      : 472 staff (305 junior + 167 senior) at ESUTH Parklane
Sample          : 217 (Yamane 1967 formula; e=0.05)
Sampling        : Convenience sampling
Instrument      : Structured questionnaire; 5-point Likert scale;
                  5 items per dimension (15 items total)
Reliability     : Test-retest r = 0.85 ✓; Cronbach's α ≥ 0.70
                  (stated as threshold; specific α not reported)
Validity        : Content validation by 2 measurement/evaluation experts
Analysis        : Descriptive statistics (mean, SD); simple linear
                  regression; ANOVA

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1: Staff orientation → employee punctuality
  Descriptive: Grand mean = 3.2 (accepted)
  Regression: β = 0.896, t = 8.239, R = 0.333, R² = 0.111,
  F(1,183) = 16.556, p = 0.000 — H0 rejected ✓

H2: Employee welfare → quality of work
  Descriptive: Grand mean = 4.3 (accepted)
  Regression: β = 0.559, t = 9.729, R = 0.342, R² = 0.117,
  F(1,183) = 17.655, p = 0.000 — H0 rejected ✓

H3: Work-life balance → task performance
  Descriptive: Grand mean = 4.4 (accepted)
  Regression: β = 1.034, t = 49.856, R = 0.125, R² = 0.016,
  F(1,183) = 2.126, p = 0.147 — ANOMALY: F-test p = 0.147
  (not significant at 0.05), yet authors reject H0.
  Extremely low R² (1.6%) confirms WLB has negligible
  explanatory power for task performance in this study.

  [METHODOLOGICAL FLAG: H3 p-value inconsistency — abstract
  states "p > 0.05" for all hypotheses, but claims rejection
  of H0. ANOVA F = 2.126, p = 0.147 for H3 indicates WLB
  is NOT a significant predictor. Do not cite H3 as confirming
  WLB → performance relationship. H1 and H2 findings are
  robust.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Provides operational definitions of HR policies in
               Nigerian healthcare: staff orientation, employee welfare,
               work-life balance. All three map onto management systems
               sub-constructs. Work-life balance policies explicitly
               include flexible scheduling — partial T3 contribution.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Nigeria healthcare empirical study — directly analogous
               to GGHN context. H1 (orientation → punctuality β=0.896)
               and H2 (welfare → quality of work β=0.559) are the
               most Nigeria-specific HR policy → employee performance
               coefficients in the corpus. Pairs with A10 (Enugu
               ministries) and A11 (Nigeria healthcare SLR) for the
               strongest Nigeria-specific empirical basis.

Ch. 2.2 use  : Four theoretical frameworks applied (HCT, Equity,
               Expectancy, Goal-Setting). HCT is primary — consistent
               with A04 and A05. Equity Theory and Expectancy Theory
               add motivational dimension not yet covered in corpus.

Statement of Problem: Confirms that Nigerian healthcare workers
               face HR policy deficiencies (inadequate compensation,
               poor welfare, lack of work-life balance) directly
               mirroring GGHN's operational context.

H3 note for Ch. 2.3: The non-significant direct effect of WLB on task
               performance (R²=0.016, p=0.147) aligns with A07
               (Alexandro2025) null finding for hybrid work → productivity.
               Together they support the thesis argument that work
               flexibility/hybrid work does not directly drive
               performance — a mediating mechanism is required.

Limitation   : Convenience sampling (not random); Cronbach's α
               threshold stated but specific values not reported;
               H3 interpretation is methodologically flawed;
               DOI missing from shortlist CSV; journal is lower-tier
               than others in corpus.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Nigeria healthcare — Enugu State Teaching Hospital; directly
      analogous to GGHN's operational context
  [+] H1 (β=0.896) and H2 (β=0.559) robust; ANOVA p=0.000
  [+] Yamane formula; test-retest reliability r=0.85; expert validation
  [+] Work-life balance as a HR policy construct with defined indicators
  [+] Four theoretical frameworks applied
  [~] Convenience sampling; n=217
  [~] Cronbach's alpha not reported numerically (threshold stated only)
  [~] H3 result (WLB → task performance) is methodologically
      problematic — p=0.147 yet authors claim significance
  [~] DOI missing from CSV; lower-tier journal
  [-] H3 interpretation is unreliable — use only H1 and H2 findings

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with A10 (Ishiwu2024) and A11 (Achief2025):
  Three-paper Nigeria cluster: A10 = Enugu State ministries;
  A11 = Nigeria healthcare SLR; A12 = Enugu State Teaching Hospital.
  Together: the strongest available Nigeria-specific management
  systems → employee performance evidence base for this thesis.

Convergence with A07 (Alexandro2025):
  Both find non-significant direct effects for work flexibility/
  WLB → performance (A07: H5/H6 p>0.05; A12: H3 p=0.147).
  Cross-national convergence (Indonesia + Nigeria) for the null
  direct effect — supports the thesis mediation argument.

Unique contribution: Only paper in corpus set in a Nigeria teaching
  hospital. Closest contextual parallel to GGHN (Nigeria-based
  global health NGO) found in Block A.

First-mention tracker updates:
  - Equity Theory (Adams, 1965): first appearance in corpus;
    fairness of HR inputs/outcomes as motivation driver
  - Expectancy Theory (Vroom, 1964): first explicit Vroom citation
    (referenced in A03 Marie2024 but not as primary framework)
  - Staff Orientation Policies: first onboarding-as-management-
    systems construct in corpus
  - Enugu State Teaching Hospital / Nigeria healthcare: most
    contextually proximate setting to GGHN in entire corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ihejirika, N. O., Agbo, I. C., & Nnabuchi, H. C. (2025). Impact of
human resource policies on employee performance: Evidence from Enugu
State Teaching Hospital. Direct Research Journal of Public Health and
Environmental Technology, 10(3), 88–100.
https://doi.org/10.26765/DRJPHET17471914I

Journal         : NG Journal of Social Development
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 19, Issue 1 (2026)
ISSN            : 0189-5958 (print); 2814-1105 (e)
DOI             : 10.4314/ngjsd.v19i1.7 ✓ (confirmed from CSV)
Open access     : Yes (AJOL)
Publisher       : AJOL
Affiliations    : Department of Management Studies, Tanzania Institute
                  of Accountancy (2 authors)

NOTE ON TIMELINE: Article received January 2026; accepted
February 2026; field data collected May 2026. The date
sequencing is anomalous (data collected after acceptance) —
possible that field data table headers refer to a different
phase or are mislabelled. The DOI and journal are confirmed;
year 2026 is within the reference window.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Descriptive; mixed-methods (quantitative + qualitative)
Aim             : To examine the cost-effectiveness of PEPMIS (Public
                  Employees' Performance Management Information System)
                  on employee performance appraisal in selected
                  Tanzanian Local Government Authorities (LGAs)
Setting/context : Selected LGAs in Tanzania (local government staff,
                  councillors, teachers); comparison of PEPMIS
                  (digital) vs OPRAS (paper-based) performance
                  management systems
Constructs      : IV: PEPMIS (digital performance management
                  information system) — operationalised as cost-
                  effectiveness of performance appraisal
                  DVs: Employee performance (productivity, motivation,
                       accountability); cost reduction; timeliness of
                       service delivery
Theoretical lens: Goal-Setting Theory (Locke, 1968; Latham, 1990) —
                  digital feedback mechanisms → performance outcomes

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Descriptive; mixed-methods; cross-sectional
Sample          : 156 respondents (local government staff n=50;
                  councillors n=30; teachers n=76); stratified random
                  sampling
Data collection : Semi-structured interviews + secondary data (PEPMIS
                  reports, policy documents, prior studies)
Analysis        : Quantitative — SPSS (descriptive/inferential);
                  Qualitative — thematic analysis
Triangulation   : Pre-testing, expert review, data triangulation
Ethics          : Informed consent; confidentiality; voluntary participation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. Cost reduction: 50% of respondents reported cost reduction through
   PEPMIS; comparison of actual costs:
   OPRAS (paper-based): Tshs. 3,960,000 (paper, ink, training)
   PEPMIS (digital): Tshs. 3,000,000 (internet, training/orientation)
   → Net saving: Tshs. 960,000 per LGA per cycle (~24% reduction)

2. Usefulness: 78 (50%) found PEPMIS very useful for improving work
   performance and service delivery; 50 (78 — likely error; probably
   32%) found it useful

3. Timely service delivery: Confirmed improvement in timely provision
   of annual leave, loans, and other HR services integrated into PEPMIS
   (previously handled in isolation under OPRAS)

4. Employee performance outcomes: PEPMIS facilitates cost-reduction in
   performance evaluation, enhances motivation, and increases overall
   institutional performance

5. Remaining challenges: inadequate internet and electricity access;
   delayed feedback; insufficient training; perceptions of bias;
   limited technical support — consistent with broader Tanzania
   PEPMIS literature

6. Conclusion: PEPMIS is cost-effective and improves performance
   management in LGAs, but implementation quality and feedback
   regularity require improvement

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Provides operational definition and illustration of
               PEPMIS as a Management Information System (MIS)
               specifically designed for performance management in
               public sector. Demonstrates how digital MIS translates
               into performance appraisal practice — extends A01
               (Raja2025 HRIS → PM theoretical link) with African
               empirical evidence of a functional MIS → performance
               appraisal system.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Empirical evidence from Tanzania LGAs (contextually
               similar to GGHN's East African public sector setting)
               that digital performance management information systems
               reduce costs and improve employee performance outcomes.
               Quantified cost comparison (PEPMIS vs OPRAS) is a
               concrete management systems effectiveness metric.
               Pairs with A09 (Mwakiluma2024 — also Tanzania LGA) for
               strong Tanzania public sector evidence base.

Statement of Problem: Implementation challenges (internet access,
               training gaps, feedback quality) in Tanzania LGAs
               mirror GGHN's operational constraints — supports
               thesis Statement of Problem narrative.

Limitation   : Data anomaly (field data dated May 2026 but received
               January 2026 — sequencing inconsistency); small sample
               (n=156); single country; no regression coefficients;
               cost comparison uses only one LGA's figures; no
               Cronbach's alpha; teacher-heavy sample (n=76/156);
               no direct hybrid work component.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Tanzania LGA public sector — contextually comparable to GGHN
  [+] Concrete cost-comparison data (PEPMIS vs OPRAS) — unique
      quantified management systems cost-effectiveness evidence
  [+] Mixed-methods (quantitative + qualitative triangulation)
  [+] Digital MIS operationalised as performance management tool
  [+] Goal-Setting Theory applied — consistent with A10, A13
  [~] Data timeline anomaly (field data May 2026 > accepted Feb 2026)
  [~] n=156; stratified but teacher-dominated; one LGA cost data
  [~] No Cronbach's alpha; no regression; descriptive only
  [~] CSV year discrepancy (2025 vs actual 2026)
  [~] Lower-tier journal; 2 authors, same institution

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with A09 (Mwakiluma2024):
  Both are Tanzania LGA public sector empirical studies. A09 tests
  MCS → employee performance (multiple regression, R²=0.871);
  A13 tests PEPMIS cost-effectiveness on employee performance
  (descriptive, mixed-methods). Together: the Tanzania LGA evidence
  pair — contextually very close to GGHN setting.

Convergence with A01 (Raja2025) and A02 (Bogale2021):
  Both A01 (HRIS → PM theoretical) and A02 (HMIS implementation
  Ethiopia) address the IT/MIS → performance management relationship;
  A13 is the only paper in corpus showing a quantified cost
  comparison between digital and paper-based performance management
  systems — a unique methodological contribution.

First-mention tracker updates:
  - PEPMIS (Public Employees' Performance Management Information
    System): first digital public-sector PMS case study in corpus
  - Cost-effectiveness comparison (digital vs paper PMS): unique
    quantified metric — PEPMIS saves ~24% vs OPRAS
  - Tanzania LGA digital PMS evidence: complements A09 MCS study

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Mtasigazya, P., & Bandapile, I. H. (2026). Cost effectiveness of
PEPMIS on employee performance appraisal in Tanzania: A case of
selected local government authorities. NG Journal of Social
Development, 19(1). https://doi.org/10.4314/ngjsd.v19i1.7
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A14
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A14
Cite key        : Pudjono2025
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management systems — PMS taxonomy, types,
                  and challenges in local government; IMPACT framework),
                  T2 (workforce productivity as performance indicator),
                  T5 (empirical/theoretical: PMS → organisational
                  performance in local government)
Title           : Advancing local governance: a systematic review of
                  performance management systems
Authors         : Pudjono, A. N. S., Wibisono, D., & Fatima, I.
Year            : 2025 (published online 19 December 2024)
Journal         : Cogent Business & Management
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 12, No. 1, Article 2442545
DOI             : 10.1080/23311975.2024.2442545 ✓ (confirmed from CSV)
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Publisher       : Taylor & Francis (Cogent OA)
Affiliations    : School of Business and Management, Institut Teknologi
                  Bandung, Indonesia; Faculty of Civil and Environmental
                  Engineering, Institut Teknologi Bandung, Indonesia
Funded by       : Indonesia Endowment Fund for Education (LPDP)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Systematic literature review (SLR); 122 studies;
                  Scopus database; 2000–2023
Aim             : To analyse trends in PMS for local governments;
                  present IMPACT framework as strategic response to
                  PMS implementation challenges
Setting/context : Local governments globally (North America, Europe,
                  Asia, Oceania, Africa, South America); literature
                  review not primary data
Constructs      : PMS types reviewed: Traditional appraisal, 360°
                  feedback, MBO, Balanced Scorecard, KPIs, Continuous
                  PM, OKRs
                  Performance indicators: financial efficiency, workforce
                  productivity, staff development, strategic alignment,
                  governance/risk management
Theoretical lens: New Public Management (NPM); Balanced Scorecard;
                  IMPACT Framework (Integrative, Multi-participatory,
                  Productive, Adaptive, Contextual, Transformative)
                  [Note: No management theory (AMO/SET/COR/HCT)
                  explicitly applied — public administration lens only]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Systematic literature review; Scopus database search
Search string   : TITLE-ABS-KEY ('performance management' OR
                  'performance measurement' AND 'local government')
Initial records : 519; narrowed to 124 (economics/business); final
                  122 articles after full-text screening
Date window     : 2000–2023
Geography       : Global: North America (29), Europe (48), Asia (48),
                  Oceania (18), Africa (6 — very limited), South America (2)
Methodology mix : Qualitative 40%; Quantitative 34%; Mixed 15%;
                  Conceptual/other 19%

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. PMS research phases:
   2000–2010: Citizen participation, service quality, cultural influence
   2010–2015: Growth; NPM reforms; data analytics; sustainability
   2015–2023: Transition; digital governance; sustainability focus

2. Regional variation:
   - North America: mature PMS; performance data + employee motivation
   - Europe: benchmarking (UK), organisational culture (Italy)
   - Asia: bureaucratic reform (Indonesia), policy in China
   - Africa: early-stage PMS; only 6 studies total; South Africa focus

3. Performance management indicators (relevant to thesis):
   - Workforce productivity (employee output per employee)
   - Staff development and training investment
   - Internal process optimisation
   - Strategic goal alignment

4. PMS types and challenges — all seven types reviewed show trade-offs
   between complexity and adaptability; no single universal PMS

5. Four influence factors on PMS outcomes:
   - Regulatory and compliance-driven factors
   - Stakeholder and community engagement
   - Organisational and management characteristics
   - Innovation and adaptation in management practices

6. IMPACT Framework proposed: Integrative, Multi-participatory,
   Productive, Adaptive, Contextual, Transformative — 6-dimension
   PMS response designed for Indonesian local government but
   applicable globally

7. Africa gap: Only 6 studies in entire 122-article corpus — major
   research gap; findings not representative of African LGA context

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Definitive taxonomy of PMS types in local government
               (7 types); PMS indicators framework (Table 4) provides
               structured operationalisation for thesis management
               systems construct. Staff development, workforce
               productivity, and strategic goal alignment are directly
               usable indicators. The IMPACT framework's adaptive and
               contextual dimensions apply to GGHN's resource-
               constrained operating environment.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Empirical map of global PMS research; identifies the
               Africa gap (only 6 studies in 122) — directly supports
               the thesis rationale that research on management systems
               in African organisations is underrepresented, justifying
               the GGHN study.

Statement of Problem: "PMS development in Africa and South America
               is still in its early stages" (p. 5) — direct citation
               for the research gap in Statement of Problem Para 3–4.

Limitation   : Indonesia-centric framework; no African data in primary
               corpus; literature review not primary study; no
               management theories (AMO/SET/COR) applied; 2000–2023
               window includes pre-2021 studies (acceptable for SLR).

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] 122 studies; rigorous Scopus search; clear PRISMA-style process
  [+] 11 citations (2024/2025) — strong uptake for a Dec 2024 paper
  [+] 9,360 article views — high engagement
  [+] Definitive PMS taxonomy and indicator framework for local govt
  [+] Explicitly identifies Africa PMS research gap — thesis rationale
  [+] Open access; Taylor & Francis (Cogent B&M); well-structured
  [~] Indonesia/ITB affiliation — framework optimised for Indonesian
      context; not Africa
  [~] Only 6 Africa studies in corpus — limited African evidence
  [~] No management theory (AMO/SET/COR/HCT) — public admin lens only
  [~] Literature window 2000–2023 — pre-2021 studies included in SLR

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with A01 (Raja2025):
  Both are SLRs of management systems literature; A01 reviews HRIS →
  PM (global, 41 studies); A14 reviews PMS in local government (122
  studies). Together: comprehensive management systems literature map
  for Ch. 2.1 and 2.3 — A01 for HRIS/MIS dimension; A14 for PMS
  in public sector/local government.

Convergence with A09 (Mwakiluma2024) and A13 (Mtasigazya2026):
  A14 establishes the global PMS taxonomy that A09 and A13 apply
  empirically in Tanzania. Reading order for Ch. 2.3: A14 → A09 → A13.

KEY CONTRIBUTION: Only paper in corpus to provide a global PMS
  taxonomy for local government with explicit research gap
  identification for Africa — provides authoritative literature base
  for the GGHN study's research rationale.

First-mention tracker updates:
  - PMS global taxonomy (7 types): traditional appraisal / 360°
    feedback / MBO / BSC / KPIs / continuous PM / OKRs
  - IMPACT Framework: Integrative, Multi-participatory, Productive,
    Adaptive, Contextual, Transformative — 6-dimension strategic PMS
  - Africa PMS research gap: only 6/122 studies — explicit gap
    identification supporting thesis research rationale
  - NPM (New Public Management): theoretical lens for public sector
    performance management globally

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Pudjono, A. N. S., Wibisono, D., & Fatima, I. (2025). Advancing local
governance: A systematic review of performance management systems.
Cogent Business & Management, 12(1), Article 2442545.
https://doi.org/10.1080/23311975.2024.2442545

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A15
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A15
Cite key        : Asghar2023
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management systems — managerial skills (OMS)
                  and technology adaptation (MIS) as constructs),
                  T2 (employee productivity / firm performance as DV),
                  T5 (empirical: management systems → productivity via
                  mediation), T8 (mediation: managerial skills/tech →
                  process/product innovation → performance)
Title           : Managerial skills, technology adaptation and firm
                  performance: Mediating role of process innovation
                  and product innovation
Authors         : Asghar, N., Khattak, A., Danish, M. H., Bokhari, I. H.,
                  & Waris, M.
Year            : 2023 ✓ within reference window
Journal         : Cogent Business & Management
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 10, No. 3, Article 2281485
DOI             : 10.1080/23311975.2023.2281485 ✓ (confirmed from CSV)
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; PLS-SEM (SmartPLS)
Aim             : To analyse how managerial skills and technology
                  adaptation affect firm productivity/performance through
                  process and product innovation as mediators
Setting/context : 150 manufacturing SMEs in Lahore, Pakistan;
                  managers as respondents; purposive sampling
Constructs      : IVs: Managerial Skills (MS, 14 items);
                       Technology Adaptation (TA, 14 items);
                       Managerial Role (MR); Training
                  Mediators: Process Innovation (PI);
                             Product Innovation (PRI)
                  DV: Productive Performance (PP, 6 items)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Direct effects on Productive Performance:
  Technology Adaptation: β=0.450, p=0.000 (STRONGEST direct effect)
  Managerial Skills:     β=0.189, p=0.028
  Process Innovation:    β=0.145, p=0.006
  Product Innovation:    β=0.141, p=0.034
  Training:              β=0.049, p=0.112 (NOT significant)
  Managerial Role:       β=0.034, p=0.696 (NOT significant)

Significant mediation:
  Process Innovation mediates MS → PP (β=0.079, p=0.012)
  Product Innovation mediates MR → PP (β=0.092, p=0.043)

All reliability thresholds met: MS α=0.958; TA α=0.967; PP α=0.866

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Managerial Skills (14-item OMS construct) and
               Technology Adaptation (14-item MIS construct) provide
               the most detailed operationalisation of Dr Balogun's
               MIS/OMS taxonomy in the corpus.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Technology Adaptation β=0.450 = second-largest tech
               systems → performance coefficient in corpus. Mediation
               via process innovation supports the thesis argument that
               management systems → productivity requires an intervening
               mechanism. Training non-significance (p=0.112) adds to
               seven-study cross-national pattern.

Limitation   : Pakistan manufacturing context; not Africa/public sector;
               no explicit management theory; cross-sectional.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Full PLS-SEM mediation model; 22 hypotheses tested; SmartPLS
  [+] 23 citations; 7,635 views — strong traction
  [+] Both OMS (managerial skills) and MIS (tech adaptation) constructs
  [+] All reliability thresholds met (α>0.7 ✓; AVE>0.5 ✓; HTMT<0.85 ✓)
  [~] Pakistan manufacturing — not Africa/health/public sector
  [~] No explicit management theory

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Asghar, N., Khattak, A., Danish, M. H., Bokhari, I. H., & Waris, M.
(2023). Managerial skills, technology adaptation and firm performance:
Mediating role of process innovation and product innovation. Cogent
Business & Management, 10(3), Article 2281485.
https://doi.org/10.1080/23311975.2023.2281485
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A16
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A16
Cite key        : Mutegi2023
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T2 (employee productivity as DV — objective
                  tri-dimensional: productive time, task accomplishment,
                  value added), T5 (empirical: safety management →
                  employee productivity), T7 (Africa — Kenya)
                  [NOTE: T1 NOT assigned — workplace safety ≠ MIS/OMS/
                  PMS construct; paper is PERIPHERAL to management
                  systems construct; cite for T2 DV definition only]
Title           : Workplace safety and employee productivity of
                  manufacturing firms in Kenya
Authors         : Mutegi, T. M., Joshua, P. M., & Kinyua, J. M.
Year            : 2023 ✓ within reference window
Journal         : Cogent Business & Management
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 10, No. 2, Article 2215569
DOI             : 10.1080/23311975.2023.2215569 ✓ (confirmed from CSV)
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : University of Embu, School of Business and Economics,
                  Kenya

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  multiple OLS regression
Aim             : To determine the effect of workplace safety programmes
                  on employee productivity in Kenyan manufacturing firms
Setting/context : 124 manufacturing firms across 14 sub-sectors in
                  Kenya; HR managers as respondents; 108 final usable
Constructs      : IVs: Workplace safety programmes — four dimensions:
                    X1: Safety Ergonomics
                    X2: Emergency Management
                    X3: Safety Training
                    X4: Safety Transfer (insurance/consultants)
                  DV: Employee Productivity — three objective measures:
                    (1) Productive Time (lost workdays from incidents)
                    (2) Degree of Accomplishment of Tasks
                    (3) Value Added (revenues / employees)
Theoretical lens: Domino Theory (Heinrich, 1931) — safety incidents
                  result from chains; removing unsafe conditions
                  improves productivity

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Index model: R²=0.79, F(4,103)=101.421, p=0.000
  X1 Ergonomics: β=0.579, p=0.021
  X2 Emergency: β=0.765, p=0.003 (strongest)
  X3 Safety Training: β=0.204, p=0.007
  X4 Safety Transfer: β=0.308, p=0.016

Correlation: Safety → Productive Time r=0.912 (strongest)
             Safety → Value Added r=0.866
             Safety → Task Accomplishment r=0.769

Mean: 24 lost workdays/year from safety incidents; value added
Ksh 3,090,580/employee/year (below African average of Ksh ~equivalent
to USD 3,300 per employee)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
SCOPE FLAG: PERIPHERAL — workplace safety ≠ management systems.
  Do NOT cite for T1 (management systems constructs) or as evidence
  that management systems drive productivity.

Ch. 2.1 use  : Employee productivity ONLY — the objective tri-
               dimensional model (productive time, task accomplishment,
               value added) is the most concrete EP operationalisation
               in corpus. Cite for DV definition.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Limited — Africa (Kenya) context; productivity data;
               confirms multi-dimensional EP measurement.

Recommendation: One citation maximum; for EP operationalisation only.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Objective productivity measures (firm-level data) — highest
      objectivity in corpus for T2 measurement
  [+] Africa (Kenya); 124 firms; 14 sub-sectors; 17 citations
  [+] R²=0.79; full OLS diagnostics
  [~] Topic mismatch — workplace safety ≠ management systems
  [~] Domino Theory not applicable to thesis
  [~] Cronbach's alpha absent; manufacturing sector only

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Mutegi, T. M., Joshua, P. M., & Kinyua, J. M. (2023). Workplace safety
and employee productivity of manufacturing firms in Kenya. Cogent
Business & Management, 10(2), Article 2215569.
https://doi.org/10.1080/23311975.2023.2215569

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      A17
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : A17
Cite key        : Saputra2024
Block           : A — Management Systems + Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management systems — capacity building as
                  management system; HRM, knowledge management,
                  technology adoption as constructs), T2 (organisational
                  performance/productivity as DV), T4 (theoretical
                  frameworks — Human Capital Theory, HRM theory,
                  Dynamic Capabilities, Innovation Theory), T5
                  (empirical/theoretical: capacity building → org
                  performance via PRISMA SLR)
Title           : Capacity building for organisational performance:
                  A systematic review, conceptual framework, and future
                  research directions
Authors         : Saputra, N., Putera, R. E., Zetra, A., Azwar,
                  Valentina, T. R., & Mulia, R. A.
Year            : 2024 (received July 2024; accepted November 2024;
                  published December 2024) ✓ within reference window
Journal         : Cogent Business & Management
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 11, No. 1, Article 2434966
DOI             : 10.1080/23311975.2024.2434966 ✓ (confirmed from CSV)
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Publisher       : Taylor & Francis (Cogent OA)
Affiliations    : Universitas Andalas, Padang, Indonesia; STISIP Imam
                  Bonjol, Padang, Indonesia (Public Administration)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Systematic literature review (PRISMA); bibliometric
                  analysis (VOSviewer); 50 articles (2014–2024);
                  Scopus database
Aim             : To present a comprehensive conceptual framework for
                  enhancing organisational performance capacity in
                  public sector organisations through synthesis of
                  key drivers and outcomes
Setting/context : Global literature review; public sector focus;
                  Indonesia context explicit; post-pandemic and
                  digitalisation themes
Constructs      : Five DRIVERS of capacity building identified:
                    (1) Innovation (process + service)
                    (2) Human Resource Management (recruitment,
                        training, diversity management)
                    (3) Knowledge Management (collection, sharing,
                        application)
                    (4) Sustainability (CSR + sustainable leadership)
                    (5) Technology (digitalisaton + technology adoption)
                  OUTCOMES of capacity building:
                    Economic: efficiency, effectiveness
                    Organisational: skills, satisfaction
                    Development: image/sustainability, productivity,
                                 quality
Theoretical lens: Human Capital Theory; HRM theory; Innovation Theory;
                  Knowledge Management Theory; Dynamic Capabilities;
                  Digital Transformation Theory; Sustainable Leadership;
                  Diversity Management Theory

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. Five capacity-building drivers (Innovation, HRM, Knowledge,
   Sustainability, Technology) are the primary determinants of
   organisational performance capacity in the public sector

2. Digital transformation and technology adoption are consistently
   the most frequently co-occurring keywords with organisational
   performance (2017–2022 bibliometric mapping)

3. Human Capital Theory confirmed: investing in employee skills and
   development is critical for organisational performance capacity

4. HRM practices (recruitment, training, diversity) significantly
   contribute to workforce competency and motivation — organisational
   performance outcome

5. Knowledge management — effective collection, retention, and
   application of information enhances adaptability and
   decision-making

6. Public sector specificity: bureaucratic institutional capabilities
   and technology integration are critical in dynamic operational
   environments — directly relevant to GGHN context

7. Post-pandemic context: COVID-19 accelerated need for organisational
   performance capacity — 2020 saw highest publication spike (9 of 50)

8. Africa/developing country gap: research focus heavily concentrated
   in USA, China, Malaysia — developing countries underrepresented
   (India 3, Indonesia 3, Nigeria cited in agricultural research only)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Capacity building framework provides a five-driver
               taxonomy (Innovation, HRM, Knowledge, Sustainability,
               Technology) that maps directly onto management systems
               constructs. HRM + Technology = MIS/OMS sub-constructs
               per Dr Balogun. The IMPACT-analogous framework supports
               the thesis conceptual model for management systems.

Ch. 2.2 use  : Eight theoretical frameworks synthesised — Human Capital
               Theory, HRM Theory, Innovation Theory, Dynamic
               Capabilities, Knowledge Management Theory all present.
               Complements A11 (Achief2025 — AMO/RBV/SET) and A04
               (Nor2025 — HCT). Together they provide comprehensive
               theoretical coverage for Ch. 2.2.

Ch. 2.3 use  : 50-study evidence base linking capacity-building
               practices to organisational performance outcomes (skills,
               satisfaction, productivity, quality, efficiency) provides
               broad empirical foundation for Ch. 2.3 narrative.
               Developing country and public sector gaps support GGHN
               study rationale.

Statement of Problem: "Studies in developing countries remain very
               limited" — directly citeable for the research gap
               justification in Statement of Problem.

Limitation   : Indonesia/Southeast Asia focus; Scopus only; 50 studies
               relatively small for bibliometric; no Africa-specific
               empirical data; no primary research; conceptual
               framework not yet validated empirically.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] PRISMA framework + bibliometric analysis (VOSviewer) — dual
      methodological approach; rigorous for a SLR
  [+] 42 citations; 12,231 views (Dec 2024) — highest citation
      count in the entire corpus; very high uptake
  [+] Five-driver capacity-building framework maps directly onto
      thesis management systems constructs (HRM + Technology = MIS/OMS)

  [~] 50 studies — relatively limited for bibliometric analysis

  A17 (broad capacity/performance framework) → A14 (PMS taxonomy)
