```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B04
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B04
Cite key        : Nweze2026
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — structured hybrid work systems:
                  management-defined schedules, technological support,
                  communication systems), T6 (empirical: structured
                  hybrid work → employee performance, Nigeria public
                  sector), T7 (Africa — Nigeria; Ebonyi State; public-
                  owned media corporations)
Title           : Structured hybrid work systems and employee
                  performance in public-owned media corporations
                  in Ebonyi State
Authors         : Nweze, C. I., Nwekpa, K. C., Umahi, R., &
                  Igboji, H. O.
Year            : 2026 (submitted April 2026; accepted April 2026)
Journal         : IOSR Journal of Business and Management (IOSR-JBM)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 28, Issue 4, Ser. 1, pp. 76–84
DOI             : 10.9790/487X-2804017683
                  [Confirmed by UBANDIYA: https://doi.org/10.9790/487X-2804017683]
Open access     : Yes (IOSR open-access)
Affiliations    : Ebonyi State University, Abakaliki, Nigeria

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; descriptive survey; regression
Aim             : To examine the relationship between structured hybrid
                  work systems and employee performance in public-owned
                  media corporations in Ebonyi State, Nigeria
Setting/context : Public-owned media corporations in Ebonyi State,
                  Nigeria; administrative, technical, and field staff
Constructs      : IVs: Structured Hybrid Work Systems — three
                  components:
                    (1) Management-Defined Work Schedules (SHWS)
                    (2) Technological Support
                    (3) Communication Systems
                  DV: Employee Performance (productivity, quality,
                      timeliness)
Theoretical lens: Work Design Theory (Hackman & Oldham, 1976) — task
                  clarity, feedback, autonomy → motivation → performance;
                  Technology Acceptance Model (TAM; Davis, 1989) —
                  perceived usefulness and ease of use → technology
                  adoption → performance

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Descriptive survey; cross-sectional; quantitative
Population      : 250 employees across selected public media corporations
Sample          : 152 (Raosoft calculator); stratified random sampling;
                  148 returned (97.4% response rate)
Instrument      : Structured questionnaire; 5-point Likert scale
Reliability     : Test-retest r > 0.70 (50 respondents from Enugu
                  State media organisations as pilot)
                  [NOTE: test-retest method, not Cronbach's α]
Validity        : Expert evaluation (face/content validity)
Analysis        : Descriptive statistics (mean, SD); simple linear
                  regression (separate model per IV); SPSS v.25; α=0.05

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Descriptive (items, mean):
  Clear management-defined schedules: mean = 4.32
  Adequate technological tools: mean = 4.05
  Effective communication systems: mean = 4.18
  Task completion efficiency: mean = 4.21
  Performance under HWS: mean = 4.10
  Quality and timeliness: mean = 4.25

Regression results (all p = 0.000; p < 0.05):
  H1: Structured HWS → Employee performance:
      B = 0.612, β = 0.658, t = 6.73, p = 0.000 ✓ (STRONGEST)
  H2: Technological Support → Employee performance:
      B = 0.488, β = 0.542, t = 5.88, p = 0.000 ✓
  H3: Communication Systems → Employee performance:
      B = 0.410, β = 0.497, t = 5.39, p = 0.000 ✓

Key conclusions:
1. Structured HWS (management-defined schedules) is the strongest
   predictor of employee performance (β=0.658)
2. Technological support and communication systems both significantly
   enhance performance under hybrid arrangements
3. Unlike flexible hybrid systems, structured/management-controlled
   hybrid systems improve accountability and clarity
4. Effective hybrid systems require three pillars: oversight +
   technology + communication

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Structured hybrid work systems defined as management-
               controlled work arrangements — directly operationalises
               the thesis's hybrid work mediator construct from the
               management systems perspective. Three components
               (schedules, technology, communication) map onto the
               thesis's management systems IV sub-constructs.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Strongest regression coefficient for management-
               structured hybrid work → employee performance in the
               entire corpus (β=0.658, Nigeria public sector).
               This directly supports the thesis claim that management
               systems (structured oversight + technology + comms)
               mediate the hybrid work → productivity relationship.
               Note: this paper frames management systems AS PART OF
               hybrid work, while the thesis frames them as mediators;
               the coefficients are still directionally supportive.

Ch. 2.2 use  : Work Design Theory + TAM combination — Work Design
               Theory (Hackman & Oldham, 1976) is a first appearance
               in corpus; TAM is now third mention (A06, B02, B04).

Statement of Problem: Nigeria public sector media corporations;
               Ebonyi State — African public sector context proximate
               to GGHN's Nigeria operating environment.

Limitation   : Test-retest reliability (not Cronbach's α);
               IOSR-JBM is a lower-tier open-access journal;
               no DOI; separate regressions rather than a joint model
               (no multivariate R²); media corporations ≠ NGO/health;
               structured hybrid systems ≠ voluntary flexible
               arrangements; cross-sectional.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Empirical; n=148; 97.4% response rate; Nigeria public sector
  [+] β=0.658 for structured HWS → performance — highest hybrid
      work → performance coefficient in corpus
  [+] All three predictors significant (p=0.000); clear hypotheses
  [+] Public-owned corporations context proximate to GGHN
  [+] Work Design Theory + TAM — new theory combination in corpus
  [~] Test-retest reliability rather than Cronbach's α
  [~] IOSR-JBM is a lower-prestige journal; no DOI
  [~] Separate regressions (not multivariate); no joint R²
  [~] Structured (management-controlled) hybrid ≠ voluntary flexible
      hybrid — important conceptual distinction for thesis
  [~] Media corporations context; Ebonyi State only

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B03 (Ayogoi2026):
  Both are Nigeria empirical hybrid work → performance studies.
  B03 (telecoms, Bayelsa; n=123): work flexibility β=0.428 strongest
  B04 (public media, Ebonyi; n=148): structured HWS β=0.658 strongest
  The contrast is significant: B03 = flexibility-driven; B04 =
  management structure-driven. Together they suggest that BOTH
  flexibility AND management structure matter — the joint system
  (management systems mediating hybrid work) is the optimal model.

Convergence with B02 (Usman2025):
  B02's conceptual argument that hybrid systems are only effective
  when properly structured by management receives empirical support
  from B04's finding that management-defined schedules (β=0.658)
  outperform technological support (β=0.542) and communication
  (β=0.497).

KEY CORPUS FINDING: β=0.658 is the single largest hybrid work →
  employee performance coefficient in the corpus, surpassing even
  the Block A management systems coefficients (A09 ICS β=0.566).
  Management-structured hybrid work drives performance more strongly
  than technology or communication alone.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Structured hybrid work systems (management-defined schedules):
    distinct from flexible hybrid work; management-controlled;
    highest performance β in corpus (0.658)
  - Work Design Theory (Hackman & Oldham, 1976): first in corpus;
    task clarity, feedback, autonomy → motivation → performance
  - Public-owned media corporations, Ebonyi State, Nigeria: first
    public-sector Nigeria hybrid work empirical study in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Nweze, C. I., Nwekpa, K. C., Umahi, R., & Igboji, H. O. (2026).
Structured hybrid work systems and employee performance in public-owned
media corporations in Ebonyi State. IOSR Journal of Business and
Management, 28(4, Ser. 1), 76–84.
https://doi.org/10.9790/487X-2804017683
```

```
                  T4 (systematic literature review — PRISMA; 12 studies;
                  Scopus + WoS; 11 countries; 2020–2024),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — digital
                  infrastructure, leadership, WLB, organisational
                  readiness), T6 (narrative: hybrid/remote work →
                  SME productivity across diverse global contexts),
                  T7 (multi-region: China, Ghana, Lithuania, Italy,
                  Romania, Colombia, Ecuador, Malaysia, India, UK,
                  Slovenia — no Africa-specific primary data)
Title           : The impact of remote and hybrid work models on small
                  and medium-sized enterprises productivity: a
                  systematic literature review
Authors         : Maity, R., & Lee, K. L.
Year            : 2025 (received 7 July 2025; accepted 17 September
                  2025; published online 26 September 2025)
Journal         : SN Business & Economics
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 5 (article number not visible in supplied text)
DOI             : 10.1007/s43546-025-00931-7
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Faculty of Industrial Management, Universiti Malaysia
                  Pahang Al-Sultan Abdullah (UMPSA), Gambang, Pahang,
                  Malaysia
Funding         : Ministry of Higher Education Malaysia, Fundamental
                  Research Grant Scheme (FRGS/1/2023/SS02/UMP/02/6);
                  Universiti Malaysia Pahang Al-Sultan Abdullah RDU
                  research grant

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Systematic Literature Review (SLR); PRISMA 2020;
                  narrative synthesis; no meta-analysis
Aim             : To systematically review how remote and hybrid work
                  models affect SME productivity in the post-COVID-19
                  environment; to identify key enablers and barriers;
                  to compare outcomes across regions and economic
                  contexts; to propose a framework for SME hybrid work
                  adoption
Setting/context : Global SME sector (11 countries); post-COVID-19
                  focus; 2020–2024 publication window; primary studies
                  range from low-income (Ghana) to high-income (UK,
                  Italy) contexts
Constructs      : Independent/predictor: Flexible Work Arrangements
                    (FWA) — operationalised as remote work, hybrid
                    work, telecommuting, flextime, compressed workweeks
                  Outcome: SME productivity (firm-level and individual
                    employee); employee well-being as secondary outcome
                  Identified mediators/moderators (narrative):
                    (1) Digital infrastructure (IT systems, cloud tools,
                        cybersecurity protocols)
                    (2) Leadership and management practices
                        (trust-based, outcome-oriented, KPI-driven)
                    (3) Work-life balance and employee well-being
                    (4) Organisational readiness and culture
                    → These four are consolidated as strategic pillars
                      (Table 2) rather than formally modelled paths
Theoretical lens: No single formal theory applied; conceptual framework
                  draws on FWA literature, SDG discourse (SDG 8, SDG 13),
                  and JD-R-adjacent reasoning (job resources buffering
                  demands) without naming JD-R explicitly

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : PRISMA 2020 systematic literature review
Databases       : Scopus and Web of Science (WoS Core Collection)
Search window   : Publications 2020–2024
Initial results : 33 probable articles
After duplicates: 23 evaluated for screening (10 removed as duplicates)
Final sample    : 12 peer-reviewed studies retained
Quality screen  : PRISMA-P framework; title/abstract screening; bias
                  assessment; inclusion/exclusion criteria applied
Inclusion       : Peer-reviewed empirical studies (quantitative,
                  qualitative, mixed-methods); English and non-English;
                  directly addressing remote/hybrid work → SME
                  productivity; published 2020–2024
Exclusion       : Review articles, book chapters, books; news,
                  reports, dissertations; articles failing quality
                  criteria; closed-access articles
Countries in
final 12 papers : China (1), Slovenia (1), Ghana (1), Lithuania (1),
                  Italy (2), Romania (1), Colombia (1), Ecuador (1),
                  Malaysia (1), India (1), UK (1)
Sample sizes
in primary      : 20 (Malaysia qualitative) to 802 (Ecuador survey)
                  across the 12 studies
Reliability     : Not applicable — SLR; no primary data collected
Cronbach's α    : Not applicable — narrative synthesis
Data availability: No new datasets generated; all data from cited sources

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS (NARRATIVE SYNTHESIS)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. Hybrid model superiority: Hybrid work consistently emerged as the
   most effective FWA across the 12 studies, combining remote
   independence with collaborative in-person advantages. It is
   positioned as a "sustainable path" to productivity and flexibility.

2. Productivity improvements documented (selected primary evidence):
   - China (Liu 2023; n=50 SMEs): balanced hybrid model enhanced
     productivity and employee satisfaction
   - Slovenia (Kokot Tomaž 2023; n=100 IT SME employees): reduced
     commuting and flexible hours improved focus and output
   - Ghana (Peprah et al. 2020; n=183): flexible schedules raised job
     satisfaction, profitability, and productivity
   - Italy (Chatterjee et al. 2022 + Barabaschi et al. 2022; n=163
     and 330): productivity boosted by remote experience and digital
     tools, with initial coordination challenges
   - Malaysia (Zahari et al. 2024; n=20 leaders): remote work improved
     productivity and well-being, conditional on technology and
     management strength
   - UK (Ayertey et al. 2023; n=10 stakeholders): remote work raised
     efficiency and lowered costs; challenges: isolation and tech costs
   - Industry-level: collaboration tools → up to 25% higher
     productivity (Linder Jannik 2025 cited in review)
   - CIPD report cited: 38% of organisations reported productivity
     improvements from remote/hybrid work vs 13% reporting declines

3. Productivity barriers documented:
   - Lithuania (Strakšienė et al. 2021): inadequate IT infrastructure
     and absent formal policies reduced productivity
   - Ecuador (Lalama Franco et al. 2023; n=802 SMEs): productivity
     gains offset by longer work hours and declining WLB
   - India (Sharma et al. 2023; n=200 SMEs): cybersecurity and
     communication breakdown challenges despite productivity gains
   - Romania (Grigorescu & Mocanu 2020): digital readiness and
     managerial practices identified as critical success factors

4. CONDITIONAL PRODUCTIVITY THESIS (KEY FOR UBANDIYA):
   The review synthesises a consistent conditional argument: hybrid/
   remote work DOES improve SME productivity BUT only when supported
   by (a) adequate digital infrastructure, (b) trust-based and
   outcome-oriented leadership and management practices, (c) WLB
   policies, and (d) organisational readiness and culture. Success is
   NOT automatic — it is contingent on contextual enablers. This
   directly maps onto the thesis mediation hypothesis.

5. Four strategic pillars framework (Table 2):
   Pillar 1 — Digital Infrastructure & Tools: stable internet, cloud
     tools, cybersecurity protocols, device access
   Pillar 2 — Leadership & Management Practices: KPI-defined remote
     work policies, digital leadership training, performance-based
     evaluation (not micromanagement)
   Pillar 3 — Work-Life Balance & Well-being: hour boundaries, mental
     health resources, autonomy, reduced commuting
   Pillar 4 — Organisational Readiness & Culture: digital readiness
     assessment, culture of trust and accountability, employee
     involvement in hybrid policy design

6. SDG alignment: hybrid work supports SDG 8 (Decent Work and
   Economic Growth) and SDG 13 (Climate Action) through reduced
   commuting, operational cost savings, and inclusive flexibility.

7. Limitations identified in the paper:
   - 12 primary studies is a small evidence base (acknowledged by
     authors as reflecting the fragmented SME-FWA literature)
   - Cross-sectional primary studies limit causal inference
   - Self-reported productivity measures in most primary studies
   - No sector-specific or country-deep comparisons possible
   - No longitudinal data available for long-term effects

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Provides the most comprehensive global typology of
               hybrid/remote FWA models in the corpus. The four-pillar
               framework (Table 2) maps directly onto the thesis's
               hybrid work mediator construct: Pillar 1 = digital/
               management systems (MIS); Pillar 2 = organisational
               management systems (OMS); Pillars 3–4 = WLB and
               organisational context. Use for constructing the Ch. 2.1
               definition of the hybrid work mediator in GGHN context.

Ch. 2.3 use  : The conditional productivity thesis is the strongest
               narrative synthesis support for the thesis mediation
               hypothesis in the entire corpus. Cite for the argument
               that "direct effects of hybrid/remote work on
               productivity are context-contingent and mediated by
               management systems rather than universal and direct."
               The four-pillar framework — especially Pillar 2
               (Leadership & Management) — provides the literature
               anchor for the MIS/OMS management systems constructs
               as mediating mechanisms.

Ch. 2.3 SLR  : Per the CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.1 construct;
               Ch. 2.3 SLR"), the paper itself is a PRISMA SLR and
               should be referenced as evidence of the SLR method
               applied to this field (methodological precedent for
               Chapter 3 approach).

Statement of
Problem      : The paper explicitly identifies an SME evidence gap
               and a lack of pre-pandemic/post-pandemic comparisons —
               useful for justifying the GGHN organisational case study
               as filling a similar gap in the Nigerian NGO/health
               development sector. Also useful for framing the
               "persistent challenges" of digital infrastructure,
               communication, and cybersecurity as directly relevant
               to GGHN's operating environment.

Limitation   : SLR with only 12 primary studies; no formal statistical
               synthesis (no effect sizes, no meta-analysis); no
               African country represented among the 12 primary studies
               [FLAG: Africa absent from primary sample — Ghana is
               included but Peprah et al. 2020 is pre-pandemic
               cross-sectional only]; no modelling of the mediating
               paths (only narrative; no path coefficients); SME focus
               rather than NGO/health sector. Productivity measured by
               primary studies via self-report in most cases. The
               review itself is from Malaysia with a Southeast Asian
               institutional perspective — citation of UK, European,
               and ASEAN SME evidence; Sub-Saharan Africa is absent as
               a region.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] PRISMA 2020 adherence explicitly stated and flow diagram
      included — methodologically rigorous for an SLR
  [+] Multi-country synthesis (11 countries; diverse economic
      contexts: low/middle/high income) — broadest geographic scope
      of any paper in Block B
  [+] Four-pillar framework (Table 2) provides structured operatio-
      nalisation of management systems as conditional enablers of
      hybrid work → productivity — directly supports thesis argument
  [+] Conditional productivity thesis is the clearest SLR-level
      statement of the mediation mechanism in the corpus
  [+] Published in SN Business & Economics (Springer Nature) —
      credible Scopus-indexed journal; open access CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
  [+] FRGS government-funded research; UMPSA institutional affiliation
  [~] Only 12 primary studies — small evidence base even for a
      PRISMA SLR; authors acknowledge this limitation explicitly
  [~] No meta-analysis or effect size synthesis; narrative only
  [~] No formal modelling of mediating paths — pillars are conceptual
      not empirically path-modelled
  [~] No Sub-Saharan African primary studies in final sample of 12
      (Ghana appears but via a 2020 pre-pandemic study, Peprah et al.)
  [~] SME focus rather than NGO/development sector — some conceptual
      distance from GGHN's organisational type
  [~] Cross-sectional primary studies throughout — causal claims weak
  [-] Productivity predominantly self-reported in underlying studies

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B01 (Ukpere2026), B02 (Usman2025), B03 (Ayogoi2026),
B04 (Nweze2026):
  B05 provides the SLR-level global synthesis that contextualises the
  Nigeria-specific findings of B01–B04. The conditional productivity
  thesis ("FWA improves productivity IF supported by digital
  infrastructure + management practices") is the SLR equivalent of the
  empirical conditional findings from B01 (Nigeria NGOs: "potential IF
  proper infrastructure in place"), B03 (Nigeria telecoms: org support
  β=0.365 mediates), and B04 (Nigeria media: structured HWS β=0.658).
  Together: global SLR (B05) → Nigeria empirical converging evidence
  (B01–B04) — a strong multi-level evidence chain.

Convergence with A07 (Alexandro2025) and A12 (Ihejirika2025):
  B05's finding that direct FWA → productivity effects are context-
  contingent and conditional provides SLR-level support for A07's
  null H5/H6 (WFH → productivity, p>0.05, Indonesia MSMEs) and A12's
  null H3 (WLB → task performance, p=0.147, Nigeria hospital). The SLR
  conditionals explain WHY those direct effects were null: in the
  absence of adequate management systems (Pillar 2), direct effects
  do not emerge.

Convergence with A11 (Achief2025):
  Both are PRISMA SLRs covering hybrid/remote work enablers and HRM
  practices in the context of performance (A11: Nigeria healthcare;
  B05: global SMEs). Methodological affinity — both use PRISMA 2020.

Convergence with B02 (Usman2025) "performance paradox":
  B02 argued conceptually that performance problems in Nigerian MDAs
  arise from misalignment between hybrid structures and management
  systems. B05 provides the global SLR-level parallel: "success is
  NOT automatic — it is contingent on contextual enablers" (i.e.
  management systems). The conceptual argument (B02) is validated at
  the SLR level (B05) across 11 countries.

IMPORTANT NEW FINDING — AFRICA GAP:
  The final 12-study sample of B05 contains NO Sub-Saharan African
  primary study (Ghana's Peprah et al. 2020 is included but is a
  pre-pandemic survey of scheduling flexibility, not hybrid work per
  se). This is significant: the GGHN Nigeria case study fills a gap
  that even the best available global SLR on this topic could not
  address. Cite in Ch. 3 as justification for the GGHN case focus.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - PRISMA 2020 SLR on hybrid/remote work → SME productivity:
    first SLR-type paper in Block B (B05); 12 primary studies;
    11 countries; 2020–2024
  - Four-pillar SME hybrid work strategy framework (Table 2):
    structured operationalisation of management + WLB + digital
    + organisational readiness as enablers; first in Block B
  - SDG alignment of hybrid work (SDG 8 + SDG 13): first in corpus
  - Collaboration tools productivity statistic: "up to 25% higher
    productivity" (Linder Jannik 2025); 82% improved communication
  - Sub-Saharan Africa gap confirmed at SLR level: no SSA primary
    study in B05 final sample; Ghana only via pre-pandemic data
  - Springer Nature (SN Business & Economics) journal: first
    Springer Nature outlet in Block B
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B06
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B06
Cite key        : Lauring2025
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — definitional framework; three
                  dimensions: modality, location, temporality;
                  dynamic multilevel conceptualisation),
                  T4 (conceptual review — 62 peer-reviewed articles;
                  Scopus + EBSCO Business Source Complete;
                  narrative synthesis; no meta-analysis),
                  T5 (HRM implications — performance management,
                  training, recruitment, organisational support,
                  leader communication and empowerment)
Title           : What is hybrid work? Towards greater conceptual
                  clarity of a common term and understanding its
                  consequences
Authors         : Lauring, J., & Jonasson, C.
Year            : 2025 (received 26 May 2023; revised 16 August 2024;
                  accepted 22 August 2024; available online
                  28 August 2024; published in Vol. 35 No. 1, 2025)
Journal         : Human Resource Management Review
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 35, No. 1, Article 101044
DOI             : 10.1016/j.hrmr.2024.101044
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Department of Management, Aarhus University, Denmark
                  (Lauring); Department of Psychology and Behavioural
                  Science, Aarhus University, Denmark (Jonasson)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Conceptual review; extensive literature review;
                  definitional/taxonomic paper; no primary data
Aim             : To address conceptual ambiguity around 'hybrid work'
                  in HRM research by (1) proposing a comprehensive
                  general definition; (2) developing a three-dimensional,
                  multilevel taxonomy; and (3) reinterpreting existing
                  HRM findings through the lens of the new definition
                  to identify new research directions
Setting/context : HRM academic domain; global; no single country or
                  sector focus — draws on 62 articles from across
                  organisational behaviour, management, social
                  psychology, and ICT fields
Constructs      : Core construct defined:
                    Hybrid work = ongoing switches between interrelated
                    traditional and non-traditional work modes in
                    relation to MODALITY, LOCATION, and TEMPORALITY,
                    where an equal balance between traditional and
                    non-traditional modes represents the highest level
                    of hybridity
                  Three dimensions:
                    (1) Modality (How?): analog/face-to-face ↔
                        digital/virtual
                    (2) Location (Where?): office-bound/co-located ↔
                        non-office-bound/distributed
                    (3) Temporality (When?): constrained/synchronous ↔
                        unconstrained/asynchronous
                  Two levels of analysis:
                    (a) Individual (personal task execution)
                    (b) Collaborative (joint task execution)
                  Key differentiating feature: DYNAMIC SWITCHES
                    between modes — not merely the presence of dual
                    modes simultaneously; frequency and balance of
                    alternation are analytically significant
Theoretical lens: Structuration Theory (Giddens, 1984; 1990) —
                  technology, space, and time as structural elements
                  organising action and interaction in modernity;
                  dynamic switches as characteristic of late modernity.
                  Person-Environment fit theory and Social Identity
                  Theory noted as most common frameworks in reviewed
                  literature but not adopted as primary lens by authors.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Extensive conceptual literature review; definitional
                  paper; taxonomy development
Databases       : Scopus; EBSCO Business Source Complete
Initial results : >50,000 articles (broad search 'hybrid' AND 'work*')
After refinement: 1,040 articles (added ICT/virtual/digital/remote/
                  flex/asynchronous terms)
After dedup     : 929 articles for manual screening
After title/
abstract screen : 744 relevant; then narrowed to 161 (HRM field);
                  then 62 articles retained for deep engagement
                  (articles that meaningfully defined or applied the
                  'hybrid work' concept)
Final sample    : 62 peer-reviewed conceptual and empirical articles
Methodology mix
in 62 articles  : Empirical: 44 (27 quantitative, 18 qualitative,
                  6 mixed-methods); Conceptual: 18
Reliability     : Not applicable — conceptual review
Cronbach's α    : Not applicable
Data availability: No new data collected

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY CONTRIBUTIONS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. DEFINITION (the key output — use verbatim for thesis Ch. 2.1):
   "Hybrid work entails ongoing switches between interrelated
   traditional and non-traditional work modes in relation to modality,
   location, and temporality where an equal balance between working
   in traditional and non-traditional work modes represents the
   highest level of hybridity. Hybrid work can be gauged as ongoing
   and interrelated switches between work modes at the personal level
   as individual task execution or the collaborative level as
   participation in joint task execution."

2. THREE-DIMENSIONAL TAXONOMY (Table 3):
   Modality — Individual: analog ↔ digital; Collaborative:
     face-to-face ↔ virtual
   Location — Individual: office-bound ↔ non-office-bound;
     Collaborative: co-located ↔ distributed
   Temporality — Individual: time-constrained ↔ non-constrained;
     Collaborative: synchronous ↔ asynchronous

3. DYNAMIC NATURE — the defining characteristic:
   Hybrid work is NOT merely the presence of both traditional and
   non-traditional modes; it is the ONGOING SWITCHING between them.
   COVID-19 remote work was a DEVIATION from balanced hybridity —
   it was predominantly non-traditional, not hybrid per se. Frequency
   of switching is analytically important.

4. INTERDIMENSIONAL INTERDEPENDENCE:
   Changes in one dimension typically cascade to others: a shift in
   location (home office) often triggers a shift in modality (digital
   tools) and temporality (unconstrained hours). The three dimensions
   are not independent.

5. MULTILEVEL INTERDEPENDENCE:
   Individual-level flexibility choices ripple into collective
   consequences ("ripple effects" — Wheatley et al., 2024). Hybrid
   work is NOT purely an individual flexibility benefit; it involves
   compelled mode-switching driven by group/organisational needs as
   well as personal preferences.

6. NOMOLOGICAL NETWORK (Fig. 1 + Table 2):
   Synthesis of antecedents, mechanisms, enablers, and outcomes:
   - Facilitators: flexibility, technology use
   - Organisational enablers: organisational support, leader
     communication, leader empowerment
   - Personal enablers: technological proficiency, adaptability,
     boundary management, time management
   - Group mechanisms: interaction, interpersonal trust, group
     identification, conflict
   - Individual mechanisms: engagement, autonomy
   - Collective outcomes: group performance
   - Individual outcomes: individual performance, job satisfaction/
     wellbeing, work-life balance, isolation

7. THREE RECOMMENDATIONS FOR HRM RESEARCH:
   R1 (Dynamics): Researchers should operationalise hybrid work as
     the frequency and balance of mode switches, not static categories.
   R2 (Multidimensionality): Modality, location, and temporality
     should be assessed jointly, not in isolation.
   R3 (Multilevel): Individual and collaborative consequences of
     hybrid work should be studied as intertwined, not independently.

8. HRM PRACTICE IMPLICATIONS:
   - Recruitment/retention: job design clarity re. expected mode-
     switching autonomy and demands
   - Training: managing modality/location/time mode interactions
   - Performance management: output controls rather than presence-
     based supervision
   - Policy: fairness and inclusive flexibility — "responsible
     autonomy" to prevent compelled mode-switching for lower-status
     employees

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : THIS IS THE DEFINITIONAL ANCHOR FOR THE THESIS HYBRID
               WORK CONSTRUCT. Per the CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.1
               definitional anchor"), cite Lauring & Jonasson (2025)
               as the primary definitional source for 'hybrid work'
               throughout the thesis. The three-dimensional taxonomy
               (modality/location/temporality) operationalises the
               mediator construct more rigorously than any other paper
               in the corpus. The distinction between individual and
               collaborative levels of analysis is directly applicable
               to GGHN's organisational context, where hybrid work
               affects both individual employee productivity and team
               coordination.
               
               Use the verbatim definition (finding #1 above) for
               Ch. 2.1 with appropriate citation.

Ch. 2.2 use  : The nomological network (Fig. 1 / Table 2) and the
               three recommendations provide the theoretical framing
               for how hybrid work (as mediator) connects to management
               systems (organisational enablers: organisational support,
               leader communication, leader empowerment) and to
               productivity outcomes (individual performance). This
               directly supports the thesis AMO/SET/COR framework —
               the organisational enablers in the nomological network
               map onto the management systems IV.

Ch. 2.3 use  : The finding that COVID-19 remote work was a DEVIATION
               from balanced hybridity is important for situating the
               Block A/B empirical evidence: much of the literature
               (including B01 Ukpere2026) studied forced remote work,
               not voluntary hybrid work. This distinction supports
               the thesis argument that properly structured (managed)
               hybrid work differs from unstructured COVID-era WFH.

Statement of
Problem      : The paper identifies that HRM research has not
               sufficiently reoriented from traditional physical
               workplace assumptions (Harney & Collings, 2021). This
               is directly applicable to GGHN, where management systems
               were designed for traditional office-based work and are
               now applied to a hybrid environment.

Limitation   : Conceptual paper — no primary data; no statistical
               evidence; no Cronbach's α; 62 articles reviewed but
               no African or Nigerian context in the literature base;
               focuses on individual and team-level analysis rather
               than organisational performance (the thesis DV is
               employee/organisational productivity, not team trust or
               identification); the dynamic switching definition, while
               theoretically elegant, is challenging to operationalise
               in a survey instrument without longitudinal data.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] HRMR is a top-tier HRM journal (Elsevier; SSCI indexed;
      Q1 ranking in HRM) — highest prestige outlet in Block B
  [+] Provides the most rigorous and comprehensive conceptual
      definition of hybrid work in the corpus — the definitional
      anchor for the thesis Ch. 2.1
  [+] Three-dimensional taxonomy (modality/location/temporality)
      is the most theoretically precise operationalisation of the
      hybrid work construct available
  [+] 62-article synthesis; systematic search (Scopus + EBSCO);
      >50,000 initial results narrowed through transparent protocol
  [+] Structuration Theory (Giddens) provides robust sociological
      grounding for the dynamic switching definition
  [+] Cited in B05 (Maity & Lee 2025) — already appearing as a
      key reference in the hybrid work SLR literature
  [+] Nomological network (Fig. 1) maps all key antecedents,
      mechanisms, and outcomes — directly scaffolds thesis Ch. 2.2

      may exclude relevant empirical studies

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B02 (Usman2025):
  B02's four-dimension hybrid work construct (flexible scheduling,
  ICT infrastructure, virtual supervision, WLB) can now be mapped
  onto the Lauring & Jonasson (2025) taxonomy. Flexible scheduling
  = temporality; ICT infrastructure = modality enabler; virtual
  supervision = collaborative modality + location; WLB = temporality
  consequence. B06 provides the definitional grounding for B02's
  operationalisation — cite both in Ch. 2.1.

Convergence with B05 (Maity2025):
  Maity & Lee (2025) cite Lauring & Jonasson (2025) in their
  conclusion as the definitional source for hybrid work superiority
  over pure remote work: "hybrid work seems to be the most
  beneficial because it combines the independence of remote work
  (Lauring & Jonasson 2025)." This confirms B06 as the
  definitional cornerstone that B05's SLR builds upon.

Convergence with B03 (Ayogoi2026) and B04 (Nweze2026):
  B03's three-dimension hybrid work model (work arrangement, work
  flexibility, organisational support) maps partially onto B06:
  work flexibility = location + temporality; work arrangement =
  management-defined structure (organisational enabler in B06's
  nomological network). B04's structured HWS similarly maps onto
  B06's organisational enablers: management-defined schedules =
  constraining temporality + location; technological support =
  modality enabler; communication systems = collaborative modality.
  Both Nigerian empirical papers operationalise components of
  B06's taxonomy without referencing it.

Convergence with A15 (Asghar2023):
  A15's OMS/MIS distinction maps onto B06's nomological network:
  OMS = organisational enablers (support, leader communication,
  empowerment); MIS = modality enablers (technology infrastructure,
  ICT systems). B06 provides the conceptual bridge between the
  management systems constructs (Block A) and the hybrid work
  mediator (Block B).

KEY CORPUS FINDING — DEFINITIONAL PRECEDENCE:
  B06 is the only paper in the corpus that provides a formally
  derived, peer-reviewed, HRM-domain definition of hybrid work.
  All other Block B papers that discuss or define hybrid work
  either cite Lauring & Jonasson (2025) (e.g. B05) or use
  informal definitions. B06 is the definitional anchor; all
  subsequent hybrid work construct discussion in the thesis
  should cite B06 first.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Formal three-dimensional hybrid work definition (Lauring &
    Jonasson 2025): modality/location/temporality + dynamic
    switching + multilevel — first and primary definitional paper
    for hybrid work in corpus; Ch. 2.1 anchor
  - Structuration Theory (Giddens 1984; 1990): first in corpus;
    technology/space/time as structural organising elements;
    dynamic switching as late-modern characteristic
  - Human Resource Management Review (HRMR): first HRMR paper
    in corpus — highest prestige HRM outlet in the extraction
  - Nomological network for hybrid work (Fig. 1 + Table 2):
    first comprehensive antecedent-mechanism-outcome map in corpus
  - "Ripple effects" concept (Wheatley et al. 2024 cited within):
    individual flexibility choices cascade to collective outcomes
  - COVID-19 WFH as deviation from hybridity: conceptual
    distinction between forced remote work and voluntary balanced
    hybrid work — first explicit framing in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Lauring, J., & Jonasson, C. (2025). What is hybrid work? Towards
greater conceptual clarity of a common term and understanding its
consequences. Human Resource Management Review, 35(1), Article 101044.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.hrmr.2024.101044
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B07

BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B07
Cite key        : AlSuwaidi2025
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T1 (management information systems — 4IR computing,
                  digitalising, and integrating technologies as IV;
                  TAM and Socio-Technical Systems Theory),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — distributive
                  fairness monitoring as mediator; QWL as mediator),
                  T3 (hybrid work — flexible work arrangements [FWA]
                  as mediator: telecommuting, flextime, compressed
                  workweeks, remote work),
                  T5 (productivity enablers — 4IR → FWA/QWL/
                  distributive fairness → employee productivity;
                  mediation model),
                  T6 (empirical: PLS-SEM; Sharjah, UAE public sector)
Title           : Integrating fourth industrial revolution technologies
                  in human resource practices: Mediating roles of work
                  flexibility, quality of work life, and distributive
                  fairness in enhancing employee productivity
Authors         : Al-Suwaidi, K., Alsyouf, I., Tahboub, Z., Alsaad,
                  A., & Alsyouf, A.
Year            : 2025 (received 18 January 2025; revised 25 July
                  2025; accepted 11 August 2025; available online
                  2 September 2025)
Journal         : Sustainable Futures
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 10, Article 101150
DOI             : 10.1016/j.sftr.2025.101150
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : University of Sharjah, UAE (Al-Suwaidi, Alsyouf I.,
                  Tahboub); Jadara University, Jordan (Alsaad);
                  Liwa University, Abu Dhabi, UAE (Alsyouf A.)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  PLS-SEM (SmartPLS4); mediation analysis
Aim             : To investigate how 4IR technologies (computing,
                  digitalising, integrating) affect employee
                  productivity in UAE public sector organisations
                  through three mediators: flexible work arrangements
                  (FWA), quality of work life (QWL), and distributive
                  fairness monitoring
Setting/context : Three public sector organisations in Sharjah, UAE:
                  Sharjah Municipality (n=289), Sharjah Electricity
                  Water and Gas Authority / SEWA (n=61), Sharjah
                  Police (n=177); data collected March–May 2024
Sample          : n = 533 valid responses; predominantly male (73%);
                  aged 25–44 (73.3%); bachelor's degree holders
                  (34.9%); supervisors (29.1%); <4 years' experience
                  (54.8%)
IV              : 4IR Technologies — higher-order construct with three
                  first-order sub-constructs:
                    (1) Computing Technologies (cloud computing, AI,
                        big data analytics)
                    (2) Digitalising Technologies (ERP, BI tools,
                        blockchain, IoT, robotics)
                    (3) Integrating Technologies (3D printing, AR,
                        RFID, M2M, smart sensors)
Mediators       : (M1) Flexible Work Arrangements (FWA): 5-item
                    scale (Bjärntoft et al., 2020); freedom of
                    schedule and location
                  (M2) Quality of Work Life (QWL): 10-item scale
                    (Robbins & Judge, 2017); safety, satisfaction,
                    growth, well-being
                  (M3) Distributive Fairness Monitoring: 5-item scale
                    (Rusbadrol et al., 2021); equity of reward
                    distribution
DV              : Employee Productivity: 7-item scale (Luthans, 2009);
                  task quantity, quality, efficiency, IT-enabled
                  output
Theoretical     : Socio-Technical Systems Theory (Appelbaum, 1997);
framework         Process Theory (Hernes, 2014); JD-R Model
                  (for FWA pathway); Social Exchange Theory — SET
                  (for QWL pathway); Equity Theory (for distributive
                  fairness pathway); Technology Acceptance Model
                  (TAM) — referenced for 4IR measurement

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Cross-sectional; online survey; 5-point Likert scale
Analysis        : PLS-SEM (consistent PLS / PLSc); SmartPLS4;
                  bootstrapping for mediation (Preacher & Hayes, 2008)
Reliability     : All Cronbach's α ≥ 0.874 (threshold: 0.70) ✓
                  Distributive Fairness Monitoring α = 0.948
                  Computing Technology α = 0.885
                  Digitalising Technology α = 0.956
                  Integration Technology α = 0.965
                  Productivity α = 0.932
                  QWL α = 0.961
                  FWA α = 0.874
Convergent      : All CR > 0.886; all AVE > 0.596 (thresholds:
validity          CR > 0.70; AVE > 0.50) ✓
Discriminant    : Fornell-Larcker criterion and HTMT ratio (all HTMT
validity          < 0.85 threshold) ✓
CMB check       : Full collinearity VIF approach (Kock, 2015); all
                  VIF < 3.3 — no common method bias ✓
Variance        : Model explains 57.7% of variance in productivity
explained         (R²); 25.6% in FWA; 18.8% in QWL; 18.2% in
                  distributive fairness monitoring
Pilot study     : n = 23 employees; April–June 2023; instrument
                  refined before main data collection

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
DIRECT EFFECTS (H1–H6):
H1: FWA → Productivity: β = 0.194, p < 0.001 ✓ (SUPPORTED)
H2: Distributive Fairness → Productivity: β = 0.151, p < 0.01 ✓
    [Note: Table 5 reports 0.138 for the same path — minor
    discrepancy between abstract and results table; Table 5
    (0.138) is the precise estimate from the structural model;
    abstract (0.151) may reflect rounded or alternate reporting.
    Use Table 5 value: β = 0.138, p < 0.01 for citation]
H3: QWL → Productivity: β = 0.511, p < 0.001 ✓ (SUPPORTED;
    strongest direct mediator-to-DV effect)
H4: 4IR → Distributive Fairness: β = 0.429, p < 0.001 ✓
    [Abstract reports 0.399; Table 5 reports 0.429 — use
    Table 5 value: β = 0.429, p < 0.001 for citation]
H5: 4IR → QWL: β = 0.436, p < 0.001 ✓
    [Abstract reports 0.409; Table 5 reports 0.436 — use 0.436]
H6: 4IR → FWA: β = 0.507, p < 0.001 ✓
    [Abstract reports 0.460; Table 5 reports 0.507 — use 0.507]

INDIRECT EFFECTS / MEDIATION (H7–H9, Table 6):
H7: 4IR → FWA → Productivity: β = 0.110, p < 0.001 ✓
    [Discussion text reports 0.088 for H7 — minor discrepancy
    with Table 6 (0.110); CI = 0.047–0.137; use Table 6: 0.110]
H8: 4IR → QWL → Productivity: β = 0.222, p < 0.001 ✓ (STRONGEST
    indirect effect; CI from discussion = 0.149–0.260)
    [Discussion text reports 0.201 for H8 — minor discrepancy
    with Table 6 (0.222); use Table 6: 0.222]
H9: 4IR → Distributive Fairness → Productivity: β = 0.059,
    p < 0.01 ✓ (WEAKEST indirect effect; CI = 0.026–0.101)
    [Discussion text reports 0.060 ≈ Table 6 0.059 — consistent]

RANKING OF INDIRECT PATHWAYS (Table 6):
1st: 4IR → QWL → Productivity: β = 0.222
2nd: 4IR → FWA → Productivity: β = 0.110
3rd: 4IR → Distributive Fairness → Productivity: β = 0.059

KEY NARRATIVE FINDING:
4IR technologies (management information systems / computing,
digitalising, integrating) do NOT directly improve employee
productivity — their effect operates THROUGH mediating HR
constructs (FWA, QWL, distributive fairness). The strongest
mediation pathway is through QWL (β = 0.222) — employees'
sense of physical safety, satisfaction, growth, and well-being
is the primary channel through which technology adoption
translates to productivity gains. FWA is the second channel
(β = 0.110) — 4IR enables flexible scheduling and remote work,
which reduces job demands and boosts output. Distributive
fairness monitoring is a weaker but still significant channel
(β = 0.059).

[FLAG — REPORTING DISCREPANCY: Several β values differ
between the abstract, body text, and tables — noted above.
Structural model tables (Table 5 and Table 6) are the
authoritative values for citation. Use these, not abstract
or discussion-section values, throughout the thesis.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : HIGH RELEVANCE. B07 provides the most direct
               empirical model of the mediation structure relevant
               to the thesis. Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3
               empirical; Ch. 2.2 mediation"), the 4IR → FWA →
               productivity chain directly maps onto the thesis
               mediation hypothesis: management systems (MIS/OMS)
               → hybrid work (FWA as partial proxy) → employee
               productivity. The three-mediator framework (FWA +
               QWL + distributive fairness) mirrors the thesis's
               multi-mediator conception, with FWA aligning to the
               hybrid work mediator and QWL aligning to the WLB
               construct. Use in Ch. 2.2 to argue that technology-
               based management systems exert their productivity
               effects indirectly through work flexibility and
               well-being pathways.

Ch. 2.3 use  : The 4IR technology construct (computing, digitalising,
               integrating) maps almost exactly onto the thesis MIS
               sub-construct from Block A. The strongest indirect
               effect (QWL β = 0.222) aligns with the A-series
               pattern where WLB is NOT a direct predictor of
               productivity but operates through an intermediate
               variable. Here that intermediate variable is QWL
               (closely related to WLB). Cite alongside B05 Maity2025
               for the argument that direct effects of FWA on
               productivity are weaker than mediated pathways.

Convergence  : Directly convergent with the cross-cutting Block A
with thesis   finding that technology/ICT is the dominant management
argument      systems predictor (β values: A09 β=0.45–0.57 range
               from Block A). B07 now extends this into the Block B
               domain: 4IR technologies (β = 0.507 → FWA; β = 0.436
               → QWL) produce indirect productivity effects of
               β = 0.222 (QWL) and β = 0.110 (FWA) — confirming
               that technology-mediated pathways are empirically
               stronger than direct flexibility → productivity paths.

Limitation   : Cross-sectional design; single UAE city (Sharjah)
               — limited generalisability to Nigeria/GGHN context;
               public sector focus (government service orgs) does
               align broadly with GGHN (NGO public health sector)
               but governance structures differ; FWA scale (5 items,
               Bjärntoft et al. 2020) focuses on schedule/location
               flexibility only — does not capture the full
               modality/temporality/location three-dimension
               definition from B06 (Lauring & Jonasson 2025);
               self-reported productivity DV; no longitudinal data;
               β value discrepancies between abstract, discussion,
               and tables require care in citation [FLAG — use Table
               5/6 values only].

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] All Cronbach's α ≥ 0.874 — strong reliability across all
      constructs; well above 0.70 threshold
  [+] Full CMB assessment (Kock full collinearity VIF < 3.3) —
      rigorous bias control, superior to Harman's single factor
      in most Block B papers
  [+] Discriminant validity confirmed by both Fornell-Larcker AND
      HTMT ratio — double-validation approach
  [+] n = 533 — adequate for PLS-SEM multi-mediator model
  [+] Higher-order construct (4IR) with repeated indicator
      approach — methodologically sophisticated
  [+] Three-mediator partial mediation model directly mirrors
      thesis mediation hypothesis structure
  [+] R² = 57.7% for productivity — strong model explanatory power
  [+] Bootstrapping for indirect effects (Preacher & Hayes 2008);
      CIs reported — best practice for mediation
  [+] Open access; Elsevier Sustainable Futures journal
  [~] Cross-sectional design — causal claims limited
  [~] Single city (Sharjah, UAE) — generalisability restricted
  [~] β value discrepancies across paper sections — minor
      reporting inconsistency flagged; use Table 5/6 values
  [~] Self-reported DV (productivity) — common limitation
  [~] FWA scale limited to schedule/location flexibility — does
      not capture full hybrid work construct per B06 taxonomy
  [-] No longitudinal follow-up to establish causality

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #1
(Technology/ICT as dominant predictor):
  B07 confirms at the Block B level what Block A established
  across 17 papers: digital/ICT systems (here: 4IR computing,
  digitalising, and integrating technologies) are the strongest
  management systems predictor of downstream HR outcomes. The
  4IR → FWA path (β = 0.507) and 4IR → QWL path (β = 0.436)
  are among the strongest IV → mediator effects in the corpus.
  This extends the Block A ICT dominance finding into the hybrid
  work domain.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #2
(Direct WLB/flexibility → productivity effects are weak):
  B07 shows that FWA (a direct measure of work flexibility)
  has a direct productivity effect of β = 0.194 (H1) but that
  its INDIRECT contribution via 4IR adoption (β = 0.110, H7)
  is substantial. More importantly, QWL (the WLB-adjacent
  mediator) also requires 4IR technologies as its antecedent
  to produce productivity gains (β = 0.222, H8). This pattern
  is consistent with Block A papers showing null or weak direct
  WLB → productivity effects (A07 null H5/H6; A12 null H3)
  when management systems are not present as enabling context.

Convergence with B05 (Maity2025) conditional productivity thesis:
  Maity's four pillars (digital infrastructure, leadership and
  management practices, WLB, organisational readiness) map
  precisely onto B07's empirical model: 4IR technologies =
  digital infrastructure + management practices (Pillars 1–2);
  QWL = WLB (Pillar 3); FWA = operationalised flexibility
  (Pillar 1). B07 provides the path coefficients that B05 could
  only describe narratively — a critical upgrade for the thesis.

Convergence with B06 (Lauring2025):
  B07's FWA construct (schedule and location flexibility)
  operationalises two of Lauring & Jonasson's three dimensions:
  location (office vs. home) and temporality (constrained vs.
  unconstrained hours). The modality dimension (digital/analog)
  is captured in B07 not through FWA but through the 4IR IV
  itself. Together, B06 + B07 provide definitional rigour (B06)
  and empirical validation of the FWA → productivity pathway (B07).

Convergence with B02 (Usman2025):
  B02 used ICT infrastructure as a dimension of hybrid work
  in Nigerian MDAs. B07 now empirically demonstrates HOW
  4IR/ICT infrastructure produces productivity effects — through
  the FWA pathway (β = 0.110) and the QWL pathway (β = 0.222).
  The mechanism Usman conceptually proposed (ICT as enabler)
  is here empirically validated in a public sector context, albeit
  in the UAE rather than Nigeria.

NEW FINDING FOR THESIS — QWL AS PRIMARY MEDIATOR:
  The strongest indirect effect in B07 is 4IR → QWL →
  Productivity (β = 0.222, p < 0.001). Quality of Work Life
  (safety, satisfaction, growth, well-being) is more powerful
  than FWA as a mediating channel. This finding is directly
  citable in Ch. 2.2 to argue that management systems affect
  productivity primarily through employee well-being (QWL/WLB)
  rather than through structural work arrangements (FWA) alone.
  This nuances the thesis mediation model: the hybrid work
  mediator may operate via its WLB sub-component more than its
  structural flexibility sub-component.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - 4IR technologies as IV for employee productivity: first in
    corpus; three-category taxonomy (computing/digitalising/
    integrating); UAE public sector; n=533
  - Distributive fairness monitoring as mediator: first in corpus
    (Equity Theory framing; AI-enabled transparent performance
    monitoring); β = 0.059 indirect effect
  - Quality of Work Life (QWL) as mediator: first explicit QWL
    measure in corpus (10-item scale; β = 0.222 indirect effect —
    strongest mediation channel in paper)
  - Socio-Technical Systems Theory + Process Theory as dual
    theoretical frame: first in corpus
  - UAE/Middle East context: first non-Africa, non-Southeast-Asia
    Global South context in Block B
  - SmartPLS4 + PLSc (consistent PLS): highest methodological
    rigour for PLS-SEM in corpus; CMB checked via full VIF method
  - Sustainable Futures (Elsevier): first appearance in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Al-Suwaidi, K., Alsyouf, I., Tahboub, Z., Alsaad, A., & Alsyouf, A.
(2025). Integrating fourth industrial revolution technologies in human
resource practices: Mediating roles of work flexibility, quality of
work life, and distributive fairness in enhancing employee
productivity. Sustainable Futures, 10, Article 101150.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.sftr.2025.101150
```
