```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B08
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B08
Cite key        : Tonnessen2026
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — formal
                  control [outcome-oriented] and informal control
                  [self-control] as mediators; OCT framework;
                  managerial trust as IV),
                  T3 (hybrid work — degree of remote work as IV;
                  remote work as a form of flexible work arrangement),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — informal
                  control mediates remote work → performance;
                  formal control does not mediate; requirements risk
                  as negative moderator)
Title           : Managerial insights into trust, control, and
                  performance in remote work environments
Authors         : Tønnessen, Ø., Baah-Peprah, P., Flåten, B.-T.,
                  & Larsen, T. B.
Year            : 2026 (received 18 February 2025; revised 5 May 2026;
                  accepted 24 May 2026; available online 30 May 2026)
Journal         : Journal of Business Research
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 215, Article 116323
DOI             : 10.1016/j.jbusres.2026.116323
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Department of Management and Innovation, School of
                  Business and Law, University of Agder, Norway

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  CB-SEM (lavaan, R); hierarchical moderation model;
                  managerial perspective (not employee self-report)
Aim             : To examine how manager–employee trust and formal and
                  informal control mechanisms shape employee performance
                  in remote work environments, and how requirements risk
                  moderates the effectiveness of these control types
Setting/context : United States; SurveyMonkey panel; managers across
                  all 50 states; 2022 data collection; post-pandemic
                  remote work environment; covers both goods-producing
                  (23%) and service-providing (77%) industries
Sample          : n = 288 managers reporting on 8,351 employees; 53%
                  male; average age 45; 39% bachelor's degree; 38%
                  middle management; 48% managing 1–10 employees;
                  39% had >50% of employee work done remotely in 2021
IV              : (1) Possibility of remote work (single item; task
                      suitability for remote execution)
                  (2) Manager–employee trust (5 items; Tzafrir &
                      Dolan, 2004; harmony, reliability, concern)
                  (3) Degree of remote work (3 items; study-developed;
                      extent employees work outside office)
Mediators       : (M1) Formal control: 3 items (Keil et al., 2013;
                    Kirsch, 1996); outcome-oriented; measurable targets
                    and performance benchmarks
                  (M2) Informal control: 3 items (Keil et al., 2013;
                    Kirsch, 1996); self-control; employee autonomy,
                    initiative, self-monitoring
Moderator       : Requirements risk (3 items; Schmidt et al., 2001;
                  Wallace et al., 2004a); task uncertainty: unclear,
                  conflicting, or insufficiently identified requirements
DV              : Employee performance (7 items; Koopmans et al.,
                  2013; Wallace et al., 2004b); task performance,
                  adaptive performance, contextual performance
Theoretical     : Organisational Control Theory — OCT (Kirsch, 1996;
framework         Ouchi, 1979) as primary; Trust Theory (Mayer et al.,
                  1995); referenced also: Job Demands-Resources (JD-R)
                  concepts implicit in requirements risk framing

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Cross-sectional; online survey (SurveyMonkey);
                  7-point Likert scale; manager-perspective only
Analysis        : CB-SEM (covariance-based; lavaan R package);
                  Satorra-Bentler robust ML (non-normal data);
                  hierarchical modelling across 4 models; pairwise
                  interaction terms (RQR × FCO; RQR × ICO); simple
                  slope analyses with 95% CIs
Reliability     : All Cronbach's α above 0.70 threshold (per paper);
                  [FLAG: individual α values not tabulated in text —
                  reported as "well above 0.70" for all constructs;
                  composite reliability (CR) values reported in Table 4]
                  CR: MET=0.87; DRW=0.96; FCO=0.75; ICO=0.70;
                  RQR=0.72; PEF=0.86
                  [FLAG: informal control CR = 0.70 is at the minimum
                  threshold; AVE = 0.41 is below the 0.50 standard —
                  authors acknowledge this limitation]
Convergent      : AVE: MET=0.58; DRW=0.88; FCO=0.50; ICO=0.41;
validity          RQR=0.50; PEF=0.51; RWP=n/a (single item)
                  [FLAG: ICO AVE=0.41 < 0.50 threshold; tolerated by
                  authors given CR=0.70 and validated tests]
Discriminant    : Fornell-Larcker criterion + HTMT ratios (all <0.90);
validity          VIF 1.50–3.20 (no multicollinearity) ✓
CMB check       : Harman's single factor (27% variance) + common
                  latent factor approach (22% variance) — both well
                  below 50% threshold ✓
Model fit       : CFA: χ²/df = 1.18 (< 3); CFI=0.984; TLI=0.981
                  (both >0.90); RMSEA=0.025; SRMR=0.045 (both <0.08)
                  — excellent fit ✓
Variance        : R² degree of remote work = 58.5%; R² formal
explained         control = 24.5%; R² informal control = 70.9%;
                  R² performance = 78.7%–85.1% across Models 2–4

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ANTECEDENTS TO REMOTE WORK ADOPTION (H1–H2):
H1: Remote work possibility → Degree of RW: β=0.743, p<0.001 ✓
H2: Manager–employee trust → Degree of RW: β=0.133, p<0.001 ✓
  → Both supported; remote work possibility is the stronger driver.
  → Post-hoc: these effects are significantly stronger in high remote
    work intensity organisations than low intensity ones (Table 8b).

TRUST AND CONTROL CHOICE (H3–H4):
Manager–employee trust → Formal control: β=0.468, p<0.001 (sig.)
Manager–employee trust → Informal control: β=0.816, p<0.001 (sig.)
H3: MET → ICO effect (β=0.740) > MET → FCO effect (β=0.462):
  Δβ=0.278, Z=4.334, p<0.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → Trust has a much stronger effect on informal than formal control.

Degree of RW → Formal control: β=0.067, p=ns (non-significant)
Degree of RW → Informal control: β=0.093, p<0.01 (significant)
H4: DRW → ICO effect (β=0.029) > DRW → FCO effect (β=0.023):
  Δβ=0.052, Z=2.465, p=0.007 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → Remote work intensity drives informal control more than formal.
  [FLAG: DRW → FCO path is non-significant (p=ns) — formal control
  is NOT significantly shaped by degree of remote work alone]

CONTROL TYPES AND PERFORMANCE (H5–H6):
H5a: DRW → FCO → Performance (indirect): β=0.014, p=ns
  ✗ NOT SUPPORTED — formal control does NOT mediate
  remote work → performance
H5b: DRW → ICO → Performance (indirect): β=0.062, p<0.01
  ✓ SUPPORTED — informal control PARTIALLY mediates
  remote work → performance

Direct effects on performance:
Formal control → Performance: β=0.204, p<0.001 (significant)
Informal control → Performance: β=0.670, p<0.001 (significant)

H6: ICO → PEF effect (β=0.871 unstandardised) > FCO → PEF
  (β=0.243 unstandardised): Δβ=0.628, Z=12.498, p<0.001 ✓
  → Informal control has a dramatically stronger direct effect on
    performance than formal control.

REQUIREMENTS RISK AS MODERATOR (H7):
H7a: Requirements risk → Performance: β=-0.285, p<0.001
  ✓ SUPPORTED — task uncertainty directly reduces performance.

H7b/H7c moderation effects on control → performance:
  RQR × ICO → Performance: β=-0.067, p<0.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
    (attenuating effect: informal control effectiveness weakened
     by requirements risk; still positive at all risk levels)
  RQR × FCO → Performance: β=0.002, p=ns ✗ NOT SUPPORTED
    (formal control effectiveness not significantly moderated
     by requirements risk)

  Effect size comparison (H7c): RQR × FCO Δβ=0.202 vs.
  RQR × ICO Δβ=0.603; Z=1.681, p=0.046 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → Requirements risk has a significantly stronger attenuating
    effect on informal controls than on formal controls under
    conditions of high task uncertainty.

DEMOGRAPHIC CONTROL FINDINGS (additional):
  - Male managers perceive higher employee performance and use
    more informal controls than female managers
  - Older managers use more formal controls than younger managers
  - Lower-level managers allow more remote work than senior managers

NIGERIA LIMITATION (cited explicitly by authors):
  The paper's own limitations section states, p.4.6: "caution is
  required in applying our findings in contexts characterized by
  high levels of trust, such as Sweden… or low in trust, such as
  Nigeria (or countries with similar cultural dynamics, e.g. Ghana,
  South Africa, and other global south countries) (Ortiz-Ospina
  et al., 2024)."
  [This is a direct methodological flag for the thesis: the authors
  explicitly caution against applying these US findings to Nigeria.
  GGHN operates in Nigeria — cite B08 findings carefully and
  acknowledge this generalisability constraint in Ch. 2.3/Ch. 3.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 empirical;
               management+hybrid"), B08 is the most methodologically
               rigorous management-perspective paper on remote work →
               performance in the corpus. The formal/informal control
               distinction maps directly onto the thesis OMS construct:
               OMS = the formal and informal systems through which
               management shapes employee behaviour. Cite for the
               argument that in remote/hybrid contexts, informal
               (trust-based, autonomy-enabling) management systems
               are empirically more effective than formal (rules-based,
               target-setting) systems in mediating work arrangement →
               performance effects.

Ch. 2.2 use  : The finding that INFORMAL control partially mediates
               remote work → performance (β=0.062, H5b) while FORMAL
               control does not mediate (H5a rejected) is directly
               relevant to the thesis mediation hypothesis. Replace
               "formal/informal control" with "OMS sub-constructs" and
               the thesis argument becomes: management systems —
               specifically their informal, trust-based, and autonomy-
               enabling components — are the mediating mechanisms
               between hybrid work arrangements and employee
               productivity. This is the OMS → performance path in
               the thesis model.

Statement of
Problem      : The paper explicitly identifies Nigeria as a low-trust
               context where US findings may not transfer (see Nigeria
               flag above). This gap is citable in the thesis Statement
               of Problem: the GGHN case study is needed precisely
               because trust dynamics and control mechanisms in Nigerian
               organisations differ from high-income country contexts.

Limitation   : US context — authors themselves flag caution for
               Nigeria; cross-sectional design; manager self-report
               only (no employee-level data); ICO AVE=0.41 below
               threshold; single-item remote work possibility measure;
               data collected 2022 (early post-pandemic); no public
               sector or NGO/health sector data.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Journal of Business Research (JBR) — Elsevier; SSCI indexed;
      A-ranked journal; highest-prestige empirical outlet in Block B
  [+] n=288 managers covering 8,351 employees — uniquely managerial
      perspective (all other Block B papers are employee self-report)
  [+] CB-SEM with Satorra-Bentler robust ML for non-normal data —
      methodologically superior to standard ML-SEM
  [+] Hierarchical modelling (4 models) with incremental R² tracking
      — rigorous approach to moderation testing
  [+] Excellent model fit: CFI=0.984, TLI=0.981, RMSEA=0.025 ✓
  [+] Both Harman's + common latent factor CMB checks — thorough
  [+] Non-response bias checked via early/late respondent comparison
  [+] Multi-group analysis (high/low remote work intensity) adds
      robustness
  [+] Explicitly cites Nigeria as a low-trust context where findings
      may not transfer — unusual and commendable transparency
  [+] Effect size comparisons (Z-statistics) for all directional
      hypotheses — best practice in the corpus
  [~] ICO AVE=0.41 below 0.50 threshold — acknowledged by authors
  [~] Cross-sectional design — causality not established
  [~] US context only; generalisability to Nigeria explicitly
      cautioned against by the authors themselves
  [~] Manager self-report only — no employee triangulation
  [~] 2022 data — specific to early post-pandemic remote work wave
  [-] Single-item remote work possibility measure

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B06 (Lauring2025) — conceptual alignment:
  Lauring & Jonasson's three-dimensional taxonomy maps onto B08's
  constructs: degree of remote work = location dimension; formal
  control = constrained temporality (defined outputs, deadlines);
  informal control = unconstrained temporality + autonomy. B08
  empirically validates B06's theoretical argument that informal
  mechanisms are more adaptive to the dynamic switching nature of
  hybrid work — organisations using informal controls can accommodate
  mode-switches without rigid procedural breakdowns.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025):
  B07 showed that FWA → performance via QWL (β=0.222, the strongest
  indirect path) and less so via structural flexibility (β=0.110).
  B08 independently confirms this logic from the managerial side:
  the trust-based informal control mechanism (analogous to QWL/
  well-being enabling) mediates remote work → performance, while
  formal outcome targets (analogous to structural work design) do
  not mediate. Together, B07 (employee survey, UAE public sector)
  and B08 (manager survey, US cross-sector) provide convergent
  evidence across very different methodologies and contexts that
  informal/well-being pathways outperform structural/formal pathways
  in linking remote work to productivity.

Convergence with B04 (Nweze2026) — Nigeria hybrid work:
  B04 found that structured HWS β=0.658 on employee performance in
  Nigerian public media. B08 explicitly flags Nigeria as a low-trust
  context where formal controls may behave differently. B04's
  structured HWS finding is consistent with B08's argument that in
  low-trust contexts, formal/structured systems may play a larger
  role than in high-trust US contexts — the thesis can use this
  contrast to argue that GGHN's management systems context requires
  a hybrid model incorporating both formal (structure, clarity) and
  informal (trust, autonomy) controls.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #3
(Training as standalone non-significant):
  B08 finds that formal outcome controls DO have a direct effect on
  performance (β=0.204) but do NOT mediate the remote work →
  performance relationship. Block A's pattern that training alone
  is non-significant maps onto B08's finding that formal structural
  mechanisms (training, target-setting) do not translate into
  performance without informal enabling conditions (trust, autonomy).

NEW FINDING FOR THESIS — REQUIREMENTS RISK AS NEGATIVE MODERATOR:
  Requirements risk (task uncertainty: unclear, conflicting, changing
  requirements) directly reduces performance (β=-0.285) and weakens
  informal control effectiveness (Δβ=0.603 attenuation). For GGHN,
  which operates in a complex, resource-constrained public health
  environment, requirements risk is structurally high. This suggests
  that management systems at GGHN must actively manage task
  uncertainty (clear role definitions, outcome specification) as a
  prerequisite for informal trust-based controls to function
  effectively. Citable in Ch. 3 discussion as a contextual
  complication for the thesis mediation model.

KEY CORPUS FINDING — MANAGERIAL PERSPECTIVE:
  B08 is the only paper in the corpus that takes a managerial
  (rather than employee) perspective on the hybrid/remote work →
  productivity relationship. All other Block B papers are employee
  self-report surveys. B08's manager-side data provides an
  orthogonal validation of the management systems → hybrid work →
  performance chain: managers' trust and control decisions are the
  mechanism through which management systems shape hybrid work
  outcomes. This is the missing management-side evidence the thesis
  needs.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Managerial perspective on remote work → performance: first in
    corpus; n=288 managers covering 8,351 employees; US; CB-SEM
  - Organisational Control Theory (OCT) as primary framework:
    first in corpus; Kirsch (1996); Ouchi (1979); formal/informal
    control typology
  - Requirements risk as negative moderator: first in corpus;
    task uncertainty (unclear/conflicting/changing requirements)
    reduces performance and attenuates informal control effects
  - Formal control non-mediation of remote work → performance:
    first explicit null mediation finding for formal controls in
    corpus (complements Block A training null findings)
  - Informal control partial mediation confirmed: β=0.062
    (remote work → informal control → performance)
  - Journal of Business Research (JBR): first JBR paper in
    Block B — highest-ranked empirical journal in corpus
  - Nigeria explicitly identified as a low-trust context by
    authors: first external validity constraint cited for
    GGHN context from within the extracted literature

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Tønnessen, Ø., Baah-Peprah, P., Flåten, B.-T., & Larsen, T. B.
(2026). Managerial insights into trust, control, and performance in
remote work environments. Journal of Business Research, 215, Article
116323. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbusres.2026.116323
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B09
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B09
Cite key        : OBrady2025
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — collective
                  worker voice, formal/informal performance management
                  controls, coaching practices as OMS components),
                  T3 (hybrid work — remote/work-from-home; transition
                  to remote performance management; monitoring and
                  discipline as management mechanisms),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — JD-R
                  framing of monitoring demands vs. coaching resources;
                  mutual gains HRM model)
Title           : Worker voice and mutual gains from remote performance
                  management: Evidence from digitalized services in
                  North America and Germany
Authors         : O'Brady, S., Doellgast, V., & Starcevic, J.
Year            : 2025 (received 11 June 2024; revised 20 March 2025;
                  accepted 22 March 2025)
Journal         : Human Resource Management Journal
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 35, pp. 864–878
DOI             : 10.1111/1748-8583.12601
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : DeGroote School of Business, McMaster University,
                  Canada (O'Brady, Starcevic); ILR School, Cornell
                  University, USA (Doellgast)
Funding         : Arts Research Board, McMaster University; AFL-CIO
                  Tech Institute; Social Sciences and Humanities
                  Research Council (Canada)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Qualitative comparative case study; "slow
                  comparativism" methodology; multi-round interviews
                  + collective agreement analysis + focus groups
Aim             : To examine conditions under which employers adopt a
                  "mutual gains" approach to remote performance
                  management that balances employer productivity
                  interests with worker autonomy and wellbeing; to
                  assess the role of collective worker voice (unions
                  and works councils) in constraining monitoring
                  intensity and supporting developmental coaching
Setting/context : 11 telecommunications call centres in Canada (3),
                  United States (5), and Germany (3); data collected
                  2021–2024 across three rounds; COVID-19 pandemic and
                  post-pandemic remote work transition
Sample          : 250 respondents (workers, union representatives,
                  managers); 26 collective agreements (9 Canadian,
                  15 US, 2 German); interviews averaging 64 min each
Constructs      : Independent: Collective worker voice — operationalised
                    as union/works council institutional power resources
                    (structural, associational, institutional)
                  Outcomes: (1) Remote performance monitoring intensity
                    and frequency; (2) discipline-based vs.
                    developmental coaching; (3) worker wellbeing and
                    autonomy in remote settings
                  Moderating context: National institutional framework
                    — co-determination rights (Germany) vs. collective
                    bargaining (Canada/US)
Theoretical     : JD-R Model (Bakker & Demerouti, 2007) — monitoring
frameworks        as demands; coaching as resources; mutual gains =
                  balancing demands with resources
                  Pluralist Employment Relations (ER) theory (Budd,
                  2004) — power asymmetry between labour and
                  management; collective voice as countervailing power
                  Power Resource Theory (structural, associational,
                  institutional power)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Matched pair comparative case study; "slow
                  comparativism" (Almond & Connolly, 2020); abductive
                  and iterative qualitative data analysis (NVivo)
Data sources    : Semi-structured interviews, focus groups, collective
                  agreements, works agreements, archival documents
Data collection : Multi-round:
                  Round 1: H1 2021 (US/Canada); Fall 2021–Spring 2022
                    (Germany)
                  Round 2: Mid-2022 (US/Canada)
                  Round 3: Mid-2023 (US/Canada); 2024 follow-up (DT)
Analysis        : Three-level coding (first-level: case summaries;
                  second-level: themes; third-level: abstract causal
                  factors); NVivo; CBA provisions coded for monitoring,
                  discipline, and coaching protections
Reliability     : Qualitative; triangulation via iterative CBA/
                  interview cross-checking; union staff validation;
                  multiple informants per firm; longitudinal design
Cronbach's α    : Not applicable — qualitative study
Sample size     : 250 respondents; 11 firms; 26 CBAs; 3 countries
Generalisability: Acknowledged limitations: call centres are among the
                  most intensively monitored workplaces; findings may
                  not generalise to higher-skilled professional workers;
                  worker-heavy sample (less manager perspective)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. MONITORING INTENSITY:
   Across all cases, digital monitoring remained extensive
   (voice tracking, screen grabs, adherence monitoring, keystroke
   and mouse-movement tracking, webcam surveillance). The shift to
   remote work did NOT reduce monitoring intensity — in many cases
   it intensified it.

   Cases with collective agreement provisions LIMITING monitoring
   showed significantly less worker interference with autonomy:
   - Caps on call monitoring frequency (8–25 calls/month) prevented
     targeting of poor performers and unfair discipline
   - Restrictions on webcam use (most North American units with
     strong CBAs) prevented invasive home surveillance
   - Germany (co-determination rights): performance data must be
     aggregated by groups of ≥5 employees — individual monitoring
     effectively prohibited; works councils could veto monitoring
     tools not aligned with worker interests

2. COACHING — DISCIPLINE-BASED VS. DEVELOPMENTAL:
   North America: majority of cases showed weak developmental
   coaching; discipline-based coaching dominant — performance data
   used to threaten termination or revocation of remote work rights.
   Revocation of remote work as discipline = new control mechanism
   unique to WFH era; workers self-censored complaints to preserve
   remote work access.

   Germany (Deutsche Telekom): Works agreement explicitly prohibited
   use of performance data for discipline — data available to
   employees only, not team leaders. "Leadership 4.0" initiative
   co-designed by works council to improve remote coaching quality.
   Works agreement required ≥3 training days/year per employee
   (company target: 4–4.5 days). Hybrid design (2–3 days WFH;
   office days used for coaching and training) settled as stable
   post-pandemic arrangement with documented positive effects on
   satisfaction, health rates, and productivity.

3. MUTUAL GAINS GRADIENT (Table 2):
   Outcomes rated from very strong to weak across monitoring
   constraints, discipline constraints, and developmental coaching
   support:
   - Deutsche Telekom: Very strong / Very strong / Very strong
   - G Telecom 2 and 3: Strong / Strong / Weak-moderate
   - Best North American cases (US Telecom1b Units A/B;
     US Telecom2 Unit A): Moderate / Moderate / Weak
   - Majority of North American cases: Weak / Weak / Weak

4. INSTITUTIONAL POWER AS EXPLANATORY VARIABLE:
   German Works Constitution Act provides co-determination rights
   over performance monitoring technology — stable regardless of
   lockdowns, associational power erosion, or worker fear. This
   institutional power resilience is the key explanatory variable
   for Germany's superior outcomes.
   North American unions relied on CBA provisions negotiated through
   structural and associational power — both eroded by pandemic
   (workers feared striking would lose them remote work). Units with
   historically stronger CBAs retained better protection even under
   these conditions (institutional power built from past struggles).

5. WORKER WELLBEING AND PERFORMANCE OUTCOMES:
   JD-R finding: balanced monitoring/coaching approach (mutual
   gains) produced documented positive outcomes at Deutsche Telekom:
   improved satisfaction, health rates, and productivity (per 2024
   works councillor report). Across all cases, 9 in 10 Canadian
   call centre workers satisfied with remote work; lower turnover
   intentions and emotional exhaustion vs. office; higher job
   satisfaction in US/Canada. Autonomy gains from remote work were
   widely experienced but undermined by intensive monitoring in
   absence of collective agreement protections.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 empirical;
               management+hybrid"), B09 provides qualitative evidence
               that management systems (formalised in CBAs and works
               agreements) directly shape the outcomes of hybrid/
               remote work → employee wellbeing and productivity.
               The JD-R framing (monitoring = demands; coaching =
               resources) is directly applicable to the thesis
               mediation model: management systems OMS/MIS determine
               whether hybrid work produces positive or negative
               performance outcomes.

Ch. 2.2 use  : The mutual gains framework (Kochan & Osterman, 1994)
               is the closest normative equivalent of the thesis
               mediation hypothesis: management systems (collective
               voice + formalised controls) mediate the hybrid work →
               performance relationship. Cite B09 for the argument
               that management systems determine the direction of the
               hybrid work effect — not hybrid work itself.

Convergence  : B09 complements B08 (Tønnessen2026) on management
with thesis   control mechanisms, but from a labour relations and
argument      institutional perspective rather than a managerial SEM
               approach. Both papers confirm that informal/trust-based
               management mechanisms are more effective than formal
               monitoring in hybrid/remote settings — but B09 adds
               the critical point that these mechanisms do not emerge
               naturally from managerial discretion; they require
               institutional structures (CBAs, co-determination) to
               be sustained.

Methodological: Qualitative study — no β values or statistical
limitation     effect sizes; generalisability limited to unionised
               telecoms call centres; not applicable to GGHN's NGO
               public health context directly; no African context;
               worker-heavy sample. However, the MECHANISM identified
               (management systems shape hybrid work outcomes) is the
               same as the thesis argument and provides qualitative
               depth that the quantitative Block B papers lack.

GGHN relevance: GGHN lacks formal collective bargaining structures,
               making the German co-determination model inapplicable
               directly. However, the paper's point that formalised
               management systems (even in their absence, discipline-
               based monitoring is the default) is directly applicable
               to GGHN's management systems constructs. The absence of
               formal worker voice mechanisms at GGHN means that
               management system design is the ONLY lever available —
               making strong OMS/MIS design (outcome orientation,
               trust-based supervision, developmental coaching) even
               more critical for hybrid work outcomes.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Human Resource Management Journal (HRMJ) — Wiley; SSCI indexed;
      A-ranked HRM journal; same prestige tier as HRMR (B06)
  [+] Multi-round longitudinal qualitative design (2021–2024) —
      rare in the corpus; tracks policy changes over time
  [+] 250 respondents + 26 CBAs across 3 countries — rich
      multi-source triangulation
  [+] Explicitly integrates JD-R (HRM) and ER (pluralist)
      literatures — bridges two otherwise siloed fields
  [+] Provides qualitative depth on management mechanisms that
      quantitative papers (B07, B08) cannot capture
  [+] Strong validity strategy: iterative CBA/interview cross-
      checking; union staff validation; multiple informants per firm
  [+] Mutual gains framework directly applicable to the thesis OMS
      mediation argument
  [+] 2024 follow-up interviews from Deutsche Telekom provide
      post-pandemic outcome data (productivity, satisfaction, health)
  [~] Qualitative study — no statistical effect sizes; no Cronbach's α
  [~] Call centre context — limited generalisability to NGO/health
      sector (GGHN)
  [~] Worker-heavy sample — less manager and employer perspective
  [~] No African context; no developing-country context
  [~] Co-determination findings not directly applicable to GGHN
      (no formal works council structure in Nigerian NGOs)
  [~] Acknowledged: high-skill professionals may differ from call
      centre workers in monitoring sensitivity

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — control mechanisms:
  B08 (quantitative, US managers): informal self-control mediates
  remote work → performance; formal outcome control does not mediate.
  B09 (qualitative, Canada/US/Germany unions): discipline-based
  formal monitoring HARMS worker wellbeing and autonomy; only where
  CBAs constrain monitoring and mandate developmental coaching do
  positive outcomes emerge. The two papers are methodologically
  orthogonal (quantitative SEM vs. qualitative comparative) but
  converge on the same theoretical point: formal controls are
  insufficient for remote work → performance; informal/trust-based
  mechanisms (B08) or institutionally constrained performance
  management (B09) are necessary.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025) — QWL pathway:
  B07's strongest indirect effect (4IR → QWL → productivity,
  β=0.222) maps onto B09's mutual gains model: developmental
  coaching and constrained monitoring = organisational QWL
  investment. B09 provides the qualitative mechanism for WHY
  QWL mediates technology → productivity: when management systems
  invest in employee development (coaching resources) rather than
  surveillance (monitoring demands), QWL improves and productivity
  follows. B07's quantitative path and B09's qualitative mechanism
  are mutually reinforcing.

Convergence with B06 (Lauring2025) — interdimensional hybrid work:
  B09's "mutual gains" approach to remote performance management
  (constrained monitoring + developmental coaching) aligns with
  B06's argument that hybrid work requires management of all three
  dimensions (modality, location, temporality) simultaneously.
  The DT "Leadership 4.0" initiative is precisely the kind of
  multidimensional, adaptive management practice that B06
  conceptualises as necessary for managing dynamic mode-switches.

Convergence with B05 (Maity2025) Pillar 2 (Leadership & Management):
  B05's Pillar 2 (develop clear KPIs, train managers in digital
  leadership, use performance-based evaluation not micromanagement)
  is the SME-level equivalent of what B09 calls "mutual gains"
  performance management. Both argue that management practices
  (not hybrid work arrangements per se) determine productivity.
  B09 adds the institutional dimension: without collective
  agreement protections, even well-intentioned managers revert to
  monitoring-heavy, development-light practices.

NEW CONTRIBUTION FOR THESIS — MANAGEMENT SYSTEMS AS PREREQUISITES:
  B09's core finding — that managers do not voluntarily adopt
  mutual gains approaches to remote performance management;
  institutional structures are required — is directly citable in
  the thesis Statement of Problem and Ch. 3 to argue that GGHN
  needs deliberately designed management systems (MIS + OMS) to
  prevent the default "discipline-based, surveillance-heavy" hybrid
  work management pattern documented in North American cases. The
  absence of formal worker voice at GGHN makes this even more
  urgent: management system design IS the institutional substitute.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Mutual gains HRM framework (Kochan & Osterman 1994):
    first in corpus; balance of organisational performance and
    employee wellbeing through collective voice
  - Collective worker voice / co-determination as management
    system enabler: first in corpus; institutional power resources
    (structural, associational, institutional)
  - Remote performance monitoring as management demand (JD-R):
    first explicit framing of monitoring as a JD-R demand (not
    enabler) in corpus; discipline-based coaching as resource-
    depleting management practice
  - Developmental coaching as management resource (JD-R): first
    in corpus; explicit treatment of coaching as management systems
    component that mediates remote work → wellbeing/performance
  - Qualitative multi-country comparative design: first qualitative
    paper in Block B; 3 countries; 11 firms; 250 respondents
  - Human Resource Management Journal (HRMJ): first HRMJ paper
    in corpus alongside HRMR (B06)
  - Revocation of remote work as discipline: novel management
    control mechanism identified in hybrid work context — first
    flagged in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
O'Brady, S., Doellgast, V., & Starcevic, J. (2025). Worker voice and
mutual gains from remote performance management: Evidence from
digitalized services in North America and Germany. Human Resource
Management Journal, 35, 864–878.
https://doi.org/10.1111/1748-8583.12601
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B10
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B10
Cite key        : Mele2023
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — telework as construct; four
                  dimensions: space, time, technological medium,
                  autonomy; antecedents and effects in public
                  organisations),
                  T4 (systematic literature review — PRISMA; 120
                  studies; Web of Science; no temporal constraint;
                  public sector focus),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — supportive
                  leadership, job control, individual productivity
                  expectations as antecedents; conflicting evidence
                  on productivity effects; professional isolation as
                  consistent negative outcome),
                  T6 (effects on WLB, job satisfaction, turnover,
                  stress, health, organisational commitment,
                  performance)
Title           : Telework in public organizations: A systematic
                  review and research agenda
Authors         : Mele, V., Belardinelli, P., & Bellé, N.
Year            : 2023 (received 15 February 2023; revised 20
                  September 2023; accepted 26 September 2023)
Journal         : Public Administration Review
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 83, No. 6, pp. 1649–1666
DOI             : 10.1111/puar.13734
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND
Affiliations    : Bocconi University, Milan, Italy (Mele); Indiana
                  University, Bloomington, USA (Belardinelli);
                  Scuola Superiore Sant'Anna, Pisa, Italy (Bellé)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Systematic Literature Review (SLR); PRISMA; Web of
                  Science; no temporal constraint; narrative synthesis
Aim             : To map and assess what is known and unknown about
                  telework in public organisations; to offer a
                  systematic baseline on antecedents, effects,
                  moderating/mediating roles, and dynamics of
                  telework; to set a research agenda; research
                  question: "How does introducing telework change
                  public organizations?"
Setting/context : Public sector exclusively — government agencies,
                  public universities, hospitals, national/sub-national
                  government; 120 primary studies; multinational (US:
                  31%, Europe: 34%, Australia: 8%, Brazil: 6%,
                  Canada: 6%, South Africa: 2%, others: 13%)
Final sample    : 120 primary studies (39 in PA journals; 81 in
                  non-PA journals across business, management,
                  education, psychology, public policy)
Search details  : Web of Science; search terms: telework*, remote
                  work*, telecommuting, smart work*, work from home,
                  agile work*, hybrid work* + public/government/agenc*;
                  English only; up to May 2023
Pre-pandemic    : 60 studies (data collected before 2020)
Post-pandemic   : 54 studies (data post-pandemic); 6 compare both
PRISMA protocol : Confirmed; PRISMA checklist reproduced as Table A1;
                  expert panel consulted (6 experts) for keyword
                  validation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONSTRUCT: TELEWORK IN PUBLIC ORGANISATIONS — FOUR DIMENSIONS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. Space: work conducted outside the conventional workplace
   (typically home or satellite offices; "remoteness" is defined
   relative to the traditional bureau)
2. Time: deviation from typical office hours; duration varies from
   a few hours per week to full-time; frequency ranges from periodic
   to regular to exclusive
3. Technological medium: enabling role of ICTs allowing remote but
   connected work
4. Autonomy: flexibility and worker control, but subject to
   authorisation, agreement, and managerial discretion

[NOTE for thesis: This four-dimension framework partially overlaps
with B06 (Lauring2025) three-dimension taxonomy (modality,
location, temporality) but adds 'autonomy' as a distinct construct.
In the public sector, autonomy is MORE constrained than in private
settings — telework is authorised, negotiated, and conditionally
granted rather than freely chosen.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS — ANTECEDENTS OF TELEWORK (Table 3 + Table A2)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Most frequently studied (total estimates reported):
1. Family responsibilities (13 estimates): positive in 7/13;
   null in 5/13; negative in 1/13
2. Individual expected productivity (13 estimates): positive
   in 11/13; null in 1; negative in 1
   [Key: pre-pandemic fear of lower productivity disappeared
   post-pandemic — managers had "epiphany" re: WFH productivity]
3. Supportive leadership (12 estimates): positive in 8/12;
   null in 4/12 ← MOST CONSISTENT ORGANISATIONAL ANTECEDENT
4. Cost of commuting (10 estimates): positive in 7/10
5. Job control (10 estimates): positive in 8/10

Highly consistent antecedents (all positive, no null or negative):
  COVID measures, legal/policy support, environmental protection
  efforts, home office space availability

Consistently negative antecedents: job unsuitability, marital
  status (both consistently negative associations with telework)

Post-pandemic antecedents (54 post-COVID studies):
  COVID measures (6 positive) + individual productivity (5 positive)
  + supportive leadership (3 positive) = same three top predictors

KEY FOR THESIS: Supportive leadership as an antecedent maps onto
the thesis OMS construct — management systems (specifically
leadership and supervisory practices) are a prerequisite for
telework adoption in public organisations. This aligns with B07,
B08, and B09 findings on management systems enabling hybrid work.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS — EFFECTS OF TELEWORK (Table 4 + Table A3)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Most frequently studied outcome: Individual productivity (19 studies)
Effect direction:
  - Positive in 12/19 studies
  - Negative in 4/19 studies
  - Null in 3/19 studies
→ CONFLICTING EVIDENCE — not consistently positive

Work-life balance (17 studies):
  - Positive in 11/17; negative in 4/17; null in 2/17
  → Predominantly positive but contested

Turnover intention (16 studies):
  - NEGATIVE (reduced turnover intent) in 11/16
  - Null in 4; positive (increased turnover) in 1
  → Most consistent directional finding: telework reduces turnover

Job satisfaction (15 studies):
  - Positive in 12/15; null in 3/15
  → Strong positive direction

Stress (10 studies): mixed — positive (stress reduced) in 4;
  negative (stress increased) in 4; null in 1

Organisational commitment (7 studies): predominantly null
  — 5 null; 2 positive; 0 negative

Perceived organisational performance (7 studies): mixed
  — 2 positive; 3 null; 2 negative

PROFESSIONAL ISOLATION: consistent negative outcome — 5/5
  studies find telework predicts professional isolation
  [FLAG: No positive finding for isolation in any study]

Travel costs: consistent reduction — 6/6 studies

Perceived fairness: consistent positive — 3/3 studies

Post-pandemic effects (60 studies with post-COVID data):
  Work-life balance: 9/12 positive (strongest positive effect)
  Resilience: 6/6 positive
  Individual productivity: 5/11 positive, 4/11 negative,
    2/11 null → STILL CONFLICTING post-pandemic

CRITICAL FINDING FOR THESIS:
  Individual productivity evidence is CONFLICTING across 19 studies
  — 12 positive, 4 negative, 3 null. This directly supports the
  thesis mediation hypothesis: the effect of telework on
  productivity is NOT universal; it depends on contextual
  conditions (i.e., management systems). The SLR-level evidence
  at the public sector confirms what B05 (Maity2025) found for
  SMEs: direct telework → productivity effects are context-
  contingent, not automatic.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS — TELEWORK AS MEDIATOR/MODERATOR
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
As moderator (6 studies):
  - Telework weakens negative association between family
    responsibilities discrimination and work satisfaction/
    turnover intention (Mullins et al., 2021)
  - Work engagement → organisational identification stronger
    among remote vs. office workers (Uru et al., 2022)
  - Telework strengthens positive association between
    organisational freedom and engagement but ALSO exacerbates
    negative association between freedom and exhaustion
    (Giauque et al., 2022) — classic JD-R double-edged finding

As mediator (3 studies):
  - Telework mediates: public sector employment status →
    job satisfaction (Gastearena-Balda et al., 2021)
  - Smart work intention mediates: drivers/constraints →
    smart work usage (Eom et al., 2016)
  - Telework frequency × organisational impact of telework →
    performance: NEGATIVE indirect effect (Park & Jae, 2022)
    [FLAG: this negative mediation chain is notable — telework
    can decrease performance through its organisational impact;
    this is consistent with the thesis argument that without
    management systems, telework has null or negative productivity
    effects]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS — DYNAMICS OF TELEWORK
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  - Social disconnection between teleworkers and non-teleworkers
    is a persistent risk
  - Relationship-oriented leadership behaviours are critical for
    team cohesion in remote settings
  - Personal contact and trust are critical for cohesion
  - Introducing new control principles in stark conflict with
    prevailing conventions risks resistance and adoption failure
    (Taskin & Edwards, 2007): cautioned against abrupt control
    shifts
  - Post-pandemic: tacit managerial control practices evolve
    rather than disappear; civil servants reconceptualise
    workspaces and redefine time and emotions

Sector-specific issues: sector MATTERS for telework outcomes
  - Public organisations: more formalised personnel procedures,
    distinct IT use patterns, stronger hierarchical cultures
  - Telework effects on well-being, satisfaction, alienation are
    sector-moderated
  - "Monolithic, administrative-focused telework model" of public
    sector needs transition to "more flexible telework
    configurations" accommodating diverse employee needs

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.1 construct; Ch. 2.3
               SLR"), this is the primary public-sector-specific
               SLR for the thesis hybrid work (telework) construct.
               The four-dimension framework (space, time, technology,
               autonomy) provides the public sector operationalisation
               of the hybrid work mediator construct that B06 (Lauring
               2025) provides for the general HRM domain. In GGHN's
               public health NGO context, the authorisation-based,
               negotiated nature of telework (autonomy dimension) is
               directly applicable. Cite B10 alongside B06 for the
               thesis hybrid work construct definition in Ch. 2.1,
               noting public sector specificities.

Ch. 2.3 use  : The conflicting productivity evidence (12 positive,
               4 negative, 3 null out of 19 studies; still conflicting
               post-pandemic) is the strongest SLR-level empirical
               support for the thesis mediation hypothesis at the
               public sector level. If direct telework → productivity
               effects were universally positive, there would be no
               need for a mediating mechanism. B10 confirms the
               mediation gap explicitly for public organisations.
               Cite for: "Evidence on the effect of telework on
               individual productivity is conflicting across 19
               independent studies in public organisations, suggesting
               that contextual mediating factors — including
               management systems — determine the direction and
               magnitude of the productivity effect."

               Also cite for: supportive leadership as a consistent
               positive antecedent of telework adoption (8/12
               positive) — maps onto OMS as a prerequisite.

               Professional isolation as a consistent negative outcome
               of telework (5/5 studies) is relevant for GGHN's
               context: as a field-operations health NGO, social
               disconnection risks may be higher than in knowledge-
               intensive service organisations.

Research gap : B10 explicitly identifies lack of research in
               developing countries and nonprofit/NGO contexts.
               Countries represented: US 31%, Europe 34%, Australia
               8%, Brazil 6%, Canada 6%, South Africa 2% — Africa
               (excluding South Africa) is essentially absent. GGHN
               as an NGO/development sector organisation in Nigeria
               fills BOTH the geographical gap (Sub-Saharan Africa)
               and the sector gap (nonprofit/development) identified
               by this SLR. Cite directly in Ch. 3 / Statement of
               Problem.

               B10 also calls for "studies that look at differences
               between public and private, but also public and
               nonprofit organizations when implementing telework" —
               this is precisely the thesis contribution framing.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Public Administration Review (PAR) — Wiley; SSCI indexed;
      top PA journal; direct relevance to public sector context
  [+] PRISMA protocol with expert panel validation (6 experts) —
      rigorous review design
  [+] 120 primary studies — largest evidence base of any SLR
      in the corpus (B05: 12 studies; A11: varies; B10: 120)
  [+] Covers literature from early 1990s to May 2023 — fullest
      temporal scope of any SLR in the corpus
  [+] Pre/post-pandemic split analysis — allows distinction of
      COVID-specific vs. structural telework effects
  [+] Conflicting productivity evidence documented systematically
      (12+/4−/3 null): strongest SLR-level support for mediation
      hypothesis
  [+] Four-dimension construct definition (space/time/ICT/autonomy)
      tailored to public sector — more applicable to GGHN than
      generic HRM definitions
  [+] Explicit Africa/NGO gap identified — directly citable for
      thesis justification
  [+] Data available via Harvard Dataverse (FAIR principles)
  [~] No meta-analysis — narrative synthesis only; no pooled
      effect sizes
  [~] Web of Science only (Scopus excluded) — some relevant papers
      may be missing
  [~] English-language publications only — may miss relevant
      non-Anglophone research
  [~] US-dominated evidence base (31% from US, 51% of PA journal
      studies from US) — limits generalisability
  [~] Self-reported productivity dominant measure in underlying
      studies — authors acknowledge this limitation explicitly
  [~] No formal quality appraisal of individual studies (GRADE or
      equivalent) — authors exclude gray literature but do not
      formally rate study quality

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B05 (Maity2025) — conditional productivity:
  Both B05 (SME private sector SLR, 12 studies, global) and B10
  (public sector SLR, 120 studies, multinational) find conflicting
  productivity evidence: the effect of telework/FWA on productivity
  is neither universally positive nor negative. B10's 19-study
  breakdown (12+/4−/3 null) and B05's conditional thesis converge
  on the same conclusion: management context mediates the
  productivity effect. Together they provide the SLR-level
  foundation across two sectors (private SME + public) for the
  thesis mediation hypothesis.

Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — requirements risk:
  B10's finding that professional isolation is a consistent
  negative outcome of telework (5/5 studies) maps onto B08's
  requirements risk concept: in ambiguous, isolated remote settings,
  performance suffers because informal coordination breaks down.
  B10 provides the evidence base; B08 provides the mechanism.

Convergence with B09 (OBrady2025) — management as prerequisite:
  B10's finding that supportive leadership is the primary
  organisational antecedent of telework adoption (8/12 positive)
  directly mirrors B09's argument that management practices (not
  worker autonomy) determine remote work outcomes. Both papers,
  from different methodological traditions (SLR vs. qualitative
  comparative), confirm that management-side factors are the
  necessary condition for positive hybrid/remote work outcomes.

Convergence with B06 (Lauring2025) — construct definition:
  B10's four-dimension public sector framework (space, time, ICT,
  autonomy) partially overlaps with but extends B06's three
  dimensions (modality, location, temporality) by adding
  'autonomy' as an explicit fourth dimension. In public sector
  contexts (GGHN), autonomy is constrained by bureaucratic
  authorisation — this is a distinctive feature not captured by
  B06's definition. Cite both: B06 as the general HRM definition;
  B10 for the public sector specification with the autonomy
  qualification.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #2
(Direct WLB/flexibility → productivity is weak):
  B10 confirms this at the public sector SLR level: WLB shows
  predominantly positive effects (11/17), but productivity shows
  conflicting evidence (12+/4−/3 null) across the same studies
  of the same phenomenon. The direct WLB benefit of telework
  does NOT automatically translate into productivity gains — an
  intermediate mechanism (management systems) is required. This
  is the mediation argument confirmed in the public sector.

UNIQUE CONTRIBUTION — PUBLIC SECTOR SPECIFICITY:
  B10 is the only paper in the corpus that focuses exclusively on
  public sector telework and provides evidence that sector matters:
  telework effects on satisfaction, well-being, and performance
  differ between public and private sector. GGHN is a public
  health NGO — its sector characteristics (hierarchical culture,
  formalised personnel procedures, authorisation-based flexibility,
  accountability to external donors) are closer to the public
  sector profile than to the private SME context of B05.
  B10 is therefore the most directly applicable SLR to GGHN's
  organisational context.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Public sector telework SLR: first exclusively public sector
    SLR in corpus; 120 studies; Web of Science; no temporal
    constraint; up to May 2023; four-dimension framework
  - Public Administration Review (PAR): first PAR paper in corpus
    — top PA journal
  - Professional isolation as consistent negative outcome:
    5/5 studies; first time this is explicitly documented as
    the most consistent negative telework effect in the corpus
  - Perceived fairness as consistent positive outcome: 3/3 studies
  - Telework as negative mediator: Park & Jae (2022): telework
    frequency × organisational impact → negative performance
    (first negative mediation chain in corpus)
  - Sector matters as moderator: public vs. private sector
    differences in telework outcomes — first explicit sector
    comparison argument in corpus
  - Africa (excl. South Africa) and nonprofit/NGO context
    identified as gaps in the public sector telework literature

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Mele, V., Belardinelli, P., & Bellé, N. (2023). Telework in public
organizations: A systematic review and research agenda. Public
Administration Review, 83(6), 1649–1666.
https://doi.org/10.1111/puar.13734
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B11
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B11
Cite key        : Curzi2026
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — performance
                  management [PM] practices as OMS components:
                  appraisal criteria, goal setting, feedback format,
                  reward/development orientation),
                  T3 (hybrid work — remote work as moderator;
                  extent of remote work as continuous variable;
                  Italy; private sector),
                  T5 (productivity enablers — PM practices → IWB
                  via signalling theory; continuous feedback as
                  critical enabler in remote settings)
Title           : Redesigning performance management practices to
                  boost innovative behaviours: the moderating role
                  of remote work
Authors         : Curzi, Y., Scapolan, A. C., & Ferrarini, F.
Year            : 2026 (received 17 March 2025; revised 30 November
                  2025 and 5 February 2026; accepted 6 February 2026;
                  published in IJOA Vol. 34, No. 12, 2026, pp. 53–71)
Journal         : International Journal of Organizational Analysis
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 34, No. 12, pp. 53–71
DOI             : 10.1108/IJOA-03-2025-5320
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Marco Biagi Department of Economics + Department of
                  Communication and Economics, University of Modena
                  and Reggio Emilia, Italy

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  OLS regression with interaction terms (moderation);
                  simple slope analyses
Aim             : To explore whether and how PM practices affect
                  employees' innovative work behaviour (IWB) when
                  work is performed remotely; to identify remote work
                  as a boundary condition of the PM → IWB relationship
Setting/context : Italian private companies using digital technologies;
                  large/multinational firms (60% >250 employees);
                  data collected June–July 2022; 44% manufacturing,
                  53% service sector
Sample          : n = 647 usable questionnaires from 1,052 surveyed
                  employees; 75% male; 80%+ over 34 years old; 70%
                  hold bachelor's degree or higher; 40% managerial,
                  60% white-collar; 92% extensive digital technology
                  users
DV              : Innovative Work Behaviour (IWB): 7-item self-report
                  scale (De Jong & Den Hartog, 2010); Cronbach's
                  α = 0.90
IVs (PM         : (1) Result-based appraisal (3 items; dichotomised)
practices)        (2) Job-related skill-based appraisal (2 items;
                      dichotomised)
                  (3) New-competence-based appraisal (1 item;
                      dichotomised)
                  (4) Employee involvement in goal setting (1 item;
                      dichotomised)
                  (5) Continuous feedback (1 yes/no item)
                  (6) Multisource feedback (1 item; dichotomised)
                  (7) Wage increases (1 yes/no)
                  (8) Bonuses (1 yes/no)
                  (9) Career development (1 yes/no)
                  (10) Training opportunities (1 yes/no)
Moderator       : Remote work (2 items from EWCS; Cronbach's α=0.73;
                  standardised; measures extent of remote work)
Control         : Age, gender, education, job position, contract type,
                  tenure, sector; task- and time-based appraisal
Theoretical     : Signalling Theory of HRM (Connelly et al., 2010;
framework         Guest et al., 2021) — PM practices as signals that
                  inform employees about organisational expectations
                  and guide behaviour; signal salience varies with
                  remote work context

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Convenience sample; online survey; cross-sectional
Analysis        : Multivariate OLS regression (SPSS v.23); three
                  models (M1: controls; M2: controls + PM; M3: M2 +
                  remote work + all interaction terms); VIF <3.0
                  (no multicollinearity); simple slope analyses for
                  moderating effects
Reliability     : IWB α=0.90 ✓; Remote work α=0.73 ✓ (just above
                  threshold); individual PM practice items not
                  reliability-tested (most dichotomised; not scalable)
R² (Model 2)   : Adj. R²=0.107 (ΔAdj.R²=0.106*** from M1)
R² (Model 3)   : Adj. R²=0.148 (ΔAdj.R²=0.056*** from M2)
Limitations     : Convenience sample; cross-sectional; Italy only;
                  self-reported IWB; most PM practice measures
                  dichotomised; single-item measures for several
                  constructs; limited to large digital-technology firms

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS (HYPOTHESIS TESTING)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
MAIN EFFECT of remote work on IWB (Model 3):
  β = 0.103, p ≤ 0.10 — marginal positive effect: remote work
  slightly increases IWB overall

DIRECT PM → IWB EFFECTS (Model 2, without moderation):
  Result-based appraisal → IWB: β=0.146, p≤0.05 ✓ POSITIVE
  New-competence-based appraisal → IWB: β=0.181, p≤0.05 ✓ POSITIVE
  Employee involvement in goal setting → IWB: β=0.317, p≤0.01 ✓
    (strongest direct effect)
  Job-related skill-based appraisal → IWB: β=0.008, p>0.10 NS
  Continuous feedback → IWB: β=0.050, p>0.10 NS (direct only)
  Compensation and developmental PM: not significant directly

MODERATION BY REMOTE WORK (Model 3 interaction terms):

H1a (result-based appraisal — NOT moderated): rejected
  → Remote work × result-based: β=−0.072, p>0.10 NS
  → Result-based appraisal supports IWB regardless of remote work
    extent ✓

H1b (new-competence-based — NOT moderated): partially supported
  → Remote work × new-competence: β=0.003, p>0.10 NS
  → New-competence-based supports IWB regardless of remote work ✓
  → Job-related skill-based: neither main effect nor moderation

H2 (employee involvement in goal setting — NEGATIVELY moderated):
  → Remote work × goal setting: β=−0.185, p≤0.05 ✓
  → Simple slopes: goal setting → IWB positive at low remote work
    (β=0.490, p≤0.01); NON-SIGNIFICANT at high remote work
    (β=0.120, p>0.10)
  → Involvement in goal setting loses effectiveness remotely

H3 (continuous feedback — POSITIVELY moderated): partially
  → Remote work × continuous feedback: β=0.154, p≤0.05 ✓
  → Simple slopes: continuous feedback → IWB POSITIVE only at high
    remote work (β=0.196, p≤0.05); non-significant at low remote
    work (β=−0.112, p>0.10)
  → Continuous feedback is the CRITICAL PM practice for remote IWB

H4a (wage increases — moderated, sign reversal):
  → Remote work × wage increases: β=0.158, p≤0.05 ✓
  → Simple slopes: wage increases REDUCE IWB at low remote work
    (β=−0.182, p≤0.05); NO effect at high remote work
    (β=0.134, p>0.10)
  → Compensation-based PM ceases to harm IWB in remote settings

H4b (training opportunities — NEGATIVELY moderated):
  → Remote work × training opportunities: β=−0.493, p≤0.05 ✓
  → Simple slopes: training → IWB POSITIVE at low remote work
    (β=0.734, p≤0.05); NO effect at high remote work
    (β=−0.252, p>0.10)
  → Developmental PM (training) loses effectiveness remotely

[FLAG: β=−0.493 is the largest interaction coefficient in the
paper — training opportunities' effectiveness DROPS sharply with
remote work. This is counter-intuitive and the authors interpret
it via signalling theory: in remote settings, training signals
are weaker without spontaneous micro-interactions that reinforce
their meaning. However, with n=647 and a convenience sample, this
large coefficient should be cited with care.]

SUMMARY OF KEY FINDINGS:
  PM practices that WORK regardless of remote work extent:
    → Result-based appraisal (β=0.109, p≤0.05 in M3)
    → New-competence-based appraisal (β=0.189, p≤0.05 in M3)
  PM practices that WORK ONLY at high remote work:
    → Continuous feedback (β=0.196, p≤0.05 at high remote)
  PM practices that LOSE effectiveness as remote work increases:
    → Employee involvement in goal setting (nullified)
    → Training opportunities (nullified)
  PM practices whose NEGATIVE effect disappears remotely:
    → Wage increases (negative at low remote, null at high)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 empirical+moderation"),
               B11 provides the only paper in the corpus with direct
               moderation analysis of remote work on the PM →
               performance (here: IWB) relationship. The signalling
               theory framing of PM practices is directly applicable
               to the thesis OMS construct: management systems
               (appraisal criteria, goal setting, feedback, rewards)
               serve as organisational signals that guide employee
               behaviour, and their effectiveness is MODERATED by
               the extent of remote/hybrid work.
               
               The key finding — continuous feedback is THE critical
               PM practice enabling IWB in remote settings — is
               directly citable for the thesis argument that
               management systems must be redesigned for hybrid work
               contexts. At GGHN, feedback frequency and quality (OMS
               component) may be the single most important management
               systems adaptation needed for hybrid work → performance.

Ch. 2.2 use  : The signalling theory framework provides an additional
               theoretical lens for the thesis mediation hypothesis:
               management systems (MIS/OMS) function as signals that
               align employee behaviour with organisational goals;
               in hybrid work contexts, the signal-to-noise ratio
               changes (fewer spontaneous co-located cues), making
               formal management systems MORE critical as mediating
               mechanisms. Cite B11 for this theoretical extension.

Thesis        : The finding that some PM practices (training, goal
contribution   setting involvement) LOSE effectiveness in remote
               settings — while others (result-based appraisal,
               continuous feedback) GAIN or MAINTAIN effectiveness
               — supports the thesis argument that GGHN must
               deliberately select and redesign management systems
               components for its hybrid context rather than applying
               a blanket OMS framework.

Limitation   : IWB (innovative work behaviour) is not the same as
               employee productivity — there is some overlap (both
               are performance outcomes) but the thesis DV is
               productivity, not innovation. The finding that
               continuous feedback is critical for remote IWB may
               or may not transfer directly to remote productivity.
               Additionally, Italy-only, convenience sample, cross-
               sectional design, and large proportions of dichotomised
               single-item measures limit confidence. The β=−0.493
               interaction for training should be cited with caution.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Only paper in corpus with moderation analysis of remote work
      on PM → performance relationship — unique empirical design
  [+] IWB scale α=0.90; EWCS-validated remote work measure α=0.73
  [+] Comprehensive PM typology — 10 distinct PM practice components
      tested simultaneously
  [+] Simple slope analyses conducted for all interaction effects
  [+] VIF <3.0 across all models (no multicollinearity)
  [+] Signalling Theory of HRM provides coherent theoretical
      framework integrating PM and remote work
  [+] n=647 — adequate for OLS regression with multiple interactions
  [+] Published in Emerald IJOA; CC BY 4.0 open access
  [~] Convenience sample — not randomly drawn; generalisability
      limited
  [~] Cross-sectional design — no causal inference
  [~] Italy only — single country; no cross-national comparison
  [~] Most PM practice measures are dichotomised single items —
      low measurement precision; no scale reliability for most IVs
  [~] DV is IWB (innovative work behaviour), not productivity —
      partial conceptual overlap but not equivalent to thesis DV
  [~] β=−0.493 (training × remote work) is very large and should
      be interpreted cautiously given the single-item measure and
      convenience sample
  [-] Self-reported DV (IWB) — common method variance risk with
      same-source PM practice measures

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B09 (OBrady2025) — continuous feedback:
  B09 found that developmental coaching (as opposed to discipline-
  based coaching) is the key management systems component for
  remote worker wellbeing. B11 empirically shows that continuous
  feedback (the closest PM equivalent of developmental coaching)
  is the ONLY PM practice that significantly increases IWB in high-
  remote-work settings (β=0.196, p≤0.05 at high remote). Together:
  B09 provides the institutional conditions for feedback-based
  management; B11 provides the individual-level outcome evidence.

Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — informal vs. formal control:
  B08 found that informal self-control (autonomy, self-monitoring)
  mediates remote work → performance more strongly than formal
  outcome control. B11's finding that continuous feedback (an
  informal, relational PM practice) drives IWB in remote settings,
  while formal goal-setting involvement and training programmes
  lose effectiveness, is the PM-level equivalent of B08's
  formal/informal control distinction.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025) — QWL/WLB as mediator:
  B07's QWL pathway (β=0.222) and B11's continuous feedback finding
  both point to the same mechanism: when management practices attend
  to employee experience (well-being, relatedness, sense of
  connection) in remote settings, performance-relevant outcomes
  improve. Continuous feedback in B11 reduces the social isolation
  risk documented in B10 (Mele2023; professional isolation: 5/5
  studies negative) and enhances QWL components (Pillar 3 in B05).

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #3
(Training as standalone non-significant):
  B11's finding that training opportunities LOSE their positive
  effect on IWB as remote work increases (β=−0.493 moderation)
  directly reinforces Block A's finding that training as a
  standalone management systems component is non-significant across
  seven independent studies. B11 now provides the mechanism: in
  remote/hybrid settings, the PM signal conveyed by training is
  weakened by the absence of contextual reinforcement from co-
  located informal interactions. This extends the Block A pattern
  from management systems → productivity to management systems →
  IWB in hybrid contexts.

NEW CONTRIBUTION — REMOTE WORK AS BOUNDARY CONDITION:
  B11 is the only paper in the corpus that explicitly tests remote
  work as a MODERATOR of management systems → performance, rather
  than a predictor or mediator. This directly addresses the thesis
  methodological gap: instead of treating hybrid work as a mediator
  (thesis model) or a moderator (B11's model), the thesis argues
  for mediation. B11's moderation evidence is complementary —
  it shows that the effectiveness of management systems DEPENDS
  on the hybrid work context, which is consistent with both a
  moderation and a mediation interpretation. Cite B11 in Ch. 3
  to justify why the thesis chose a mediation (rather than
  moderation) model while acknowledging B11's boundary condition
  framing.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - OLS regression moderation analysis: first explicit moderation
    test (interaction terms) of remote work on PM → IWB in corpus
  - Innovative Work Behaviour (IWB) as DV: first in corpus —
    closest performance-adjacent outcome to productivity not
    previously tested
  - Signalling Theory of HRM (Guest et al. 2021): first in corpus
    as primary theoretical framework
  - Continuous feedback as remote-work-specific PM enabler: first
    empirical evidence in corpus that continuous feedback uniquely
    drives performance-relevant outcomes in remote settings
  - Italy context: first Italian empirical study in Block B
  - IJOA (International Journal of Organizational Analysis):
    first Emerald IJOA paper in corpus
  - Training opportunities × remote work negative interaction:
    first empirical test of this relationship; reinforces Block A
    pattern #3 in a remote work context

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Curzi, Y., Scapolan, A. C., & Ferrarini, F. (2026). Redesigning
performance management practices to boost innovative behaviours: the
moderating role of remote work. International Journal of
Organizational Analysis, 34(12), 53–71.
https://doi.org/10.1108/IJOA-03-2025-5320
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```
