```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B12
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B12
Cite key        : Tsang2023
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — WFH as flexible work arrangement;
                  WFH days per week as moderator; COVID-19 mandatory
                  remote work in Taiwan; SME context),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — FWC as
                  barrier; WE and SE as mediators; WFH days as
                  negative moderator; gender differences),
                  T6 (empirical: FWC → WE/SE → WFH productivity;
                  partial mediation; moderated-mediation; Taiwan;
                  COVID-19 pandemic)
Title           : Family–work conflict and work-from-home
                  productivity: do work engagement and self-efficacy
                  mediate?
Authors         : Tsang, S.-S., Liu, Z.-L., & Nguyen, T. V. T.
Year            : 2023 (received 22 October 2022; accepted 11 July
                  2023)
Journal         : Humanities and Social Sciences Communications
                  (Springer Nature)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 10, Article 1 (2023); Springer Nature open
DOI             : 10.1057/s41599-023-01929-y
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : National Taiwan University of Science and
                  Technology, Taipei, Taiwan (Tsang, Liu); Ho Chi
                  Minh City University of Foreign Languages —
                  Information Technology, Vietnam (Nguyen)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  CFA (AMOS 20); PROCESS Macro Model 5 (moderated
                  mediation); OLS regression controls
Aim             : To investigate the association between family–work
                  conflict (FWC) and work-from-home productivity
                  (WFHP) among Taiwanese SME employees during the
                  COVID-19 pandemic; to test mediation by work
                  engagement (WE) and self-efficacy (SE); to test
                  moderation by WFH days per week
Setting/context : Taiwan; COVID-19 Level 3 Alert period (May–
                  December 2021, first wave); SME employees in
                  Taipei, New Taipei, Taichung, and Tainan; WFH was
                  government-mandated
Sample          : n=785 valid cases (from 1,177 returned; from 1,307
                  distributed; response rate 90.1%); 54.5% male;
                  43.4% aged 31–40; predominantly experienced (34.2%
                  >10 years); 51% worked 4–5 WFH days/week
IVs             : Family–Work Conflict (FWC): 5 items (Netemeyer
                  et al., 1996); Cronbach's α=0.90; family demands
                  interfering with work
Mediators       : (M1) Work Engagement (WE): 9 items (Schaufeli
                    et al., 2006); α=0.89; vigour, dedication,
                    absorption
                  (M2) Self-Efficacy (SE): 6 items (Rigotti et al.,
                    2008); α=0.88; occupational SE during WFH
Moderator       : WFH Days per week (WFHDs): categorical (<2, 2–3,
                  4–5, >5 days); moderates FWC → WFHP path
DV              : WFH Productivity (WFHP): 7 items (Irawanto et al.,
                  2021); α=0.88; perceived self-reported productivity
                  during WFH
Theoretical     : Role Conflict Theory (Kahn et al., 1964) — limited
framework         time/energy resources must be distributed across
                  roles; conflict reduces engagement and productivity
                  Resource Drain Theory (Rothbard & Edwards, 2003)
                  — investing in one role depletes resources for
                  others; FWC → reduced SE

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Convenience sample; printed questionnaire; cross-
                  sectional; COVID-19 pandemic context
Analysis        : CFA (AMOS 20) for construct validity; PROCESS Macro
                  Model 5 (Preacher & Hayes, 2004) for moderated
                  mediation; OLS for demographic controls (ANOVA);
                  all VIFs <5; CMV: Harman's single-factor (30.42% <
                  50% threshold — no CMV)
Reliability     : All Cronbach's α ≥ 0.88 (threshold 0.60; all above
                  0.80) ✓
Convergent      : All AVE >0.50; CR >0.70; all factor loadings >0.60
validity          ✓; CFA fit: CMIN/df=2.83 (<3); CFI=0.917; IFI=0.918
                  (both >0.90); RMSEA=0.048 (<0.05) ✓
Discriminant    : All inter-construct correlations < √AVE ✓
validity

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS (HYPOTHESIS TESTING)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
DIRECT EFFECTS:
H1: FWC → WFHP: β=−0.26, p<0.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → Family–work conflict directly reduces WFH productivity

H2: FWC → WE: β=−0.30, p<0.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → FWC significantly reduces work engagement

H3: WE → WFHP: β=0.11, p<0.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → Work engagement positively affects WFH productivity

H5: FWC → SE: β=−0.25, p<0.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → FWC significantly reduces self-efficacy

H6: SE → WFHP: β=0.37, p<0.001 ✓ SUPPORTED (STRONGEST PATH)
  → Self-efficacy is the strongest positive predictor of WFHP

MEDIATION EFFECTS (PROCESS Macro Model 5):
H4: WE mediates FWC → WFHP:
  Indirect effect β=−0.03 (LLCI=−0.0634, ULCI=−0.0063) ✓
  → PARTIAL MEDIATION (direct path remains significant)

H7: SE mediates FWC → WFHP:
  Indirect effect β=−0.09 (LLCI=−0.1242, ULCI=−0.0621) ✓
  → PARTIAL MEDIATION; SE is the stronger mediating pathway
    (β=−0.09 vs. WE β=−0.03)

MODERATION EFFECT:
H8: WFH days moderates FWC → WFHP:
  Interaction β=−0.07, SE=0.02, t=−2.87, p<0.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → FWC × WFH days negatively affects WFHP
  → Conditional effects at:
      Mean WFHDs: β=−0.28, p<0.001
      −1SD (fewer days): β=−0.14, p<0.01
      +1SD (more days): β=−0.35, p<0.001
  → The more WFH days, the stronger the negative effect of
    FWC on WFHP (i.e. WFHDs amplify the harm of FWC)

[FLAG: This is counter-intuitive for the thesis. WFH days strengthen
the NEGATIVE effect of FWC on productivity — more remote work
without family support systems = worse productivity. This is NOT
a direct refutation of hybrid work but of unstructured/unmanaged
WFH without WLB supports. The implication is that management
systems (family-friendly policies, WLB supports, engagement
mechanisms) must accompany hybrid work to prevent this negative
amplification.]

CONTROL VARIABLE FINDING — GENDER:
  Males: WFHP M=3.60; Females: WFHP M=3.37
  F(1,783)=20.478, p<0.001, η²=0.03
  → Significant gender gap in WFHP; female employees report
    lower productivity, attributable to Confucian family role
    norms and heavier domestic burden during WFH

OVERALL PATH MODEL (see Fig. 3):
  FWC → WFHP direct: −0.26
  FWC → WE → WFHP indirect: −0.03 (partial mediation)
  FWC → SE → WFHP indirect: −0.09 (partial mediation)
  FWC × WFHDs → WFHP: −0.07 (moderation: more days = worse)
  SE → WFHP: 0.37 (strongest coefficient in model)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 empirical+mediation"),
               B12 provides mediation evidence at the individual
               psychological level that complements the thesis's
               management systems mediation argument at the
               organisational level. The finding that WE (β=−0.03)
               and SE (β=−0.09) partially mediate FWC → WFHP
               demonstrates that employee psychological states
               (engagement and self-efficacy) are transmission
               mechanisms between work arrangement stressors and
               productivity — directly analogous to but distinct from
               the management systems mediation path.

               Cite B12 for the argument that WFH productivity is
               NOT determined solely by the work arrangement itself
               but by the psychological resources employees bring
               to it (engagement, SE) — which are in turn shaped
               by management practices (B09, B11). The chain:
               Management systems → Employee engagement/SE → WFH
               productivity maps exactly onto the thesis mediation
               model, with B12 providing the individual-level
               mechanism between the mediator (hybrid work supported
               by management systems) and the DV (productivity).

               Also cite the moderating finding: WFH days without
               family support systems amplify the negative productivity
               effects of FWC — the implication is that management
               systems must actively enable WLB and family-friendly
               policies to prevent the WFH duration from becoming
               counter-productive.

Ch. 2.1 use  : WFH days (WFHDs) as the operationalisation of the
               location/temporality dimension of hybrid work is
               directly consistent with B06 (Lauring2025) taxonomy.
               The finding that more WFH days = stronger negative
               productivity effects without support systems supports
               B06's argument that hybrid work (balanced switching)
               is preferable to full remote work.

Limitation   : Taiwan-only; COVID-19 mandatory WFH context (not
               voluntary hybrid work); convenience sample; cross-
               sectional; self-reported WFHP; WFHDs measured
               categorically; Confucian gender norms context may
               not generalise to Nigeria/GGHN; SME employees not
               directly comparable to GGHN health NGO staff.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] All Cronbach's α ≥ 0.88 — excellent reliability across all
      four constructs (threshold 0.60; far exceeds 0.70 standard)
  [+] CFA-confirmed convergent and discriminant validity; all AVE
      >0.50; all CR >0.70
  [+] Excellent CFA model fit: CMIN/df=2.83; CFI=0.917;
      RMSEA=0.048 — meets all standard thresholds
  [+] PROCESS Macro Model 5 with bootstrapped CIs for indirect
      effects — best practice for mediation testing
  [+] CMV tested via Harman's single-factor (30.42% — no CMV)
  [+] n=785 valid cases — adequate for moderated mediation model
  [+] Strong response rate 90.1% — minimises non-response bias
  [+] SE as mediator: novel contribution (first to test SE between
      FWC and WFHP in WFH/pandemic context)
  [+] Humanities and Social Sciences Communications (Springer
      Nature): respectable multidisciplinary open-access journal
  [~] Convenience sample — not probability-based
  [~] Cross-sectional — causal inference limited
  [~] Taiwan/SME context — limited generalisability to GGHN
  [~] COVID-19 mandatory WFH ≠ voluntary hybrid work
  [~] Self-reported WFHP — perceived not objective productivity
  [~] WFHDs measured categorically rather than continuously
  [~] Confucian gender norms finding may be culture-specific

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #2
(Direct WLB/flexibility → productivity effects are weak):
  B12 provides the MECHANISM for why this pattern holds. When
  FWC (a form of WLB failure) is high, it does NOT directly
  eliminate productivity — it works THROUGH work engagement
  (β=−0.03) and self-efficacy (β=−0.09). The direct FWC →
  WFHP path (β=−0.26) is partially but not fully mediated.
  This partial mediation architecture mirrors Block A's finding
  that WLB is a weak direct predictor of productivity: the WLB
  → productivity relationship is mediated by psychological states
  (WE, SE) that are in turn shaped by management practices.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025) — QWL pathway:
  B07's QWL → WFHP indirect effect (β=0.222) and B12's SE →
  WFHP path (β=0.37) both converge on the same conclusion: the
  strongest path to WFH productivity runs through employee
  well-being and psychological resources, not through structural
  work arrangements. SE in B12 and QWL in B07 are conceptually
  adjacent (both capture employee capability, confidence, and
  sense of value). B12's stronger β for the SE pathway (0.37)
  vs. B07's QWL pathway (0.222) may reflect the different
  construct operationalisations but the directional pattern is
  identical.

Convergence with B09 (OBrady2025) — WLB/family:
  B09 identified that developmental coaching and constrained
  monitoring improve remote worker wellbeing. B12 now shows
  WHY: when management practices improve WLB (reducing FWC),
  this restores work engagement (β: FWC→WE=−0.30) and SE
  (β: FWC→SE=−0.25), which then improves productivity. The
  management system interventions in B09 (coaching, fair
  monitoring) can be understood as FWC-reduction mechanisms
  from B12's perspective.

Convergence with B10 (Mele2023) — WLB effects:
  B10 found WLB predominantly positive but conflicting across
  17 public sector studies. B12 provides the individual-level
  explanation: WLB effects on productivity are mediated by WE
  and SE — making the WLB → productivity relationship context-
  dependent on these psychological states and thus appearing
  "conflicting" at the aggregate level.

Convergence with B11 (Curzi2026) — PM as boundary condition:
  B11 showed continuous feedback is critical for remote IWB.
  B12 shows why: continuous feedback (a management system
  component) reduces uncertainty and social isolation, thereby
  maintaining SE (β: SE→WFHP=0.37) and WE (β: WE→WFHP=0.11)
  in remote settings. B11 identifies the PM intervention; B12
  provides the psychological mechanism.

NEW FINDING FOR THESIS — WFH DURATION AS RISK AMPLIFIER:
  B12's moderation finding (WFH days strengthen FWC→WFHP
  negativity) implies that unmanaged, prolonged remote work
  is counter-productive. For GGHN, where hybrid work may
  involve extended remote periods without robust family support
  systems, this is a direct risk. The implication: GGHN's
  management systems must include family-friendly policies and
  WFH duration guidelines as specific OMS components to prevent
  the WFH-days-amplification effect documented in B12.

GENDER FINDING FOR THESIS:
  B12's gender gap (male WFHP > female WFHP, F=20.478,
  p<0.001) in a Confucian context may have partial relevance
  to GGHN's Nigerian socio-cultural context, where similar
  gendered domestic burden dynamics exist. If GGHN has
  a predominantly female health workforce (common in NGO
  public health organisations), the FWC → WFHP risk may be
  disproportionately concentrated in female employees. Flag
  for GGHN-specific discussion in Ch. 3.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - FWC (Family-Work Conflict) as antecedent of WFHP: first
    in corpus; β=−0.26, p<0.001; partial mediation via WE
    and SE; Taiwan SMEs; COVID-19 mandatory WFH
  - Work Engagement (WE) as mediator: first explicit WE
    mediation in corpus (β=−0.03 indirect via WE; WE→WFHP
    β=0.11); vigour/dedication/absorption operationalisation
  - Self-Efficacy (SE) as mediator: first in corpus; strongest
    coefficient (SE→WFHP β=0.37); occupational SE during WFH
  - WFH days (duration) as NEGATIVE moderator: first in corpus;
    more WFH days amplifies FWC harm (β=−0.07, p<0.01)
  - Role Conflict Theory + Resource Drain Theory: first joint
    application of these dual theories in corpus
  - PROCESS Macro Model 5 moderated mediation: first in corpus
  - Humanities and Social Sciences Communications: first
    Springer Nature HSSC paper in corpus
  - Gender gap in WFH productivity: first empirical gender
    effect confirmed in corpus (ANOVA F=20.478, p<0.001)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Tsang, S.-S., Liu, Z.-L., & Nguyen, T. V. T. (2023). Family–work
conflict and work-from-home productivity: do work engagement and
self-efficacy mediate? Humanities and Social Sciences Communications,
10, Article 1. https://doi.org/10.1057/s41599-023-01929-y
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B13
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B13
Cite key        : Andeyo2023
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work / flexible work arrangements —
                  flexi-time and job sharing as FWA constructs;
                  Kenyan public university context),
                  T6 (empirical: FWA → employee performance; Africa;
                  Kenya; public sector; non-teaching university
                  employees; cross-sectional regression)
Title           : Effects of flexible work arrangements on employee
                  performance among non-teaching employees in
                  selected public universities, Western Kenya
Authors         : Andeyo, L. M., Otuya, W. I., & Misigo, G.
Year            : 2023
Journal         : African Journal of Empirical Research
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 5, No. 3, pp. 214–228
DOI             : 10.51867/ajernet.5.3.21
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes (AJOL open access)
Affiliations    : Masinde Muliro University of Science and
                  Technology (MMUST), Western Kenya

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  Pearson correlation + simple linear regression (OLS);
                  descriptive statistics; interview schedule for
                  qualitative triangulation
Aim             : To assess the effect of flexible work arrangements
                  (FWA) on employee performance among non-teaching
                  employees in selected public universities in
                  Western Kenya
Setting/context : Eight selected public universities in Western Kenya;
                  non-teaching staff (administrative, support, hostel,
                  catering, library, security, health services);
                  cross-sectional design
Sample          : Target population: 3,538 non-teaching employees;
                  sample size: 343 (Yamane, 1967 formula); stratified
                  random sampling; 55% female; predominantly aged
                  30–39; 100% effective response rate
                  [FLAG: 100% response rate is unusual; questionnaires
                  appear to have been administered directly (controlled
                  distribution), not mailed. This reduces non-response
                  bias but limits external validity.]
IV              : Flexible Work Arrangements (FWA): operationalised
                  as (1) Flexi-time and (2) Job sharing; 4-item
                  composite scale (5-point Likert); composite mean
                  4.01; composite SD 1.25
DV              : Employee Performance: operationalised as (1) Work
                  output and (2) Attendance; 4-item composite scale
                  (5-point Likert); composite mean 4.37; composite
                  SD 1.30
Theoretical     : Spill-Over Theory (Piotrkowski, 1979) — work and
frameworks        non-work domains spill over bidirectionally; FWA
                  facilitates positive spillover → better performance
                  Social Exchange Theory (Homans, 1958; Blau, 1964)
                  — employer provision of FWA creates reciprocal
                  employee commitment and performance improvement

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Cross-sectional; stratified random sampling;
                  questionnaire + interview schedule
Reliability     : Cronbach's α = 0.896 (composite, all instruments)
                  [FLAG: Single overall α reported, not per construct;
                  individual scale αs not disaggregated. Threshold
                  0.70; 0.896 exceeds threshold overall ✓]
Validity        : Content validity (expert judgment); construct
                  validity (pilot study at University of Eldoret)
CMB             : Not tested; same-source single questionnaire
Model fit       : Not applicable (OLS regression, not SEM)
Analysis        : SPSS v.27; descriptive statistics (frequencies,
                  means, SD); Pearson's r correlation; simple linear
                  regression; content analysis for interview data
Regression      : Model R=0.771; R²=0.595; Adj.R²=0.594;
equation          F(1,341)=501.098, p<0.001
                  β=0.765 (unstandardised); standardised β=0.771
                  t=22.385, p=0.001 <0.05 ✓
                  Constant = 0.833
                  Performance = 0.833 + 0.765 × FWA

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H0 (null): FWA has no significant effect on performance among non-
  teaching employees in selected public universities, Western Kenya.
  → REJECTED at 5% significance level

H1 (alternative): FWA has a significant positive effect on
  employee performance: t=22.385, p=0.001 (<0.05) ✓ SUPPORTED

REGRESSION RESULTS (Table 4):
  β (unstandardised) = 0.765
  Standardised β = 0.771
  R² = 0.595 (59.5% of variance in performance explained by FWA)
  F(1,341) = 501.098, p < 0.001

  Interpretation: a one-unit increase in FWA is associated with a
  0.765-unit increase in employee performance. The relationship is
  strong (R=0.771) and accounts for nearly 60% of outcome variance.

DESCRIPTIVE FINDINGS:
  FWA composite mean = 4.01 (>neutral midpoint of 3.0); most
  respondents agree/strongly agree that FWA improves performance
  Employee performance composite mean = 4.37 (high; most
  respondents report high performance under FWA conditions)

QUALITATIVE TRIANGULATION (interviews with HR managers):
  Work-life balance strategies strongly linked to performance;
  employees under FWA feel more motivated than under fixed schedules;
  counselling services and recreational facilities provided to
  reduce stress; minimal absenteeism reported in flexible shift
  contexts; supervisors co-design flexible schedules with employees.

SECTOR CONTEXT:
  Non-teaching employees in Kenyan public universities face:
  heavy workload, reduced government funding, short-staffing,
  obsolete equipment, strikes, delayed salaries — all of which
  contextualise the strong positive FWA → performance finding as
  employees under FWA perceive greater autonomy and satisfaction
  relative to a challenging baseline.

[FLAG — METHODOLOGICAL CAUTION:
  (1) 100% response rate is exceptional and suggests direct
      classroom/administered collection rather than independent
      survey distribution — minimises non-response bias but
      raises social desirability concerns.
  (2) R²=0.595 is very high for a single-IV regression model in
      social science; this may reflect limited scale variation,
      shared method variance, or the small item sets.
  (3) FWA measured only via flexi-time and job sharing — does not
      capture telework, hybrid work, compressed workweeks, or
      other dimensions. Construct coverage is narrow.
  (4) Single overall Cronbach's α (0.896) is reported without
      per-construct disaggregation — limits reliability assessment.
  (5) No CMB testing; same-source data.
  (6) Published in AJOL (African Journal of Empirical Research) —
      a less prestigious outlet than most Block B papers, but
      AJOL-indexed and peer-reviewed.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 Africa empirical"),
               B13 is the only Block B paper with a direct African
               public sector empirical finding on FWA → employee
               performance. The Kenyan public university context
               (government-funded, resource-constrained, unionised,
               administrative staff) is the closest available
               analogue to GGHN's organisational context in the
               corpus. Cite B13 for the Africa-specific empirical
               foundation: "In the Kenyan public sector, flexible
               work arrangements significantly and positively
               predict employee performance (β=0.765, t=22.385,
               p<0.001), with FWA explaining 59.5% of performance
               variance among non-teaching public university
               employees (Andeyo et al., 2023)."

               The finding is particularly relevant given that B10
               (Mele2023) and B05 (Maity2025) both confirmed an
               Africa gap in the global literature. B13 is the
               empirical paper that partially fills that gap for
               the thesis.

               Use with caution: the high R² and unusual response
               rate suggest the estimate should be qualified as
               potentially conservative (social desirability) or
               overestimated (method variance). Acknowledge the
               methodological limitations in Ch. 2.3.

Thesis        : B13 provides the direct-effect FWA → performance
contribution   evidence for the African/Kenyan public sector context.
               Given the thesis mediation hypothesis (management
               systems mediate this relationship), the finding that
               FWA directly predicts performance (β=0.765) without
               management systems as explicit mediator is NOT a
               challenge to the thesis — it is consistent with the
               thesis prediction. In a well-managed, positive
               baseline context, FWA will produce performance
               improvements. The mediation question is whether
               management systems explain HOW this happens. B13
               documents the baseline direct effect; the thesis adds
               the mediating mechanism.

Limitation   : Single-country (Kenya); public university sector;
               non-teaching staff only; narrow FWA construct
               (flexi-time + job sharing only; no telework/hybrid);
               very high R² and 100% response rate warrant caution;
               no CMB testing; per-construct reliability not reported;
               no management systems or WLB measures included as
               potential mediators. Cross-sectional causal inference
               limited.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Africa-specific empirical evidence: first and only Kenyan
      public sector FWA → performance study in Block B
  [+] MMUST institutional affiliation — credible Kenyan academic
      institution; stratified random sampling from 3,538 population
  [+] Qualitative triangulation via HR manager interviews provides
      contextual depth beyond the quantitative model
  [+] Overall Cronbach's α = 0.896 — strong composite reliability
  [+] Spill-Over + Social Exchange Theory dual-theoretical frame
      is coherent and appropriate to the African public sector
  [+] Regression clearly reported (R²=0.595; β=0.765; t=22.385;
      F=501.098; p<0.001)
  [+] AJOL-indexed; peer-reviewed; open access
  [~] 100% response rate unusual — administered rather than
      independent distribution likely
  [~] Single overall Cronbach's α; per-construct αs not reported
  [~] R²=0.595 is very high for a single-predictor social science
      model — potential shared method variance
  [~] FWA construct narrowly operationalised (flexi-time + job
      sharing only; no telework/hybrid/compressed workweek)
  [~] No CMB testing
  [~] Simple linear regression only — no mediation analysis,
      no moderation, no control variables beyond demographics
  [-] AJOL/African Journal of Empirical Research — lower
      international ranking than core Block B outlets

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #2
(Direct WLB/FWA → productivity effects are weak in the broader corpus):
  B13's β=0.765 (FWA → performance) is the HIGHEST direct
  flexibility → productivity coefficient in the entire Block B
  corpus. This is more consistent with the Kenyan public university
  baseline than with the global pattern. The difference may reflect:
  (a) method variance in a single-source, 100% response rate study;
  (b) the Kenyan public sector baseline is so severely resource-
      constrained that even basic FWA (flexi-time, job sharing)
      has outsized effects; (c) limited FWA construct coverage
      (only two dimensions tested). Use B13 as the Africa baseline
      direct effect, but note its methodological caution for any
      effect size comparison with B07, B08, or other papers.

Convergence with B10 (Mele2023) — Africa gap:
  B10 confirmed Sub-Saharan Africa (excluding South Africa) as
  absent from the public sector telework literature. B13 directly
  fills part of this gap for the Kenyan public sector. Both papers
  together establish: (1) global literature lacks African evidence
  (B10); (2) where African evidence exists (Kenya, B13), flexible
  work positively predicts performance in the public sector;
  (3) GGHN Nigeria is therefore even more under-researched.

Convergence with B04 (Nweze2026) — Nigeria public sector:
  B04 found structured HWS β=0.658 on performance in Nigerian
  public media (Ebonyi State). B13's β=0.765 in Kenyan public
  universities: both are African public sector empirical papers
  confirming strong positive FWA/HWS → performance effects.
  The similarity in coefficient magnitude (0.658 vs. 0.765) across
  different countries, sectors, and methodologies provides
  convergent evidence for Africa-specific positive FWA effects.
  This is the most geographically proximate convergence in the
  entire corpus for the GGHN Nigeria context.

Convergence with B12 (Tsang2023) — construct alignment:
  B12 showed FWC (family-work conflict) negatively mediates WFH
  productivity via WE and SE. B13's spillover theory framework
  is the direct theoretical parallel: when FWA enables positive
  spillover (work-life balance), performance improves; when rigid
  schedules create role conflict (negative spillover = FWC in B12
  terms), performance suffers. The two papers together provide the
  spillover mechanism (B12: psychological mediation) and the
  direct outcome evidence in an African context (B13).

NEW CONTRIBUTION — AFRICA BASELINE EVIDENCE:
  B13 is the empirical anchor for the Africa-specific direct
  effect of FWA → performance that the thesis requires to
  justify the GGHN case study. Without B13, the thesis would
  need to extrapolate from Asian (B12), UAE (B07), US (B08),
  or European (B09) evidence. B13 provides Sub-Saharan African
  (Kenyan) public sector grounding, which is the most relevant
  comparative baseline for GGHN's organisational context.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Kenya public sector FWA → performance: first in corpus;
    public universities; non-teaching staff; n=343; β=0.765
  - African Journal of Empirical Research (AJOL): first AJOL
    paper in Block B
  - Spill-Over Theory (Piotrkowski 1979): first in corpus as
    primary theoretical framework
  - Flexi-time + Job sharing as FWA operationalisation: first
    explicit construct breakdown of FWA into these two dimensions
    in corpus
  - Africa-specific empirical FWA→performance: partially fills
    the Sub-Saharan Africa gap confirmed by B05 and B10
  - 100% response rate note: first unusual response rate flag
    in corpus; administered questionnaire

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Andeyo, L. M., Otuya, W. I., & Misigo, G. (2023). Effects of flexible
work arrangements on employee performance among non-teaching employees
in selected public universities, Western Kenya. African Journal of
Empirical Research, 5(3), 214–228.
https://doi.org/10.51867/ajernet.5.3.21
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B14
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B14
Cite key        : Enaifoghe2023
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — WFH/telework as flexible work
                  arrangement; post-COVID-19 South Africa; ICT
                  integration as enabler),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — ICT as
                  enabler; WFH barriers: poor policies, inadequate
                  training, blurred work-family boundaries, isolation;
                  Trust-Based Working Theory),
                  T6 (exploratory qualitative + survey; South Africa;
                  WFH productivity challenges and opportunities)
Title           : The rapidly evolving situation of employee
                  work-from-home productivity and the integration of
                  ICT in post-COVID-19 pandemic
Authors         : Enaifoghe, A., & Zenzile, N.
Year            : 2023 (received 9 July 2022; revised 6 May 2023;
                  accepted 8 May 2023)
Journal         : Scientific African (Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 20, Article e01709
DOI             : 10.1016/j.sciaf.2023.e01709
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Department of Public Administration, University of
                  Zululand, South Africa (Enaifoghe); Department of
                  Operations Management, Cape Peninsula University of
                  Technology, South Africa (Zenzile)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Mixed-methods; primarily qualitative/documentary
                  analysis with an email survey component; thematic
                  content analysis of secondary literature + primary
                  survey data from selected businesses
Aim             : To explore the changing nature of COVID-19's impact
                  on WFH productivity in South Africa; to examine the
                  role of ICT integration in enabling WFH productivity;
                  to recommend policy and regulatory improvements for
                  WFH in South Africa
Setting/context : South Africa (Western Cape and KwaZulu-Natal
                  provinces); selected businesses (sector unspecified);
                  post-COVID-19 pandemic context; public and private
                  sector combined
Sample          : Email survey sent to selected businesses from
                  statistics business registration; response counts
                  and sampling details not reported
                  [FLAG: No sample size, response rate, or demographic
                  breakdown reported for the primary survey — major
                  methodological gap. Survey data presented as
                  qualitative quotations rather than quantitative
                  tables. The study is primarily a literature review
                  with secondary survey quotations for illustration.]
Theoretical     : Trust-Based Working (TBW) Theory (Viete &
framework         Erdsiek, 2018): highly skilled/qualified workers
                  can operate autonomously through trust-based
                  management; TBW more common in productive firms;
                  trust enables remote work without direct supervision
                  [FLAG: TBW Theory is applied descriptively, not
                  formally modelled or tested. Connection to empirical
                  findings is narrative rather than analytical.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Documentary analysis (systematic review of available
                  literature) + email survey (exploratory)
Data sources    : (1) Desktop secondary review of academic literature
                    on COVID-19 and WFH (predominantly 2020–2022
                    international and South African sources)
                  (2) Survey questionnaire sent by email to selected
                    businesses in Western Cape and KwaZulu-Natal
Data analysis   : Thematic content analysis; no quantitative outputs
                    reported; findings presented as survey quotations
                    interspersed with literature review narratives
Reliability     : Not applicable — no scales measured
Cronbach's α    : Not reported — qualitative/documentary study
Quantitative    : None — no β values, R², t-statistics, or correlation
outputs           coefficients reported from the primary survey
CMB check       : Not applicable
[FLAG: The primary survey data is presented only as quoted text
snippets from respondents, with no sample size, demographic
information, or analytical aggregation. Standard reliability and
validity criteria for quantitative studies do not apply, but by
qualitative standards the rigour is also limited — no reflexivity
statement, no saturation criteria, no member-checking.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS (NARRATIVE SYNTHESIS)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. WFH CHALLENGES IN SOUTH AFRICA (COVID-19 context):
   - Transition to WFH was rapid and unplanned — no clear
     government guidelines or preparation
   - Workers "uninformed of what WFH implies and lack the tools
     needed for this transformation" (software, document access,
     suitable home environment)
   - WFH's formerly attractive image was undermined: "formerly
     coveted, highly attractive WFH did not show to be one of
     the best solutions for most employees"
   - Social isolation; blurred work-family boundaries; shared
     WFH costs (electricity, internet) falling on employees

2. BENEFITS IDENTIFIED (survey quotations):
   - Reduced commuting time; avoidance of office politics;
     increased flexibility; improved work-life balance; higher
     job satisfaction; better gender diversity outcomes
   - "Telework can minimize turnover while increasing employee
     productivity, job engagement, and job performance"
   - WFH helped employees manage WLB and reduced work-life
     conflict
   - More than 80% of survey respondents preferred at least
     partial WFH arrangements post-pandemic

3. ICT AS ENABLING MECHANISM:
   - ICT adoption (cloud computing, communication tools,
     collaboration platforms) is the key enabler of WFH
     productivity
   - Countries/firms with higher ICT access, broadband, and
     computer ownership have higher WFH capacity
   - Three ICT benefits identified from secondary sources:
     (a) positive time and psychological effects of technology
         use during free time
     (b) family support positively moderates ICT use in WFH
     (c) family support reduces psychological detachment,
         lowering work-family conflict intensity

4. TRUST-BASED WORKING THEORY APPLICATION:
   - TBW more common in productive and larger firms
   - Highly skilled workers better suited to autonomous WFH
     (telework more common in higher-skilled jobs)
   - Qualified managers more likely to develop trust-based
     relationships enabling effective WFH
   - Remote workers exhibit higher organisational commitment
     and trust than comparable office workers in most studies
     reviewed

5. PRODUCTIVITY MEASUREMENT CHALLENGE:
   - Results-based metrics rather than presence-based monitoring
     are recommended for WFH productivity assessment
   - Micromanagement risks from over-frequent measurement;
     privacy concerns from surveillance technologies
   - "Well-managed remote teams can be significantly more
     productive than physically managed workers"

6. POLICY RECOMMENDATIONS (South Africa specific):
   - Formal WFH guidelines for employers and employees
   - Technology training and minimum ICT facility standards
   - Labour regulation review to include home workers in
     insurance protections
   - Subsidies for SMEs implementing WFH
   - WFH no longer forced but as a "flexible option"

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 Africa empirical"),
               B14 provides the only South Africa-based paper on
               WFH productivity in Block B. The contextual alignment
               with GGHN's Sub-Saharan African setting is significant:
               South Africa shares with Nigeria similar ICT
               infrastructure constraints, colonial bureaucratic
               traditions, COVID-19 WFH policy gaps, and public
               sector challenges. Use B14 to contextualise WFH
               productivity challenges specific to the Sub-Saharan
               African context, particularly: inadequate ICT
               infrastructure, lack of formal WFH regulation, and
               rapid unplanned WFH transitions.

               The TBW Theory application is also directly relevant
               to the thesis OMS construct: trust-based management
               (a component of OMS) is the mechanism that enables
               WFH productivity in South African organisations — this
               maps onto the thesis mediation hypothesis (management
               systems → hybrid work → productivity).

               Cite for: "In the South African context, WFH
               productivity is constrained by inadequate ICT
               infrastructure, absent policy frameworks, and lack of
               trust-based management systems — suggesting that
               without deliberate management system design, remote
               work will not deliver expected productivity gains."

Limitation   : No quantitative evidence; no sample size or response
               rate reported for primary survey; predominantly
               secondary literature synthesis; findings presented as
               unattributed survey quotations without analytical
               aggregation; South Africa ≠ Nigeria (different
               institutional contexts); COVID-19 mandatory WFH
               context not equivalent to voluntary hybrid work;
               published in Scientific African (lower-tier Elsevier
               journal vs. core HRM outlets in corpus).

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Africa-specific evidence: South Africa WFH productivity
      context; public + private sector; post-COVID-19
  [+] Trust-Based Working Theory provides a coherent theoretical
      frame directly relevant to the thesis OMS construct
  [+] ICT as enabler is directly mapped to thesis MIS construct
  [+] Policy recommendations for South Africa provide comparative
      institutional context for GGHN's Nigerian setting
  [+] Elsevier Scientific African — respectable open-access
      outlet for African research; Elsevier CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
  [+] Secondary literature synthesis is thorough and covers
      global pre/during-COVID-19 telework evidence
  [~] No sample size, response rate, or demographics reported
      for primary survey — major methodological gap
  [~] Primary survey data presented as quotations only, not
      aggregate statistics — quality equivalent to narrative
      review, not primary empirical study
  [~] No quantitative outputs — no β values, R², or correlations
  [~] South Africa ≠ Nigeria — different institutional,
      regulatory, and cultural context
  [~] TBW Theory applied descriptively, not formally tested
  [~] COVID-19 forced WFH context — not voluntary hybrid work
  [-] Predominantly literature review masquerading as empirical
      study; primary data contribution is minimal and
      methodologically underdescribed

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — trust as prerequisite:
  B14's TBW Theory (trust enables WFH productivity; highly
  skilled workers + qualified managers required) directly mirrors
  B08's finding that manager–employee trust is the primary driver
  of remote work adoption (β=0.133, p<0.001). Both papers, from
  opposite methodological extremes (qualitative South Africa vs.
  CB-SEM US), converge: trust is non-negotiable for WFH
  productivity. For GGHN, where institutional trust structures
  may differ from both South African and US contexts, this is a
  critical management systems design consideration.

Convergence with B09 (OBrady2025) — management as enabler:
  B14's finding that "well-managed remote teams can be
  significantly more productive than physically managed workers"
  is the South African qualitative parallel of B09's quantitative
  finding (North America/Germany): mutual gains management
  practices (not remote work per se) determine performance.
  Both papers argue that unmanaged WFH defaults to surveillance-
  heavy, development-light outcomes.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025) — ICT as management system:
  B14's ICT enablement argument (ICT access, broadband, tools)
  maps onto B07's 4IR technologies → FWA pathway (β=0.507). Both
  papers position ICT/4IR as the management system component
  that enables flexible work arrangements to function effectively.
  B14 provides the Africa-specific context (lower baseline ICT
  infrastructure) that explains WHY B07's findings may manifest
  differently in GGHN's environment.

Convergence with B05 (Maity2025) and B10 (Mele2023) — Africa gap:
  B14, alongside B13 (Kenya), constitutes the Africa-specific
  empirical evidence for Block B. Both B05 and B10 confirmed that
  Sub-Saharan Africa is absent from global systematic reviews.
  B14 (South Africa) and B13 (Kenya) together establish a partial
  African evidence base, while the Nigeria/NGO gap for GGHN
  itself remains unaddressed — justifying the thesis.

NEW CONTRIBUTION — REGULATORY CONTEXT FOR GGHN:
  B14's policy analysis of WFH in South Africa is the closest
  proxy for GGHN's Nigerian regulatory environment. Like South
  Africa in B14, Nigeria lacks formal WFH regulation, many
  organisations transitioned to WFH without preparation, and
  ICT infrastructure is uneven. B14's recommendations (formal
  WFH guidelines, ICT training standards, SME subsidies) are
  directly translatable to GGHN recommendations in Ch. 3/Ch. 4.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - South Africa WFH/telework context: first in corpus;
    University of Zululand + CPUT; mixed qualitative study
  - Trust-Based Working (TBW) Theory (Viete & Erdsiek 2018):
    first in corpus; trust as the organisational prerequisite
    for autonomous WFH productivity
  - Scientific African (Elsevier): first Scientific African
    paper in corpus; African Institute of Mathematical
    Sciences / Next Einstein Initiative editorial board
  - WFH policy vacuum: first explicit analysis of South African
    WFH regulatory gap in corpus — directly analogous to GGHN
    Nigerian context
  - ICT access as fundamental productivity enabler: convergent
    with Block A pattern #1 (technology/ICT dominant); now
    confirmed at the Africa-specific level from the demand side
    (employees lack ICT tools for WFH) rather than the supply
    side (management systems provide ICT infrastructure)

of employee work-from-home productivity and the integration of ICT in
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B15
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B15
Cite key        : Dekker2025
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — outcome
                  control [performance information, targets, KPIs] as
                  OMS component; formal/informal/behaviour control
                  typology; OCT framework; management control systems
                  design for WFH),
                  T3 (hybrid work — WFH as work arrangement; abrupt
                  COVID-19 shift to full remote; Nordea KYC banking
                  teams; Nordic context),
                  T5 (productivity enablers — outcome control →
                  team communication effectiveness → task performance;
                  mediation via communication)
Title           : Working apart together: How outcome control supports
                  operations when working from home
Authors         : Dekker, H. C., Feldhues, M. L., & Georgiev, N.
Year            : 2025 (received 23 August 2023; accepted 18 February
                  2025; Decision Sciences Vol. 56, pp. 471–488)
Journal         : Decision Sciences
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 56, pp. 471–488
DOI             : 10.1111/deci.70002
                  [Confirmed from shortlist CSV and paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Vrije Universiteit Amsterdam, School of Business
                  and Economics, Netherlands (Dekker, Feldhues);
                  Copenhagen Business School, Frederiksberg, Denmark
                  (Dekker, Feldhues); Nordea, Group Financial Crime
                  Prevention, Copenhagen, Denmark (Georgiev)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Field study; multilevel analysis (multilevel
                  regression + multilevel SEM); matched TL–TM
                  surveys + archival firm data; qualitative
                  interviews (14 interviews + 6 formal meetings)
Aim             : To investigate how team leaders' (TLs') reliance on
                  outcome control — using performance information —
                  supports remote team operations and performance
                  change after an abrupt shift to WFH (COVID-19
                  disruption); to identify team communication
                  effectiveness as the mediating mechanism
Setting/context : Nordea bank, Group Financial Crime Prevention
                  (GFCP), KYC Operations; 31 teams across Denmark,
                  Estonia, Finland, Norway, Poland, Sweden; May/June
                  2020 (10 weeks after COVID-19 shift to WFH)
Sample          : 31 TLs (100% response rate); 245 TMs (61.3%);
                  matched TL–TM data; n=168–184 in analyses
IV              : TL Outcome Control: 11-item scale measuring
                  perceived usefulness of performance information
                  (global + local KPI reports) for managing team
                  (case allocation, resource planning, skill matching,
                  prioritising, trade-offs, evaluating throughput,
                  quality, timeliness, team performance, individual
                  performance); Cronbach's α=0.95
Mediator        : Team Communication Effectiveness Change: 3-item
                  scale (meeting effectiveness, standard team
                  communication, informal communication changes
                  post-COVID vs. pre); Cronbach's α=0.75
DV              : Performance Change: 4-item self-reported change in
                  performance post vs. pre COVID (throughput, quality,
                  on-time completion, contribution to team);
                  Cronbach's α=0.92
Control DV      : Prior Performance: 4-item pre-COVID performance
                  baseline; Cronbach's α=0.86
Additional      : 1:1 meetings; team meetings; change in meeting
controls          structure; TL tenure/experience/gender; team size/
                  dispersion; TM tenure/expertise/gender/case risk/
                  customer contact; unit and country fixed effects
Theoretical     : Organisational Control Theory — OCT (Ouchi, 1979;
framework         Sitkin et al., 2020): outcome control (performance
                  information, targets, KPIs), behaviour control
                  (direct supervision), informal/clan control
                  (shared values, norms) as three complementary
                  control alternatives; WFH drastically reduces
                  behaviour and informal control options, shifting
                  emphasis to outcome control

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Field study; matched TL–TM cross-sectional surveys
                  (May/June 2020) + archival data + qualitative
                  interviews; CMB avoided by separating TL (IV) and
                  TM (DV/mediator) measures across different surveys
Analysis        : H1: Two-level multilevel regression (random team
                  effects; unit + country FE; weighted by team
                  response rate)
                  H2/H3: Multilevel generalised SEM (GSEM)
CMB             : Avoided by design (TL survey = IV; TM survey = DV
                  and mediator; different sources)
Reliability     : TL outcome control α=0.95 ✓; Performance change
                  α=0.92 ✓; Prior performance α=0.86 ✓; Team
                  communication change α=0.75 ✓ (all above 0.70)
Discriminant    : CFA two-factor model (performance change + team
validity          communication change): CFI=0.98; TLI=0.97;
                  RMSEA=0.07; SRMR=0.05 — good fit ✓
Validation      : Robustness tests with alternative measures reported
                  in Supporting Information Appendix

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1: TL outcome control → Performance change (direct):
  b=0.12, p<0.05 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → After shifting to WFH, TMs whose TL relied more strongly
    on performance information (outcome control) experienced
    more favourable changes in task performance

H2: TL outcome control → Team communication effectiveness change:
  b=0.14, p<0.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → Greater TL reliance on outcome control is positively
    associated with more favourable changes in team
    communication effectiveness after shifting to WFH

H3: Team communication effectiveness change → Performance change:
  b=0.47, p<0.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
  Indirect effect (H3 mediation): b=0.07, p<0.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
  → Team communication effectiveness MEDIATES the relationship
    between TL outcome control and TM performance change

ADDITIONAL SIGNIFICANT FINDINGS:
  - More 1:1 meetings pre-COVID → better communication and
    performance change post-WFH
  - TL outside experience → better communication and performance
  - Female TLs → better communication change (but lower direct
    performance change from TL)
  - Team dispersion (multiple countries) → better communication
    (prior experience with virtual meetings)
  - TM tenure in team → better communication

NARRATIVE FINDING — CONTROL LOSS UNDER WFH:
  WFH drastically reduced behaviour control (direct supervision)
  and informal control (office floor interaction, stand-ups,
  spontaneous conversations). TL quotes confirm the challenge:
  "A challenge is missing the small conversations and questions
  that are easily picked up on in the office." Outcome control
  — using existing KPI reports about throughput, quality, and
  timeliness — was the remaining effective control lever.

KEY MECHANISM:
  Performance information (outcome control) → structured virtual
  meetings (communication) → task performance. The mechanism is
  NOT a direct monitoring effect; it is a communication and
  coordination facilitation effect: outcome control gives TLs
  a common reference frame for virtual meetings, enabling
  effective decision facilitation and decision influencing.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 theoretical;
               management+hybrid"), B15 provides the most
               theoretically sophisticated treatment of management
               systems (outcome control) as the mechanism bridging
               WFH and performance in the corpus. The OCT framework
               (Ouchi, 1979) is the theoretical basis for the thesis
               OMS construct: formal performance management systems
               (targets, KPIs, reporting) = outcome control;
               supervisory and monitoring practices = behaviour
               control; organisational culture and norms = informal
               control. The finding that WFH shifts the effective
               control mix TOWARD outcome control (and away from
               behaviour and informal control) directly supports the
               thesis mediation model: in hybrid/remote settings,
               management information systems (MIS) become the
               primary mechanism through which management systems
               affect performance.

               Use in Ch. 2.2 to argue: "As work arrangements shift
               towards hybrid models, the effectiveness of management
               systems shifts from behaviour-based and informal
               controls toward outcome-based controls (MIS); this
               transition amplifies the mediating role of management
               systems in the hybrid work → performance relationship."

Ch. 2.3 use  : The mediation finding (outcome control → team
               communication → performance; indirect b=0.07,
               p<0.01) directly parallels the thesis mediation
               hypothesis. Here the mechanism is communication
               effectiveness (analogous to WLB/QWL in other Block B
               papers); in all cases, management systems produce
               their performance effects THROUGH an intermediate
               mechanism, not directly. Cite B15 for the strongest
               causal mechanism evidence of the OMS → hybrid work
               → performance chain in the corpus.

Theoretical   : B15 provides the OCT grounding that the thesis
contribution   framework needs. The decision facilitation /
               decision influencing distinction (Abernethy et al.,
               2021) maps directly onto the thesis MIS (decision
               facilitating via information systems) and OMS
               (decision influencing via management practices)
               sub-constructs.

Limitation   : Single firm (Nordea bank), single sector (financial
               services, anti-money-laundering KYC), Nordic context
               (high trust, high digitalisation) — very different
               from GGHN's Nigerian public health NGO context;
               COVID-19 forced WFH ≠ voluntary hybrid work; 10-week
               window post-disruption; no pre-disruption data for
               clean causal inference; performance change is self-
               reported; WFH was total (100% team) not partial —
               not a hybrid arrangement.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Decision Sciences (Wiley) — SSCI-indexed; ABS 3-rated;
      quantitative management science journal; highest-ranked
      management control paper in Block B
  [+] Multilevel matched TL–TM design: CMB avoided by design
      (IV and DV from different respondents); 100% TL response
  [+] Rich field study: 14 interviews + 6 formal meetings;
      archival data (HR, task complexity, People Pulse); deep
      contextualisation
  [+] All Cronbach's α ≥ 0.75; CFA discriminant validity
      confirmed (CFI=0.98)
  [+] Robustness tests reported in Supporting Information
  [+] OCT framework (Ouchi, 1979) is the most theoretically
      grounded management control theory in the corpus
  [+] Mediation mechanism identified (communication effectiveness)
      — goes beyond simple IV → DV to explain HOW outcome control
      enables WFH performance
  [+] Practical implications: concrete KPI design and
      communication structure recommendations
  [~] Single firm, single sector (banking/financial crime)
  [~] Nordic context — high institutional trust, high ICT
      infrastructure — very different from GGHN
  [~] COVID-19 total WFH ≠ voluntary hybrid work; 10-week window
      limits long-term inference
  [~] No pre-disruption baseline for clean difference-in-
      differences causal identification
  [~] Small TL sample (n=31) — limits precision of TL-level
      estimates
  [~] Self-reported performance change (DV)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — formal/informal control:
  B08 found informal self-control mediates remote work →
  performance (β=0.062) while formal outcome control does not
  mediate. B15 finds the opposite in a different setting (forced
  WFH, banking): outcome control DOES mediate (b=0.07) via
  communication. The apparent contradiction resolves when
  contextualised: B08's US managers had both formal and informal
  controls available; B15's Nordea TLs had ONLY outcome control
  available (informal was eliminated by WFH). Both papers
  converge on the same meta-conclusion: the effective control
  type in WFH is context-dependent, but some form of management
  systems mediation is always present.

Convergence with B09 (OBrady2025) — communication as mechanism:
  B09 identified developmental coaching (a form of structured
  communication) as the key management practice enabling remote
  performance. B15 empirically demonstrates that outcome
  control → team communication → performance is the exact
  mediation chain. Communication effectiveness is the proximal
  mechanism in both papers. B15 quantifies what B09 describes
  qualitatively.

Convergence with B11 (Curzi2026) — continuous feedback:
  B11 showed continuous feedback (an informal, relational PM
  practice) uniquely drives IWB in high-remote-work settings.
  B15 shows outcome control (formal, information-based PM)
  drives performance via communication. Both point to the same
  conclusion: in remote settings, the PM component that WORKS
  is the one that maintains communication and reduces ambiguity.
  B11 identified continuous feedback as the communication-
  enabling PM practice; B15 identified outcome control KPIs as
  the communication-structuring PM practice. Together: both
  formal (B15) and informal (B11) feedback mechanisms are
  necessary, serving complementary communication roles.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025) — ICT as enabler:
  B07's 4IR → FWA pathway (β=0.507) operates through similar
  logic: digital systems enable flexible work arrangements.
  B15's outcome control system (KPI dashboards, performance
  reports) is precisely the type of ICT/MIS infrastructure
  that B07's 4IR construct operationalises. Both papers
  confirm that formal information systems (MIS/4IR/outcome
  control) are the enabling mechanisms for WFH productivity —
  not the remote work itself.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #1
(Technology/ICT dominant management systems predictor):
  B15's finding that outcome control (performance information
  systems) mediates WFH → performance extends Block A's
  finding (β=0.45–0.57 for technology/ICT as MIS predictor)
  into the hybrid work domain. The management information
  system (KPI reports, dashboards) is the specific mechanism
  — this is the MIS sub-construct of the thesis. B15 provides
  the WFH-specific empirical evidence for why technology-based
  management systems have consistently been the dominant
  predictor in Block A.

KEY THESIS CONTRIBUTION — THEORETICAL GROUNDING:
  B15 is the only Block B paper that explicitly applies OCT
  (Ouchi, 1979) as its theoretical framework. This makes it
  the theoretical anchor for the thesis OMS construct. The
  three-type taxonomy (outcome, behaviour, informal) maps
  directly onto the thesis management systems framework:
  MIS = outcome control (information-based); OMS = behaviour
  control (formal procedures) + informal control (culture,
  trust). B15 provides the theoretical justification for why
  hybrid work shifts the effective control mix toward MIS
  (outcome control) and away from behaviour/informal control —
  directly supporting the thesis mediation hypothesis.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - OCT (Organisational Control Theory, Ouchi 1979): first in
    corpus as primary framework in a Block B empirical paper;
    outcome/behaviour/informal control taxonomy
  - Decision Sciences (Wiley): first Decision Sciences paper
    in corpus; ABS 3; management control field
  - Matched TL–TM multilevel design: first matched two-source
    multilevel study in corpus; 100% TL response + 61.3% TM
    response; CMB avoided by design
  - Team communication effectiveness as mediator: first in
    corpus; communication = the mechanism through which outcome
    control enables WFH performance (b=0.07 indirect, p<0.01)
  - Decision facilitation / decision influencing distinction:
    first explicit use of Abernethy et al. (2021) framework
    in corpus; maps onto MIS/OMS sub-construct distinction
  - Nordea bank KYC banking context: first private sector
    banking/financial services paper in corpus
  - Nordic (Denmark, Estonia, Finland, Norway, Poland, Sweden)
    context: first Nordic multi-country study in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Dekker, H. C., Feldhues, M. L., & Georgiev, N. (2025). Working apart
together: How outcome control supports operations when working from
home. Decision Sciences, 56, 471–488.
https://doi.org/10.1111/deci.70002
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B16
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B16
Cite key        : Bernard2025
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — four-dimension remote work
                  taxonomy: telecommuting, hybrid scheduling, mobile
                  work arrangement, home-based operation; Nigerian
                  commercial banking context; Calabar, Cross River
                  State),
                  T6 (empirical: remote work dimensions → employee
                  productivity; Nigeria; cross-sectional OLS;
                  customer-facing banking personnel; JD-R model)
Title           : Remote work and employees' productivity in Nigerian
                  commercial banks: Evidence from Calabar, Cross
                  River State
Authors         : Bernard, S. E.
Year            : 2025
Journal         : African Journal of Management and Business Research
                  (AJMBR; Afropolitan Journals)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 20, No. 1, pp. 549–571
DOI             : 10.62154/ajmbr.2025.020.
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes (Afropolitan Journals open access)
Affiliations    : Department of Business Management, University of
                  Calabar, Nigeria

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  multiple linear regression (OLS); descriptive
                  statistics; SPSS v.26
Aim             : To investigate the effect of remote work
                  arrangements (telecommuting, hybrid scheduling,
                  mobile work arrangement, home-based operation) on
                  employees' productivity in commercial banks in
                  Calabar, Cross River State, Nigeria
Setting/context : 17 commercial bank branches in Calabar Metropolis,
                  Cross River State, Nigeria; customer-facing
                  personnel (cashiers, tellers, marketers, loan
                  officers, customer care and relationship officers);
                  post-COVID-19 context; 2025 data collection
Sample          : Target population: 457 customer-facing staff
                  (census sampling); 334 retrieved questionnaires
                  (73.1% response rate); multiple regression on n=334
IVs             : (1) Telecommuting work (β=0.249, t=4.181)
                  (2) Hybrid scheduling (β=0.208, t=4.371)
                  (3) Mobile work arrangement (β=0.526, t=9.245)
                  (4) Home-based operation (β=0.307, t=4.427)
DV              : Employees' productivity (timely service completion,
                  error-free transactions, queue flow, account
                  accuracy, complaint resolution, account setup speed)
Theoretical     : Job Demands–Resources (JD-R) Model (Bakker &
framework         Demerouti, 2007): remote work arrangements function
                  as job resources (autonomy, digital tools, flexible
                  scheduling) that buffer demands and stimulate
                  productivity

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Cross-sectional; census sampling (all 457
                  accessible employees surveyed); structured
                  questionnaire; Likert scales
Analysis        : Multiple linear regression (OLS); SPSS v.26;
                  descriptive statistics (frequencies, means, SD)
Reliability     : Not explicitly reported per construct — authors
                  reference prior validated scales (Akaegbobi et al.,
                  2025; Brown & Li, 2022; Obomeghia & Onuoha, 2023;
                  Pepple et al., 2025 for IVs; Conti & Bianchi, 2023;
                  Gomez & Alvarez, 2025 for DV)
                  [FLAG: No Cronbach's α or factor loadings reported
                  for this study's instrument — reliability gap]
CMB             : Not tested; single-source self-report data
                  [FLAG: Authors acknowledge social desirability and
                  CMB as limitations]
Regression      : R=0.868; R²=0.753; Adj.R²=0.750
model fit         F(4,329)=250.654, p=0.000
                  [FLAG: R²=0.753 is very high — authors caution this
                  may reflect focused study population + structured
                  banking roles, not solely remote work effects]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS (HYPOTHESIS TESTING)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall model: R²=0.753 — remote work dimensions explain 75.3% of
  variance in productivity. F(4,329)=250.654, p<0.001 ✓

H1: Telecommuting → Productivity: β=0.249, t=4.181, p<0.05 ✓
H2: Hybrid scheduling → Productivity: β=0.208, t=4.371, p<0.05 ✓
H3: Mobile work → Productivity: β=0.526, t=9.245, p<0.05 ✓
  (STRONGEST PREDICTOR — largest standardised β in model)
H4: Home-based operation → Productivity: β=0.307, t=4.427, p<0.05 ✓

Ranking by effect size:
  1. Mobile work arrangement: β=0.526 (dominant)
  2. Home-based operation: β=0.307
  3. Telecommuting: β=0.249
  4. Hybrid scheduling: β=0.208

CONTEXTUAL FINDING — NIGERIAN BANKING BARRIERS:
  Despite positive productivity effects, Nigerian commercial banks
  face significant constraints:
  - Infrastructure: unreliable internet, frequent power outages,
    limited advanced digital banking tools
  - Managerial: traditional control mindset; reluctance to adopt
    flexible models due to compliance and security concerns
  - Sector structure: old-generation banks more conservative;
    even new-generation banks limit remote work to hybrid/mobile
    for customer-facing roles only
  → These constraints mean actual implementation is narrow and
    cautious relative to global banking norms

JD-R INTERPRETATION:
  Mobile work's dominant effect (β=0.526) attributed to enhanced
  job resource availability: extends operational capacity beyond
  fixed locations, enables real-time customer response, reduces
  task delays — functions as highest-yield job resource in the
  Nigerian banking context.

[FLAG — METHODOLOGICAL CAUTION:
  (1) R²=0.753 is exceptionally high for behavioural research;
      authors themselves note this warrants cautious interpretation;
      may reflect concentrated study population, structured banking
      roles, or CMB inflating correlations.
  (2) No Cronbach's α or factor loadings reported — reliability
      of the instrument cannot be independently assessed.
  (3) Single-city study (Calabar) — generalisability to Lagos,
      Abuja, Port Harcourt banking centres uncertain.
  (4) Single-source self-report — CMB uncontrolled.
  (5) Cross-sectional — causal inference limited.
  (6) Published in AJMBR (Afropolitan Journals) — a Nigerian
      regional journal; lower international standing than most
      Block B outlets, but peer-reviewed and open access.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 Nigeria empirical"),
               B16 is the most directly relevant empirical paper in
               the entire corpus for the thesis. It provides:
               (a) Nigeria-specific evidence: the only paper in the
                   corpus with Nigerian commercial banking sector data
               (b) Multiple remote work dimensions: four-construct
                   taxonomy directly maps onto thesis hybrid work
                   mediator variable
               (c) JD-R framework: convergent with Block A and B
                   papers that use JD-R
               (d) Post-COVID-19 context: relevant to GGHN's
                   current hybrid work transition

               Cite for: "In Nigerian commercial banks, all four
               dimensions of remote work — telecommuting (β=0.249),
               hybrid scheduling (β=0.208), mobile work arrangement
               (β=0.526), and home-based operation (β=0.307) —
               significantly and positively predict employee
               productivity, collectively explaining 75.3% of
               variance (Bernard, 2025)."

               The paper's frank discussion of Nigerian
               infrastructure constraints (internet unreliability,
               power outages, managerial conservatism) is directly
               citable for the GGHN organisational context section
               in Ch. 3.

Thesis        : B16's contextual findings validate the thesis
contribution   argument that management systems (managerial
               adaptation, ICT infrastructure investment, clear
               supervision frameworks) are required to extract
               productivity benefits from remote/hybrid work in the
               Nigerian context. Without management systems enabling
               the transition, the positive effects documented by
               B16 cannot be realised — this is the GGHN-specific
               version of the thesis mediation argument.

Limitation   : Single-city (Calabar) — may not represent Nigeria's
               major financial centres; commercial banking ≠ NGO/
               health sector (GGHN context); R²=0.753 is very high
               and should be cited with appropriate caution; no
               reliability statistics; single-source CMB; cross-
               sectional. The four remote work dimensions (IVs) are
               not tested as a mediator — direct effects only.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Nigeria-specific empirical evidence: most contextually
      proximate paper to GGHN in the entire corpus
  [+] Four-dimension remote work taxonomy (telecommuting,
      hybrid scheduling, mobile work, home-based) is the most
      comprehensive operationalisation of the hybrid work

  [+] Substantial n=334 from census of 457; 73.1% response rate
  [+] JD-R theoretical framework — well-established, convergent
      with multiple Block B papers
  [+] Explicit discussion of Nigerian banking sector constraints
      (infrastructure, compliance, managerial culture) — uniquely
      contextually relevant

  [+] University of Calabar; AJMBR peer-reviewed

  [~] No Cronbach's α or factor loadings reported

  [~] No mediation analysis — direct effects only
```
