```
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B04 (Nweze2026) — Nigeria public sector:
  B04 found structured HWS β=0.658 in Nigerian public media
  (Ebonyi State). B16 finds all four remote work dimensions
  significant in Nigerian commercial banking (Calabar). Together,
  these are the two Nigeria-specific empirical anchors of the
  thesis corpus: B04 provides the public sector/media evidence;
  B16 provides the private sector/banking evidence. Both confirm
  that structured remote/hybrid work positively predicts
  productivity in Nigerian organisational contexts.

Convergence with B13 (Andeyo2023) — Africa baseline:
  B13 found FWA→performance β=0.765 in Kenyan public universities.
  B16's dominant mobile work effect (β=0.526) and overall R²=0.753
  are in the same high-magnitude range. Both are Africa-specific
  papers using similar OLS methodologies, and both document
  stronger-than-expected direct remote/flexible work → productivity
  effects. This may reflect Africa-specific baseline dynamics
  (high value of flexibility given rigid traditional structures)
  or shared method variance.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025) — 4IR/ICT pathway:
  B07's 4IR→FWA pathway (β=0.507) and B16's mobile work
  dominance (β=0.526) converge: in both studies, technology-
  enabled remote work (4IR in B07; mobile platforms in B16) is
  the strongest predictor of productivity. Both are from non-
  Western developing economy contexts (UAE and Nigeria) and both
  confirm that ICT/digital infrastructure is the primary enabler.
  B16 provides the Nigeria-specific confirmation of B07's finding.

Convergence with B14 (Enaifoghe2023) — South Africa:
  B14 documented WFH challenges in South Africa (infrastructure
  gaps, policy vacuum, training deficit). B16 confirms the same
  Nigerian version of these constraints, validating the Africa-
  specific ICT infrastructure challenge as a cross-national
  pattern across Sub-Saharan Africa. Together they establish that
  without deliberate management system investment (ICT, training,
  policy), the positive remote work → productivity effects
  documented in both papers cannot be sustainably realised.

Convergence with B12 (Tsang2023) — JD-R framework:
  B12 applied JD-R to FWC → WE/SE → WFHP in Taiwan SMEs; B16
  applies JD-R to remote work dimensions → productivity in
  Nigerian banking. Both use JD-R as the organising framework
  and both find that remote work functions as a job resource that
  buffers demands and enhances productivity. B16 provides the
  Nigeria-specific JD-R application confirming the framework's
  cross-cultural applicability.

KEY THESIS CONTRIBUTION — NIGERIA CONTEXT:
  B16 is the empirical centrepiece for the Nigeria-specific
  section of the thesis literature review. It provides the only
  available evidence on remote work → productivity in a Nigerian
  organisational context beyond public media (B04). The paper
  fills the most critical geographical gap for the GGHN case
  study. Its frank discussion of Nigerian banking constraints
  (infrastructure, managerial conservatism, compliance concerns)
  directly parallels GGHN's operational environment.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Nigeria commercial banking sector: first in corpus; Calabar,
    Cross River State; customer-facing personnel; 17 bank branches
  - Four-dimension remote work taxonomy (telecommuting + hybrid
    scheduling + mobile work + home-based operation): first paper
    in corpus to operationalise all four simultaneously as IVs
  - Mobile work arrangement dominant (β=0.526): first in corpus
    to identify mobile work as the strongest productivity
    predictor among remote work dimensions
  - AJMBR (Afropolitan Journals): first AJMBR paper in corpus
  - University of Calabar, Nigeria: first University of Calabar
    paper in corpus; closest Nigerian institutional affiliation
    to GGHN's NGO healthcare context
  - JD-R applied to Nigerian banking: first Nigeria-specific
    JD-R application in corpus
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B17
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B17
Cite key        : Bloom2024
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — randomised control trial; hybrid
                  schedule 3 days office + 2 days WFH; Chinese
                  technology company; university-graduate employees),
                  T5 (productivity enablers — null equivalence testing
                  on performance grades, promotions, lines of code;
                  performance NOT harmed by hybrid; retention strongly
                  improved),
                  T6 (landmark RCT evidence; Nature; n=1,612;
                  Trip.com Shanghai; 2021–2022)
Title           : Hybrid working from home improves retention without
                  damaging performance
Authors         : Bloom, N., Han, R., & Liang, J.
Year            : 2024 (received 15 August 2023; accepted 30 April
                  2024; published online 12 June 2024; issue date
                  27 June 2024)
Journal         : Nature
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 630, No. 8018, pp. 920–925
DOI             : 10.1038/s41586-024-07500-2
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Stanford University, USA (Bloom); Chinese University
                  of Hong Kong, Shenzhen, China (Han); Peking
                  University + Trip.com, Shanghai, China (Liang)
                  [Note: Bloom and Han contributed equally; co-first
                  authors]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Randomised control trial (RCT); 6-month experiment;
                  field study; t-tests; null equivalence testing
                  (TOST); longitudinal follow-up (2-year performance
                  tracking post-experiment)
Aim             : To provide causal evidence on the effects of a
                  hybrid WFH schedule (2 days at home, 3 days in
                  office per week) on employee performance, retention,
                  job satisfaction, and promotions; to address the
                  gap in causal research on HYBRID (vs. fully remote)
                  working for university-graduate knowledge workers
Setting/context : Trip.com (third-largest global travel agent by
                  sales in 2019; ~35,000 employees; NASDAQ-listed
                  c.$20bn market cap); Airfare and IT divisions;
                  Shanghai, China; August 2021 – January 2022
                  [COVID-19 incidence rates very low in Shanghai
                  during experiment period — not a lockdown study]
Sample          : n=1,612 employees (820 control; 792 treatment);
                  395 managers + 1,217 non-managers; 65% male; all
                  university graduates; 32% postgraduate; mean tenure
                  6.4 years; 48% with children; mid-30s average age
Randomisation   : Odd birthday (born 1st, 3rd, 5th… of month) =
                  treatment (WFH on Wednesday + Friday optional);
                  Even birthday = control (office all 5 days).
                  First wave: 518 volunteers; began week of 9 Aug
                  2021 (odd birthdays only);
                  Second wave: remaining 1,094 non-volunteers told
                  6 Sept 2021; began week of 13 Sept 2021 (both
                  waves pooled in analyses)
                  [Note: WFH was optional, not mandatory; take-up
                  ~55% volunteers; ~40% non-volunteers]
Outcomes        : (1) Attrition rate (6-month quit rate)
                  (2) Job satisfaction (10-point survey scale)
                  (3) Performance reviews (six-monthly, A–D grades;
                      up to 4 review periods over 2 years)
                  (4) Promotions (up to 4 periods over 2 years)
                  (5) Lines of code (n=653 engineers; daily upload)
                  (6) Performance subcategories (9 categories:
                      communication, development, efficiency,
                      execution, innovation, leadership, learning,
                      project, risk)
                  (7) Self-assessed productivity perceptions
                      (pre- and post-experiment manager and
                      non-manager surveys)
Analysis        : Two-tailed Student t-tests for primary outcomes;
                  null equivalence testing (TOST, R package) for
                  performance/promotions/code metrics; SESOI=±0.5
                  grade for performance reviews; ±2pp for promotions;
                  ±29 lines of code for code metric; Stata v17/v18,
                  R v4.2.2

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RETENTION (PRIMARY POSITIVE FINDING):
  Overall attrition: 33% REDUCTION in quit rates
    Control: 7.20%; Treatment: 4.80%
    Difference: −2.40pp; t(1610)=2.02; p=0.043 ✓
  Non-managers: 40% reduction (8.59% → 5.33%; p=0.026) ✓
  Female employees: 54% reduction (9.19% → 4.18%; p=0.017) ✓
  Long commuters (>90 min two-way): 52% reduction
    (6.00% → 2.89%; p=0.062, borderline significant)
  Managers: non-significant change (2.96% → 3.13%; p=0.922)

JOB SATISFACTION (PRIMARY POSITIVE FINDING):
  Treatment group significantly higher on satisfaction scale
    Mean control: 7.84 vs. mean treatment: 8.19
    t(1343)=4.17; p<0.001 ✓
  Treatment group higher on: WLB, work satisfaction, life
  satisfaction, recommendation to friends; lower on intention
  to quit (all significant, Extended Data Table 2)

PERFORMANCE (NULL EQUIVALENCE RESULTS — KEY FINDING):
  Performance grades over 4 six-monthly review periods:
    H1: Jul–Dec 2021: difference=0.056; p=0.198 (NS)
    H2: Jan–Jun 2022: difference=0.034; p=0.440 (NS)
    H3: Jul–Dec 2022: difference=−0.019; p=0.677 (NS)
    H4: Jan–Jun 2023: difference=0.046; p=0.369 (NS)
    All 4 equivalence tests: p<0.001 → TRUE NULL EFFECT
    (not merely 'no evidence of a difference')
  Promotions: No evidence of difference in 4 review periods
    (borderline equivalence test results; interpreted as null)
  Lines of code (n=653 engineers):
    Null equivalence test: t(92362)=−2.74; p=0.003 → NULL
  Performance subcategories (9 categories including innovation,
    leadership, development): No evidence of difference

SELF-ASSESSED PRODUCTIVITY PERCEPTIONS:
  Baseline (pre-experiment) mean: −0.06% (managers: −2.6%;
    non-managers: +0.7%; manager vs. non-manager: p<0.001)
  End-line (post-experiment) mean: +1.48% (managers: +1.05%;
    non-managers: +1.62%; no significant difference between
    groups post-experiment: p=0.345)
  → Managers REVISED perceptions from net-negative to net-
    positive after experiencing hybrid work: key managerial
    updating finding

PRACTICAL OUTCOME:
  After experiment, Trip.com executive committee voted to extend
  hybrid WFH to ALL employees across ALL divisions immediately
  (announced 14 February 2022). Rationale: ~US$20,000 per quit
  → one-third reduction in attrition → millions in savings

CRITICAL CONTEXTUAL QUALIFICATION:
  Take-up rates: ~55% volunteers; ~40% non-volunteers actually
  WFH on the permitted days (most only WFH Friday, not both
  Wednesday and Friday). Therefore, the treatment is effectively
  ~1 day/week WFH for many employees, not the full 2 days.
  Authors note the experiment analyses 3-days-in-office + 2-days
  WFH; findings may not extend to <3 days per week in office.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 landmark RCT;
               hybrid+performance"), B17 is the most
               methodologically rigorous paper in the entire corpus
               and the definitive empirical answer to the thesis's
               central empirical question: does hybrid work harm
               productivity?

               The null equivalence tests (not merely non-
               significant t-tests) formally establish that hybrid
               working has a TRUE null effect on performance —
               performance grades, promotions, and lines of code are
               all equivalent between hybrid and office-only
               conditions. This is the gold-standard causal evidence
               for the thesis mediation hypothesis: hybrid work per
               se does not damage productivity, but it also does not
               automatically improve it. The effect of hybrid work
               on productivity is NEUTRAL; what explains the
               retention and satisfaction improvements is the WLB
               benefit (reduced commute, flexibility). Management
               systems must therefore be the mechanism through which
               hybrid work affects PRODUCTIVITY (not just retention).

               Cite for: "The landmark randomised control trial by
               Bloom et al. (2024) established that a hybrid
               schedule of two days WFH per week did not
               significantly affect performance grades, promotions
               or coding output over a two-year follow-up period in
               a Chinese technology firm (n=1,612). Hybrid working
               improved job satisfaction and reduced quit rates by
               one-third, but left performance unchanged, suggesting
               that productivity gains from hybrid work are not
               automatic and require enabling management systems."

Ch. 2.1 use  : The paper's definitional precision — hybrid = 3 days
               office + 2 days WFH — provides the empirical
               operationalisation for the thesis hybrid work
               mediator construct. Cite alongside B06 (Lauring2025)
               for the hybrid work definition.

               The manager–non-manager divergence in pre-experiment
               perceptions (managers: −2.6%; non-managers: +0.7%)
               aligns directly with B08 (Tonnessen2026) and B09
               (OBrady2025): management mindset is the key
               organisational boundary condition for hybrid work
               adoption. The updating of manager perceptions to
               net-positive (+1.0%) post-experiment suggests that
               management systems (exposure and trust-building)
               moderate the effectiveness of hybrid work.

Limitation   : Single firm (Trip.com) and single sector (technology
               travel); China-specific (Shanghai; low COVID period);
               knowledge workers only (engineers, marketing,
               finance) — not applicable to healthcare/field
               operations roles at GGHN; WFH was optional (not
               mandatory) with partial take-up (~40–55%); findings
               may not generalise to <3 days/week in office.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] RCT design — randomisation by birthday; cleanest causal
      identification in the corpus; registered with AEA
      (16 August 2021 — after experiment start but before
      data were received by academic team; standard practice
      for firm-initiated field experiments)
  [+] Nature (Springer Nature) — highest-prestige journal in
      the corpus; IF ~64; global reach; 339,000 accesses;
      224 citations; Altmetric 1,795 (extraordinary)
  [+] n=1,612 — largest experimental sample in the corpus
  [+] Two-year longitudinal performance tracking — 4 review
      periods; rare in hybrid work literature
  [+] NULL EQUIVALENCE TESTING (TOST) — formally establishes
      true null effects, not merely non-significance; gold
      standard for establishing 'no difference'
  [+] Multiple objective performance measures: grades,
      promotions, lines of code, 9 subcategories
  [+] Data available (Harvard Dataverse: 10.7910/DVN/6X4ZZL)
  [+] CC BY 4.0 open access
  [~] Single firm (Trip.com) — generalisability limited to
      technology sector knowledge workers
  [~] China-specific context — cultural, institutional and
      labour-market differences from Nigeria/GGHN
  [~] WFH take-up was partial (~40–55%) — effective treatment
      closer to 1 day than 2 days for many employees
  [~] Not applicable to roles requiring physical presence
      (healthcare, field operations — directly relevant to
      parts of GGHN's work)
  [~] No explicit management systems measures — the role of
      MIS/OMS in enabling the null performance effect is
      inferred, not directly tested

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B05 (Maity2025) — conditional productivity thesis:
  B05 (PRISMA SLR, 12 studies) proposed that hybrid work benefits
  are conditional on enabling factors. B17 provides the most
  rigorous causal evidence for this: hybrid work improves
  RETENTION and SATISFACTION (the enabling-factor benefits) but
  has a NULL effect on PERFORMANCE (the productivity outcome).
  This split finding — positive WLB/retention but null
  productivity — directly supports the conditional thesis.

Convergence with B10 (Mele2023) — conflicting productivity evidence:
  B10's finding that productivity evidence is conflicting across
  19 public sector studies (12+/4−/3 null) is now contextualised
  by B17: the conflicting evidence may reflect whether studies
  measure performance (null — B17) or retention/satisfaction
  (positive — B17). Papers that conflate these outcomes will
  show conflicting results.

Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — manager resistance:
  B17's finding that managers had net-negative pre-experiment
  views (−2.6%) mirrors B08's discussion of manager distrust as
  the key adoption barrier. Both papers converge: manager mindset
  is the critical management systems (OMS) variable determining
  whether hybrid work can be implemented effectively.

Convergence with B11 (Curzi2026) — innovation/performance:
  B11 found no remote work × IWB interaction for result-based
  appraisal and new-competence appraisal — these maintained their
  positive effects regardless of remote work extent. B17 confirms
  this from the opposite direction: hybrid work left innovation
  subcategory scores unchanged (null equivalence). Together they
  establish that well-managed hybrid work maintains innovation
  and performance — it does not enhance them, but neither does it
  harm them.

KEY THESIS CONTRIBUTION — THE CAUSAL BENCHMARK:
  B17 establishes the causal benchmark against which all other
  Block B correlational findings must be interpreted. The RCT
  evidence is unambiguous: hybrid work → performance is NULL.
  This means that any positive productivity effects of hybrid
  work documented in observational studies (B04, B13, B16) must
  work THROUGH mediating mechanisms, not directly. This is the
  strongest available causal evidence for the thesis mediation
  hypothesis: the direct effect of hybrid work on productivity
  is null; the mediated effect (via management systems) is the
  true pathway. Cite B17 as the causal foundation for why the
  thesis tests management systems mediation rather than a direct
  hybrid work → productivity relationship.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - RCT design: first randomised control trial in corpus; gold
    standard causal identification; experiment pre-registered
  - Nature journal: highest-prestige outlet in corpus; IF~64
  - Null equivalence testing (TOST): first formal equivalence
    test in corpus — establishes true null effect vs. merely
    non-significant t-test
  - Trip.com (travel technology): first Chinese technology firm
    in corpus; largest experimental sample (n=1,612)
  - Two-year longitudinal performance tracking: longest outcome
    follow-up in corpus
  - Manager perception updating: first paper in corpus to
    document pre-to-post manager attitude revision on hybrid
    work productivity (−2.6% → +1.0%)
  - 224 citations + Altmetric 1,795: highest impact metrics in
    corpus by a wide margin
  - Causal null effect: first causal evidence in corpus that
    hybrid work → performance is null (not merely non-
    significant in correlational data)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Bloom, N., Han, R., & Liang, J. (2024). Hybrid working from home
improves retention without damaging performance. Nature, 630(8018),
920–925. https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-024-07500-2
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B18
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B18
Cite key        : Broccardo2025
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — organisational
                  control as conceptual bridge; OCT framework; KPIs/
                  KRIs for remote work; eco-control; digital
                  infrastructure as moderator),
                  T3 (hybrid work — WFH, remote work, hybrid models;
                  post-COVID-19 transformation; coworking spaces;
                  context-specific conditions),
                  T4 (systematic literature review — SLR; 126 empirical
                  papers; Scopus; NVivo 14.0 qualitative coding; three
                  emergent themes; international multi-institutional),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — conflicting
                  evidence on WFH → productivity; mediators and
                  moderators; e-leadership; cyberloafing; technostress)
Title           : Remote work, organisational control and environmental
                  impact: A review and research agenda
Authors         : Broccardo, L., Crocco, E., Agarwal, R., & Alghafes, R.
Year            : 2025 (received 8 July 2024; revised 2 September 2025;
                  accepted 30 September 2025; available online
                  5 October 2025)
Journal         : European Management Journal
Volume/Issue    : In press (available online); EMJ; Elsevier
DOI             : [10.1016/j.emj.2025.09.010](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.emj.2025.09.010)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : University of Turin, Italy (Broccardo, Crocco);
                  Jaipuria Institute of Management, Lucknow, India
                  (Agarwal); Princess Nourah bint Abdulrahman
                  University, Riyadh, Saudi Arabia (Alghafes)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Systematic Literature Review (SLR); Scopus database;
                  iterative qualitative coding (open + axial coding in
                  NVivo 14.0); expert panel validation (n=4: 1
                  accounting professor, 1 organisational studies
                  professor, 2 HR practitioners); TBL framework
Aim             : To critically assess current state of the art at the
                  intersections of remote work, organisational control,
                  and environmental impact; to identify gaps; to develop
                  a cohesive theoretical framework; to set a research
                  agenda
Research        : RQ1: What can we infer from the current state of the
questions         art and conceptual overlaps of remote work,
                  organisational control, and environmental impact?
                  RQ2: What future research directions emerge from a
                  critical analysis of the sample?
Setting/context : International, multidisciplinary; 126 empirical
                  papers from Scopus; Business, Management and
                  Accounting category; no temporal restriction; English
                  only; predominantly post-2020 (COVID-19 surge)
Sample          : 1,892 initial records → 267 after exclusion criteria
                  → 126 final empirical papers (after full-text review
                  excluding 141); methodology mix: quantitative n=57;
                  qualitative n=49; mixed-methods n=11; SLR n=9
Search strings  : Three combined strings (AND operator):
                  (1) Remote work variants: "virtual work", "remote
                      work*", "telework*", "distributed work*",
                      "home-based work*", "flexible work*", "smart
                      work*", "mobile work*", "virtual team" etc.
                  (2) Performance/control: "employee productivity",
                      "job performance", "firm performance",
                      "organisational control", "eco-control",
                      "management control"
                  (3) Environment: "environmental sustainability",
                      "ecological footprint", "environmental impact",
                      "environment*"
Theoretical     : Organisational Control Theory (Flamholtz et al.,
frameworks        1985; Flamholtz, 1996) — control as channelling human
                  effort toward company goals via performance assessment
                  and incentive systems; Triple Bottom Line (TBL)
                  framework; eco-control (Henri & Journeault, 2010)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
THREE EMERGENT THEMES (from NVivo qualitative coding)
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
THEME 1 — Effects of remote work on organisational control
  (productivity and performance):
  - Research concentrated in IT and service sectors; limited
    evidence for offline/manufacturing industries
  - WFH enhances productivity in some studies (autonomy,
    empowerment, reduced commute) but CONFLICTING overall:
    technostress, cyberloafing, lack of social interaction
    can reduce productivity
  - KEY SYNTHESIS: "virtual teams require various enabling
    factors to perform at levels comparable to co-located teams"
    — scholars consistently find direct WFH → productivity
    effects are CONTEXT-SPECIFIC and MEDIATED by organisational
    factors
  - Identified mediators: psychological well-being (Ng et al.,
    2022); technostress (Jaiswal et al., 2022); agile mindset
    (Sathe & Panse, 2022); leadership effectiveness
  - Naqshbandi et al. (2023): WFH not always significant
    predictor of engagement/performance, particularly where
    infrastructure is poor

THEME 2 — New challenges of remote work for organisational control:
  - Cyberloafing: surge without structured formal controls
  - Employee monitoring: more difficult than co-located; increased
    monitoring risks undermining well-being and productivity
  - Risk management: data leaks, regulatory compliance, misconduct
  - Job satisfaction: mixed — positive for employees with family
    responsibilities; negative where monitoring is intrusive
  - Coworking spaces: additional control paradoxes ('paying to
    work'; surveillance limitations)

THEME 3 — Environmental and urbanisation effects of remote work:
  - COVID-19 lockdowns reduced carbon emissions (NYC −25% air
    pollution; LA longest clear-air period on record; −31% in
    2020 vs. 2019)
  - Long-term consequences contested: WFH may encourage
    suburbanisation → longer commutes; household energy
    consumption increases
  - Companies increasingly embedding WFH in sustainability
    strategies; eco-control frameworks needed

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS FOR THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. MANAGEMENT SYSTEMS (OCT) AS BRIDGE BETWEEN REMOTE WORK AND
   PRODUCTIVITY:
   Organisational control is identified as the conceptual and
   practical bridge between remote work arrangements and
   performance outcomes. Without control systems (KPIs, feedback
   mechanisms, monitoring frameworks), remote work does not
   reliably predict productivity. This is the SLR-level
   theoretical support for the thesis mediation model.

2. DIRECT WFH → PRODUCTIVITY IS CONTEXT-SPECIFIC AND CONFLICTING:
   Across 126 empirical papers, the SLR confirms that the direct
   relationship between WFH and productivity is neither
   consistently positive nor negative — it depends on
   organisational context, leadership, infrastructure, and
   control systems. This is the broadest SLR confirmation of
   the thesis mediation hypothesis in the corpus.

3. DIGITAL INFRASTRUCTURE AS MODERATOR:
   Firms investing in enabling technologies are more effective at
   supporting remote work and sustaining productivity. This maps
   onto the thesis MIS construct: technology infrastructure is a
   necessary precondition for hybrid work → productivity.

4. E-LEADERSHIP AS KEY MODERATOR/MEDIATOR:
   E-leadership identified as a key factor mediating the effects
   of remote work on productivity. Leadership effectiveness in
   virtual settings (adapting KPIs, managing distributed teams,
   building trust) is an OMS component that shapes productivity.

5. FRAMEWORK — KPIs AND KRIs FOR REMOTE WORK CONTROL:
   The proposed TBL-based framework recommends:
   KPIs (positive externalities): retention, satisfaction,
     knowledge sharing, communication effectiveness,
     environmental indicators (commute reduction, emissions)
   KRIs (negative externalities): cyberloafing risk, data
     security breaches, monitoring-induced stress, household
     energy increases, suburbanisation-driven commute growth
   This KPI/KRI framework is directly applicable to the thesis
   recommendation that GGHN implement management systems
   components suited to its hybrid work context.

6. GAPS CONFIRMED FOR THESIS:
   - Offline/non-digital sectors (NGO/health sector) absent
   - Developing economy evidence absent
   - Africa entirely absent from country distribution
   - Longitudinal designs lacking
   - Theory-building rather than theory-testing studies needed

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2+2.3 SLR; OC+WFH"),
               B18 provides the most comprehensive SLR-level
               theoretical grounding for the thesis mediation model.
               The OCT framework (Flamholtz et al., 1985) — management
               control as channelling human effort toward firm goals —
               is the T1-level theoretical anchor for the OMS
               sub-construct. Cite B18 alongside B15 (Dekker2025) for
               the argument that organisational control is the
               necessary bridge between hybrid work and productivity.

Ch. 2.3 use  : The SLR's main finding — that WFH → productivity is
               context-specific and requires enabling factors — is the
               126-paper empirical confirmation of the thesis mediation
               hypothesis. Cite for the argument that "across 126
               empirical studies, the direct effect of remote work on
               productivity is neither consistently positive nor
               negative, confirming that organisational control
               mechanisms serve as the mediating bridge."

               The KPI/KRI framework (Section 6.4) provides direct
               practical vocabulary for the thesis recommendations
               section: GGHN should implement hybrid work management
               using both performance indicators (KPIs) and risk
               indicators (KRIs), as recommended by Broccardo et al.
               (2025) for organisations transitioning to remote work.

Ch. 3 use    : The identified gaps (offline/non-digital sectors,
               developing economies, Africa absent) directly justify
               the GGHN case study. The country distribution figure
               (Fig. B3) explicitly confirms Africa as a dark/
               under-represented region in the literature. Citable
               alongside B05, B10, and B17 for gap justification.

Limitation   : SLR (not primary empirical study) — no original data;
               Scopus only (Web of Science excluded); English only;
               organisational control focus means broader WFH
               literature may be underrepresented; qualitative coding
               introduces subjectivity risk; no formal GRADE
               assessment of individual papers.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] European Management Journal (EMJ; Elsevier) — ABS 3-rated;
      SSCI-indexed; leading European management outlet
  [+] 126 empirical papers — largest Scopus-based SLR in corpus
      on remote work + organisational control intersection
  [+] Rigorous seven-step SLR protocol (Fisch & Block, 2018;
      Tranfield et al., 2003); Scopus (broader than WoS)
  [+] Expert panel validation (n=4) for theme coding
  [+] NVivo 14.0 qualitative coding with open + axial coding;
      inter-coder reliability managed through collaborative review
  [+] VOSViewer keyword analysis; country distribution mapping
  [+] TBL theoretical framework with KPI/KRI distinction —
      directly applicable to thesis recommendations
  [+] CC BY 4.0 open access; Elsevier
  [+] International team (Italy, India, Saudi Arabia) — multi-
      cultural perspective reduces single-context bias
  [~] Scopus only — Web of Science and other databases excluded
  [~] English only — non-Anglophone research missed
  [~] Organisational control focus means some WFH literature
      excluded if lacking explicit control perspective
  [~] No formal quality appraisal of individual papers (no
      GRADE or equivalent)
  [~] In press at time of extraction — not yet fully paginated

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B05 (Maity2025) and B10 (Mele2023) — SLR level:
  B05 (12 SME studies; conditional productivity thesis), B10
  (120 public sector studies; conflicting productivity evidence),
  and B18 (126 cross-sector studies; context-specific productivity)
  together constitute a three-SLR convergence confirming that the
  direct WFH → productivity effect is neither automatic nor
  universal. Across 258 combined primary studies, no consistent
  direct effect emerges — the mediation hypothesis is supported
  at the SLR level across three independent reviews covering
  different sectors and time periods.

Convergence with B15 (Dekker2025) — OCT framework:
  Both B15 and B18 apply OCT (Ouchi/Flamholtz traditions) to
  remote/hybrid work. B15 provides the field evidence that outcome
  control (a specific OCT mechanism) enables WFH performance via
  communication. B18 provides the SLR-level synthesis showing
  that OCT is the dominant theoretical framework for understanding
  how management systems mediate WFH → productivity. Together
  they establish OCT as the theoretical foundation for the thesis
  OMS construct.

Convergence with B09 (OBrady2025) — e-leadership:
  B09 identified developmental coaching as the key management
  practice for remote work well-being; B18 identifies e-leadership
  as a key mediator of WFH → productivity at the SLR level. Both
  converge: leadership-based management systems components are
  the critical human mechanism through which remote work
  arrangements produce performance outcomes.

Convergence with B17 (Bloom2024) — KPI evolution:
  B18's KPI/KRI framework for remote work control directly
  complements B17's empirical findings. B17 showed that Trip.com's
  outcome-based KPIs (performance grades, promotions, lines of
  code) were unaffected by hybrid work — confirming that well-
  designed KPI systems maintain performance under hybrid
  arrangements. B18 provides the SLR-level rationale for WHY:
  KPIs anchor organisational control when behaviour and informal
  controls are reduced by physical separation.

NEW CONTRIBUTION — ENVIRONMENTAL DIMENSION:
  B18 introduces an environmental sustainability dimension absent
  from all other Block B papers. For GGHN, a global health NGO
  with sustainability commitments and donor reporting obligations,
  the eco-control angle (monitoring environmental KPIs alongside
  productivity KPIs) is directly relevant. The finding that WFH
  reduces commuting emissions but increases household energy
  consumption is a practical consideration for GGHN's hybrid
  work policy design.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - European Management Journal (EMJ): first EMJ paper in corpus
  - 126-paper SLR: largest SLR in corpus by primary study count
    (B05: 12; B10: 120; B18: 126)
  - Eco-control / environmental dimension: first explicit
    environmental sustainability + remote work + control
    intersection in corpus
  - TBL (Triple Bottom Line) framework: first TBL application
    in corpus to remote work
  - KPI/KRI distinction for remote work control: first in
    corpus to explicitly distinguish performance from risk
    indicators for hybrid/remote management design
  - NVivo 14.0 qualitative coding: first paper in corpus to
    report NVivo-based SLR coding
  - VOSViewer keyword clustering: first bibliometric
    visualisation tool reported in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Broccardo, L., Crocco, E., Agarwal, R., & Alghafes, R. (2025). Remote
work, organisational control and environmental impact: A review and
research agenda. European Management Journal. Advance online
publication. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.emj.2025.09.010
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B19
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B19
Cite key        : Kagerl2023
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — WFH, remote work, hybrid
                  scheduling; firm-level perspective; Germany;
                  COVID-19 pandemic; post-pandemic future of work),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — firm-
                  reported WFH → productivity assessment; productivity
                  and hours-worked mechanisms; barriers to WFH;
                  investment in digital infrastructure as enabler),
                  T6 (empirical: representative firm survey; n~2,000
                  establishments per month; IAB BeCovid; Oct 2020 –
                  Jun 2022; descriptive statistics + OLS/logit
                  regressions; conditional correlations)
Title           : Working from home for good? Lessons learned from the
                  COVID-19 pandemic and what this means for the future
                  of work
Authors         : Kagerl, C., & Starzetz, J.
Year            : 2023 (accepted 1 November 2022; published online
                  24 November 2022; print 2023)
Journal         : Journal of Business Economics
                  (Zeitschrift für Betriebswirtschaft; Springer)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 93, pp. 229–265
DOI             : [10.1007/s11573-022-01124-6](https://doi.org/10.1007/s11573-022-01124-6)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Institute for Employment Research (IAB), Nuremberg,
                  Germany (Kagerl); Faculty of Business and Economics,
                  University of Basel, Switzerland (Starzetz)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; repeated cross-sectional
                  survey (rotating panel); descriptive statistics;
                  OLS/logit regressions with rich controls; IAB
                  BeCovid panel; firm-level perspective
Aim             : To provide evidence on the experiences of German
                  private-sector establishments with WFH during the
                  COVID-19 pandemic; to assess effects of WFH on
                  business success; to evaluate productivity/hours-
                  worked mechanisms; to examine firms' plans for
                  post-pandemic WFH
Setting/context : Germany; private-sector establishments; IAB BeCovid
                  survey; October 2020 – June 2022; representative of
                  all privately owned German establishments required
                  to notify within the social security system
Sample          : ~1,500–2,000 establishments per monthly wave;
                  oversampling of large firms (250+ employees); 16
                  strata (5 sectors × 4 size classes); matched with
                  administrative data from Establishment History Panel
                  (BHP; 2010–2019); employment-weighted results
                  representative of German private-sector employment
Key outcomes    : (1) WFH adoption rate (share of establishments
                      enabling WFH over time)
                  (2) WFH potential (25–30% of private-sector
                      employees in Germany)
                  (3) WFH → business success (negative crisis
                      effects; revenue change; profitability)
                  (4) Mechanisms: productivity channel (ProdIncr)
                      and hours-worked channel (HoursIncr)
                  (5) Firms' plans for post-pandemic WFH
Theoretical     : Production function framing (two channels: labour
framework         productivity and labour input); WFH as decentralisation
                  of decision-making (Aghion et al., 2014); shirking
                  vs. intrinsic motivation debate

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. WFH ADOPTION OVER TIME:
   - Pre-pandemic 2019: ~25% of German establishments offered WFH
   - During pandemic: peaked at ~50% of establishments (summer
     2021), accounting for 75% of total German employment
   - 40% of pandemic WFH firms had never offered WFH before
   - Large firms (250+ employees): >90% enabled WFH throughout
     pandemic; small firms (<10 employees): ~36–43%

2. WFH POTENTIAL IN GERMANY:
   - Employer-based estimate: 25–30% of private-sector employees
     have tasks suitable for WFH
   - Lower than other estimates (Dingel & Neiman 2020: 37%;
     Alipour et al. 2021: 56%) — reflects practical rather than
     theoretical WFH capacity as assessed by employers
   - ICT sector and knowledge-intensive services have highest
     WFH potential; manufacturing and non-knowledge-intensive
     production lowest

3. BARRIERS TO WFH:
   - Task unsuitability: 98% of non-WFH firms cited this
   - Lack of technical equipment: 37%
   - Data protection/security concerns: 25%
   - ICT firms and knowledge-intensive services significantly
     more likely to enable WFH; each 10pp increase in highly
     complex occupation share raises WFH probability by ~6pp

4. WFH → BUSINESS SUCCESS (suggestive evidence, not causal):
   - Higher WFH share negatively related to reporting negative
     crisis effects: each 10pp increase in WFH share associated
     with 1.5–2.5pp lower probability of negative crisis impact
   - Higher WFH share associated with ~2pp lower probability
     of revenue decrease
   - Higher WFH share associated with 1.3pp higher probability
     of turning a profit in 2020 (controlling for pre-crisis
     profitability)

5. MECHANISMS (Table 1, Table 2):
   Productivity channel (ProdIncr):
     - 22% of WFH firms report WFH increased worker productivity;
       13% report decrease; 60% no change
     - When ProdIncr=1 AND HoursIncr=1:
       WFH → profitability: β+τ+θ = 0.209** (p<0.05)
       WFH → lower negative crisis effects: β+τ+θ = −0.242** (p<0.05)
     - Productivity channel alone: stronger for negative crisis
       effects; hours channel alone: stronger for profitability
   Hours-worked channel (HoursIncr):
     - ~15% of firms report employees worked more hours WFH;
       only ~5% report fewer hours (80% no change)
     - Key finding: minimal evidence of shirking

6. PRODUCTIVITY ASSESSMENT (November 2021 / June 2022):
   - Net positive: 27% of employees in WFH firms assessed by
     employers as more productive WFH; only 9% less productive
   - Similarly positive for: work ethic/team spirit; quality
     of work performed
   - Problem areas: communication between employees (55%
     reported problems); on-boarding of new employees (58%);
     communication between management and employees (44%)

7. INVESTMENTS IN DIGITAL TECHNOLOGIES:
   - 2/3 of WFH firms invested in digital technologies during
     pandemic; only 1/3 of non-WFH firms did so
   - WFH firms more likely to invest in hardware, communication
     software, collaboration software, remote access, faster
     internet, cybersecurity
   - 35% of WFH firms conducted IT-related training (vs. 8% of
     non-WFH firms)

8. POST-PANDEMIC PLANS (November 2021):
   - 62% of WFH-able employees work in firms planning to EXPAND
     WFH post-pandemic (up from 40% in October 2020)
   - Largest firms (250+): ~20% of employees projected to WFH
     for up to 2 days per week → hybrid model predominates
   - Most frequent reasons for expanding WFH:
     (1) Better reconciliation of work and family life (73%)
     (2) Employee flexibility (73%)
     (3) Employer attractiveness (54%)
     (4) WFH worked better than expected (45%)
   - Most frequent reasons for NOT expanding WFH:
     (1) Tasks unsuitable (63%)
     (2) Cooperation more difficult (55%)
     (3) Does not fit corporate culture (39%)
     (4) Fear of unequal treatment of employees (34%)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 empirical"), B19
               provides the firm-level evidence on WFH → productivity
               from a country representative perspective. The key
               finding — 60% of WFH firms report no productivity
               change; 22% positive; 13% negative — is the firm-
               level confirmation of the thesis mediation hypothesis:
               WFH does not automatically produce productivity gains.
               It is only when WFH is accompanied by productivity-
               enabling mechanisms (digital infrastructure, IT
               training, management adaptation) that positive business
               outcomes result.

               The mechanism analysis (Tables 1–2) is particularly
               citable: the effect of WFH on firm profitability is
               strongest (β+τ+θ=0.209**, p<0.05) when BOTH the
               productivity channel AND the hours-worked channel
               operate. Neither works sufficiently in isolation —
               this is the firm-level analogue of the thesis mediation
               model: management systems enable the conditions
               (productivity, effort, digital tools) through which
               WFH translates into performance.

               Also citable for: 2/3 of WFH firms invested in digital
               technologies; 35% conducted IT training — confirming
               Block A cross-cutting pattern #1 (ICT/technology as
               dominant management systems enabler) at the firm level
               in a European context.

Limitation   : Germany-specific — not directly comparable to Nigerian/
               GGHN context (different labour market, infrastructure,
               regulatory environment); conditional correlations, not
               causal; firm-level (establishment) rather than
               employee-level data; self-reported assessments of
               productivity; rotating panel limits longitudinal
               tracking; manufacturing-heavy German economy may
               systematically understate WFH potential relative to
               service-sector dominated developing economies.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Journal of Business Economics (Zeitschrift für
      Betriebswirtschaft; Springer) — Scopus and SSCI indexed;
      reputable German economics journal
  [+] IAB BeCovid survey: most comprehensive German establishment
      survey on COVID-19 and WFH; ~2,000 establishments per wave;
      12 waves covering Oct 2020 – Jun 2022
  [+] Representative of entire German private-sector (employment-
      weighted); oversampled large firms; matched with BHP admin
      data — highest data quality in Block B for firm-level studies
  [+] Rich set of controls: sector, size, age, export status,
      works council, occupational requirement levels, pre-pandemic
      productivity (AKM fixed effects), employment trend
  [+] Two mechanisms explicitly tested (productivity and hours-
      worked channels) with interaction terms in regressions
  [+] Temporal variation across 8 WFH survey waves: rare in the
      literature
  [+] CC BY 4.0 open access; data available via IAB FDZ
  [+] Authors transparent about non-causal interpretation of
      results ("suggestive evidence")
  [~] Conditional correlations only — not causal
  [~] Firm-level assessment of employee productivity — not
      individual measures
  [~] Germany-specific; manufacturing-heavy economy
  [~] Rotating panel structure limits longitudinal individual
      firm tracking
  [~] Some questions asked only once or in selected waves —
      limits comparability across full time series

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B17 (Bloom2024) — null/mixed productivity:
  B17's null equivalence test on performance confirms what B19
  finds at the firm level: 60% of WFH firms report NO productivity
  change. The 22% positive / 13% negative split in B19 mirrors
  B17's overall null result and B10/B18's conflicting evidence
  narrative. B19 provides the German firm-perspective confirmation
  of B17's Chinese RCT finding: WFH does not automatically change
  productivity — it requires enabling conditions.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #1 (ICT dominant):
  B19's finding that 2/3 of WFH firms invested in digital
  technologies and 35% conducted IT training, and that ICT sector
  firms are 30.7pp more likely to enable WFH (Table 4), is the
  firm-level European confirmation of Block A's finding that
  technology/ICT is the dominant management systems predictor
  across seven independent studies. Both point to the same
  conclusion: ICT infrastructure is the foundational management
  systems component for productive remote/hybrid work.

Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — hours-worked channel:
  B08 found that informal self-control (employee effort and
  autonomy) mediates remote work → performance. B19's hours-
  worked channel (HoursIncr) is the firm-level counterpart:
  employees working more hours when WFH is observed in ~15% of
  firms, with no evidence of shirking (80% no change). Both papers
  contradict the managerial 'shirking hypothesis' and support the
  intrinsic motivation/autonomy narrative.

Convergence with B12 (Tsang2023) — FWC as productivity barrier:
  B19 identifies on-boarding (58%) and inter-employee
  communication (55%) as the primary WFH problem areas. B12
  identified FWC → reduced SE/WE → reduced WFHP. Both point to
  social and relational factors (collaboration, connection) as the
  primary barriers to WFH productivity — not task unsuitability
  or technology per se.

Convergence with B15 (Dekker2025) — hybrid as solution:
  B19's conclusion that large German firms (250+ employees) are
  planning hybrid work (up to 2 days WFH, mandatory office days
  for coordination and socialising) exactly mirrors B15's
  Nordea OCT finding that outcome control must replace/supplement
  behaviour and informal control in WFH settings. Both papers
  recommend hybrid rather than full remote as the optimal design.
  B19 provides the economy-wide firm survey confirmation; B15
  provides the field study mechanism.

NEW CONTRIBUTION — FIRM INVESTMENT AS MANAGEMENT SYSTEM:
  B19 explicitly links WFH adoption → digital technology
  investment → productivity channel. The finding that WFH
  firms invest more in IT (hardware, software, training) than
  non-WFH firms, and that this investment correlates with the
  productivity channel, provides the clearest firm-level evidence
  that management system investment (MIS/ICT) is the mediating
  mechanism between WFH adoption and business success — the core
  thesis argument at the firm level. This is directly analogous
  to the GGHN context: if GGHN adopts hybrid work without
  investing in IT infrastructure and training, the productivity
  mechanism will not operate.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Journal of Business Economics (Springer): first JBE paper
    in corpus
  - Germany firm-level representative survey: first economy-wide
    representative establishment survey in corpus
  - IAB BeCovid dataset: first institute-based longitudinal
    establishment panel in corpus
  - 8-wave repeated survey: longest temporal span of any primary
    data study in corpus (Oct 2020 – Jun 2022)
  - Hours-worked channel: first explicit test of WFH → increased
    working hours mechanism at firm level in corpus
  - 25–30% WFH potential estimate: first country-level WFH
    potential calculation from employer perspective in corpus
  - German manufacturing-sector context: first major European
    manufacturing economy context in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Kagerl, C., & Starzetz, J. (2023). Working from home for good?
Lessons learned from the COVID-19 pandemic and what this means for
the future of work. Journal of Business Economics, 93, 229–265.
https://doi.org/10.1007/s11573-022-01124-6
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B20
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B20
Cite key        : Blahopoulou2022
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — crisis-induced telework vs.
                  conventional telework; COVID-19 lockdown; Spain;
                  mandatory full-time WFH),
                  T5 (productivity enablers — telework satisfaction
                  → wellbeing → performance; partial mediation;
                  children as dual resource and demand),
                  T6 (empirical: prospective two-wave panel; n=111;
                  Spain; PROCESS Macro Model 4; WFH satisfaction
                  → subjective wellbeing → self-reported performance)
Title           : Telework satisfaction, wellbeing and performance in
                  the digital era. Lessons learned during COVID-19
                  lockdown in Spain
Authors         : Blahopoulou, J., Ortiz-Bonnín, S., Montañez-Juan,
                  M., Torrens Espinosa, G., & García-Buades, M. E.
Year            : 2022 (accepted 2 February 2022; published online
                  20 February 2022)
Journal         : Current Psychology (Springer)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 42, pp. 22579–22588 (2023)
DOI             : [10.1007/s12144-022-02873-x](https://doi.org/10.1007/s12144-022-02873-x)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Department of Psychology, University of the Balearic
                  Islands (UIB), Palma de Mallorca, Spain

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; prospective two-wave panel
                  (time-lagged design); PROCESS Macro Model 4
                  (mediation with lagged effects); SPSS v.27
Aim             : To examine the effects of telework satisfaction (T1)
                  on subjective wellbeing and self-reported performance
                  (T2) during the COVID-19 lockdown; to test the
                  mediating role of subjective wellbeing; to explore
                  the role of having children under 18 as a moderating
                  contextual variable
Setting/context : Spain; COVID-19 strict lockdown (March–June 2020);
                  first lockdown wave; only essential workers permitted
                  to leave homes; children under strict lockdown for
                  6 weeks; Spanish workforce had very low pre-pandemic
                  telework experience (4% regular teleworkers)
Sample          : n=111 Spanish employees (from T1: N=456; T2: N=118;
                  final n=111 completing both waves); 80.2% women;
                  80.2% university graduates; 63.1% with children
                  under 18; 75.7% first-time teleworkers; Mean age
                  42.45 (SD=8.34); diverse sectors (education 25.2%;
                  admin 15.3%; health 9%; tourism 7.2%; IT 4.5% etc.)
                  Non-probabilistic snowball sampling; two time points
                  T1 (1 April 2020) and T2 (1 May 2020); 1-month lag
IV (T1)         : Telework Satisfaction: 9-item scale (adapted from
                  Staples et al., 1999 + 6 new items); facets: amount
                  of work, type of work, working hours organisation,
                  concentration/interruptions, family/partner respect
                  of workspace, supervisor support, colleague support,
                  ICT availability, physical conditions; α=0.84;
                  6-point scale (1=very unsatisfied; 6=very satisfied)
Mediator (T2)   : Subjective Wellbeing: WHO-5 Wellbeing Index
                  (Spanish version; Topp et al., 2015); 5 items;
                  α=0.84; percentage score 0–100 (below 52 = poor
                  wellbeing; threshold for depression screening)
DV (T2)         : Self-reported Performance: 3-item scale (quantity,
                  quality, goal achievement; based on González-Romá
                  & Gamero, 2012; Koopmans et al., 2012); α=0.85;
                  5-point scale (1=very bad; 5=very good)
Controls        : Age; gender; prior telework experience; having
                  children under 18 years old
Theoretical     : Spillover/Spillover-Crossover hypothesis (Bakker &
frameworks        Demerouti, 2013): job satisfaction spills over into
                  subjective wellbeing
                  Happy-Productive Worker Thesis (Judge et al., 2001):
                  wellbeing → positive performance
                  Social Exchange Theory (Blau, 1964): satisfactory
                  telework conditions → employee reciprocates with
                  productive behaviour
                  Broaden-and-Build Model (Fredrickson, 2001):
                  positive emotions broaden thought-action repertoires

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Prospective two-wave panel; 1-month lag between T1
                  and T2; snowball sampling via LinkedIn, university
                  website, professional contacts
Analysis        : PROCESS Macro Model 4 (Hayes, 2013); regression
                  analyses with lagged effects; bootstrapped CIs for
                  indirect effects (5,000 subsamples); Shrout & Bolger
                  (2002) method; t-tests for exploratory group
                  comparisons
Reliability     : All α ≥ 0.84 (threshold 0.70) ✓
CMB             : Mitigated by time-lagged design (T1 IV; T2 DV/
                  mediator); self-report limitations acknowledged
Sample size     : n=111 — small; authors acknowledge; limits
                  statistical power and generalisability

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
DESCRIPTIVE:
  Telework satisfaction mean: 4.52/6 (SD=0.90) — generally
    satisfied despite crisis context
  Wellbeing mean WHO-5 score: 53.8 (SD=18.6) — ~12 points
    lower than 2012 European norm; just above the poor-wellbeing
    threshold of 52; many employees at risk of depression
  Performance mean: 3.80/5 (SD=0.82) — moderate-good

CORRELATIONS:
  Telework satisfaction ↔ wellbeing: r=0.20, p=0.030 ✓
  Telework satisfaction ↔ performance: r=0.27, p=0.004 ✓
  Wellbeing ↔ performance: r=0.27, p<0.01 ✓
  Children under 18 ↔ telework satisfaction: r=−0.34, p<0.001

HYPOTHESIS TESTING (PROCESS Model 4, bootstrapped):
H1: Telework satisfaction (T1) → Wellbeing (T2):
  Path a: β=0.26, p=0.008 ✓ SUPPORTED

H2: Telework satisfaction (T1) → Performance (T2):
  Total effect path c: β=0.32, p=0.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  Direct effect path c': β=0.26, p=0.009 ✓ (remains significant
    after including wellbeing → PARTIAL mediation confirmed)

H3: Wellbeing (T2) → Performance (T2):
  Path b: β=0.21, p=0.029 ✓ SUPPORTED

H4: Wellbeing mediates telework satisfaction → Performance:
  Indirect effect: β=0.06, Boot SE=0.03,
    Boot 95% CI [0.0015, 0.1404] ✓ SUPPORTED (CI excludes zero)
  → PARTIAL mediation confirmed
  Overall model R²=14.6%; F(6,104)=2.96, p=0.010

EXPLORATORY FINDING — CHILDREN:
  Children under 18 → LOWER telework satisfaction:
    With children: M=4.19 vs. without: M=4.88; t=4.36, p<0.001
  Children under 18 → HIGHER subjective wellbeing:
    With children: M=3.65 vs. without: M=3.41; t=−2.14, p=0.033
  Interpretation: children are simultaneously a DEMAND (reducing
    telework satisfaction by creating interruptions, shared
    devices, homeschooling burden) and a RESOURCE (providing
    social connection, compensating lockdown isolation)

[FLAG — KEY CONTEXTUAL NOTE:
  This study examines CRISIS-INDUCED telework (mandatory,
  full-time, no preparation, children at home) in Spain, which
  had only 4% regular telework pre-pandemic. This is the most
  adverse telework context in the corpus. Despite this context:
  (a) telework satisfaction was above neutral (4.52/6)
  (b) satisfaction predicted performance (β=0.32)
  (c) wellbeing partially mediated this relationship
  This suggests the satisfaction → wellbeing → performance chain
  is ROBUST even under highly adverse conditions — strengthening
  the argument for its generalisability to GGHN's context.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 empirical+mediation"),
               B20 provides the individual-level psychological
               mediation chain for the thesis: telework satisfaction
               (a management-system-enabled state) → wellbeing
               (analogous to QWL in B07; WE/SE in B12) → performance.
               This is the satisfaction-based mediation pathway that
               complements B12's FWC-based mediation.

               Key citation: "Telework satisfaction predicted
               self-reported performance both directly (β=0.32,
               p=0.001) and indirectly through subjective wellbeing
               (indirect β=0.06, 95% CI [0.0015, 0.1404]), even
               during the adverse conditions of COVID-19 lockdown
               in Spain (Blahopoulou et al., 2022). This partial
               mediation confirms that employee wellbeing is the
               proximal psychological mechanism through which
               telework arrangements produce performance outcomes."

               The 9-facet telework satisfaction scale is also
               directly useful for the thesis: ICT availability,
               supervisor support, and colleague support are the
               telework satisfaction facets that map onto OMS/MIS
               components. Management systems that address these
               facets (technology provision, supervisory practices,
               team communication structures) enable the satisfaction
               → wellbeing → performance chain.

Ch. 2.1 use  : The conventional vs. crisis-induced telework
               distinction (Table 1) is useful for the thesis hybrid
               work construct definition in Ch. 2.1. GGHN's hybrid
               work transition may share features of both: it may
               be partially mandatory, may lack preparation, and may
               occur under infrastructure constraints — making B20's
               crisis-induced context more relevant than some studies'
               voluntary/partial telework findings.

Limitation   : n=111 — small; limits statistical power and
               generalisability; 80.2% women — heavily feminised
               sample; Spain only; crisis-induced mandatory WFH ≠
               voluntary hybrid work; snowball sampling; self-
               reported performance; short T1-T2 lag (1 month);
               only first-time teleworkers (75.7%) — no experienced
               telework comparison.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Prospective two-wave panel design — rare in telework
      literature; reduces cross-sectional CMB; allows lagged
      effect testing
  [+] All Cronbach's α ≥ 0.84 — good reliability for all
      three primary scales (telework satisfaction, WHO-5
      wellbeing, self-reported performance)
  [+] PROCESS Macro Model 4 with bootstrapped CIs (5,000
      subsamples) — best-practice mediation testing
  [+] WHO-5 Wellbeing Index — validated, internationally
      standardised wellbeing measure with clinical threshold
  [+] 9-facet telework satisfaction scale captures ICT,
      supervisor support, colleague support — maps directly
      onto thesis OMS/MIS constructs
  [+] Unique crisis context (Spain's strictest lockdown;
      first-time teleworkers; children at home) provides
      extreme-condition robustness test
  [+] Current Psychology (Springer) — Scopus-indexed;
      peer-reviewed; CC BY 4.0 open access
  [~] n=111 — small; limits statistical power; β values
      are small-medium in magnitude
  [~] 80.2% women — heavily feminised; limits gender balance
  [~] Snowball sampling — not representative
  [~] Spain only; crisis-induced context — limited
      generalisability to GGHN voluntary hybrid work
  [~] Self-reported performance DV
  [~] Very short T1–T2 lag (1 month) — may not capture
      longer-term performance dynamics
  [-] R²=14.6% — relatively low variance explained;
      substantial unexplained variance in performance

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B12 (Tsang2023) — psychological mediation:
  B12 showed FWC → WE/SE → WFHP (partial mediation); B20 shows
  telework satisfaction → wellbeing → performance (partial
  mediation). Both confirm that employee psychological states
  (engagement/SE in B12; subjective wellbeing in B20) are the
  proximal mediators between work arrangement quality and
  performance. The mechanism is the same; the antecedents differ
  (FWC = demand in B12; telework satisfaction = resource in B20).
  Together they complete the JD-R mediation picture: demands
  (FWC) deplete psychological resources → lower performance;
  resources (satisfactory telework conditions) build psychological
  resources → higher performance.

Convergence with B07 (AlSuwaidi2025) — QWL pathway:
  B07's QWL → WFHP β=0.222 and B20's wellbeing → performance
  β=0.21 are nearly identical in magnitude and direction. Both
  confirm the same wellbeing/psychological quality pathway as the
  strongest mediator of remote work → performance. B07 is from
  UAE public sector (β=0.222; SEM); B20 is from Spain/crisis-
  induced context (β=0.21; PROCESS). The cross-cultural and
  cross-methodological convergence strengthens the thesis
  argument substantially.

Convergence with B18 (Broccardo2025) — OCT and satisfaction:
  B18's SLR confirmed that satisfactory telework conditions
  require management systems (digital infrastructure, monitoring,
  leadership) to sustain. B20 provides the individual-level
  empirical mechanism: when those management systems produce
  satisfactory telework conditions (as measured by the 9-facet
  scale including ICT, supervisor support, colleague support),
  wellbeing and performance improve. B18 identifies the
  organisational prerequisites; B20 identifies the psychological
  mechanism that follows.

Convergence with B19 (Kagerl2023) — productivity under WFH:
  B19 found 22% of German firms report WFH increased worker
  productivity; the mechanism is partly through the productivity
  channel (ProdIncr). B20 provides the psychological explanation
  for B19's productivity channel: satisfaction with WFH conditions
  → wellbeing → performance. The firm-level aggregate (B19) and
  individual-level mechanism (B20) converge on the same causal
  chain: quality WFH conditions → positive psychological state
  → improved performance.

CONVENTIONAL vs. CRISIS-INDUCED TELEWORK DISTINCTION:
  B20's Table 1 taxonomy (conventional vs. crisis-induced) is the
  most practically useful typology in the corpus for framing
  GGHN's situation. GGHN's hybrid work transition may share
  crisis-induced features (rapid implementation, limited
  infrastructure, health sector context) but also conventional
  features (partially voluntary, not full-time). Cite B20 when
  positioning GGHN's hybrid work context relative to existing
  literature.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Current Psychology (Springer): first Current Psychology
    paper in corpus
  - Spain context (COVID-19 crisis-induced WFH): first Spanish
    empirical study in corpus
  - Two-wave prospective panel design: first time-lagged
    panel study in corpus with distinct T1 (IV) and T2
    (mediator + DV) measurement
  - WHO-5 Wellbeing Index: first standardised clinical
    wellbeing measure in corpus; threshold 52 = poor wellbeing
  - Telework satisfaction (9-facet scale): first study in
    corpus to operationalise satisfaction with specific telework
    conditions (ICT, supervisor support, concentration,
    physical conditions) as the key IV
  - Conventional vs. crisis-induced telework distinction:
    first formal taxonomy of telework types in corpus
  - Children under 18 as dual resource/demand: first in corpus
    to empirically demonstrate children as simultaneously
    reducing telework satisfaction but increasing wellbeing
  - Happy-Productive Worker Thesis: first explicit application
    of this thesis in corpus as theoretical grounding for
    wellbeing → performance

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Blahopoulou, J., Ortiz-Bonnín, S., Montañez-Juan, M., Torrens
Espinosa, G., & García-Buades, M. E. (2022). Telework satisfaction,
wellbeing and performance in the digital era. Lessons learned during
COVID-19 lockdown in Spain. Current Psychology, 42, 22579–22588.
https://doi.org/10.1007/s12144-022-02873-x
```
