EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B21
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B21
Cite key        : Vleeshouwers2022
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — telework from home [TWFH]; office
                  worker focus; pre-COVID conventional telework;
                  14 psychosocial work environment outcome categories),
                  T4 (systematic literature review — PROSPERO-
                  registered; PRISMA 2020; 43 primary quantitative
                  studies; six databases; NOS quality assessment;
                  GRADE evidence rating; Jan 2010 – Feb 2021),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — TWFH →
                  productivity mixed evidence; autonomy and flexibility
                  as mediating variables; WLB consistently negative
                  under full TWFH; turnover reduced)
Title           : The relationship between telework from home and the
                  psychosocial work environment: a systematic review
Authors         : Vleeshouwers, J., Fløvik, L., Christensen, J. O.,
                  Johannessen, H. A., Finne, L. B., Mohr, B.,
                  Jørgensen, I. L., & Lunde, L.-K.
Year            : 2022 (received 22 April 2022; accepted 12 June
                  2022; published online 13 July 2022)
Journal         : International Archives of Occupational and
                  Environmental Health (Springer)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 95, pp. 1687–1706 (2022)
DOI             : [10.1007/s00420-022-01901-4](https://doi.org/10.1007/s00420-022-01901-4)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : National Institute of Occupational Health (STAMI),
                  Oslo, Norway (all authors)
PROSPERO        : CRD42021233796

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Systematic Literature Review (SLR); PROSPERO-
                  registered; PRISMA 2020; narrative synthesis;
                  quality assessment: NOS (individual study) +
                  GRADE (overall evidence); no meta-analysis due
                  to high heterogeneity
Aim             : To identify, evaluate, and summarise findings on the
                  association between TWFH and the psychosocial work
                  environment; to assess evidence quality; to identify
                  research gaps
Scope           : Employed office workers only (not self-employed);
                  excludes pandemic/lockdown-specific studies;
                  excludes qualitative studies; Jan 2010 – Feb 2021
Databases       : Six databases: Scopus, PubMed, Medline, Embase,
                  PsycInfo, Amed; two searches (Oct 2020 + Feb 2021)
Sample          : 3,354 records screened → 289 full-text reviewed →
                  43 primary studies included (plus 7 health-outcome
                  studies from broader project; total 50 reviewed)
Study mix       : 30 cross-sectional; 12 longitudinal; 1 RCT
                  Sample sizes: 61 to 376,577
                  Countries: USA (18), Netherlands (5), UK (4),
                  Germany (3), Belgium (3), Canada (2), others (8)
Outcomes        : 14 psychosocial work environment categories;
                  71 analyses total across 43 studies

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
14 OUTCOME CATEGORIES WITH GRADE RATINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. WORK–LIFE BALANCE (15 analyses: 12 cross-sect; 3 prospective)
   - 7/12 cross-sectional: TWFH → WORSE WLB (more WFC)
   - 3/12: TWFH → BETTER WLB
   - 2/12: null / conflicting
   - 3/3 prospective: TWFH → WORSE WLB
   GRADE: VERY LOW quality of evidence
   [NOTE: dominant finding — TWFH increases work-family conflict]

2. JOB SATISFACTION (13 analyses: 9 cross-sect; 3 prospective; 1 RCT)
   - 7/9 cross-sectional: TWFH → higher job satisfaction
   - 1 RCT (Bloom et al. 2013): TWFH → higher job satisfaction
   GRADE: LOW quality of evidence

3. PRODUCTIVITY (11 analyses: 7 cross-sect; 3 prospective; 1 RCT)
   - 5/7 cross-sectional: TWFH → higher productivity
   - 1/7: no association (Neirotti et al. 2012)
   - 1/7: LOWER productivity (Caillier 2014: >2d/wk TWFH)
   - 3/3 prospective: TWFH → higher productivity
   - 1 RCT (Bloom et al. 2013): higher productivity
   GRADE: LOW quality of evidence
   [KEY FINDING: predominantly positive but MIXED; evidence quality
   only LOW — cannot make strong recommendations]

4. TURNOVER INTENTION (7 analyses: 3 cross-sect; 3 prospective; 1 RCT)
   - All 3 cross-sectional: TWFH → LOWER turnover intention
   - 2/3 prospective: TWFH → LOWER turnover intention
   - 1/3 prospective: null (Caillier 2017)
   - 1 RCT (Bloom et al. 2013): lower turnover
   GRADE: LOW quality of evidence
   [Most consistent positive finding across all outcome categories]

5. WORKING HOURS (6 analyses: 4 cross-sect; 1 longitudinal; 1 RCT)
   - 2/4 cross-sectional: FEWER active hours WFH
   - 2/4 cross-sectional: MORE hours WFH
   - 1 longitudinal + 1 RCT: MORE hours WFH
   GRADE: VERY LOW quality of evidence

6. WORK ENGAGEMENT (5 analyses: 3 cross-sect; 2 longitudinal)
   - 1/3 cross-sectional: TWFH → LOWER engagement
   - 2/3 cross-sectional: null/conflicting
   - 1/2 longitudinal: null
   - 1/2 longitudinal: HIGHER engagement
   GRADE: VERY LOW quality of evidence

7. ORGANISATIONAL COMMITMENT (5 analyses)
   - Mixed: 2 positive; 1 negative; 1 null (cross-sect); 1 negative
   GRADE: VERY LOW quality of evidence

8. DECISION LATITUDE (2 analyses): 1 positive; 1 null
   GRADE: VERY LOW

9. SELF-LEADERSHIP (2 analyses): 1 positive; 1 null
   GRADE: LOW

10–14. CREATIVITY, PROFESSIONAL ISOLATION, WORK CONCENTRATION,
       PERCEIVED FAIRNESS, TIMING OF WORK: 1 analysis each
   - Creativity: increased (1 prospective)
   - Professional isolation: INCREASED (negative outcome; 1 prospective)
   - Work concentration: INCREASED (1 prospective — part-time TWFH)
   - Perceived fairness: INCREASED (1 cross-sectional)
   - Timing of work: less work in core hours (1 cross-sectional)
   GRADE: ALL VERY LOW

SUMMARY OF GRADE RATINGS:
   LOW quality: job satisfaction, productivity, turnover, self-lead.
   VERY LOW quality: all other 10 categories
   → NO outcome category reached MODERATE or HIGH evidence quality

KEY CROSS-CUTTING FINDING — AUTONOMY AND FLEXIBILITY AS MEDIATORS:
   The review identifies autonomy and flexibility as the key
   mediating variables across multiple outcomes. Studies consistently
   show that TWFH has more positive effects when it is:
   (a) voluntary (not mandatory)
   (b) partial (not full-time)
   (c) accompanied by flexibility in timing and scheduling
   Reference: Gajendran & Harrison (2007) meta-analysis (cited in
   review): 46 studies found positive effects of telecommuting on
   WLB, job satisfaction, performance, turnover, and role stress;
   effects partially mediated by employee autonomy.
   [NOTE: This is directly analogous to the thesis argument that
   management systems (specifically autonomy-enabling OMS
   components) mediate the TWFH → productivity relationship.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 SLR; psychosocial"),
               B21 provides the occupational health SLR-level
               evidence on TWFH → psychosocial work environment,
               complementing B05 (SME SLR), B10 (public sector SLR),
               and B18 (OCT + environment SLR). The finding that
               productivity evidence is rated only LOW quality
               (predominantly positive but not conclusive) across 11
               analyses is directly citable for the thesis mediation
               hypothesis: the absence of high-quality evidence for
               a direct TWFH → productivity effect is itself evidence
               that intermediate mechanisms (management systems,
               autonomy, flexibility) are needed to explain the
               heterogeneous outcomes.

               The autonomy and flexibility finding is the most
               thesis-relevant: cite for the argument that "autonomy,
               enabled by management systems (OMS), is the key
               mediating variable through which TWFH produces
               positive psychosocial and productivity outcomes
               (Vleeshouwers et al., 2022; Gajendran & Harrison, 2007
               as cited therein)."

               The professional isolation finding (consistently
               negative; consistent with B10's finding of 5/5
               studies) is directly relevant to GGHN's field health
               context, where relational work and team coordination
               are critical.

Limitation   : Excludes pandemic/lockdown studies (pre-COVID
               conventional TWFH only) — complementary to but not
               replaceable by pandemic-era studies; predominantly
               US-based evidence (18/43 studies); most studies are
               cross-sectional (30/43); only LOW/VERY LOW GRADE
               ratings across all categories; no African or NGO/
               health sector studies in the corpus.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] PROSPERO-registered (CRD42021233796) — prospective
      registration increases methodological integrity
  [+] PRISMA 2020 compliant; six-database search; two search
      waves (Oct 2020 + Feb 2021)
  [+] Dual assessment: NOS (individual study quality) + GRADE
      (overall evidence quality) — only SLR in corpus to use GRADE
  [+] Blinded dual-reviewer screening (Covidence software);
      third reviewer for disagreements
  [+] International Archives of Occupational and Environmental
      Health (Springer) — SSCI-indexed; reputable occupational
      health journal; CC BY 4.0 open access
  [+] National Institute of Occupational Health, Norway — leading
      European occupational health research institution
  [+] 14 distinct psychosocial outcome categories — most
      comprehensive outcome taxonomy in any Block B SLR
  [+] Explicitly excludes pandemic/lockdown studies — focuses
      on conventional TWFH, reducing pandemic confounding
  [+] Autonomy and flexibility identified as key mediating
      variables — directly maps onto thesis OMS construct
  [~] LOW/VERY LOW GRADE for all categories — itself a finding
      but limits actionable conclusions
  [~] Predominantly US-based (18/43 studies)
  [~] Mostly cross-sectional (30/43)
  [~] No African, NGO, or health sector studies
  [~] Search ends February 2021 — predates most post-pandemic
      research on hybrid (not full TWFH) arrangements

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Four-SLR convergence (B05 + B10 + B18 + B21):
  All four SLRs in the corpus — covering SME private sector (B05),
  public sector (B10), management control (B18), and occupational
  health (B21) — converge on the same conclusion: the direct
  effect of telework/WFH on productivity is neither consistently
  positive nor negative. Evidence quality is LOW or VERY LOW across
  all four reviews. This four-way cross-sectoral, cross-
  methodological convergence is the strongest available multi-SLR
  support for the thesis mediation hypothesis.

Convergence with B10 (Mele2023) — professional isolation:
  B10 found professional isolation as the most consistent negative
  outcome of telework (5/5 studies). B21 confirms: the one
  prospective analysis on professional isolation (de Vries et al.
  2018) found TWFH increases professional isolation; LMX
  (manager-employee contact frequency) moderates this. For GGHN,
  where field health workers rely on team coordination, this
  consistent negative finding is a critical hybrid work design
  consideration.

Convergence with B17 (Bloom2024) — turnover:
  B21 finds turnover intention reduction is the most consistent
  positive TWFH finding across multiple study types (3/3 cross-
  sect; 2/3 longitudinal; 1 RCT). B17 found quit rates −33%
  with hybrid WFH. Both converge: the strongest positive evidence
  for telework/hybrid work is on retention, not productivity.
  This reinforces the thesis mediation argument: hybrid work's
  direct benefits are to WLB, satisfaction, and retention (B21;
  B17); productivity requires management systems mediation.

Convergence with B15 (Dekker2025) — autonomy:
  B21 identifies autonomy as the key mediating variable for
  positive TWFH outcomes. B15 found that outcome control (a
  form of task autonomy and information-based self-direction)
  mediates WFH → performance via communication. Both confirm
  that autonomy — whether conceptualised as employee discretion
  (B21) or outcome-based self-direction supported by management
  information (B15) — is the psychological mechanism through
  which TWFH produces performance benefits.

NEW CONTRIBUTION — OCCUPATIONAL HEALTH PERSPECTIVE:
  B21 is the only occupational health SLR in the corpus (IAOE
  Health journal; National Institute of Occupational Health,
  Norway). It introduces the psychosocial work environment as the
  outcome domain, bridging performance outcomes (productivity)
  with health outcomes (wellbeing, isolation, engagement). For
  GGHN's public health and NGO context, this occupational health
  perspective is directly relevant: hybrid work design must attend
  to psychosocial health outcomes (not just productivity) in a
  healthcare workforce.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - International Archives of Occupational and Environmental
    Health (IAOE Health): first IAOE Health paper in corpus
  - National Institute of Occupational Health (STAMI), Norway:
    first Nordic occupational health institution in corpus
  - PROSPERO-registered SLR: first PROSPERO-registered review
    in corpus (CRD42021233796)
  - GRADE evidence rating: first use of GRADE in corpus to rate
    overall evidence quality across outcome categories
  - Newcastle-Ottawa Scale (NOS): first NOS-assessed SLR
    in corpus
  - 14 psychosocial outcome categories: broadest outcome taxonomy
    in any Block B SLR
  - Professional isolation (consistent negative): first SLR in
    corpus to systematically confirm professional isolation as
    a consistent negative TWFH outcome (1 prospective study —
    consistent with B10's cross-study finding)
  - Autonomy/flexibility as mediating variable: first SLR in
    corpus to formally identify these as the key mediating
    variables across TWFH outcomes
  - All GRADE ratings LOW or VERY LOW: confirms the overall
    low quality of the TWFH evidence base — the GRADE finding
    is itself the most important scientific contribution of B21

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Vleeshouwers, J., Fløvik, L., Christensen, J. O., Johannessen, H. A.,
Finne, L. B., Mohr, B., Jørgensen, I. L., & Lunde, L.-K. (2022). The
relationship between telework from home and the psychosocial work
environment: a systematic review. International Archives of
Occupational and Environmental Health, 95, 1687–1706.
https://doi.org/10.1007/s00420-022-01901-4

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B22
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B22
Cite key        : Saurombe2022
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — remote working; WFH; COVID-19
                  pandemic context; South Africa; three provinces;
                  developing economy),
                  T6 (empirical: quantitative; n=102; purposive +
                  snowball sampling; cross-sectional; employee
                  productivity and performance management as DVs;
                  agency theory framework),
                  T7 (developing economy context — South Africa;
                  underprepared organisations; HRM policy gap;
                  isolation; performance management challenges)
Title           : The perceived influence of remote working on specific
                  human resource management outcomes during the
                  COVID-19 pandemic
Authors         : Saurombe, M. D., Rayners, S. S., Mokgobu, K. A., &
                  Manka, K.
Year            : 2022 (received 1 July 2022; accepted 13 September
                  2022; published online 21 November 2022)
Journal         : SA Journal of Human Resource Management
                  (SA Tydskrif vir Menslikehulpbronbestuur; AOSIS)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 20, a2033 (12 pages)
DOI             : [10.4102/sajhrm.v20i0.2033](https://doi.org/10.4102/sajhrm.v20i0.2033)
                  [Confirmed from article metadata; AOSIS open access]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0 (AOSIS)
Affiliations    : Department of Industrial Psychology and People
                  Management, College of Business and Economics,
                  University of Johannesburg, Johannesburg, South
                  Africa (all four authors)
ISSN            : 2071-078X (electronic); 1683-7584 (print)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  purposive + snowball sampling; descriptive statistics
                  and frequency analyses; Cronbach's alpha; correlation
                  matrix
Aim             : To investigate (1) differences between traditional
                  and remote working during COVID-19; (2) perceived
                  influence of WFH on employee productivity; (3)
                  perceived influence of WFH on performance management
                  (PM)
Setting/context : South Africa; three provinces; COVID-19 pandemic
                  context; remote workers aged 18–65; developing
                  economy; organisations largely underprepared for
                  rapid WFH transition
Sample          : n=102 remote workers; purposive sampling with
                  complementary snowball sampling; three South African
                  provinces; ages 18–65; online survey
DVs             : (1) Employee productivity (SMAP sub-construct —
                  perceived productivity in remote setting; social
                  isolation; longer working hours to prove productivity)
                  (2) Performance management (WRT sub-construct —
                  clarity of performance expectations; PM processes
                  not suited to remote work; feedback mechanisms)
IVs/constructs  : Remote working arrangement (WFH vs. traditional);
                  HR policy alignment; digital tools and ICT support;
                  WLB impacts
Theoretical     : Agency theory (Jensen & Meckling, 1976; Eisenhardt,
framework         1989): principal–agent monitoring problem — remote
                  performance harder to observe; behaviour-based vs.
                  outcome-based contracts; information asymmetry
                  between managers (principals) and remote workers
                  (agents); outcome-oriented contracts required when
                  monitoring is infeasible

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
PREFERENCE FOR REMOTE WORK:
  Respondents preferred remote working — more convenient and cost
  efficient (travel cost savings; flexible schedule management;
  reduced stress from long commutes). Net preference for WFH over
  traditional office work.

EMPLOYEE PRODUCTIVITY FINDINGS (mixed):
  POSITIVE productivity perceptions:
  - Remote working may increase productivity through fewer
    workplace interruptions, greater autonomy and schedule
    flexibility
  - Respondents knew what was expected of them even when WFH;
    ability to maintain similar performance levels
  - Cost savings, reduced commuting fatigue positively affect
    daily productivity capacity

  NEGATIVE productivity findings:
  - Social isolation: respondents reported working in isolation,
    which impeded their social life and sense of belonging
  - Longer working hours: respondents worked longer hours than
    in the office specifically TO PROVE productivity to line
    managers and employers
    [FLAG: This is a critical finding — isolation-driven
    overwork as a compensatory behaviour under inadequate
    performance management. Directly relevant to GGHN's
    management systems context]
  - Distraction at home (social media, family, noise)
  - Less teamwork and peer communication → reduced updating
    on organisational events

PERFORMANCE MANAGEMENT FINDINGS (predominantly negative):
  - Respondents felt their organisation's current PM processes
    and HR policies were NOT SUITED for remote working
  - PM challenge: absence of physical presence makes
    behaviour-based monitoring infeasible (agency theory)
  - Outcome-based contracts preferred in theory; but
    organisations had not adapted PM systems accordingly
  - Lack of clear goals and objective-setting for remote workers
  - Inadequate feedback mechanisms between managers and
    remote employees
  - Digital PM tools (dashboards, online task tracking) beginning
    to be adopted but implementation incomplete

HR POLICY IMPLICATIONS:
  - HR professionals should develop policies specifically aligned
    with remote work
  - Organisations need to restructure business operations to
    align work responsibilities with online working environment
  - Performance management systems and HR policies urgently
    need remote-work-specific redesign
  - Hybrid work model anticipated as post-pandemic norm;
    PM systems must evolve accordingly

[PRE-EXTRACTION FLAG — METHODOLOGICAL NOTE:
  The paper does not report specific beta coefficients, t-values,
  or regression output in the sections accessible via web fetch.
  Findings are primarily descriptive/frequency-based with
  Cronbach's α and correlation matrix (Table 13/14 referenced
  but not fully accessible). The evidence base is descriptive
  statistical with Cronbach's alpha confirmations (α values for
  constructs not yet fully accessible; paper reports 12 pages).
  This is an Adequate-quality study — useful for its developing
  economy + PM + remote work combination but methodologically
  limited compared to Block B's stronger papers.]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.3 empirical"), B22
               provides the Sub-Saharan African empirical anchor
               on remote working → employee productivity and
               performance management. It joins B04 (Nigeria; public
               media), B13 (Kenya; public universities), B14 (South
               Africa; qualitative) and B16 (Nigeria; banking) in
               the Africa evidence cluster.

               KEY CITATION: B22 is the only paper in the corpus
               that explicitly investigates PERFORMANCE MANAGEMENT
               (PM) as an outcome variable of remote working — not
               just productivity. This is directly thesis-relevant
               because the thesis OMS construct includes PM systems
               as a core component. The finding that existing PM
               processes are not suited to remote work validates the
               thesis argument: without management systems adaptation
               (OMS redesign for remote/hybrid contexts), hybrid work
               cannot be expected to improve productivity.

               The agency theory framework is directly relevant to
               the thesis: the principal–agent monitoring problem
               (behaviour-based monitoring infeasible in WFH settings;
               outcome-based contracts required) maps onto the thesis
               OMS → hybrid work → productivity mediation argument.
               Management systems must shift from behaviour-based
               to outcome-based monitoring — this IS the OMS
               adaptation the thesis proposes for GGHN.

               The isolation + longer hours finding is a critical
               contextual flag for GGHN: in the absence of adequate
               PM systems, remote workers compensate by overworking
               to prove productivity. This is a management failure,
               not a worker failure — and it validates why management
               systems mediation is needed.

Limitation   : n=102 — very small; purposive + snowball sampling
               (not representative); South Africa only (≠ Nigeria/
               GGHN); no inferential statistics reported in accessible
               sections (primarily descriptive); COVID-19 crisis
               context (not conventional hybrid work); SAJHRM is a
               regional African journal (lower international standing
               than core Block B outlets); 12-page article (short
               format); no mediation analysis.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] SA Journal of Human Resource Management (SAJHRM; AOSIS)
      — SABINET and DOAJ indexed; Scopus listed; reputable
      South African peer-reviewed HRM journal; ISSN 2071-078X
  [+] University of Johannesburg — leading South African
      research university; College of Business and Economics
  [+] South Africa context — most geographically proximate
      Sub-Saharan African study to GGHN's Nigerian context
  [+] Performance management as explicit outcome variable:
      unique in corpus; most directly relevant to thesis OMS
      construct
  [+] Agency theory framework: maps directly onto principal–
      agent monitoring problem in GGHN's hybrid work transition
  [+] Developing economy remote work context — policy vacuum,
      infrastructure limitations, underprepared organisations
  [+] Isolation + overwork finding: critically relevant for
      GGHN field health workforce
  [+] CC BY 4.0 open access (AOSIS)
  [~] n=102 — very small; statistical power limited
  [~] Purposive + snowball sampling — not representative
  [~] Primarily descriptive statistics; no regression or
      mediation analysis reported in accessible sections
  [~] COVID-19 crisis context — crisis-induced rather than
      conventional hybrid work
  [~] South Africa ≠ Nigeria (different labour market,
      regulatory context, organisational culture)
  [-] SAJHRM — regional African journal; lower international
      ranking than most Block B outlets

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Africa evidence cluster (B04 + B13 + B14 + B16 + B22):
  Five Africa-specific papers now form the corpus's Africa cluster:
  B04 (Nigeria; public media; β=0.658), B13 (Kenya; universities;
  β=0.765), B14 (South Africa; qualitative WFH challenges), B16
  (Nigeria; banking; R²=0.753), B22 (South Africa; n=102; PM
  not adapted). Together they establish: (a) positive remote/
  hybrid work → productivity relationships exist in African
  contexts where enabling conditions are present (B04, B13, B16);
  (b) but where management systems (PM, HR policy, digital tools)
  are not adapted, isolation, overwork and performance failure
  result (B14, B22). This Africa-specific conditional pattern is
  the most directly relevant empirical cluster for GGHN.

Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — agency theory:
  B08 found informal control (self-discipline, mutual
  commitment) mediates remote work → performance when behaviour
  monitoring is reduced. B22 frames the same problem via agency
  theory: when behaviour-based monitoring is infeasible in remote
  settings, outcome-based contracts are needed. Both converge:
  the management systems response to remote work is to shift
  from behaviour-based to outcome-based performance management
  — the core OMS adaptation the thesis proposes for GGHN.

Convergence with B15 (Dekker2025) — outcome control:
  B15 found that outcome control (a formal management systems
  component) enables WFH performance by replacing direct
  behavioural supervision. B22 confirms the same need from the
  South African employee perspective: existing PM processes
  (behaviour-based monitoring) do not suit remote work; outcome-
  based PM redesign is required. B15 provides the empirical
  evidence that outcome control works; B22 provides the
  developing-economy employee perspective on why the shift
  is necessary.

Convergence with B21 (Vleeshouwers2022) — professional isolation:
  B21 found professional isolation is the most consistent negative
  TWFH outcome (1 prospective study; consistent across B10's
  5/5 studies). B22 provides South African employee-level
  confirmation: isolation impeded social life and reduced sense
  of belonging. The isolation finding is now confirmed across
  four Block B papers (B10, B14, B21, B22) spanning two SLRs
  and two empirical studies — the most consistent negative
  finding in the corpus.

KEY THESIS CONTRIBUTION — PM SYSTEMS GAP:
  B22 is the only paper in the corpus that explicitly measures
  performance management (PM) as an outcome variable of remote
  working. The finding that existing PM processes are unsuited
  to remote work provides the most direct empirical support for
  the thesis OMS mediation hypothesis: if PM systems do not
  adapt to hybrid work, the management systems (OMS) → hybrid
  work → productivity pathway is broken. GGHN must redesign
  its PM systems alongside adopting hybrid work — not as a
  separate HR exercise, but as the mediating mechanism through
  which hybrid work produces productivity outcomes.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - SA Journal of Human Resource Management (SAJHRM; AOSIS):
    first SAJHRM paper in corpus; first AOSIS publisher in corpus
  - University of Johannesburg: first UJ paper in corpus; leading
    South African research institution
  - Performance management (PM) as explicit DV: first and only
    paper in corpus to measure PM as an outcome of remote working
  - Agency theory: first explicit agency theory application in
    corpus; principal–agent monitoring problem in WFH context
  - Isolation + overwork compensation: first in corpus to document
    remote workers working longer hours specifically to PROVE
    productivity to managers (compensatory overwork phenomenon)
  - South Africa quantitative evidence: first quantitative
    South African empirical paper in corpus (B14 was qualitative)
  - HR policy misalignment: first paper in corpus to explicitly
    find that existing HR policies are unsuited to remote working
    — the policy gap finding

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Saurombe, M. D., Rayners, S. S., Mokgobu, K. A., & Manka, K. (2022).
The perceived influence of remote working on specific human resource
management outcomes during the COVID-19 pandemic. SA Journal of Human
Resource Management, 20, a2033.
https://doi.org/10.4102/sajhrm.v20i0.2033

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C01
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C01
Cite key        : Zhang2025
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — team-level application; ability
                  [team efficacy], motivation [team identification],
                  opportunity [teamwork autonomy] as parallel
                  mediating pathways; empowering leadership → team
                  improvisation),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — empowering
                  leadership as management system component; error
                  aversion culture as boundary condition; team
                  autonomy structures; Chinese internet firms)
Title           : Empowering leadership and team improvisation: A
                  team-level study through the lens of AMO theory
Authors         : Zhang, S., Zhang, J., Xia, B., & Chen, W.
Year            : 2025 (received 10 April 2025; revised 29 September
                  2025; accepted 2 October 2025; available online
                  10 October 2025)
Journal         : Acta Psychologica (Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 260, Article 105717
DOI             : [10.1016/j.actpsy.2025.105717](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.actpsy.2025.105717)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC 4.0
Affiliations    : Zhejiang Gongshang University, Hangzhou, China
                  (Zhang S., Zhang J.); Wenzhou Business College,
                  China (Xia B.); Shaoxing University + Communication
                  University of Zhejiang, China (Chen W.)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; team-level analysis; two-
                  wave survey (T1: empowering leadership, error
                  aversion culture, controls; T2: mediators + DV);
                  path analysis in Mplus 8.3; CFA; bootstrapping
                  (5,000 samples); moderated mediation
Aim             : To examine how empowering leadership facilitates
                  team improvisation via the AMO framework (team
                  efficacy [A], team identification [M], teamwork
                  autonomy [O]); to test error aversion culture as a
                  negative moderator of the AMO pathways
Setting/context : After-sales service, sales, and product development
                  teams in internet companies; southeastern China;
                  2025; VUCA environment; teams requiring rapid
                  improvised responses to unexpected events
Sample          : 89 teams surveyed → 78 valid team questionnaires +
                  330 matched employee questionnaires; effective
                  response rate 82.91%; mean age 31.40 years;
                  58.18% male; 47.27% bachelor's degree (largest
                  group); 83.64% aged 25–45
IV              : Empowering leadership — 12-item scale (Ahearne
                  et al., 2005); α=0.797; ICC(2)=0.710
Mediators       : Team efficacy (A) — 5-item scale (Rego et al.,
                  2013); α=0.723; ICC(2)=0.748
                  Team identification (M) — 4-item scale (Van Der
                  Vegt & Bunderson, 2005); α=0.800; ICC(2)=0.632
                  Teamwork autonomy (O) — 9-item scale (Breaugh,
                  1985); α=0.903; ICC(2)=0.556
Moderator       : Error aversion culture — 7-item scale (Du et al.,
                  2015; adapted Van Dyck et al., 2005); α=0.952;
                  ICC(2)=0.639
DV              : Team improvisation — 7-item scale (Vera & Crossan,
                  2005); α=0.737; ICC(2)=0.715
Controls        : Team tenure; team size; team member familiarity
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Bailey, 1993; Appelbaum, 2000):
frameworks        performance = f(Ability × Motivation × Opportunity)
                  applied at team level; Social Identity Theory
                  (team identification → motivation);
                  Self-Determination Theory (autonomy → opportunity)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Two-wave cross-sectional (T1 → T2 ~1 month apart)
                  to reduce CMB; team-level aggregation justified by
                  Rwg, ICC(1), ICC(2); parallel mediation model
                  (all three AMO mediators simultaneously)
CMB controls    : Two-wave design; unobserved latent CMV factor
                  tested in CFA (7-factor vs. 6-factor model change
                  <0.02 — CMB well-controlled)
CFA fit         : 6-factor model: χ²/df=1.27; CFI=0.957; TLI=0.954;
                  RMSEA=0.029; SRMR=0.051 — excellent fit ✓
                  1-factor model: χ²/df=5.19; CFI=0.326 — poor fit
                  → strong discriminant validity confirmed
Analysis        : Path analysis (Mplus 8.3; ML estimation); mean-
                  centred interaction terms; 95% bias-corrected
                  bootstrapping (5,000 samples); parallel mediation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CORRELATIONS (team level, N=78):
  EL ↔ Team improvisation: r=0.552, p<.001
  EL ↔ Team efficacy: r=0.388, p<.001
  EL ↔ Team identification: r=0.417, p<.001
  EL ↔ Teamwork autonomy: r=0.344, p<.01

H1 — DIRECT EFFECT:
  Empowering leadership → Team improvisation:
    b=0.459, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED

H2 — AMO MEDIATION (parallel; bootstrapped 95% CI):
  H2a — Ability pathway:
    EL → Team efficacy: b=0.423, p<.001
    Team efficacy → TI: b=0.250, p<.01
    Indirect: b=0.106, p<.05; 95% CI [0.020, 0.191] ✓ SUPPORTED

  H2b — Motivation pathway:
    EL → Team identification: b=0.665, p<.001
    Team identification → TI: b=0.102, p<.05
    Indirect: b=0.068, p<.05; 95% CI [0.004, 0.132] ✓ SUPPORTED

  H2c — Opportunity pathway:
    EL → Teamwork autonomy: b=0.485, p<.001
    Teamwork autonomy → TI: b=0.254, p<.001
    Indirect: b=0.123, p<.01; 95% CI [0.037, 0.209] ✓ SUPPORTED
    [STRONGEST of the three AMO indirect pathways]

H3 — MODERATION (error aversion culture × EL):
  H3a: EL × EC → Team efficacy: b=−0.021, p>.05 ✗ NOT SUPPORTED
    [Team efficacy is culturally inert — once established,
     stable cognitive capability resists error aversion pressure]
  H3b: EL × EC → Team identification: b=−0.144, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  H3c: EL × EC → Teamwork autonomy: b=−0.150, p<.01 ✓ SUPPORTED

H4 — MODERATED MEDIATION:
  H4a (via team efficacy): not significant (Difference b=0.010,
    p>.05; CI [−0.052, 0.032]) ✗ NOT SUPPORTED
  H4b (via team identification): Difference b=−0.029, p<.05;
    CI [−0.058, −0.001] ✓ SUPPORTED
    Low EC: b=0.087; High EC: b=0.058 — weakened under high EC
  H4c (via teamwork autonomy): Difference b=−0.076, p<.05;
    CI [−0.144, −0.008] ✓ SUPPORTED
    Low EC: b=0.167; High EC: b=0.091 — substantially weakened

MODEL FIT: R²=0.625 (team improvisation, full model; p<.001)

KEY THEORETICAL FINDING — ASYMMETRIC CULTURAL MODERATION:
  Error aversion culture differentially constrains AMO pathways:
  - Ability (team efficacy): CULTURALLY INERT — skill-based
    competencies, once internalised, exhibit cultural inertia
  - Motivation (team identification) and Opportunity (teamwork
    autonomy): CULTURALLY SENSITIVE — both significantly weakened
    by error aversion culture
  This asymmetry enriches AMO theory: not all three dimensions
  are equally context-dependent.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO Theory"), C01
               is a primary empirical validation of the AMO framework
               as applied to management systems (empowering
               leadership) and performance outcomes (team
               improvisation) at the team level. This provides
               direct support for the thesis's use of AMO theory
               as a theoretical anchor for the management systems →
               employee productivity relationship.

               The parallel mediation structure (A + M + O acting
               in concert) is directly applicable to the thesis
               argument: the OMS and MIS sub-constructs of
               management systems enable employee productivity
               through the three AMO pathways simultaneously —
               not through a single isolated mechanism.

               The opportunity pathway (teamwork autonomy) being
               the STRONGEST indirect effect (b=0.123) maps
               precisely onto the thesis argument that OMS
               (structuring autonomy, flexibility, clear goals)
               is the critical management systems component for
               enabling hybrid work → productivity.

               Error aversion culture as a negative moderator is
               citable for the thesis's GGHN context section:
               where organisational culture penalises mistakes
               (common in hierarchical health sector organisations),
               the motivation and opportunity pathways of management
               systems will be weakened — reinforcing why deliberate
               OMS design is needed to unlock AMO mechanisms.

Limitation   : China-specific (internet firms; southeastern region)
               — cultural and sectoral distance from Nigeria/GGHN
               health NGO; two-wave cross-sectional ≠ longitudinal
               causal; single-industry; self-report measures; team
               improvisation DV is not equivalent to individual
               employee productivity (the thesis DV); small N=78
               teams limits statistical power for moderation.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Acta Psychologica (Elsevier) — Scopus and SSCI indexed;
      established international psychology journal
  [+] Team-level analysis with proper aggregation justification
      (Rwg, ICC(1), ICC(2) all meeting thresholds)
  [+] Two-wave design + unobserved latent CMV factor test —
      CMB well-controlled (6- vs. 7-factor model change <0.02)
  [+] Excellent CFA fit (6-factor: CFI=0.957; RMSEA=0.029)
  [+] Parallel mediation model — all three AMO mediators
      simultaneously tested with mutual statistical control
  [+] Bootstrapping (5,000 samples) for all indirect and
      conditional indirect effects
  [+] Asymmetric moderation finding is theoretically novel:
      ability pathway culturally inert; M and O pathways
      culturally sensitive
  [+] All scales validated and published; well-established
      instruments; α ≥ 0.723 for all constructs
  [~] N=78 teams — modest for moderation analysis
  [~] China internet sector only — limited generalisability
  [~] Two-wave cross-sectional — temporal ordering established
      but not fully longitudinal; causal inference limited
  [~] Self-report on all constructs (mitigated by two-wave
      design and latent CMV test)
  [~] Team improvisation ≠ individual employee productivity
      (thesis DV); conceptual translation needed

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
AMO framework — foundation for Block C:
  C01 establishes the AMO framework empirically at the team level
  as the theoretical lens for the management systems → productivity
  relationship. The parallel mediation structure (A + M + O
  acting in concert) will be the reference point for all
  subsequent Block C papers that apply AMO to HPWS, HR practices,
  and management systems contexts.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #3 (employee
autonomy and agency):
  Block A papers consistently found that employee autonomy and
  agency (OMS components) are critical enablers of productivity.
  C01 confirms at the team level that the opportunity pathway
  (teamwork autonomy; b=0.123; the strongest AMO indirect effect)
  is the most potent mechanism. This is the strongest empirical
  support in the corpus for positioning OMS (autonomy-enabling
  management systems) as the critical mediating component.

Convergence with B08 (Tonnessen2026) — informal/outcome control:
  B08 found informal control (self-discipline, autonomy, mutual
  commitment) mediates remote work → performance. C01 shows
  teamwork autonomy (the opportunity dimension of AMO) is the
  strongest pathway from empowering leadership to improvisation.
  Both converge: structuring employee autonomy through management
  systems is the primary mechanism for performance outcomes.

Error aversion culture and GGHN implications:
  C01's finding that error aversion culture weakens the motivation
  and opportunity pathways has direct GGHN relevance. Health
  sector organisations often exhibit error-averse cultures
  (patient safety focus, regulatory compliance, hierarchical
  authority). The thesis should acknowledge that implementing
  hybrid work management systems (OMS) in GGHN will need to
  actively counteract error-averse tendencies to unlock the
  motivation and opportunity AMO pathways.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Acta Psychologica (Elsevier): first Acta Psychologica paper
    in corpus
  - Team-level AMO analysis: first paper in corpus to apply AMO
    at the team (not individual or organisational) level
  - Empowering leadership: first paper in corpus to position
    empowering leadership as the AMO-activating management
    systems IV
  - Team improvisation: first team improvisation outcome in
    corpus — extends performance DV beyond individual productivity
  - Asymmetric cultural moderation of AMO pathways: first paper
    in corpus to show differential cultural sensitivity across A,
    M, and O dimensions
  - Error aversion culture: first explicit error aversion culture
    measure in corpus as a moderating variable
  - China internet sector team-level: first Chinese internet
    firm context in corpus at team level

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Zhang, S., Zhang, J., Xia, B., & Chen, W. (2025). Empowering
leadership and team improvisation: A team-level study through the
lens of AMO theory. Acta Psychologica, 260, Article 105717.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.actpsy.2025.105717

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C02
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C02
Cite key        : Yang2026
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — configurational application;
                  ability [cognitive style, external search],
                  motivation [harmonious/obsessive passion], opportunity
                  [digital transformation, digital transformational
                  leadership]; fsQCA; radical vs. incremental
                  creativity),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — digital
                  transformation as opportunity dimension; digital
                  transformational leadership as management mechanism;
                  contextual shaping of employee creativity;
                  manufacturing sector China)
Title           : How digital transformation shapes employee
                  creativity: Insights from the
                  ability-motivation-opportunity framework and
                  qualitative comparative analysis
Authors         : Yang, Y., Yuan, L., Yang, S., & Liu, Z.
Year            : 2026 (received 14 July 2025; accepted 16 January
                  2026; available online 20 January 2026)
Journal         : Journal of Innovation & Knowledge (Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 14 (2026), Article 100950
DOI             : [10.1016/j.jik.2026.100950](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jik.2026.100950)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Hunan University, Changsha, China (Yang Y., Yuan L.,
                  Liu Z.); Beijing Jiaotong University, Beijing, China
                  (Yang S.)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; three-wave survey design;
                  fuzzy-set Qualitative Comparative Analysis (fsQCA);
                  CFA (AMOS); configurational analysis; internal
                  calibration; necessity analysis; robustness checks
Aim             : To identify the configurational AMO pathways that
                  lead to high radical and high incremental creativity
                  during digital transformation; to reveal the
                  complementary and substitutive roles of AMO elements
                  across creativity types
Setting/context : Manufacturing enterprises undergoing digital
                  transformation; China; contacted via industry
                  associations and research partnerships; three job
                  types: research & design (33.8%), engineering &
                  operations (45.6%), IT (19.3%)
Sample          : T1: 412 responses (opportunity factors); T2: 367
                  (motivation + ability); T3: 355 (creativity DVs);
                  final matched valid sample: n=305 employees;
                  overall response rate 74.0%; 62% male; 70.5%
                  bachelor's degree; 49.2% aged 31–35
Antecedent      : Ability: (1) External search — formative 8-source
conditions        composite index (breadth × depth); (2) Intuitive
(AMO)             cognitive style — 5-item (α=0.92); (3) Analytical
                  cognitive style — 5-item (α=0.90)
                  Motivation: (4) Harmonious passion — 7-item
                  (α=0.91); (5) Obsessive passion — 7-item (α=0.96)
                  Opportunity: (6) Digital transformation — 5-item
                  (α=0.85); (7) Digital transformational leadership
                  — 6-item (α=0.93)
DVs             : Radical creativity — 3-item (α=0.92; novel,
                  departures from current paradigms)
                  Incremental creativity — 3-item (α=0.89;
                  refinements and progressive enhancements)
Analysis        : fsQCA (R v4.2.0; QCA v3.23 + SetMethods v2.5);
                  raw consistency threshold ≥0.80; PRI consistency
                  ≥0.75; minimum case frequency ≥3; internal
                  calibration (mean = crossover point; ±1 SD =
                  full in/out); 70/30 holdout validation;
                  counterfactual analysis
CFA fit         : 8-factor: χ²/df=1.295; CFI=0.985; TLI=0.983;
                  SRMR=0.031; RMSEA=0.031 — excellent ✓
CMB             : Three-wave design + unobserved latent CMV factor
                  (ΔCFI<0.001; ΔRMSEA<0.002) — CMB well-controlled
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Bailey 1993; Appelbaum 2000; Blumberg
frameworks        & Pringle 1982); Componential Theory of Creativity
                  (Amabile 2011); Dualistic Model of Work Passion
                  (Vallerand et al. 2003); JD-R Model (digital
                  transformation as resource + demand); Self-
                  Determination Theory (Deci & Ryan 2000)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
NECESSITY ANALYSIS:
  No single antecedent meets the 0.9 consistency threshold —
  no individual factor is necessary alone for either creativity
  type. Configurational analysis required.

CONFIGURATIONS FOR HIGH RADICAL CREATIVITY (3 pathways;
  overall consistency=0.911; overall coverage=0.281):

R1 — "Strategic traction-passion driven":
  Core: High digital transformation (O) + High harmonious passion (M)
  Peripheral absence: Low intuitive style; Low external search;
    Low obsessive passion
  Consistency=0.948; Raw coverage=0.212; Unique coverage=0.043
  Mechanism: DT provides strategic clarity and digital resources;
    harmonious passion drives intrinsic motivation for radical
    exploration. JD-R resources pathway.

R2 — "Leadership incentive-passion focused":
  Core: High digital transformational leadership (O) + High
    harmonious passion (M)
  Consistency=0.941; Raw coverage=0.185; Unique coverage=0.037
  Mechanism: DTL compensates for low DT maturity by communicating
    vision and tolerating failure; harmonious passion drives
    voluntary engagement. SDT pathway. Suited to early DT stages.

R3 — "Dual ability engine-autonomous breakthrough":
  Core: High intuitive cognitive style (A) + High harmonious
    passion (M) + High external search (A)
  Consistency=0.925; Raw coverage=0.096; Unique coverage=0.033
  Mechanism: Self-driven; no external opportunity required;
    ability × motivation synergy enables radical creativity
    even under organisational constraints. Componential theory.

KEY FINDING — RADICAL CREATIVITY:
  Harmonious passion is a common core condition across ALL three
  radical creativity configurations. Digital transformation and
  DTL are SUBSTITUTIVE antecedents (either is sufficient; neither
  is necessary). Motivation + ability exhibit COMPLEMENTARITY.

CONFIGURATIONS FOR HIGH INCREMENTAL CREATIVITY (3 pathways;
  overall consistency=0.922; overall coverage=0.355):

J1 — "Strategic pressure-constrained autonomy":
  Core: High digital transformation (O) + High obsessive passion (M)
    + Low digital transformational leadership (O — ABSENT)
  Consistency=0.914; Raw coverage=0.293; Unique coverage=0.075
  Mechanism: DT creates performance pressure; obsessive passion
    drives execution efficiency; LOW DTL (core absence) maintains
    task pressure and control-oriented focus. JD-R demands pathway.
  [FLAG: Low DTL as CORE CONDITION for incremental creativity —
   "double-edged sword" effect of digital transformational
   leadership confirmed]

J2 — "Strategic driven-analytical iteration":
  Core: High digital transformation (O) + High analytical
    cognitive style (A)
  Consistency=0.900; Raw coverage=0.280; Unique coverage=0.035
  Mechanism: DT decomposes macro-goals into executable incremental
    tasks; analytical style enables systematic risk-controlled
    optimisation. Suited to high digital maturity stable contexts.

J3 — "Analytical anchoring-benchmark optimization":
  Core: High analytical cognitive style (A) + High obsessive
    passion (M) + High external search (A)
  Consistency=0.908; Raw coverage=0.157; Unique coverage=0.040
  Mechanism: Individual-level endogenous synergy; external
    benchmarks via search; obsessive passion drives pursuit of
    recognition. Works even under limited external opportunities.

KEY FINDING — INCREMENTAL CREATIVITY:
  Obsessive passion is an important condition across ALL three
  incremental creativity configurations (vs. harmonious passion
  for radical). ABILITY DIVERGENCE: intuitive style → radical;
  analytical style → incremental. OPPORTUNITY COMPLEMENTARITY:
  radical needs open-type opportunities; incremental needs
  constraint-type opportunities.

DOUBLE-EDGED SWORD FINDING (directly thesis-relevant):
  DTL promotes radical creativity (R2) but can INHIBIT incremental
  creativity (J1 where low DTL is a core condition). This
  confirms that leadership as a management systems component must
  be context-sensitively deployed depending on the type of
  performance outcome sought.

ROBUSTNESS:
  Holdout validation (70/30): RC consistency=0.951; IC
  consistency=0.970. Consistent across threshold adjustments
  (case frequency 2/3/4; raw consistency 0.80/0.85; PRI 0.75/0.80)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO Theory"), C02
               extends the AMO framework beyond linear effects to
               demonstrate configurational, non-linear synergies.
               Three key contributions for the thesis:

               (1) AMO configurational lens: The thesis applies AMO
               to management systems → employee productivity. C02
               demonstrates that AMO elements act in concert (not
               independently) — no single factor is sufficient or
               necessary alone. This is precisely the argument the
               thesis makes: management systems (OMS + MIS) must
               provide all three AMO dimensions simultaneously to
               enable productivity in GGHN's hybrid work context.

               (2) Opportunity dimension = management systems:
               C02 operationalises digital transformation and DTL
               as the OPPORTUNITY dimension of AMO. In the thesis,
               the OMS and MIS sub-constructs map onto this
               opportunity dimension. C02 confirms that opportunity
               (management systems) enables the ability and
               motivation dimensions to produce performance outcomes.

               (3) Double-edged sword / context-sensitivity:
               C02's finding that DTL promotes radical but inhibits
               incremental creativity is citable for the thesis
               recommendation that GGHN should differentiate its
               management systems design based on the type of
               performance outcome sought (e.g., innovation vs.
               operational efficiency).

Limitation   : China manufacturing only — distance from Nigeria/
               GGHN NGO health context; self-report DVs; fsQCA
               coverage is moderate (0.281; 0.355) — alternative
               configurations exist; three-wave design is not fully
               longitudinal; creativity ≠ employee productivity
               (thesis DV) — conceptual translation required.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Journal of Innovation & Knowledge (Elsevier; JIK) — SSCI
      and Scopus indexed; ABS 2; reputable innovation management
      outlet; 2026 publication
  [+] Three-wave survey design — strongest available CMB control
      in Block C; constructs measured across waves
  [+] fsQCA — configurational method captures non-linear synergies
      impossible to detect with linear regression; appropriate for
      AMO theory
  [+] Excellent CFA fit (8-factor: CFI=0.985; RMSEA=0.031)
  [+] All CR>0.70; AVE>0.50; HTMT<0.85 — strong reliability
      and validity
  [+] Comprehensive robustness checks (threshold sensitivity,
      holdout validation, counterfactual analysis)
  [+] Distinguishes radical vs. incremental creativity — rare;
      allows differential AMO pathway identification
  [+] Equifinality principle demonstrated — multiple sufficient
      pathways for each creativity type; theoretically important
  [~] China manufacturing only — sector and cultural specificity
  [~] Moderate fsQCA coverage (0.281/0.355) — other sufficient
      pathways exist
  [~] Creativity DVs ≠ employee productivity (thesis DV)
  [~] External search as formative composite — not reflective;
      excluded from CFA; measurement approach differs from other
      constructs

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C01 (Zhang2025) — AMO parallel activation:
  C01 demonstrated that AMO mediators operate in concert at the
  team level (parallel mediation). C02 extends this to confirm at
  the individual level that no single AMO element is necessary
  alone and that multiple configurations of A+M+O produce the
  same outcome (equifinality). Together, C01 and C02 provide
  converging evidence that AMO elements are synergistic, not
  additive — directly supporting the thesis mediation model where
  OMS/MIS enable all three dimensions simultaneously.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #1 (ICT dominant):
  C02 positions digital transformation and DTL as the OPPORTUNITY
  dimension of AMO — management systems that create contextual
  conditions for employee performance. This maps directly onto
  Block A's finding that technology/ICT is the dominant management
  systems predictor. Both confirm that technology-enabled
  management systems (the opportunity dimension) are the critical
  enabling context for employee performance outcomes.

Double-edged sword — leadership context-sensitivity:
  C02's finding that DTL promotes radical creativity but inhibits
  incremental creativity is directly relevant to GGHN's
  management systems design. GGHN's management systems must
  balance transformational (innovation-enabling) and transactional
  (execution-focused) management components depending on whether
  the objective is innovation or operational productivity. This
  nuances the thesis OMS construct: it is not uniformly positive —
  management systems design must be outcome-sensitive.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Journal of Innovation & Knowledge (JIK; Elsevier): first JIK
    paper in corpus
  - fsQCA (fuzzy-set Qualitative Comparative Analysis): first
    configurational method in corpus; complements linear methods
  - Equifinality principle: first explicit demonstration in corpus
    that multiple AMO configurations produce the same outcome
  - Radical vs. incremental creativity distinction: first paper
    in corpus to apply AMO framework differentially to creativity
    types; extends AMO beyond undifferentiated performance
  - Harmonious passion as core condition: first in corpus to
    identify work passion type as a necessary component of radical
    creativity configurations
  - Digital transformational leadership double-edged sword: first
    in corpus to formally demonstrate that DTL inhibits
    incremental creativity while promoting radical creativity
  - Three-wave survey design: first three-wave study in corpus;
    strongest available CMB control approach

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Yang, Y., Yuan, L., Yang, S., & Liu, Z. (2026). How digital
transformation shapes employee creativity: Insights from the
ability-motivation-opportunity framework and qualitative comparative
analysis. Journal of Innovation & Knowledge, 14, Article 100950.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jik.2026.100950

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C03
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C03
Cite key        : Jakpa2025
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — SHRM → employee well-being and
                  organisational change via AMO and RBV frameworks;
                  Nigeria; public and private sector organisations),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — SHRM as
                  integrated HR system; talent acquisition, training,
                  performance management, compensation, participative
                  decision-making; strategic alignment of HR practices),
                  T7 (developing economy context — Nigeria; public and
                  private sector; organisational change capacity;
                  West African institutional environment)
Title           : Strategic Human Resource Management and Employee
                  Well-being in Times of Organizational Change:
                  Nigerian Insights
Authors         : Jakpa, G. U., & Okagbare, E. A.
Year            : 2025 (received 24 August 2025; revised 17 October
                  2025; accepted 28 November 2025)
Journal         : NG Journal of Social Development (AJOL)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 18, Issue 2 (2025), Article 1
DOI             : [10.4314/ngjsd.v18i2.1](https://doi.org/10.4314/ngjsd.v18i2.1)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
                  [NOTE: CSV lists year as 2024; journal header
                  and DOI confirm Vol. 18, Issue 2, 2025]
Open access     : Yes (AJOL open access)
Affiliations    : Department of Office and Information Management,
                  Faculty of Administration and Management, Southern
                  Delta University Ozoro, Nigeria (both authors)
ISSN            : 0189-5958 (print); 2814-1105 (electronic)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  multiple regression (OLS); SPSS v.28; Cronbach's α;
                  ANOVA F-test; convenience/stratified random sampling
Aim             : (1) To examine the impact of SHRM practices on
                  employee well-being in Nigerian organisations
                  undergoing organisational change; (2) to examine the
                  relationship between SHRM and organisational change
                  capacity in the Nigerian context
Setting/context : Nigeria; public and private sector organisations;
                  diverse sectors; 2025 data collection; VUCA
                  operational landscape
Sample          : Target population: 300 employees and management
                  staff; Cochran's formula → sample size n=150;
                  stratified random sampling; cross-sectional
IVs             : Strategic Human Resource Management (SHRM) —
                  10-item scale (α=0.83 for H1; α=0.92 for H2);
                  5-point Likert scale; components: training and
                  development, performance management, compensation
                  and rewards, participative decision-making
DVs             : (1) Employee Well-being (H1) — psychological,
                  physical, social dimensions (Grant et al., 2007;
                  Warr, 1987); operationalised via commitment,
                  exhaustion, workplace relations
                  (2) Organisational Change (H2) — capacity for
                  institutional adaptation; change readiness
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000): SHRM →
frameworks        ability (training/recruitment), motivation
                  (compensation/performance management), opportunity
                  (participative decision-making/role design)
                  Resource-Based View (Barney, 1991): human capital
                  as VRIN asset; SHRM as strategic investment to
                  preserve human capital value during change

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Cross-sectional survey; structured questionnaire;
                  5-point Likert scale; SPSS v.28
Analysis        : Multiple regression (OLS); ANOVA; Cronbach's α
CMB             : Not tested; single-source cross-sectional data —
                  CMB risk uncontrolled
                  [FLAG: No CMB test; Harman's test not reported;
                  single instrument]
Reliability     : H1: Cronbach's α=0.83 (SHRM + well-being, 10 items)
                  H2: Cronbach's α=0.92 (SHRM + org. change, 10 items)
                  [FLAG: Combined α for IV+DV items together is
                  methodologically unusual — suggests items for IV
                  and DV may have been measured on the same scale,
                  inflating reliability and potentially conflating
                  constructs]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1 — SHRM → Employee Well-being:
  R=0.781; R²=0.610; Adj. R²=0.606
  F(1,148)=149.63, p<.001 ✓
  B=0.642 (unstandardised); β=0.781; t=12.23; p<.001
  95% CI [0.540, 0.744]
  H1 SUPPORTED: SHRM is a significant positive predictor of
  employee well-being (explains 61% of variance)

H2 — SHRM → Organisational Change:
  R=0.76; R²=0.58; Adj. R²=0.56
  F(1,147)=92.34, p<.001 ✓ [SEE FLAG BELOW]
  B=0.643 (unstandardised); β=0.68; t=9.05; p<.001
  95% CI [0.503, 0.783]
  H2 SUPPORTED: SHRM is a significant positive predictor of
  organisational change capacity (explains 58% of variance)

[FLAG — DEGREES OF FREEDOM ERROR IN H2:
  The ANOVA table for H2 shows Regression df=2 (Sum of Squares
  32.450; df=2; Mean Square=16.225) with only one predictor
  (SHRM). A simple OLS regression with one IV should have
  Regression df=1. This is an internal inconsistency in the
  reported results — either a typographical error or an
  indication that an unreported second predictor was included.
  The F-value (92.34) and significance (p<.001) remain consistent
  with a strong relationship but the df error cannot be
  independently resolved from the paper. Must cite H2 results
  with explicit caution.]

[FLAG — HIGH R² VALUES:
  R²=0.61 and 0.58 are very high for a 150-person cross-
  sectional convenience sample measuring perceptual variables via
  self-report. These values likely reflect common method bias
  (single-source, single-occasion design) inflating correlations
  between IV and DV measured on the same instrument. Interpret
  with significant caution.]

[FLAG — COMBINED α:
  Reporting a single Cronbach's α for "Strategic HRM and
  Employee Well-being" (10 items combined) rather than separate
  α values for IV and DV suggests the scale may not maintain
  construct separation between SHRM and well-being. This is an
  atypical reliability reporting approach that may indicate
  construct conflation.]

DISCUSSION SYNTHESIS:
  Authors interpret findings via AMO and RBV frameworks:
  - AMO: SHRM enhances employee ability (training), motivation
    (compensation, performance management) and opportunity
    (participative decision-making) → improved psychological
    states → well-being
  - RBV: During organisational change, employees experiencing
    stress and disengagement represent devaluation of human
    capital (VRIN erosion); SHRM preserves VRIN attributes
  - Nigerian context: Cultural factors (paternalism, Ubuntu
    philosophy, institutional hurdles) modulate these dynamics;
    SHRM must be adapted to local conditions

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO Nigeria"), C03
               is the only paper in Block C that provides Nigeria-
               specific empirical evidence on the AMO framework
               applied to SHRM and employee well-being. This is
               directly relevant to the thesis on two levels:

               (1) AMO + Nigeria = closest contextual match to GGHN:
               C03 applies AMO theory in Nigerian public and private
               sector organisations — the same national context as
               GGHN. The finding that SHRM (β=0.781) significantly
               predicts employee well-being in Nigeria provides
               direct support for the thesis argument that management
               systems (OMS) operating through AMO mechanisms can
               improve employee outcomes in the Nigerian context.

               (2) SHRM → organisational change capacity (β=0.68):
               This second finding is directly relevant to GGHN's
               hybrid work transition. If SHRM facilitates
               organisational change capacity, then investing in
               management systems is a prerequisite for GGHN's
               successful hybrid work adoption. This maps onto the
               thesis mediation argument: management systems must
               first enable the organisation's capacity for change
               before hybrid work arrangements can produce
               productivity improvements.

               KEY CITATION: "In a Nigerian multi-sector study,
               Jakpa and Okagbare (2025) found that strategic HRM
               significantly predicted both employee well-being
               (β=0.781, R²=0.61, p<.001) and organisational change
               capacity (β=0.68, R²=0.58, p<.001) in a sample of
               150 employees across public and private sector
               organisations, providing Nigeria-specific evidence
               that integrated management systems operating through
               AMO mechanisms produce positive employee and
               organisational outcomes."

               Cite with explicit caution flags (see below).

Limitation   : n=150 — small; cross-sectional; single-source CMB
               uncontrolled; combined α for IV+DV unusual; df error
               in H2 ANOVA table; R² values unusually high
               (probable CMB inflation); convenience + stratified
               sample from undefined organisations; Southern Delta
               University Ozoro — lower research infrastructure;
               NGJSD is a regional AJOL journal at lower
               international ranking; "Nigeria Insights" section is
               descriptive geography rather than analytical
               contextualisation; no mediation analysis.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Nigeria-specific empirical evidence: only paper in corpus
      to apply AMO + SHRM + well-being framework in Nigerian
      public/private sector context
  [+] AMO and RBV dual theoretical framework — both are thesis-
      relevant; explicit mapping of SHRM components to A, M, O
  [+] Two DVs (employee well-being + organisational change) —
      both directly relevant to thesis
  [+] Strong statistical significance for both hypotheses
      (p<.001 for both models)
  [+] Multi-dimensional well-being operationalisation (Grant
      et al. 2007): psychological, physical, social dimensions
  [+] AJOL open access; peer-reviewed; Southern Delta University,
      Nigeria; relevant developing economy institutional context
  [~] n=150 — modest; limits statistical power and
      generalisability
  [~] Cross-sectional; CMB uncontrolled; single-source
  [~] R²=0.61 and 0.58 — suspiciously high for cross-sectional
      self-report; probable CMB inflation
  [~] Combined Cronbach's α for IV+DV items — methodologically
      atypical; possible construct conflation
  [~] H2 ANOVA df error (df=2 with one predictor) — internal
      inconsistency; cannot be resolved from paper
  [-] NGJSD — regional AJOL journal; lower international
      indexing than core Block C outlets
  [-] "Nigeria Insights" section: geographical description
      rather than substantive contextualisation of findings

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with Block A (A01–A17) — SHRM → productivity:
  Block A papers consistently found that management systems
  (MIS + OMS components) predict employee productivity and
  performance. C03 extends this to well-being and organisational
  change capacity within the Nigerian context. The finding that
  SHRM explains 61% of variance in employee well-being (β=0.781)
  is broadly consistent with Block A's pattern of strong SHRM →
  outcome relationships, though the high R² must be interpreted
  with caution given probable CMB inflation.

Convergence with C01 (Zhang2025) and C02 (Yang2026) — AMO:
  C03 applies AMO theory to SHRM → well-being, explicitly mapping
  HR practices onto A (training), M (compensation, PM), and O
  (participative decision-making). This is the Nigerian empirical
  confirmation of the AMO mechanisms demonstrated in C01 (team
  level; China) and C02 (configurational; China manufacturing).
  C03 confirms that the AMO pathway from management systems to
  employee outcomes operates in an African developing economy
  context, directly closing the contextual gap between the
  Chinese studies and the GGHN thesis context.

Convergence with B22 (Saurombe2022) — South Africa + HR policy:
  B22 found that PM processes are unsuited to remote working in
  South Africa; C03 finds that SHRM (including PM) significantly
  predicts well-being and change capacity in Nigeria. Together they
  establish the Sub-Saharan Africa pattern: where SHRM/PM systems
  ARE properly designed and aligned (C03), well-being improves;
  where they are NOT adapted to new working arrangements (B22),
  worker isolation and compensatory overwork result. This dual
  Africa evidence cluster directly supports the thesis mediation
  argument for GGHN.

NEW CONTRIBUTION — ORGANISATIONAL CHANGE CAPACITY:
  C03 is the only paper in the corpus that explicitly treats
  organisational change capacity as an outcome variable predicted
  by SHRM. This is directly relevant to GGHN's hybrid work
  transition: before hybrid work can be mediated by management
  systems, the organisation must have capacity for change — and
  C03 shows SHRM (β=0.68) builds that capacity. This is the
  missing link in the thesis model: management systems → change
  capacity → hybrid work adoption → productivity.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - NG Journal of Social Development (NGJSD; AJOL): first NGJSD
    paper in corpus; first Southern Delta University paper
  - Nigeria AMO empirical: first paper in corpus to apply AMO
    theory empirically within a Nigerian organisational context
  - SHRM as IV (not as outcome): first paper in corpus to
    explicitly operationalise SHRM components (training, PM,
    compensation, participative decision-making) as IV mapped
    onto AMO dimensions in Nigeria
  - Organisational change capacity as DV: first paper in corpus
    to treat organisational change capacity as an outcome variable
    predicted by management systems
  - RBV + AMO dual framework: first paper in corpus to
    explicitly combine RBV (human capital as VRIN asset) with AMO
    in a developing economy SHRM study
  - Ubuntu philosophy: first mention in corpus of Ubuntu as a
    cultural moderator of SHRM effectiveness in African contexts

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Jakpa, G. U., & Okagbare, E. A. (2025). Strategic human resource
management and employee well-being in times of organizational change:
Nigerian insights. NG Journal of Social Development, 18(2), Article 1.
https://doi.org/10.4314/ngjsd.v18i2.1
