EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C04
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C04
Cite key        : Omolo2026
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — green HRM operationalised via AMO:
                  green ability [training/recruitment], green
                  motivation [eco-rewards/appraisal], green opportunity
                  [participative decision-making/eco-innovation]; SET
                  and SDT as complementary psychological mechanisms),
                  T4 (systematic literature review — narrative SLR;
                  Scopus, WoS, Google Scholar; 2015–2026; MMAT
                  quality checklist; thematic narrative synthesis),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — green
                  psychological climate as mediator; green performance
                  paradox; green fatigue; OCBE; GWE)
Title           : Theoretical foundations and empirical evidence: A
                  systematic review of employee-level responses to
                  green human resource management (HRM) practices
Authors         : Omolo, J. W.
Year            : 2026 (publication date per journal header)
Journal         : African Journal of Empirical Research (AJER; AJOL)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 7, No. 2, pp. 192–199
DOI             : [10.51867/ajernet.7.2.16](https://doi.org/10.51867/ajernet.7.2.16)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes (AJOL open access)
Affiliations    : Rongo University, Kenya
ORCID           : 0009-0002-6250-5787

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Narrative Systematic Literature Review (SLR);
                  thematic narrative synthesis; MMAT quality checklist
                  [FLAG: Not PRISMA-registered; no PROSPERO ID; no
                  explicit sample count of included studies; brief
                  methodology section — lighter than PRISMA-compliant
                  SLRs in corpus]
Aim             : (1) To review theoretical links between green
                  workplace practices and individual performance;
                  (2) to examine how green HR practices affect staff
                  and identify missing/inconsistent data in the field
Databases       : Scopus, Web of Science, Google Scholar (primary);
                  additional repositories not specified
Time window     : 2015–2026 (11-year span)
Quality         : Mixed Methods Appraisal Tool (MMAT)
Synthesis       : Thematic narrative synthesis — no meta-analysis
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000; Renwick et al.,
frameworks        2013): green ability, green motivation, green
                  opportunity
                  Social Exchange Theory (Blau, 1964; Paillé et al.,
                  2014): norm of reciprocity → OCBE
                  Self-Determination Theory (Deci & Ryan, 2000):
                  autonomy, competence, relatedness → intrinsic
                  green motivation → GWE
                  Natural Resource-Based View (Hart, 1995): green
                  human capital as VRIN strategic asset

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. AMO OPERATIONALISATION OF GREEN HRM:
   - Green Ability: selective recruitment + green training →
     technical eco-literacy; directly predicts in-role
     task proficiency
   - Green Motivation: eco-centred rewards + appraisal →
     aligns personal goals with sustainability targets
   - Green Opportunity: participatory decision-making +
     eco-innovation circles → enables discretionary effort
   Together these transform sustainability from a secondary
   mandate into a core driver of high-performance output

2. GREEN PSYCHOLOGICAL CLIMATE AS MEDIATOR:
   Critical boundary condition between GHRM and outcomes.
   When employees perceive green practices as AUTHENTIC:
   - Norm of reciprocity (SET) → OCBE + discretionary effort
   - Psychological needs satisfied (SDT: autonomy + relatedness)
     → Green Work Engagement (GWE) + meaningful work
   When employees perceive GREENWASHING:
   - Green Cynicism emerges → trust destroyed → SET benefits
     neutralised → cynicism and disengagement

3. GREEN PERFORMANCE PARADOX:
   Intensive environmental monitoring + complex reporting →
   green fatigue + cognitive overload → diminishing returns
   on task performance. Key finding: sustainability management
   systems have a tipping point beyond which they impair
   rather than enable employee productivity — directly
   relevant to thesis OMS design for GGHN.

4. GEOGRAPHICAL AND SECTORAL GAPS IDENTIFIED:
   - Literature heavily skewed toward manufacturing in
     developed/emerging Asian economies
   - Public and service sectors in developing economies
     (especially Kenya and Sub-Saharan Africa) severely
     under-researched
   - Longitudinal data largely absent
   - In developing economies, external regulatory factors
     (government directives, institutional compliance)
     play a MORE dominant role than intrinsic motivation
     (contradicting Western-centric models)

5. GREEN HUMAN CAPITAL AS VRIN ASSET (NRBV):
   When AMO + SET + SDT mechanisms are integrated:
   → green habitus (internalised eco-values) → VRIN
   → competitive advantage + institutional resilience
   This positions GHRM as a strategic management systems
   investment, not merely an administrative function

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO+SET SLR"), C04
               provides the most comprehensive available synthesis
               of AMO + SET applied to HR management systems and
               individual performance outcomes. Three thesis-
               relevant contributions:

               (1) AMO + SET combined: C04 is the only SLR in the
               corpus that explicitly integrates AMO, SET, and SDT
               within a single synthesis on HR systems → individual
               outcomes. The thesis uses AMO as its primary
               theoretical framework and SET as the psychological
               mechanism; C04 validates this integration.

               (2) Green performance paradox → OMS design caution:
               The finding that intensive monitoring can trigger
               green fatigue is directly analogous to the thesis
               risk: if GGHN's hybrid work management systems (OMS)
               are overly surveillance-oriented, they risk harming
               the very productivity they are designed to enable.
               This validates the thesis recommendation for
               outcome-based rather than behaviour-based OMS.

               (3) Developing economy regulatory context: The
               finding that external regulatory factors dominate
               intrinsic motivation in developing economies is
               directly relevant to GGHN's Nigerian public health
               NGO context. The thesis should acknowledge that
               GGHN's employees may respond more to institutional
               regulatory signals than to intrinsic motivation
               — shaping how management systems should be designed.

Limitation   : Not PRISMA-registered; no explicit sample count of
               included studies; brief methodology; Google Scholar
               included (lowers selectivity); AJER is a regional
               AJOL journal; green HRM focus is contextually
               peripheral to the thesis (management systems + hybrid
               work + productivity) — must be translated carefully;
               single-author review; no inter-rater reliability
               reported for MMAT assessments.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] AMO + SET + SDT + NRBV — most theoretically comprehensive
      multi-framework synthesis in corpus on HR systems →
      individual outcomes
  [+] Green performance paradox — novel and directly thesis-
      relevant finding on management system overload risk
  [+] East Africa / developing economy focus — aligns with
      GGHN's geographical and institutional context; Kenya
      as reference context (Rongo University)
  [+] 2015–2026 coverage — most recent synthesis window in
      corpus; captures 2024–2026 evidence
  [+] MMAT quality appraisal applied
  [+] AJOL open access; peer-reviewed
  [~] Not PRISMA-registered; no PROSPERO ID
  [~] No explicit count of included studies
  [~] Brief methodology section — replicability limited
  [~] Google Scholar included — reduces selectivity
  [~] Single-author review — no inter-rater reliability
  [~] Green HRM focus is contextually adjacent but not
      identical to thesis (management systems + hybrid work)
  [-] AJER (African Journal of Empirical Research) — regional
      AJOL journal; lower international indexing than core
      corpus outlets

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C01, C02, C03 — AMO framework:
  C04 extends the AMO synthesis established by C01 (team level),
  C02 (configurational), and C03 (Nigeria SHRM) by providing
  SLR-level integration of AMO with SET and SDT in the context
  of green HRM. The three-framework synthesis confirms that
  management systems → employee outcomes requires not only the
  structural enablement of AMO (ability, motivation, opportunity)
  but also the psychological reciprocity of SET and the
  autonomous motivation of SDT. This enriches the thesis
  theoretical framework for Chapter 2.2.

Convergence with B18 (Broccardo2025) — OMS design risk:
  B18's green performance paradox (monitoring-induced fatigue)
  directly mirrors B18's finding that excessive OMS monitoring
  can undermine employee well-being and productivity. Both
  confirm: management systems must balance accountability with
  autonomy. This is the thesis OMS design principle for GGHN.

Convergence with B21 (Vleeshouwers2022) — autonomy as mediator:
  B21 found autonomy and flexibility as key mediating variables
  for TWFH outcomes. C04 confirms at the SLR level that
  autonomy (SDT: self-determination) is the critical
  psychological mechanism through which HR management systems
  produce sustainable performance outcomes. Both converge:
  OMS must enable autonomy, not constrain it.

Convergence with B22 (Saurombe2022) and B14 (Enaifoghe2023) —
  developing economy context:
  C04 explicitly identifies that in developing economies,
  external regulatory factors dominate intrinsic motivation —
  consistent with B22 (South Africa: PM systems unsuited) and
  B14 (South Africa: qualitative WFH challenges). All three
  converge: developing economy management contexts require
  institutionally anchored rather than purely intrinsic
  management systems design.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - African Journal of Empirical Research (AJER; AJOL): first
    AJER paper in corpus; Rongo University, Kenya
  - Green HRM (GHRM): first explicit GHRM paper in corpus;
    extends management systems framing into environmental HRM
  - Green Work Engagement (GWE): first GWE concept in corpus
  - Green performance paradox: first explicit paradox of
    management system overload → performance impairment in corpus
  - AMO + SET + SDT combined: first tri-framework integration
    in a single SLR in corpus
  - Natural Resource-Based View (NRBV): first NRBV application
    in corpus; extends RBV (C03) to environmental dimension
  - Green human capital (VRIN): first VRIN framing of green
    HR outcomes in corpus
  - Kenya / East Africa SLR: first Kenya-specific SLR in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Omolo, J. W. (2026). Theoretical foundations and empirical evidence:
A systematic review of employee-level responses to green human resource
management (HRM) practices. African Journal of Empirical Research,
7(2), 192–199. https://doi.org/10.51867/ajernet.7.2.16

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C05
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C05
Cite key        : Stirpe2022
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HRPs as resource-delivery
                  mechanisms mapped onto AMO: training/development →
                  ability; performance management/compensation →
                  motivation; autonomy/participation → opportunity;
                  satisfaction with HRPs as evaluation of resource
                  quality),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HR practices
                  as management systems components; satisfaction with
                  HRPs as employee-level evaluation of OMS quality;
                  JD-R model applied to HR causal chain),
                  T5 (productivity enablers — engagement as mediator
                  of HRP satisfaction → extra-role performance; health
                  as moderator; in-role vs. extra-role performance
                  differentiation)
Title           : Satisfaction with HR practices and employee
                  performance: A moderated mediation model of
                  engagement and health
Authors         : Stirpe, L., Profili, S., & Sammarra, A.
Year            : 2022 (accepted 1 June 2021; available online
                  7 June 2021; print 2022)
Journal         : European Management Journal (Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 40 (2022), pp. 295–307
DOI             : [10.1016/j.emj.2021.06.003](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.emj.2021.06.003)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Universidad Carlos III de Madrid, Spain (Stirpe);
                  Università Europea di Roma, Italy (Profili);
                  Università degli Studi dell'Aquila, Italy (Sammarra)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional field study
                  (with partial temporal separation for in-role
                  performance); OLS regression; bootstrapped
                  mediation (PROCESS SPSS macro; 5,000 samples);
                  first-stage moderated mediation; EFA + CFA
Aim             : To examine (1) whether satisfaction with HRPs
                  affects in-role and extra-role performance; (2) how
                  engagement mediates these relationships; (3) when
                  employee health moderates the satisfaction with
                  HRPs-engagement link and, thus, performance
Setting/context : Major pharmaceutical multinational enterprise;
                  Italy; 2017; 430 full-time white-collar employees;
                  diverse occupational roles (administration, R&D,
                  operations, commercial)
Sample          : Census survey (430 employees); n=274 retained after
                  missing values; response rate 84.6%; mean age 47.2;
                  55% male; single organisation
IV              : Satisfaction with HRPs — 5-item additive index
                  (α from EFA; Conway & Monks, 2008; Kinnie et al.,
                  2005): autonomy, compensation, performance
                  management, training (beyond-job), training
                  (current job)
Mediator        : Work engagement — 9-item scale (Schaufeli et al.,
                  2006); three sub-scales (vigour, dedication,
                  absorption); used as overall composite
Moderator       : Employee health — single-item (European Social
                  Survey); 5-point scale; physical + mental health
DVs             : In-role performance — supervisor appraisal score
                  (company records; 10-point scale; collected 3-5
                  months after survey); log-transformed
                  Extra-role performance — 6-item scale (Williams &
                  Anderson, 1991); informal productive actions toward
                  co-workers and organisation
Controls        : 18 business unit dummies; 3 occupational category
                  dummies; tenure; salary grade; family burden; age;
                  gender
Theoretical     : JD-R model (Schaufeli & Bakker, 2004): HRPs as
frameworks        resource-delivery mechanisms; satisfaction reflects
                  resource quality; engagement as motivational
                  process; health as personal resource
                  AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000): training →
                  ability; PM/compensation → motivation; autonomy →
                  opportunity — explicitly mapped onto HRP components

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CFA fit         : 3-factor model: χ²=275.37; df=164; RMSEA=0.047;
                  CFI=0.92; TLI=0.91; SRMR=0.056 — acceptable ✓
                  Superior to all alternative (1-, 2-factor) models
CMB             : Items in different sections of 17-page
                  questionnaire; CFA comparison of factor models;
                  in-role performance from separate company records
                  (partial temporal separation) — CMB partially
                  mitigated
Mediation       : Baron & Kenny (1986) + PROCESS bootstrapping
                  (5,000 samples; 95% CI)
Moderated       : PROCESS first-stage moderated mediation (Hayes,
mediation         2018); variables standardised; conditional
                  indirect effects at ±1 SD

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CORRELATIONS:
  Satisfaction with HRPs ↔ engagement: r=0.38, p<.01
  Satisfaction with HRPs ↔ in-role performance: r=0.16, p<.05
  Satisfaction with HRPs ↔ extra-role performance: r=0.25, p<.01
  Engagement ↔ extra-role performance: r=0.53, p<.01
  Health ↔ engagement: r=0.28, p<.01
  Health ↔ extra-role performance: r=0.28, p<.01

H1a — Satisfaction with HRPs → In-role performance:
  β=0.13, p<.05 ✓ SUPPORTED (but weaker effect)

H1b — Satisfaction with HRPs → Extra-role performance:
  β=0.18, p<.01 ✓ SUPPORTED (stronger than in-role)

H2a — Engagement mediates HRP satisfaction → In-role:
  Engagement → in-role performance: non-significant
  ✗ NOT SUPPORTED (engagement does not mediate in-role)
  [FLAG: Explains how job uncertainty and external factors
  in pharma R&D/sales attenuate the engagement → in-role
  performance link; authors acknowledge this is context-
  specific and may reflect task controllability boundary]

H2b — Engagement mediates HRP satisfaction → Extra-role:
  Full mediation confirmed (satisfaction with HRPs → extra-role:
  becomes non-significant when engagement controlled)
  Bootstrap CI: [0.0757, 0.1815] — excludes zero ✓ SUPPORTED

H3 — Health moderates HRP satisfaction → Engagement:
  Satisfaction × Health interaction: positive and significant ✓
  Simple slope (high health): t=5.885, p<.001 ✓ (positive/sig)
  Simple slope (low health): t=1.200, p=.231 (non-significant)
  SUPPORTED: satisfaction with HRPs → engagement only significant
  for healthier employees

H4b — Health moderates indirect effect (via engagement) on
  extra-role performance:
  Conditional indirect effects:
    −1 SD health: CI [−0.024, 0.092] — includes zero (n.s.)
    Mean health: CI [0.047, 0.133] ✓ significant
    +1 SD health: CI [0.091, 0.233] ✓ significant
  SUPPORTED: indirect effect stronger for healthier employees

KEY CROSS-CUTTING FINDINGS FOR THESIS:
  (1) HRPs operationalised via AMO (training=A; PM/comp=M;
      autonomy=O) — satisfaction with this HRP bundle drives
      performance outcomes through engagement
  (2) DIFFERENTIAL EFFECTS: extra-role performance is more
      strongly affected (β=0.18) than in-role (β=0.13);
      engagement fully mediates extra-role but not in-role —
      suggests discretionary effort is the more responsive
      performance domain in HR systems research
  (3) HEALTH AS PERSONAL RESOURCE: HRP resources have potential
      downsides (cognitive burden of training; anxiety from PM
      monitoring; pressure from autonomy) — employee health
      determines whether these downsides can be withstood →
      engagement conversion; directly analogous to thesis OMS
      design caution from C04

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 SET"), C05 provides
               the most methodologically rigorous empirical test in
               Block C of the AMO-mapped HR practices → engagement →
               performance chain. Three thesis-relevant contributions:

               (1) AMO operationalised as HRP bundle: C05 explicitly
               maps training (ability), performance management and
               compensation (motivation), and autonomy/participation
               (opportunity) onto the AMO framework and tests their
               combined satisfaction as a predictor of performance.
               This is precisely how the thesis operationalises
               management systems: OMS and MIS sub-constructs enable
               the three AMO dimensions. C05 provides the strongest
               available empirical test of this chain.

               (2) Employee satisfaction with HRPs as the critical
               evaluation: The finding that satisfaction with HRPs —
               not just their presence — drives performance outcomes
               is directly applicable to GGHN. Management systems
               that GGHN adopts for hybrid work must be evaluated
               favourably by employees (need-supportive) to produce
               engagement and performance benefits. Design quality
               matters, not just system implementation.

               (3) Health as moderator = wellbeing prerequisite:
               The finding that health moderates the satisfaction →
               engagement link confirms C04's green performance
               paradox: HR/management system resources can burden
               employees. For GGHN's healthcare workforce — already
               operating in a demanding field health environment —
               employee wellbeing is a prerequisite for management
               systems to produce engagement and performance gains.

Limitation   : Single organisation (Italian pharma); cross-sectional
               for most variables; in-role performance from
               supervisor records (partially objectively measured but
               limited to one rater); single-item health measure;
               Italy high-individualism context — may not generalise
               to Nigeria/GGHN collectivist context; white-collar
               only; 2017 data; engagement mediates extra-role but
               NOT in-role — boundary condition unexplained by the
               model.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] European Management Journal (EMJ; Elsevier; ABS 3; SSCI)
      — same high-prestige outlet as B18 (Broccardo2025)
  [+] Partial objective measurement: in-role performance from
      company records (not self-report); temporal separation
      (3–5 months) reduces reverse causality for in-role DV
  [+] 84.6% response rate — excellent; census of all 430 FTE
  [+] CFA + EFA; 3-factor model superior to alternatives
      (CFI=0.92; RMSEA=0.047) ✓
  [+] PROCESS bootstrapping (5,000 samples) for mediation
      and moderated mediation — best-practice approach
  [+] Explicitly maps AMO onto HRP components — unique
      theoretical clarity in corpus
  [+] Health as personal resource — theoretically novel;
      directly relevant to thesis OMS design caution
  [+] Rich controls: 18 business unit dummies; salary grade;
      tenure; family burden
  [~] Single organisation — limits generalisability
  [~] Cross-sectional for most variables — reverse causality
      possible (engagement ↔ satisfaction)
  [~] Italy only — high individualism context; GGHN distance
  [~] White-collar pharmaceutical — sector specificity
  [~] Single-item health measure (mitigated by Wanous &
      Hudy 2001 validation)
  [~] Engagement does not mediate in-role performance —
      boundary condition unexplained; limits model completeness

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C01 (Zhang2025) and C02 (Yang2026) — AMO pathways:
  C05 provides the individual-level empirical confirmation of
  what C01 (team-level) and C02 (configurational) demonstrated:
  AMO dimensions (A/M/O mapped onto HR practices) → engagement
  → performance. C05's moderated mediation (satisfaction →
  engagement → extra-role performance, moderated by health)
  is the employee-level mechanistic complement to C01's parallel
  team-level mediation (team efficacy/identification/autonomy).

Convergence with B12 (Tsang2023) — engagement pathway:
  B12 found FWC → SE/WE → WFHP (partial mediation; Taiwan SMEs).
  C05 finds satisfaction with HRPs → engagement → extra-role
  performance (full mediation; Italy). Both confirm engagement
  as the psychological mechanism through which management
  system quality (HR practices or flexible work conditions)
  produces performance outcomes. Together they establish the
  engagement pathway across two methodologically distinct studies
  in two different national contexts.

Convergence with C04 (Omolo2026) — OMS burden risk:
  C04 identified the green performance paradox (monitoring →
  fatigue). C05 provides the mechanistic explanation: HRP
  resources carry burdens (training → cognitive load; PM →
  anxiety; autonomy → pressure). Health moderates whether
  these burdens can be withstood. Both converge: management
  system design must account for resource burdens, not just
  resource benefits. Overly demanding OMS will impair
  engagement for less healthy employees.

Convergence with Block A cross-cutting pattern #2 (PM and
  feedback as critical MIS): C05 finds performance management
  providing feedback resources is a core AMO-M component of
  the HRP satisfaction bundle. This is the individual-level
  empirical support for Block A's finding that structured PM
  and feedback systems are critical management system
  components for productivity. C05 shows the mechanism: PM
  → satisfaction → engagement → extra-role performance.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Moderated mediation: first first-stage moderated mediation
    model in corpus; PROCESS macro (Hayes 2018) applied to
    HR practices → engagement → performance
  - In-role vs. extra-role performance distinction: first paper
    in corpus to formally distinguish and differentially test
    in-role and extra-role performance as DVs in an HR systems
    model
  - Satisfaction with HRPs as evaluation construct: first paper
    in corpus to frame employee HRP evaluation (not just HRP
    implementation) as the IV; "perceived" vs. "intended" HRP
    distinction
  - Health as personal resource (JD-R): first paper in corpus
    to test employee health as a moderator (not just outcome)
    in the HR systems → performance chain
  - Single-organisation pharma census: first paper in corpus
    with near-census response rate (84.6%) within a single firm
  - Partial temporal separation: first paper in corpus with
    DV (in-role performance) collected from objective company
    records months after survey — partial quasi-longitudinal

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Stirpe, L., Profili, S., & Sammarra, A. (2022). Satisfaction with HR
practices and employee performance: A moderated mediation model of
engagement and health. European Management Journal, 40, 295–307.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.emj.2021.06.003

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C06
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C06
Cite key        : Alqudah2022
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HPHRMP operationalised via AMO:
                  selective staffing [A], extensive training [A/M],
                  results-oriented appraisal [M], communication/job
                  description [O], participation [O]; readiness for
                  change as AMO-mediated outcome → employee
                  performance),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HPHRMP as
                  management systems bundle; hierarchy culture as
                  moderator; affective commitment as mediator;
                  SET framework; Jordanian banking sector),
                  T7 (developing economy context — Jordan; Arabic/
                  Islamic cultural context; tribal society; hierarchy
                  culture; MENA region banking sector)
Title           : High-performance human resource management practices
                  and readiness for change: An integrative model
                  including affective commitment, employees'
                  performance, and the moderating role of hierarchy
                  culture
Authors         : Alqudah, I. H. A., Carballo-Penela, A., &
                  Ruzo-Sanmartín, E.
Year            : 2022 (received 7 July 2021; revised 26 November
                  2021; accepted 7 December 2021; available online
                  25 December 2021; print 2022)
Journal         : European Research on Management and Business
                  Economics (ERMBE; Elsevier España / AEDEM)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 28 (2022), Article 100177
DOI             : [10.1016/j.iedeen.2021.100177](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.iedeen.2021.100177)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Universidade de Santiago de Compostela, Spain
                  (all three authors)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  SEM (maximum likelihood; LISREL/AMOS implied);
                  moderation via PROCESS macro (Hayes, 2017);
                  CFA; bootstrapping (5,000 samples)
Aim             : To examine: (1) which HPHRMP predict affective
                  commitment and readiness for change; (2) whether
                  affective commitment predicts readiness for change;
                  (3) whether readiness for change predicts employee
                  performance; (4) whether hierarchy culture
                  moderates HPHRMP → affective commitment
Setting/context : All 25 banks in Jordan (13 commercial, 8 foreign
                  commercial, 3 Islamic, 1 foreign Islamic); March–
                  September 2020; Jordanian banking sector; Arabic/
                  Islamic cultural and institutional context
Sample          : 1,398 distributed → 510 valid responses; response
                  rate 36.48%; 68% male; 75.3% bachelor's degree;
                  43.5% aged 30–39; mixed managerial levels (lower
                  39.6%, middle 44.4%, upper 16.0%)
IVs             : HPHRMP bundle — 5 sub-constructs:
                  HP.com: Communication and clear job description
                    (CR=0.872; AVE=0.695)
                  HP.app: Results-oriented appraisal
                    (CR=0.920; AVE=0.794)
                  HP.sta: Selective staffing
                    (CR=0.888; AVE=0.664)
                  HP.tra: Extensive training
                    (CR=0.898; AVE=0.689)
                  HP.par: Participation
                    (CR=0.872; AVE=0.696)
Mediator        : Affective commitment (AFF.COM; CR=0.906; AVE=0.708)
Moderator       : Hierarchy culture (HIE; CR=0.932; AVE=0.696)
DV (mediated)   : Readiness for change (REA; CR=0.936; AVE=0.745)
DV (final)      : Employee performance (PRF; CR=0.962; AVE=0.739)
Theoretical     : SET (Blau, 1964): HPHRMP perceived as investment →
frameworks        reciprocity → affective commitment → readiness
                  AMO (Appelbaum et al., 2000): readiness for change
                  → ability + motivation + opportunity → performance
                  Competing Values Framework (Cameron & Quinn):
                  hierarchy culture as institutional moderator

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CFA fit         : χ²=1112.425; df=510; CMIN/df=2.18; CFI=0.964;
                  IFI=0.964; TLI=0.958; RMSEA=0.048 — good fit ✓
SEM fit         : χ²=1145.163; df=515; CMIN/df=2.22; CFI=0.962;
                  IFI=0.963; TLI=0.957; RMSEA=0.049 — good fit ✓
Discriminant    : All AVE square roots exceed inter-construct
validity          correlations (Fornell & Larcker, 1981) ✓
CMB             : Harman one-factor test (20.01% — below threshold);
                  one-factor CFA model rejected (RMSEA=0.132) —
                  CMB not a significant concern ✓
Moderation      : PROCESS macro (Hayes, 2017); mean-centred;
                  bootstrapped CIs (5,000 samples); conditional
                  effects at 16th, 50th, 84th percentiles;
                  Johnson-Neyman significance regions

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1 — HPHRMP → Affective commitment (PARTIALLY SUPPORTED):
  HP.com → AFF.COM: β=0.242, p<.05 ✓
  HP.tra → AFF.COM: β=0.157, p<.05 ✓
  HP.par → AFF.COM: β=0.213, p<.01 ✓
  HP.app → AFF.COM: ns (results-oriented appraisal not significant)
  HP.sta → AFF.COM: ns (selective staffing not significant)
  [NOTE: Tribal/wasta culture in Jordan explains ns for selective
  staffing — merit-based staffing conflicts with tribal norms]

H2 — HPHRMP → Readiness for change (PARTIALLY SUPPORTED):
  HP.com → REA: β=0.601, p<.001 ✓ [DOMINANT predictor]
  HP.tra → REA: β=0.211, p<.05 ✓
  HP.app → REA: β=−0.299, p<.01 [NEGATIVE — results-oriented
    appraisal REDUCES readiness in Jordan context; wasta culture
    makes objective appraisal perceived as threatening]
  HP.sta → REA: ns
  HP.par → REA: ns [Islamic/hierarchical culture attenuates
    participation → readiness link]

H3 — Affective commitment → Readiness for change:
  AFF.COM → REA: β=0.122, p<.05 ✓ SUPPORTED

H4 — Readiness for change → Employee performance:
  REA → PRF: β=0.564, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED (strong effect)

H5 — Hierarchy culture moderates HPHRMP → AFF.COM:
  HP.com × HIE → AFF.COM: β=0.090, p<.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
    (POSITIVE moderation — contrary to prediction; hierarchy
    STRENGTHENS the communication → commitment link in Jordan)
  HP.par × HIE → AFF.COM: β=0.061, p<.10 ✓ SUPPORTED
    (POSITIVE moderation — hierarchy strengthens participation
    → commitment link)
  HP.tra × HIE → AFF.COM: ns (training not moderated)
  [KEY UNEXPECTED FINDING: Hierarchy culture POSITIVELY moderates
   HPHRMP → commitment — the opposite of the hypothesis. In
   Jordan (high power-distance society), hierarchical structures
   create a SUPPORTIVE environment that reinforces commitment,
   not a restrictive one. National culture alignment explains
   this reversal.]

CHAIN: HPHRMP → AFF.COM → REA → PRF
  Most effective HPHRMP for driving the full chain:
  communication/clear job description (β=0.601 on REA; dominant)
  and extensive training (β=0.211 on REA)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO+SET"), C06
               provides converging evidence for the thesis in three
               ways:

               (1) AMO + SET combined in HPHRMP → performance chain:
               C06 explicitly uses both AMO (readiness for change →
               ability/motivation/opportunity → performance) and SET
               (HPHRMP → perceived support → affective commitment →
               readiness) in a single integrated model. This is the
               most complete dual-framework empirical test in Block C
               alongside C04. The thesis Chapter 2.2 can cite C06
               for the claim that management systems (HPHRMP) operate
               through both SET (commitment) and AMO (readiness/
               capability) pathways to produce employee performance.

               (2) Communication and training as dominant HPHRMP:
               The finding that communication/clear job description
               (β=0.601) and extensive training (β=0.211) are the
               most effective HPHRMP for producing readiness for
               change is directly applicable to GGHN's hybrid work
               transition. Clear communication about hybrid work
               arrangements and training in digital tools are the
               management systems components most likely to build
               employee readiness for GGHN's change — and C06
               provides the strongest empirical estimate for this.

               (3) Hierarchy culture as positive moderator:
               The unexpected finding that hierarchy culture
               STRENGTHENS HPHRMP → commitment in Jordan (a high
               power-distance developing economy) is directly
               relevant for GGHN, which operates in Nigeria — also
               a high power-distance society. This suggests that
               GGHN's hierarchical health sector culture may
               REINFORCE rather than undermine management systems
               → employee commitment effects, contrary to what
               Western-centric models would predict. The thesis
               should acknowledge this developing-economy nuance.

Limitation   : Jordan only (MENA banking sector) — cultural and
               sectoral distance from Nigeria/GGHN NGO health;
               cross-sectional; self-report performance DV (not
               objective); response rate 36.48% — below 50%
               threshold; ERMBE is a regional Spanish management
               journal (lower international tier than core outlets);
               moderation H5 supported in opposite direction to
               hypothesis — unexpected result not fully theorised;
               no mediation analysis of indirect paths.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Large sample: n=510; all 25 banks in Jordan — near-census
      of Jordanian banking sector
  [+] Excellent CFA + SEM fit (CFI=0.964; RMSEA=0.048) ✓
  [+] All CR>0.87; AVE>0.66; Fornell-Larcker discriminant
      validity confirmed ✓
  [+] CMB tested via Harman + one-factor CFA — not significant ✓
  [+] PROCESS bootstrapping (5,000 samples) for moderation ✓
  [+] Differentiated testing of 5 HPHRMP sub-constructs
      separately — reveals which practices matter in context;
      methodological rigour
  [+] Unexpected H5 finding (positive hierarchy moderation)
      is theoretically important for developing economy HRM;
      well-explained via national culture theory
  [+] AMO + SET dual theoretical framework — thesis-relevant
  [~] 36.48% response rate — below 50%; non-response bias
      tested (not significant) but still modest
  [~] Cross-sectional; all variables self-report (except
      readiness for change scale)
  [~] Jordan MENA banking — cultural and sectoral specificity
  [~] ERMBE — regional Spanish journal (Elsevier España);
      lower international ranking than SSCI core outlets
  [~] Performance DV is self-reported — no objective validation
  [~] No mediation path testing (indirect effects not estimated)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C03 (Jakpa2025) — SHRM + change capacity:
  C03 found SHRM → organisational change capacity (β=0.68) in
  Nigeria. C06 provides a more rigorous structural model:
  HPHRMP → readiness for change → employee performance (β=0.564)
  in Jordan. Both converge: management systems build
  organisational and individual change capacity. C06 provides
  the mechanistic detail (affective commitment as mediator;
  communication and training as dominant practices) that C03
  lacked. Together they establish the Sub-Saharan Africa + MENA
  developing economy evidence base for management systems →
  change readiness → performance.

Convergence with C05 (Stirpe2022) — AMO HRP bundle:
  C05 found satisfaction with HRPs (training, PM, autonomy)
  → engagement → extra-role performance (Italy pharma). C06
  finds HPHRMP (communication, training, participation) →
  affective commitment → readiness → performance (Jordan
  banking). Both use AMO-mapped HR bundles; both find training
  and participation/autonomy as the most effective components;
  both show engagement/commitment as the psychological mediator.
  Cross-national convergence (Italy + Jordan) strengthens the
  evidence that AMO-mapped management systems produce performance
  through psychological mediation.

Hierarchy culture + developing economy nuance:
  C06's unexpected positive moderation of hierarchy culture
  converges with C04 (Omolo2026) and B22 (Saurombe2022):
  in developing economies, institutional and cultural structures
  operate differently from Western models. Hierarchy in Jordan
  creates a supportive context; regulatory compliance in Kenya
  dominates intrinsic motivation; PM absence impairs performance
  in South Africa. The thesis must explicitly acknowledge that
  the Western-centric AMO/SET model requires adaptation for
  GGHN's Nigerian developing economy context — and C06 provides
  the empirical basis for this acknowledgement.

Communication as dominant HPHRMP (β=0.601 → readiness):
  The finding that communication and clear job description is
  by far the strongest HPHRMP predictor of readiness for change
  converges with Block A papers (A04, A08, A11) that found
  communication clarity and role definition as critical MIS
  components. Together they establish the thesis sub-argument:
  clear communication is both an MIS component and the primary
  management systems mechanism for building employee readiness
  and productivity in change contexts.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - European Research on Management and Business Economics
    (ERMBE): first ERMBE paper in corpus; Elsevier España/AEDEM
  - Jordanian banking sector: first MENA/Arabic context in corpus
  - Readiness for change as explicit DV: first paper in corpus
    to treat employee readiness for change as a DV predicted by
    HPHRMP in the AMO chain
  - Hierarchy culture (Competing Values Framework): first
    explicit use of Cameron & Quinn's hierarchy culture construct
    as moderator in corpus
  - Wasta/tribal culture: first mention of wasta and tribal
    norms as contextual moderators of HRM effectiveness in corpus
  - Positive unexpected moderation: first paper in corpus where
    moderation finding is opposite to hypothesis direction —
    theoretically important for developing economy HRM

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Alqudah, I. H. A., Carballo-Penela, A., & Ruzo-Sanmartín, E. (2022).
High-performance human resource management practices and readiness for
change: An integrative model including affective commitment, employees'
performance, and the moderating role of hierarchy culture. European
Research on Management and Business Economics, 28, Article 100177.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.iedeen.2021.100177

EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C10
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C10
Cite key        : Dorta-Afonso2025
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HPWS operationalised as AMO
                  bundle: recruitment/selection [A], training [A],
                  employment security [A/M], performance management
                  [M], incentives/rewards [M], job design [O],
                  participation in decision-making [O]; HPWS as
                  job resources in JD-R framework),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HPWS as
                  management systems bundle; JD-R theory; challenge
                  vs. hindrance job demands; mutual gains perspective
                  vs. conflicting outcomes; hospitality/tourism sector;
                  Spain),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — HPWS →
                  burnout reduction (direct + mediated); HPWS → QoL
                  increase; challenge/hindrance job demands as
                  mediating mechanisms; burnout → QoL negative)
Title           : High-performance work systems in job
                  demands-resources theory: Implications for employee
                  burnout and quality of life
Authors         : Dorta-Afonso, D., & Romero-Domínguez, L.
Year            : 2025 (received 11 March 2024; revised 27 November
                  2024; accepted 9 December 2024; available online
                  13 December 2024; print 2025)
Journal         : International Journal of Hospitality Management
                  (IJHM; Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 126 (2025), Article 104066
DOI             : [10.1016/j.ijhm.2024.104066](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijhm.2024.104066)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Universidad de Las Palmas de Gran Canaria, Spain
                  (both authors)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  PLS-SEM (SmartPLS implied); bootstrapping (10,000
                  subsamples); disjoint two-step approach for second-
                  order constructs; PLSpredict for predictive power
Aim             : To examine how HPWS are related to both the
                  motivational and health impairment processes of
                  JD-R theory via challenge and hindrance job demands;
                  specifically: (1) HPWS → burnout (direct + mediated
                  via job demands); (2) HPWS → QoL; (3) challenge job
                  demands → QoL; (4) burnout → QoL
Setting/context : Gran Canaria (Canary Islands), Spain; hospitality
                  and tourism sector (hotels, restaurants, car rental);
                  2021 spring-summer (post-COVID recovery period);
                  convenience sampling; self-administered questionnaire
Sample          : n=417; 59.5% female; 33.1% aged >44; 55.2%
                  hospitality; 22.5% restaurants; 22.3% car rental;
                  power test minimum n=103 confirmed ✓
IV              : HPWS — 20-item scale (Kloutsiniotis & Mihail, 2020c);
                  7 dimensions: recruitment/selection, training/
                  development, employment security, performance
                  management, incentives/rewards, job design,
                  participation in decision-making; α range 0.772–0.926
Mediators       : Challenge job demands — 6-item (Cavanaugh et al.,
                  2000); α=0.920
                  Hindrance job demands — 5-item (Cavanaugh et al.,
                  2000); α=0.843
DVs             : Burnout — 15-item MBI-GS (Schaufeli et al., 1996);
                  exhaustion (α=0.927), cynicism (α=0.879), personal
                  efficacy (α=0.903); formative second-order construct
                  Quality of Life (QoL) — 3-item adapted scale
                  (Choi et al., 2018; Zhao et al., 2016); α=0.935
Controls        : Age; gender; working sector
Theoretical     : JD-R theory (Bakker & Demerouti, 2017; Demerouti
frameworks        et al., 2001): health impairment process (job
                  demands → burnout) and motivational process (job
                  resources → well-being); distinction between
                  challenge and hindrance job demands
                  AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000): HPWS as A+M+O
                  enabling bundle; job resources interpretation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Outer model     : All loadings >0.708 ✓; CR>0.7 ✓; AVE>0.5 ✓;
                  HTMT ratio <0.85 (discriminant validity) ✓
                  VIF values all <3.3 (no collinearity) ✓
CMB             : Full collinearity test (VIF<3.3) + Harman single
                  factor (22.1%) — CMB not problematic ✓
PLS-SEM         : Bootstrapping 10,000 subsamples; 95% CI reported
                  Disjoint two-step approach for second-order
                  constructs (HPWS formative; burnout formative)
                  PLSpredict: Q² positive for all QoL items; MAE
                  PLS-SEM < LM benchmark (2/3 items) → medium
                  predictive power ✓
R²              : Challenge JD: 13.1%; Hindrance JD: 30.0%;
                  Burnout: 54.3%; QoL: 16.0%

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1 — HPWS → Burnout (direct):
  β=−0.410, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  [Strong direct negative effect — HPWS substantially reduces burnout]

H2a — HPWS → Challenge job demands → Burnout (mediated):
  HPWS → Challenge JD: β=−0.362, p<.001
  Challenge JD → Burnout: β=0.190, p<.001
  Indirect: β=−0.060, p<.01 ✓ SUPPORTED (partial mediation)

H2b — HPWS → Hindrance job demands → Burnout (mediated):
  HPWS → Hindrance JD: β=−0.515, p<.001
  Hindrance JD → Burnout: β=0.296, p<.001
  Indirect: β=−0.153, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED (partial mediation)
  [Hindrance JD pathway stronger than challenge JD pathway]

H3 — HPWS → QoL:
  β=0.130, p<.05 ✓ SUPPORTED
  [Positive but modest direct effect — motivational path confirmed]

H4 — Challenge job demands → QoL:
  β=0.093, p=ns ✗ NOT SUPPORTED
  [Explained by post-COVID context: challenge demands perceived as
  hindrance during recovery period; future replication needed in
  stable conditions]

H5 — Burnout → QoL:
  β=−0.321, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  [Burnout substantially reduces quality of life]

KEY SUMMARY:
  HPWS → burnout: β=−0.410 direct; −0.060 via challenge JD;
                   −0.153 via hindrance JD
  HPWS → QoL: β=0.130 direct; also via burnout reduction
  HPWS is a source of job resources that simultaneously reduces
  demands (demand-reduction pathway) and improves well-being
  (motivational pathway) — supporting mutual gains perspective

MUTUAL GAINS PERSPECTIVE CONFIRMED:
  Results support the mutual gains view (HPWS beneficial for
  both organisation AND employees in win-win manner), as opposed
  to the conflicting outcomes perspective (HPWS exploitative).
  Caution: if HPWS increase job demands rather than reduce them,
  the conflicting outcomes perspective may apply — design matters.

GENDER FINDING:
  Women face more challenge and hindrance job demands than men
  in hospitality/tourism (β=−0.123 and −0.121, p<.01) —
  gender moderates the demands experienced; relevant for GGHN's
  predominantly female health workforce context.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 COR/JD-R"), C10
               provides the most rigorous available empirical test of
               HPWS (management systems) operating as job resources
               in JD-R theory to reduce burnout and improve well-being.
               Four thesis-relevant contributions:

               (1) HPWS as job resources (JD-R): C10 explicitly
               frames HPWS as a bundle of job resources operating
               through the JD-R motivational and health impairment
               processes. This is the JD-R-level theoretical
               validation for the thesis argument that management
               systems (OMS + MIS) provide employees with the
               resources needed to perform in hybrid work arrangements.

               (2) Demand-reduction pathway: HPWS reduce both
               challenge (β=−0.060) and hindrance (β=−0.153) job
               demands → reduce burnout. For GGHN's hybrid work
               context: management systems that reduce job demands
               (e.g., clear role definitions via MIS; workload
               management via OMS) are the mechanism through which
               hybrid work enables rather than harms employee
               productivity. C10 provides the pathway evidence.

               (3) Mutual gains vs. conflicting outcomes: C10 confirms
               that HPWS can be mutually beneficial IF designed
               correctly. If HPWS increase demands (e.g., performance
               monitoring intensity), they may harm well-being.
               This is directly applicable to GGHN's OMS design:
               outcome-based PM systems (not surveillance-based) are
               needed to realise mutual gains in hybrid work — exactly
               the C04/C05 OMS design caution from earlier.

               (4) QoL as outcome: C10 expands the performance outcome
               from task performance to quality of life — relevant
               for GGHN where employee wellbeing in a public health
               field context is a secondary concern alongside
               task performance.

Limitation   : Hospitality/tourism sector only (Spain; Gran Canaria)
               — high sectoral and cultural distance from Nigeria/GGHN
               NGO health context; cross-sectional; self-reported;
               convenience sample; post-COVID recovery context
               attenuates challenge JD → QoL relationship; H4 not
               supported (challenge JD → QoL); QoL ≠ employee
               productivity (thesis DV) — conceptual translation
               needed.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] International Journal of Hospitality Management (IJHM;
      Elsevier) — one of the top hospitality management journals;
      Scopus, SSCI indexed; ABS 3
  [+] n=417; power test confirmed (minimum n=103); well-powered
  [+] PLS-SEM with bootstrapping (10,000 subsamples); disjoint
      two-step for second-order constructs — methodologically
      rigorous
  [+] All loadings >0.708; CR>0.7; AVE>0.5; HTMT<0.85;
      VIF<3.3 — outer and inner model both validated ✓
  [+] PLSpredict predictive power test conducted ✓
  [+] CMB: full collinearity + Harman test ✓
  [+] 7-dimension HPWS measurement — most granular HPWS
      operationalisation in corpus; AMO-mappable
  [+] Challenge vs. hindrance job demands distinction —
      theoretically important extension of JD-R
  [+] Mutual gains vs. conflicting outcomes debate addressed
  [~] Cross-sectional; self-reported; single source
  [~] Hospitality/tourism (Spain) — sectoral and cultural
      distance from thesis context
  [~] Post-COVID recovery context attenuates H4 results
  [~] QoL DV ≠ employee task productivity (thesis DV)
  [~] Convenience sampling
  [~] H4 not supported — partial model confirmation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C05 (Stirpe2022) — HRP satisfaction → engagement:
  C05 found satisfaction with HRPs (AMO-mapped) → engagement →
  extra-role performance (Italy pharma). C10 finds HPWS → reduced
  job demands → reduced burnout → improved QoL (Spain hospitality).
  Both use AMO-mapped HR bundles; both find the JD-R motivational
  and health impairment pathways operational; both confirm that
  management systems quality (as perceived by employees) drives
  well-being outcomes. Cross-sectoral convergence (pharma + tourism)
  strengthens the evidence base for the thesis management systems →
  employee outcomes chain.

Convergence with C06 (Alqudah2022) — HPWS + mutual gains:
  C06 found HPHRMP → affective commitment → readiness for change →
  performance (Jordan banking). C10 finds HPWS → burnout reduction
  → QoL (Spain hospitality). Both confirm mutual gains perspective
  under AMO/JD-R: management systems benefit both organisation
  (performance/readiness) and employees (well-being/burnout). The
  convergence of Jordan banking and Spanish hospitality strengthens
  the cross-national generalisability of the mutual gains argument.

Convergence with C04 (Omolo2026) — management system overload risk:
  C04 identified the green performance paradox (monitoring → fatigue);
  C10 explicitly confirms this risk: if HPWS INCREASE job demands
  rather than reduce them, the conflicting outcomes perspective
  applies and employee well-being worsens. Both converge on the
  thesis OMS design principle: outcome-based, autonomy-enabling
  management systems are needed; surveillance-heavy systems harm
  the very outcomes they are designed to improve.

JD-R + AMO convergence across Block C:
  C01 (AMO team-level), C02 (AMO configurational), C03 (AMO Nigeria),
  C04 (AMO+SET+SDT SLR), C05 (AMO+JD-R HRP), C06 (AMO+SET SEM),
  C10 (AMO+JD-R HPWS) now form a seven-paper Block C convergence
  on the AMO framework applied to management systems → employee
  outcomes. C10 adds the demand-reduction JD-R pathway as the
  mechanistic bridge between HPWS (management systems) and both
  burnout and well-being.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - International Journal of Hospitality Management (IJHM): first
    IJHM paper in corpus; Elsevier; top hospitality management journal
  - PLS-SEM: first PLS-SEM study in corpus (vs. OLS, SEM/ML used
    elsewhere); appropriate for mixed reflective-formative constructs
  - Challenge vs. hindrance job demands: first paper in corpus to
    explicitly test the challenge/hindrance distinction within JD-R
  - Quality of Life (QoL) as DV: first paper in corpus to use QoL
    (life satisfaction) as an outcome variable predicted by HPWS
  - Demand-reduction pathway: first paper in corpus to show HPWS
    → reduced job demands → reduced burnout as the mechanistic
    pathway (vs. resource addition pathway used in other papers)
  - Mutual gains vs. conflicting outcomes: first explicit test of
    this debate in corpus; mutual gains confirmed under correct
    HPWS design conditions
  - Hospitality/tourism sector: first hospitality context in corpus
    (vs. pharma, banking, manufacturing used elsewhere)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Dorta-Afonso, D., & Romero-Domínguez, L. (2025). High-performance
work systems in job demands-resources theory: Implications for employee
burnout and quality of life. International Journal of Hospitality
Management, 126, Article 104066.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijhm.2024.104066

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C11
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C11
Cite key        : Zhai2025
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HPWS conceptualised via AMO:
                  ability-enhancing [recruitment/selection,
                  training/development], motivation-enhancing
                  [compensation, PM, promotion], opportunity-enhancing
                  [employee relations]; training/development as
                  dominant ability-enhancing component; country-level
                  human capital moderates AMO effectiveness),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HPWS as
                  ownership-specific strategic advantage; AMO + eclectic
                  paradigm; cross-level interaction of firm-level HPWS
                  with country-level human capital; 20 countries/
                  regions; meta-analysis 1994–2024),
                  T4 (systematic review / meta-analysis — k=232 sample
                  studies; N=56,868 business entities; HLM; three-level
                  meta-analytic model; PRISMA flow)
Title           : The role of country-level human capital in the
                  high-performance work systems and organizational
                  performance association: A meta-analysis
Authors         : Zhai, X., Huang, F., & Tian, X.
Year            : 2025 (received 1 June 2023; revised 16 October 2024;
                  accepted 16 October 2024; available online 28 October
                  2024; print 2025)
Journal         : Human Resource Management Review (HRMR; Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 35 (2025), Article 101057
DOI             : [10.1016/j.hrmr.2024.101057](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.hrmr.2024.101057)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Murdoch University, Perth, Western Australia,
                  Australia (all three authors)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Meta-analysis with three-level hierarchical linear
                  modelling (HLM); systematic literature search
                  (ProQuest, Scopus, WoS, Google Scholar; 1994–April
                  2024); PRISMA flowchart; Schmidt & Hunter (2015)
                  corrections; PLSpredict; fail-safe N; Egger's test
Aim             : To examine whether country-level human capital
                  development moderates the HPWS–organisational
                  performance (OP) relationship; and whether
                  ability-enhancing HPWS practices (recruitment/
                  selection; training/development) strengthen or
                  interact with this moderation
Databases       : ProQuest, Scopus, Web of Science, Google Scholar;
                  university library; reference lists of prior meta-
                  analyses
Inclusion       : k=232 independent sample sets from 228 studies;
                  N=56,868 business entities; 20 countries/regions;
                  1994–April 2024; quantitative; HPWS as system;
                  bivariate correlations reported; firm/establishment-
                  level OP
Countries       : Australia, Canada, China, France, Germany, Greece,
                  Hong Kong, India, Ireland, Japan, Korea, Malaysia,
                  Netherlands, New Zealand, Singapore, Spain, Taiwan,
                  Turkey, UK, US
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000; Bos-Nehles et
frameworks        al., 2013, 2023): HPWS as AMO-structured bundle;
                  ability-enhancing practices critical for OP
                  Eclectic paradigm (Dunning, 1977, 1980, 2000):
                  HPWS as ownership-specific advantage; country human
                  capital as location-specific advantage;
                  internalisation advantage via training/development

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1 — HPWS → Organisational performance:
  Weighted mean r=0.259, p<.001; corrected rc=0.302
  95% CI [0.213, 0.304]; 95% PI [0.154, 0.595]
  I²=91.50% → significant heterogeneity → moderators exist
  H1 SUPPORTED: HPWS consistently and positively predicts OP
  across 20 countries/regions; consistent with prior meta-analyses
  (Combs et al., 2006: r=0.210; Rabl et al., 2014: r=0.220;
   Saridakis et al., 2017: r=0.360; Zhai & Tian, 2020: r=0.256)

H2 — National human capital NEGATIVELY moderates HPWS→OP:
  β=−0.007, p<.001 (Model 1) ✓ SUPPORTED
  HPWS generates STRONGER OP effect in countries with LOWER
  human capital development
  Interpretation: HPWS substitutes for country-level human capital
  scarcity — where talent is scarce, management systems matter more
  [KEY FINDING FOR DEVELOPING ECONOMIES: In countries like Nigeria
  with lower national human capital (lower secondary education
  investment), HPWS has a STRONGER effect on OP than in developed
  economies. This directly supports the thesis argument that
  management systems matter MORE in GGHN's Nigerian context.]

H3a — Recruitment/selection moderates HPWS→OP:
  β=0.185, ns ✗ NOT SUPPORTED
  External talent acquisition does not significantly strengthen
  HPWS–OP relationship (possible: external hires need time to
  integrate and reach performance capacity)

H3b — Training/development moderates HPWS→OP:
  β=0.517, p<.01 ✓ SUPPORTED
  Higher T&D proportion in HPWS → stronger HPWS–OP association
  [CRITICAL: Training/development is the ONLY HPWS component that
  significantly strengthens the HPWS–OP relationship]

H4a — R/S × national human capital interaction: ns ✗ NOT SUPPORTED

H4b — T&D × national human capital interaction:
  β=−0.037, p<.05 ✓ SUPPORTED
  Simple slopes: Low HC context: T&D effect=1.057, p<.001
                 High HC context: T&D effect=0.154, ns
  T&D has DRAMATICALLY stronger influence on HPWS–OP in low
  human capital countries — it is the key internalisation mechanism

SUBSTITUTION RELATIONSHIP (central finding):
  Country-level human capital (location-specific advantage) and
  firm-level HPWS (ownership-specific advantage) are SUBSTITUTES.
  In low HC countries: HPWS critical; T&D within HPWS is the key
  mechanism for internalising external human capital into
  firm-specific productive resources.
  In high HC countries: HPWS adds less marginal value because
  skilled workers are widely available.

ABILITY PRIMACY IN AMO:
  Supports revision P=f[A(1+M+O)] over P=f(A+M+O):
  without ability, motivation and opportunity contribute little.
  T&D as the critical ability-enhancing practice confirms ability
  as the foundational AMO dimension for HPWS effectiveness.

COUNTRY SUBGROUP EFFECTS (selected):
  Singapore: r=0.628*; Turkey: r=0.625***; Greece: r=0.585***
  (high effect, lower HC development countries)
  Netherlands: r=0.073 ns (only insignificant subgroup; high HC)
  China: r=0.344***; Korea: r=0.235***; US: r=0.224***

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO meta-analysis"),
               C11 is the most methodologically powerful paper in
               the entire corpus for the thesis argument. Four
               direct contributions:

               (1) HPWS matters MORE in developing economies:
               The substitution relationship (H2) demonstrates that
               management systems (HPWS) have stronger effects on
               organisational performance in countries with lower
               human capital development. Nigeria has lower secondary
               education investment than developed economies. C11
               therefore provides the strongest available meta-analytic
               evidence that management systems like those the thesis
               proposes for GGHN will have a STRONGER effect on
               productivity in Nigeria than comparable systems in
               developed economies would. This is the most powerful
               contextualisation argument in the corpus.

               (2) Training/development as the critical AMO-A
               mechanism: T&D is the only HPWS component that
               significantly strengthens HPWS–OP, especially in low
               HC countries (slope=1.057 vs 0.154). For GGHN's
               hybrid work context: training employees in digital
               tools, hybrid work protocols, and MIS usage is not
               just good practice — C11 demonstrates it is the
               primary HPWS lever for performance improvement in
               exactly the kind of developing economy GGHN operates
               in.

               (3) AMO ability primacy: The finding that T&D (ability)
               is the dominant HPWS component supports the thesis
               argument that management systems must first enable
               employee ability (through training in hybrid work
               tools and processes) before motivation and opportunity
               mechanisms can produce productivity gains.

               (4) Convergent meta-analytic estimate: r=0.259 (k=232;
               N=56,868) is the most reliable available estimate of
               the HPWS → OP effect. This is the baseline effect the
               thesis can cite when asserting that management systems
               improve organisational performance.

Limitation   : No Africa-specific studies in the 20 countries (Nigeria
               absent; sub-Saharan Africa absent — significant gap);
               national human capital proxied by government secondary
               education expenditure (% GDP per capita) — proxy
               validity debatable; OP measured at firm/establishment
               level (not individual employee productivity — thesis DV);
               cross-sectional firm-level OP (not longitudinal);
               publication bias tested (minimal) but cannot be fully
               excluded; HPWS practice composition varies across
               studies reducing comparability.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Human Resource Management Review (HRMR; Elsevier) — one of
      the premier HRM journals; Scopus, SSCI indexed; ABS 4
  [+] Largest meta-analysis in corpus: k=232; N=56,868;
      20 countries/regions; 1994–2024 (30-year span)
  [+] Three-level HLM — methodologically most sophisticated
      analysis in corpus; accounts for nesting of organisations
      within studies within countries
  [+] Schmidt & Hunter (2015) corrections for sampling error
      and measurement error ✓
  [+] Publication bias: fail-safe N=344,719; funnel plot;
      Egger's test (ns) — publication bias minimal ✓
  [+] VIF all <5; no multicollinearity ✓
  [+] Robustness check with alternative HC proxy (total education
      expenditure % GDP) — results consistent ✓
  [+] Post-hoc analysis testing all HPWS components — only T&D
      significant; reinforces robustness of H3b/H4b
  [+] 95% interrater agreement on coding ✓
  [+] CC BY 4.0 open access; Murdoch University
  [~] Africa/Nigeria absent from 20 country sample — limits
      direct application but increases importance of findings
      for developing economies generally
  [~] National HC proxy (secondary education expenditure) may
      not fully capture human capital quality
  [~] OP at firm level ≠ individual employee productivity
  [~] HPWS practice composition varies across pooled studies

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C03 (Jakpa2025) — Nigeria developing economy:
  C03 found SHRM → well-being (β=0.781) and → organisational change
  (β=0.68) in Nigerian multi-sector sample. C11 provides the
  meta-analytic foundation: management systems (HPWS) have stronger
  effects on OP in low human capital countries (like Nigeria) because
  HPWS substitutes for HC scarcity. C03 provides the Nigerian
  empirical evidence; C11 provides the 232-study meta-analytic
  theoretical and empirical basis. Together they are the strongest
  cross-referenced argument for management systems effectiveness
  in GGHN's Nigerian context.

Convergence with C06 (Alqudah2022) — HPHRMP + performance:
  C06 found HPHRMP (5 sub-constructs) → readiness for change →
  performance in Jordan (developing economy; lower HC). C11
  confirms at meta-analytic level: in countries with lower human
  capital (like Jordan and Nigeria), HPWS generates stronger OP
  effects. C06 provides the Jordan-specific SEM evidence; C11
  provides the cross-national meta-analytic basis.

Training/development as dominant HPWS component:
  C11's finding that T&D is the ONLY HPWS component significantly
  strengthening HPWS–OP converges with C06 (training β=0.211→
  readiness) and C05 (training satisfaction → engagement) in
  identifying training/development as the critical management
  systems mechanism. Three papers from three different
  methodologies (meta-analysis, SEM, OLS) and three countries
  converge on this finding — the strongest cross-method evidence
  in Block C.

AMO ability primacy across Block C:
  C01 (O pathway strongest in China), C02 (ability divergence
  radical/incremental), C05 (training→ability→engagement), C06
  (training→readiness), C11 (T&D only significant HPWS moderator)
  all converge: the ability dimension of AMO — and specifically
  training/development as the key ability-enabling practice —
  is the most critical management systems component for employee
  and organisational performance. This is the dominant pattern
  in Block C and directly supports the thesis OMS argument.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Human Resource Management Review (HRMR; Elsevier): first HRMR
    paper in corpus; ABS 4; top-tier HRM journal
  - Meta-analysis as method (Block C): first meta-analysis in
    Block C (B21 was SLR; B18 was narrative SLR; C11 is the first
    formal meta-analysis with HLM in corpus)
  - N=56,868: largest N in any single study in corpus
  - k=232: largest number of included studies in any SLR/meta-
    analysis in corpus
  - Three-level HLM: first use in corpus; methodologically
    most sophisticated analysis
  - Substitution relationship (HPWS × country human capital):
    first cross-level interaction between firm-level HPWS and
    country-level human capital in corpus
  - Eclectic paradigm (OLI framework; Dunning 1977): first
    international business theory applied to HPWS context in corpus
  - Ability primacy P=f[A(1+M+O)]: first explicit revised AMO
    performance equation in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Zhai, X., Huang, F., & Tian, X. (2025). The role of country-level
human capital in the high-performance work systems and organizational
performance association: A meta-analysis. Human Resource Management
Review, 35, Article 101057.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.hrmr.2024.101057
