```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C12
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C12
Cite key        : VanBeurden2025
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HPWPs operationalised via AMO:
                  training/development [A], performance management [M],
                  participation/information sharing [O]; dyadic
                  perspective on HPWP implementation and perception;
                  commitment as AMO motivational mediator),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HPWPs as
                  management systems; manager-employee (mis)alignment
                  in HPWP perception; formal/informal/unused/no-use
                  HPWP typology; frames of reference theory;
                  Netherlands; diverse sectors)
Title           : Do managers and employees see eye to eye? A dyadic
                  perspective on high-performance work practices and
                  their impact on performance
Authors         : Van Beurden, J., Van de Voorde, K., Van Veldhoven,
                  M., & Jiang, K.
Year            : 2025 (received 6 October 2023; revised 9 January
                  2025; accepted 9 January 2025; available online
                  3 February 2025)
Journal         : Journal of Business Research (JBR; Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 190 (2025), Article 115190
DOI             : [10.1016/j.jbusres.2025.115190](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbusres.2025.115190)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Tilburg University, Netherlands (Van Beurden, Van de
                  Voorde, Van Veldhoven); Peking University, China
                  (Jiang)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; dyadic cross-sectional
                  survey; line manager–employee dyads; Hayes PROCESS
                  macro (Model 4; bootstrapping 5,000 samples);
                  CFA (Mplus v8.3; WLSMV estimator); mediation
                  analysis
Aim             : To examine how (mis)alignment between line manager
                  and employee reports of HPWPs for individual workers
                  influences employee commitment and manager-rated
                  job performance; to develop a typology of four
                  (mis)alignment types
Setting/context : Netherlands; diverse sectors (business services,
                  education, production, government, healthcare);
                  Dutch research university student-recruited dyads;
                  2025
Sample          : 325 dyads approached; 44 removed (incomplete);
                  N=252 valid dyads (78% response rate); employee
                  mean age 37 years; 59% female; mean organisational
                  tenure 9 years; manager mean age 44 years; 35%
                  female; mean tenure 12 years
IV              : HPWP (mis)alignment — four types derived from
                  15-item dyadic HPWP scale (AMO-structured):
                  (1) Formal HPWPs: manager offers + employee uses
                  (2) No use of HPWPs: both report absence
                  (3) Unused HPWPs: manager offers; employee does not
                  (4) Informal HPWPs: employee uses; manager unaware
                  KR20 reliability: manager α=0.627; employee α=0.735
Mediator        : Affective organisational commitment — 3-item
                  (Meyer & Allen, 1991); α=0.781
DV              : Manager-rated job performance — 4-item (Griffin et
                  al., 2007): task proficiency, task proactivity, task
                  adaptivity + overall performance item; α=0.783
Controls        : Employee age; gender; sector (public/private);
                  organisational size
Theoretical     : Frames of reference theory (Budd et al., 2022):
frameworks        cognitive frames → HPWP (mis)alignment typology
                  Self- and other-rater theory (Atwater & Yammarino,
                  1997): degree of overlap between raters → outcomes
                  AMO theory (Appelbaum, 2000; Jiang et al., 2012):
                  HPWPs as A+M+O bundle
                  Social Exchange Theory (Blau, 1964): HPWP
                  perception → reciprocity → commitment → performance

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CFA             : Second-order factor model (AMO sub-factors) fits
                  data: employee HPWPs: χ²=114.261(87), p<.01;
                  TLI=0.956; CFI=0.963; RMSEA=0.035 ✓
                  Manager HPWPs: χ²=93.269(87), p<.01; TLI=0.977;
                  CFI=0.981; RMSEA=0.017 ✓ (superior to 1-factor)
CMB             : Manager-rated DV (job performance) and employee-
                  rated IV (commitment) from different sources;
                  student recruitment with quality checks; mitigates
                  CMB concerns
Analysis        : Hayes PROCESS macro (SPSS; Model 4); 95% bias-
                  corrected CI; 5,000 bootstraps; formal HPWPs tested
                  separately (high inter-correlation with other types)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
MEANS (HPWP types; proportion of 15 practices):
  Formal HPWPs (manager offers + employee uses): M=0.59 (59%)
  No use of HPWPs (both absent): M=0.10 (10%)
  Unused HPWPs (manager offers; employee not using): M=0.21 (21%)
  Informal HPWPs (employee uses; manager unaware): M=0.07 (7%)
  [NOTE: Manager perceptions (M=12.30) significantly higher than
  employee perceptions (M=10.12): F=4.124, p<.001 — consistent
  with prior literature; managers consistently over-report HPWP
  implementation relative to employee experience]

CORRELATIONS:
  Formal HPWPs ↔ Commitment: r=0.28, p<.01
  No use HPWPs ↔ Commitment: r=−0.18, p<.01
  Unused HPWPs ↔ Commitment: r=−0.32, p<.01
  Commitment ↔ Job performance: r=0.19, p<.01

H1 — Commitment mediates Formal HPWPs → Job performance:
  Indirect effect: B=0.18; 95% CI [0.03, 0.35] — excludes zero
  ✓ SUPPORTED (positive mediation)
  Formal HPWP alignment → higher commitment → better performance

H2 — Commitment mediates No use of HPWPs → Job performance:
  Indirect effect: B=−0.19; 95% CI [−0.41, −0.03] — excludes zero
  ✓ SUPPORTED (negative mediation)
  Both parties recognise absence → lower commitment → worse performance

H3 — Commitment mediates Unused HPWPs → Job performance:
  Indirect effect: B=−0.23; 95% CI [−0.44, −0.06] — excludes zero
  ✓ SUPPORTED (negative mediation)
  Manager implements but employee does not experience → loss of trust
  → lower commitment → worse performance

H4 — Commitment mediates Informal HPWPs → Job performance:
  Indirect effect: B=0.07; 95% CI [−0.10, 0.26] — includes zero
  ✗ NOT SUPPORTED (non-significant)
  [Explanation: informal HPWPs represent only 7% of practices (low
  base); effect too small to detect via commitment pathway]

KEY FINDING — UNUSED HPWPs:
  The strongest negative mediation is for unused HPWPs (B=−0.23).
  When management systems (HPWPs) are implemented but employees do
  not perceive or experience them, this is MORE damaging than when
  neither party uses HPWPs at all. This is the most practically
  important finding: management systems that exist on paper but do
  not reach employees create more harm (trust erosion, commitment
  loss, performance decline) than having no systems at all.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO"), C12 provides
               the most directly applicable evidence for the thesis
               argument about management systems implementation
               quality. Three thesis-relevant contributions:

               (1) The implementation gap as a critical management
               systems risk: C12 demonstrates that unused HPWPs
               (strongest negative indirect effect: B=−0.23) are
               MORE damaging than no HPWPs. For GGHN's hybrid work
               context: if GGHN implements OMS and MIS for hybrid
               work but employees do not experience or use these
               systems (e.g., due to poor communication, inadequate
               training, or digital access gaps), the management
               systems will reduce rather than improve productivity.
               This is the most critical implementation risk for
               the thesis OMS design recommendation.

               (2) Alignment = the missing link in HPWP → performance:
               C12 shows that it is not the presence of HPWPs per se
               that drives performance, but the ALIGNMENT between
               what management implements and what employees
               experience. This extends the thesis argument: GGHN
               must not only design appropriate management systems
               (OMS + MIS) but ensure employee-level alignment —
               that employees perceive, understand, and use these
               systems. Communication and training (C06's dominant
               HPWP; C11's critical T&D mechanism) are the alignment
               tools.

               (3) Manager over-reporting: Managers consistently
               report higher HPWP implementation than employees
               experience (M=12.30 vs. 10.12; p<.001). For GGHN:
               line managers' assessments of hybrid work management
               system implementation cannot be taken at face value —
               employee-level perception audits are needed to verify
               actual alignment. This is a direct HR audit implication
               for the thesis recommendations chapter.

Limitation   : Netherlands only (high power-equality, high
               individualism context; distant from Nigeria); student-
               recruited dyads — potential selection bias; KR20 for
               manager scale only 0.627 (below 0.70 threshold);
               dichotomous scale for HPWPs (present/absent) loses
               nuance of degree of implementation; H4 not supported;
               cross-sectional; N=252 dyads — moderate size;
               informal HPWPs very rare (7%) limiting H4 power.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Journal of Business Research (JBR; Elsevier) — SSCI,
      Scopus indexed; ABS 3; high-prestige international journal
  [+] Dyadic design: manager-rated DV + employee-rated IV —
      reduces CMB substantially; novel dyadic approach in HRM
  [+] 78% response rate — excellent for dyadic survey
  [+] CFA (Mplus WLSMV): second-order AMO model; excellent fit
      (CFI>0.96; RMSEA<0.04 for both rater scales) ✓
  [+] PROCESS bootstrapping (5,000 samples; 95% CI) ✓
  [+] Unused HPWPs finding (strongest negative mediation B=−0.23)
      — novel and practically important
  [+] Conceptually rigorous four-type typology (formal/no use/
      unused/informal) — well-grounded in frames of reference
      theory and self-other rater theory
  [+] Manager over-reporting confirmed empirically (consistent
      with prior literature)
  [~] N=252 dyads — moderate; H4 underpowered
  [~] KR20=0.627 for manager scale — below 0.70 threshold;
      acknowledged by authors; limitation noted
  [~] Dichotomous HPWP scale — binary present/absent loses
      degree information
  [~] Netherlands only; student-recruited dyads
  [~] Cross-sectional; no causal inference

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C05 (Stirpe2022) — employee evaluation of HRPs:
  C05 found that employee SATISFACTION with HRPs (not just
  presence) drives engagement → performance. C12 now specifies
  the mechanism more precisely: it is ALIGNMENT between manager
  implementation and employee experience that determines whether
  management systems produce commitment and performance. Unused
  HPWPs (manager implements; employee does not experience) are
  WORSE than no HPWPs. C05 and C12 together establish: management
  systems must be both high-quality (C05) AND well-communicated/
  aligned (C12) to produce performance outcomes.

Convergence with C11 (Zhai2025) — T&D as alignment mechanism:
  C11 found that T&D is the critical HPWS component that
  internalises human capital and strengthens HPWS→OP. C12
  provides the micro-level explanation: T&D creates alignment
  (employee perceives and uses what manager implements); without
  T&D, unused HPWPs result (manager offers training but employee
  does not experience it as such). Together: T&D is both the
  critical ability-enhancing mechanism (C11) and the primary
  alignment tool (C12) within management systems.

Convergence with B22 (Saurombe2022) — PM systems gap:
  B22 found that PM systems were not suited to remote work in
  South Africa — employees worked longer hours to prove
  productivity (compensatory overwork). C12 provides the
  mechanism: this is an unused HPWP scenario — management
  implements PM systems that employees do not experience as
  supporting their work. The result (B22: overwork; C12: trust
  loss, lower commitment) is consistent. For GGHN: PM system
  implementation without employee alignment creates unused
  HPWPs — the most damaging management systems failure.

Convergence with C06 (Alqudah2022) — communication:
  C06 found communication/clear job description (β=0.601) was
  the dominant HPWP predictor of readiness for change — far
  stronger than training, appraisal, or participation. C12
  explains why: communication is the primary mechanism that
  converts implemented HPWPs from unused into formal (aligned).
  Without clear communication, management systems remain unused
  — confirming C06's empirical finding from a different
  theoretical angle.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Journal of Business Research (JBR): first JBR paper in corpus;
    Elsevier; ABS 3; SSCI
  - Dyadic research design: first line manager-employee dyadic
    study in corpus; novel methodology for HRM research
  - Four-type HPWP (mis)alignment typology: first formal
    (mis)alignment typology in corpus (formal/no use/unused/
    informal)
  - Frames of reference theory (Budd et al., 2022): first
    application in corpus; extends cognitive frames to HRM
  - Self- and other-rater theory: first explicit application
    in corpus for HR perceptions
  - Unused HPWPs as strongest negative mediator: first paper
    in corpus to demonstrate that manager-implements-but-
    employee-does-not-experience is MORE harmful than neither
    party using HPWPs
  - Manager over-reporting confirmed: first paper in corpus to
    empirically confirm (via ANOVA F-test) that managers
    report significantly more HPWPs than employees experience
    (M=12.30 vs. 10.12, p<.001)
  - KR20 reliability: first use of Kuder-Richardson Formula 20
    for dichotomous reliability in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Van Beurden, J., Van de Voorde, K., Van Veldhoven, M., & Jiang, K.
(2025). Do managers and employees see eye to eye? A dyadic perspective
on high-performance work practices and their impact on performance.
Journal of Business Research, 190, Article 115190.
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbusres.2025.115190
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C13
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C13
Cite key        : AlMamun2024
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — SRHRM operationalised via AMO:
                  employee selection/training [A], performance
                  management/compensation [M], flexible work design/
                  decision-making participation [O]; PGC as
                  opportunity-enabling climate; EGB as behavioural
                  outcome; AMO + Common Good HRM + SET),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — SRHRM as
                  integrated management system; PGC as contextual
                  enabler; Bangladesh manufacturing; developing economy)
Title           : Socially responsible human resource management for
                  sustainable performance in a moderated mediation
                  mechanism
Authors         : Al Mamun, A., Uddin, Md. A., Rana, T., Biswas, S.
                  R., & Dey, M.
Year            : 2024 (received 21 April 2024; revised 24 September
                  2024; accepted 28 October 2024; available online
                  2 November 2024; print 2024)
Journal         : Sustainable Futures (Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 8 (2024), Article 100362
DOI             : [10.1016/j.sftr.2024.100362](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.sftr.2024.100362)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : University of Chittagong, Bangladesh (Al Mamun,
                  Uddin, Biswas, Dey); RMIT University, Australia
                  (Rana); Washington State University, USA (Biswas)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  convenience sampling; SEM (AMOS v23; covariance-
                  based); bootstrapping (5,000 cases; 95% bias-
                  corrected CI); moderation via hierarchical regression
Aim             : To examine SRHRM → employee green behavior (EGB) →
                  sustainable performance (SP); and the role of
                  psychological green climate (PGC) as direct predictor
                  and moderator of SRHRM → EGB
Setting/context : Bangladesh; manufacturing sector (shipbuilding,
                  pharmaceuticals, tobacco, steel, leather); Dhaka and
                  Chattogram; 2024; developing economy context
Sample          : 435 distributed → 307 received (64.42% response
                  rate) → 302 retained (5 dropped); 73% male; 52%
                  postgraduate; 39% aged 30–40; mean tenure 9 years
IVs             : SRHRM — three dimensions (Shen & Zhu, 2011):
                  (1) Employee orientation HRM (flexible work, WLB,
                      participation, training)
                  (2) Legal compliance HRM (labour law, health/safety,
                      child labour prohibition)
                  (3) General CSR facilitation HRM (CSR staffing,
                      rewards, local hiring)
                  CR=0.882
Mediator        : Employee green behavior (EGB) — 7-item (Robertson
                  & Barling, 2013); CR=0.905
Moderator       : Psychological green climate (PGC) — 4-item (Norton
                  et al., 2017); CR=0.850; AVE=0.588
DV              : Sustainable performance (SP) — three dimensions
                  (Lee & Ha-Brookshire, 2017): financial (CR=0.826),
                  environmental, social performance
Controls        : Age; experience; organisational size; education;
                  gender
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000): SRHRM
frameworks        provides ability (training), motivation (rewards/
                  performance management), opportunity (participation/
                  green climate) → EGB → SP
                  Common Good HRM (Aust et al., 2020): SRHRM as
                  strategic vehicle for environmental common good
                  Social Exchange Theory (implied): PGC → reciprocity
                  → EGB

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CFA             : Standardised loadings all >0.700; AVE>0.500;
                  CR>0.800; Fornell-Larcker discriminant validity
                  confirmed (square root AVE > inter-construct
                  correlations) ✓
CMB             : Harman's single-factor test only (no factor >50%
                  variance) — acknowledged limitation; anonymity
                  and back-translation as procedural controls
                  [FLAG: Harman's test is weakest available CMB
                  check; single-source cross-sectional design remains
                  a concern]
SEM fit         : Not explicitly reported in full; path estimates and
                  R² provided; VIF<5 (no multicollinearity) ✓
Moderation      : Hierarchical regression (SPSS); interaction term
                  SRHRM × PGC in Model 4

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1 — SRHRM → Sustainable performance (SP):
  β=0.048, p=0.076 ✗ NOT SUPPORTED (p>0.05)
  [CRITICAL: SRHRM does NOT directly predict SP — only via EGB
  mediation. This is thesis-relevant: management systems alone
  are insufficient; they must change employee behaviour first]

H2 — SRHRM → Employee green behavior (EGB):
  β=0.246, t=5.381, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED

H3 — Employee green behavior → Sustainable performance:
  β=0.126, t=3.564, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED

H4 — Psychological green climate → Employee green behavior:
  β=0.546, t=7.496, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED
  [PGC is the STRONGEST predictor of EGB — stronger than SRHRM
  itself (β=0.546 vs. β=0.246)]

H5 — PGC MODERATES SRHRM → EGB:
  Interaction term β=0.202, p<0.10 ✗ NOT SUPPORTED (p>0.05)
  [Despite plotting showing higher PGC strengthening SRHRM→EGB,
  the interaction was not statistically significant at p<0.05]

MEDIATION PATTERN:
  Full mediation: SRHRM → EGB → SP (SRHRM→SP direct ns; indirect
  via EGB significant)
  SRHRM explains variance in EGB (β=0.246)
  EGB explains variance in SP (β=0.126)
  PGC is a stronger direct predictor of EGB than SRHRM (β=0.546)

CORRELATION MATRIX (key):
  EGB ↔ PGC: r=0.517***
  EGB ↔ Legal compliance (SoLC): r=0.318***
  EGB ↔ General facilitation (SoGF): r=0.358***
  EGB ↔ Employee orientation (SoEO): r=0.245***

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 SET"), C13 provides
               supporting evidence for AMO + SET applied to SHRM →
               employee behaviour → performance in a developing
               economy context. Two thesis-relevant contributions:

               (1) Full mediation: management systems → behaviour →
               performance: The finding that SRHRM has NO direct
               effect on SP (H1 not supported) but a fully mediated
               effect via EGB is directly parallel to the thesis
               argument: management systems (OMS/MIS) do not
               directly improve productivity — they do so by enabling
               employee behaviour (in the thesis: hybrid work
               engagement, task performance, reduced isolation).
               C13 provides an empirical illustration of this
               mediation logic in a developing economy HRM context.

               (2) Organisational climate (PGC) as stronger predictor
               than formal HR practices (β=0.546 vs. 0.246): This
               finding suggests that the psychological/cultural
               context created by management systems matters more
               than the formal practices themselves. For GGHN:
               creating a supportive hybrid work climate (analogous
               to PGC) through management systems may be more
               important than any individual OMS/MIS component.

               Cite with explicit caution flags (see below).

Limitation   : Bangladesh manufacturing only — high contextual
               distance from Nigeria/GGHN NGO health; single-source
               cross-sectional CMB (Harman only); green HRM focus
               is contextually peripheral to hybrid work/productivity
               thesis; sustainable performance (economic/social/
               environmental) ≠ individual employee task productivity;
               Sustainable Futures journal — newer, lower-standing
               than most corpus outlets; H1 and H5 not supported —
               partial model confirmation; convenience sampling.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Developing economy context (Bangladesh) — provides
      geographic/institutional proximity to thesis context
      compared to Western studies
  [+] SEM (AMOS covariance-based) — more rigorous than OLS;
      CFA with Fornell-Larcker discriminant validity ✓
  [+] 64.42% response rate; n=302 — adequate for SEM ✓

  [+] AMO + Common Good HRM + SET multi-framework integration
  [+] VIF<5; no multicollinearity concerns ✓
  [+] Full mediation finding (SRHRM→EGB→SP) is theoretically
      important and directly applicable to thesis argument
  [~] Single-source cross-sectional; Harman's test only for CMB
  [~] Sustainable Futures — newer Elsevier journal; Scopus but
      not yet SSCI indexed at time of publication
  [~] Bangladesh manufacturing only — sectoral and geographic
      distance from GGHN
  [~] H1 and H5 not supported — partial model

      but not identical to hybrid work/employee productivity
  [~] Convenience sampling; no representativeness guarantee

      — acknowledged limitation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C04 (Omolo2026) — AMO + SET synthesis:
  C04 provided the narrative SLR synthesis of AMO + SET + SDT
  applied to green HRM in an East African context. C13 provides
  an empirical test of AMO + Common Good HRM in Bangladesh. Both
  confirm: in developing economy contexts, the opportunity
  dimension of AMO (PGC in C13; participatory practices in C04)
  is the strongest predictor of employee green/pro-environmental
  behaviour. Together they establish an AMO + SET developing
  economy green HRM pattern — parallel to the thesis AMO + SET
  management systems → hybrid work → productivity argument.

Full mediation convergence across Block C:
  C05 (Stirpe2022): HRP satisfaction → engagement → extra-role
  performance (full mediation for extra-role)
  C13 (AlMamun2024): SRHRM → EGB → SP (full mediation; SRHRM→SP
  direct ns)
  Both confirm: management systems do not directly produce
  performance outcomes — they do so via employee psychological/
  behavioural mediators (engagement in C05; green behaviour in
  C13). This is precisely the thesis mediation logic: management
  systems → hybrid work → productivity.

Convergence with C03 (Jakpa2025) — developing economy SHRM:
  C03 found SHRM directly predicts well-being and change capacity
  in Nigeria (β=0.781; β=0.68) — direct effects supported. C13
  finds SRHRM does NOT directly predict SP in Bangladesh (H1 ns);
  only via EGB. This divergence between C03 and C13 is itself
  informative: the direct vs. mediated pattern may vary by
  outcome (well-being vs. SP) and context. The thesis should
  acknowledge: for employee-level productivity outcomes (thesis
  DV), a fully mediated pathway (management systems → hybrid
  work behaviour → productivity) is more likely than a direct
  management systems → productivity effect.

PGC as dominant predictor (β=0.546) — climate over practices:
  C13's finding that organisational climate (PGC) is a stronger
  predictor of employee behaviour than formal HR practices
  (β=0.546 vs. 0.246) converges with C12 (VanBeurden2025):
  what employees experience (alignment; climate) matters more
  than what management formally implements. For GGHN: the hybrid
  work climate created by management systems — whether employees
  experience the OMS/MIS as supportive and enabling — is the
  critical determinant of productivity outcomes.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Sustainable Futures (Elsevier): first Sustainable Futures
    paper in corpus; newest journal in corpus
  - SRHRM (Socially Responsible HRM): first explicit SRHRM
    construct in corpus; extends green HRM (C04) with CSR/ethics
  - Common Good HRM framework (Aust et al., 2020): first Common
    Good HRM application in corpus
  - Employee green behavior (EGB): first EGB as DV in an
    empirical paper in corpus (C04 discussed it in SLR)
  - Bangladesh context: first Bangladesh study in corpus;
    extends developing economy evidence beyond Nigeria/East Africa
  - Full mediation with non-significant direct effect: first
    paper in corpus where the direct management systems→performance
    path is explicitly non-significant (H1 rejected; β=0.048,
    p=0.076) while full mediation via behaviour is confirmed
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C14
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C14
Cite key        : Demerouti2025
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — COR and
                  JD-R as competing and complementary theoretical
                  frameworks for understanding how job demands and
                  resources affect well-being and performance; hybrid
                  work and remote work as future job design context;
                  technology integration in job design),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — JD-R
                  health impairment process [demands → burnout];
                  motivational process [resources → engagement →
                  performance]; job crafting as proactive resource
                  management; future job design implications)
Title           : Job demands-resources and conservation of resources
                  theories: How do they help to explain employee
                  well-being and future job design?
Authors         : Demerouti, E.
Year            : 2025 (received 15 May 2024; revised 12 February
                  2025; accepted 2 March 2025; available online
                  10 March 2025; print 2025)
Journal         : Journal of Business Research (JBR; Elsevier)
                  [Special issue: JBR 50]
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 192 (2025), Article 115296
DOI             : [10.1016/j.jbusres.2025.115296](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbusres.2025.115296)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Eindhoven University of Technology, Netherlands

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Theoretical review / conceptual paper; invited
                  contribution to JBR 50th anniversary special issue;
                  single-authored by one of the originators of JD-R
                  theory (Demerouti et al., 2001)
Aim             : To compare COR theory (Hobfoll, 1989) and JD-R
                  theory (Demerouti et al., 2001) in terms of shared
                  features, differences, unresolved issues, and
                  implications for future job design; to propose
                  predictions about how job demands and resources will
                  evolve in technology-rich and hybrid/remote work
                  contexts
Scope           : COR theory propositions and corollaries; JD-R
                  theory seven core propositions; comparison table;
                  future research agenda; future job design
                  implications; technology (AI/automation) effects on
                  demands and resources; hybrid/remote work

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY THEORETICAL CONTENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
COR THEORY (Hobfoll, 1989; 2001):
  Core: individuals strive to acquire, protect, and maximise
  valued resources (objects, states, personal characteristics,
  energies) to prevent psychological distress and burnout
  Principles: (1) loss > gain in salience; (2) invest resources
  to prevent loss; (3) resource scarcity amplifies value of
  acquisition; (4) resource depletion → defensive behaviour
  Resource caravans: resources exist in interconnected networks
  — supportive environments (leadership, flexible work, training)
  generate resource caravan passageways; toxic environments
  deplete them
  Resource loss/gain spirals: those with more resources are less
  vulnerable to loss and more able to accumulate further
  Limitation: high abstraction; difficult to operationalise,
  test, or falsify; demands virtually absent; fatalistic view of
  loss spirals

JD-R THEORY (Demerouti et al., 2001; Bakker & Demerouti, 2017):
  Core: all job characteristics can be classified as demands
  (sustained effort → physiological/psychological costs) or
  resources (goal achievement, demand reduction, growth/learning)
  Seven propositions:
  P1: Resources aid goal achievement, mitigate demands, foster growth
  P2: Dual process — health impairment (demands→burnout) and
      motivational (resources→engagement→performance)
  P3: Demands and resources jointly moderate well-being: buffering
      hypothesis (resources reduce demand-strain) and boosting
      hypothesis (demands enhance resource-engagement effect)
  P4: Personal resources have reciprocal relationships with job
      resources
  P5: Personal resources mitigate the demand → strain relationship
  P6: High engagement → proactive behaviours (job crafting) →
      gain cycle of resources
  P7: High strain → dysfunctional behaviours (self-undermining) →
      loss cycle of demands
  Meta-analytic support: Alarcon (2011): high demands + low
  resources → burnout; Mazzetti et al. (2023): job + personal
  resources → engagement; Lesener et al. (2019): reciprocal
  demand/resource relationships over time confirmed

SIMILARITIES (COR and JD-R — eight shared features):
  (1) Resources shape well-being and performance
  (2) Both explain stress/impaired health
  (3) Resources buffer stressors/demands
  (4) Dynamic relationship between resources and outcomes
  (5) Optimization of resources (proactively and reactively)
  (6) Emphasis on objective nature of demands/events
  (7) Resources related over time (spirals)
  (8) Practical implications for organizations

KEY DIFFERENCES (COR vs. JD-R — see Table 1):
  Focus: COR = resource preservation; JD-R = job characteristics
  Scope: COR = broader (beyond work); JD-R = work-focused
  Stress process: COR = threat/loss of resources; JD-R = demand
  Level of analysis: COR = individual; JD-R = individual + org
  Demands: COR = absent (demands = resource investments); JD-R =
    central (health impairment pathway)
  Specificity: COR = abstract; JD-R = specific and testable
  Behaviour: COR = self-regulation (defensive); JD-R = job
    crafting (proactive) / self-undermining (destructive)
  JD-R is NOT a subset of COR — distinct elements include: role
  of demands; specific mediators (burnout, engagement); moderators
  (personal resources); active individual behaviours

HYBRID/REMOTE WORK AND FUTURE JOB DESIGN:
  Remote work increases some demands (work-family conflict,
  surveillance/monitoring, administrative tasks) while providing
  some resources (flexibility in time and location)
  Flexibility: employer-led (overtime/restrictions) = demand;
  employee-led (control over start/end time) = resource
  [KEY DISTINCTION for GGHN hybrid work model: employee-controlled
  flexibility is a resource; employer-mandated schedule is a
  demand. GGHN's hybrid work arrangements must give employees
  meaningful control over scheduling to function as job resources]

  Future resource emphasis: development/learning opportunities
  and flexibility as the two resources most likely to increase
  with technology; social resources at risk if technology
  becomes dominant

  Resource substitution hypothesis (Ross & Mirowsky, 2010;
  Demerouti & Bakker, 2023): buffering potency of one resource is
  stronger among those who lack other resources — resources can
  substitute for each other
  [KEY FOR GGHN: where employees lack one resource (e.g., strong
  supervisor support in hybrid work), other resources (peer
  connection via MIS; digital collaboration tools) can substitute]

  Objective vs. perceived demands/resources: objective and
  subjective measures capture related but different information;
  moderate correlations (emotional demands r=0.53; physical
  demands r=0.63; autonomy r=0.46)
  [FLAG for thesis methodology: if GGHN collects only employee
  self-report data, this is subjective — acknowledge that
  objective demands may differ]

FUTURE JOB DESIGN IMPLICATIONS (Table 3):
  Dynamic job design: adaptive structures adjust demands and
  resources based on evolving capabilities
  Proactive job design: job crafting; bottom-up employee input
  Human-centred design: prioritise well-being, autonomy, skill
  development, healthy work-life balance
  Prospective job design: anticipate future demands and design
  for resource conservation before problems arise
  Enhancing resources: AI-personalised training; flexible work
  Mitigating demands: technology reduces repetitive tasks;
  transparency in monitoring; ergonomic support
  Promoting resource fit: match demands with appropriate resources
  (emotional demands → social support; cognitive demands →
  decision-support tools)
  Proactive behaviours: encourage job crafting; empower employees
  to adjust demands and resources

UNRESOLVED ISSUES (for future research — Table 2):
  Challenge/hindrance demand distinction still debated
  Tipping points for resources (too much autonomy = ambiguity)
  Temporal matching of demands with appropriate resources
  Resource substitutability across contexts
  Falsifiability of both theories
  Integration of AI/technology as dual demand and resource

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 COR review"), C14
               provides the most comprehensive single-authored
               comparison of COR and JD-R available from one of
               JD-R's originators. Five thesis contributions:

               (1) JD-R as the primary theoretical lens for hybrid
               work and management systems: C14 confirms JD-R (not
               COR) as the more specific, operationalisable, and
               testable framework for understanding how management
               systems (job resources) reduce job demands and enable
               employee performance. Supports the thesis decision
               to use JD-R alongside AMO.

               (2) Hybrid/remote work within JD-R: C14 directly
               addresses remote work and flexibility in the JD-R
               framework. Flexibility is a resource when
               employee-led, a demand when employer-imposed. This
               is the core theoretical justification for the thesis
               argument that GGHN's hybrid work arrangements must
               preserve employee agency to function as a resource.

               (3) Resource caravan passageways (COR): supportive
               leadership, flexible work, and training create
               resource caravan passageways. For GGHN: the OMS and
               MIS proposed in the thesis are precisely the kinds
               of structural conditions that create passageways for
               employee resource development.

               (4) Resource substitution: where employees lack one
               resource, another can substitute. For GGHN's hybrid
               context: digital collaboration tools (MIS) can
               substitute for face-to-face social support lost in
               remote work days — this is the resource substitution
               rationale for investing in MIS.

               (5) Future job design agenda aligns with thesis
               recommendations: human-centred design, proactive job
               design, promoting resource-demand fit, and
               encouraging job crafting are directly applicable to
               GGHN's hybrid work management system design.
               C14 provides the leading theoretical authority
               (Demerouti herself) for these recommendations.

Limitation   : Theoretical/conceptual review only — no original
               empirical data; limited generalisability of specific
               claims to Nigeria/GGHN without empirical grounding;
               Netherlands academic context (Eindhoven); technology-
               focused predictions may not fully apply to NGO
               health sector with limited tech infrastructure;
               no Africa/developing economy consideration.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Journal of Business Research (JBR; Elsevier) — SSCI, ABS 3;
      50th anniversary special issue; high-prestige invited
      contribution
  [+] Single-authored by Evangelia Demerouti — co-originator of
      JD-R theory (Demerouti et al., 2001); highest possible
      theoretical authority on the subject
  [+] Comprehensive COR vs. JD-R comparison with formal
      differences table — definitive reference for thesis
      theoretical framework chapter
  [+] Direct treatment of remote/hybrid work, flexible
      arrangements, and technology within JD-R framework
  [+] Resource substitution hypothesis formalised — directly
      applicable to GGHN MIS justification
  [+] Future job design implications (Table 3) provide a
      framework for thesis recommendations
  [+] Open access CC BY 4.0
  [~] Conceptual review — no original empirical data; citations
      to own prior empirical work for support
  [~] Netherlands/Western European context; no developing economy
      consideration
  [~] Technology predictions (AI/automation) may outpace GGHN's
      current infrastructure realities

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C10 (Dorta-Afonso2025) — JD-R health impairment:
  C10 empirically tested HPWS as job resources reducing burnout
  via challenge/hindrance demand reduction in JD-R. C14 provides
  the theoretical grounding for exactly this pathway from the
  theory's originator. Together: C14 gives the theory; C10
  gives the HPWS-specific empirical evidence. The thesis can
  cite C14 for the theoretical framework and C10 for empirical
  support of how management systems (HPWS) operate within it.

Convergence with C04 (Omolo2026) — COR in developing economies:
  C04 applied COR as a supporting theory in an East African
  green HRM context. C14 clarifies COR's limitations
  (abstractness; fatalistic spirals; absence of demands) and
  argues JD-R is the more operationalisable framework. The
  thesis should use COR as a supplementary theory (for resource
  preservation motivation) and JD-R as the primary framework
  — exactly as C04 and C14 together suggest.

Convergence with B07/B10 (COR in hybrid work, earlier corpus):
  Earlier Block B papers used COR to explain resource loss in
  remote work. C14 now provides the definitive comparison:
  JD-R is more specific for work-domain predictions; COR
  supplements with resource caravan / spiral insights. The
  thesis can integrate both with C14 as the authoritative
  source for their similarities and differences.

Employee-led flexibility as resource (C14) vs. employer-imposed:
  C14 explicitly distinguishes: flexibility as employer-led
  (demand) vs. employee-led (resource). This is the key
  theoretical distinction for GGHN's hybrid work model —
  schedule autonomy (employee-led) is a resource; mandatory
  remote days (employer-imposed) may be a demand. This is the
  most precise theoretical claim in the corpus for GGHN's
  hybrid work operationalisation.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Single-authored theoretical review: first in corpus from
    a named theory originator (Demerouti co-created JD-R in 2001)
  - Resource substitution hypothesis (Ross & Mirowsky, 2010):
    first formal treatment in corpus; distinct from C10's
    challenge/hindrance distinction
  - Resource caravan passageways: first explicit treatment in
    corpus (C04 mentioned resource caravans informally)
  - Employee-led vs. employer-led flexibility distinction:
    first explicit formalisation in corpus (Kattenbach et al.,
    2010 cited in C14)
  - Self-undermining behaviour (JD-R P7): first definition in
    corpus; counterpart to job crafting (P6)
  - Tipping point for resources: first concept in corpus that
    too much of a resource (e.g., high autonomy → ambiguity)
    can become a demand — nuances the prevailing assumption
    that more resources are always better
  - JBR 50 special issue: first special-issue invited paper
    in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Demerouti, E. (2025). Job demands-resources and conservation of
resources theories: How do they help to explain employee well-being
and future job design? Journal of Business Research, 192, Article
115296. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbusres.2025.115296
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C15
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C15
Cite key        : Kakakhel2022
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HPWS conceptualised via AMO:
                  selection [A], training [A], incentives [M],
                  performance management [M], empowerment [O], job
                  design [O]; AMO + SET; social capital as mediator
                  in HPWS → performance black box),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HPWS as
                  HR system; social capital as structural/relational/
                  cognitive mediating mechanism; HPWS → innovation
                  via social capital; Pakistan banking; developing
                  economy)
Title           : Deciphering the black box of HPWS–innovation link:
                  Modeling the mediatory role of internal social capital
Authors         : Kakakhel, F. J., & Khalil, S. H.
Year            : 2022 (received 1 December 2021; revised 4 February
                  2022; accepted 17 February 2022; available online
                  25 April 2022)
Journal         : International Journal of Innovation Studies (IJIS;
                  KeAi/Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 6, Issue 2 (2022), pp. 78–91
DOI             : [10.1016/j.ijis.2022.04.004](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijis.2022.04.004)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Abdul Wali Khan University, Mardan, Pakistan

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional survey;
                  PLS-SEM (SmartPLS 3); disjoint two-stage approach
                  for second-order formative construct; PLSPredict;
                  bootstrapping for mediation
Aim             : To investigate the black box of HPWS → radical
                  innovation (RI) and incremental innovation (II)
                  in Pakistan's banking sector, with internal social
                  capital (SC) as mediator; drawing on AMO + SET
Setting/context : Pakistan; banking sector (27 banks); data collected
                  November–December 2020; developing economy; simple
                  random sampling; self-administered questionnaires
Sample          : 570 targeted; 328 received and useable
                  (57.54% response rate)
IVs             : HPWS — 6-dimension second-order formative construct
                  (27 items): selection, training/development,
                  incentives, empowerment, performance management,
                  job design; items from Ang et al. (2013),
                  Delery & Doty (1996), Zacharatos et al. (2005)
Mediator        : Internal social capital (SC) — 5-item reflective
                  (Subramaniam & Youndt, 2005); CR=0.921; AVE=0.700+
DVs             : Incremental innovation (II) — 3-item; CR=0.904
                  Radical innovation (RI) — 3-item; CR=0.915
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000): HPWS provides
frameworks        ability (training/selection), motivation (incentives/
                  PM), opportunity (empowerment/job design) → SC →
                  innovation
                  Social Exchange Theory (Blau, 1964; Hayton et al.,
                  2013): HPWS investment → employee reciprocity →
                  discretionary behaviour → SC → innovation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Measurement     : Reflective LOCs: all loadings >0.7; CR>0.7;
model             AVE>0.5; HTMT<0.85 — outer model validated ✓
                  Formative HOC (HPWS): redundancy analysis path
                  coefficient=0.866 (>0.700); all outer weights
                  significant (p=0.000); all VIF<5 ✓
Structural      : Inner VIF<5; R²(SC)=0.542; R²(II)=0.457;
model             R²(RI)=0.213; Q²>0 for all endogenous variables;
                  PLSPredict: Q²_predict>0; PLS-MAE<LM-MAE
                  (most indicators) — moderate predictive power ✓
CMB             : FLAG — no CMB controls explicitly reported;
                  single-source (self-administered, same banks);
                  no Harman test, no procedural remedies documented
                  — this is a significant methodological limitation

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
H1 — HPWS → Radical innovation (RI):
  β=0.109, p=0.335 ✗ NOT SUPPORTED (direct effect ns)

H2 — HPWS → Incremental innovation (II):
  β=0.455, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED

H3 — HPWS → Social capital (SC):
  β=0.736, p<.001 ✓ SUPPORTED (large effect; R²SC=0.542)

H4 — SC → Incremental innovation:
  β=0.267, p=.004 ✓ SUPPORTED

H5 — SC → Radical innovation:
  β=0.352, p=.003 ✓ SUPPORTED

H6 — SC mediates HPWS → II (PARTIAL mediation):
  Indirect: β=0.197, p=.018; CI [0.079, 0.369] ✓ SUPPORTED
  [Direct HPWS→II significant + indirect significant → partial]

H7 — SC mediates HPWS → RI (FULL mediation):
  Indirect: β=0.259, p=.011; CI [0.098, 0.470] ✓ SUPPORTED
  [Direct HPWS→RI ns + indirect via SC significant → full]

KEY PATTERN:
  HPWS → SC (β=0.736***): strong; HPWS builds social capital
  Social capital → RI (β=0.352**) stronger than SC → II (β=0.267**)
  Full mediation via SC for radical innovation (black box confirmed)
  Partial mediation via SC for incremental innovation
  HPWS is the strongest predictor of SC (R²=54.2%)
  Developmental practices are the highest-weight HPWS component
  (outer weight=0.270) — ability-enhancing practices dominate

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO mediation"),
               C15 provides empirical evidence for the black-box
               mediation argument within AMO + SET applied to HPWS
               in a developing economy. Three thesis contributions:

               (1) Full mediation confirms management systems do
               not directly produce performance outcomes — they do
               so via employee social/relational mechanisms. The
               pattern parallels the thesis argument: management
               systems (OMS/MIS) → hybrid work behaviours
               (productivity mediator) → employee productivity.
               C15 adds to the full mediation evidence base from
               C05 and C13, establishing a consistent pattern.

               (2) HPWS → social capital (β=0.736) — management
               systems build social connections between employees.
               For GGHN's hybrid work context: MIS tools that
               enable communication and knowledge sharing build
               the social capital that mediates productivity
               outcomes. This is a direct application of the
               social capital pathway.

               (3) Developing economy banking context: Pakistan
               banking sector (27 banks) provides a non-Western,
               developing economy empirical base consistent with
               the thesis's argument that management systems
               matter in contexts with lower institutional
               infrastructure — parallel to C11's finding that
               HPWS has stronger effects in low human capital
               countries.

               Cite with caution flags (CMB, single source,
               innovation DV ≠ individual productivity).

Limitation   : Pakistan banking only; 2020 COVID data; single-
               source; no CMB controls; IJIS journal lower standing;
               innovation DV ≠ individual employee productivity;
               cross-sectional; AMO + SET only; no hybrid work
               relevance; social capital mediator ≠ hybrid work
               mediator but provides analogical black-box support.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] PLS-SEM with SmartPLS; disjoint two-stage approach for
      formative HOC; PLSPredict conducted ✓
  [+] 57.54% response rate; n=328 — adequate for PLS-SEM ✓
  [+] HTMT<0.85 for all LOCs; AVE>0.5; CR>0.7 ✓
  [+] VIF<5 (outer and inner); no multicollinearity ✓
  [+] Bootstrapped mediation analysis ✓
  [+] Full mediation confirmation (HPWS→RI via SC) with CI
      not containing zero ✓
  [+] Developing economy context; AMO + SET integration;
      services sector (extends manufacturing-dominated HPWS)
  [-] No CMB controls documented — significant limitation;
      single-source, self-report, same banking organisation
  [~] IJIS (KeAi/Elsevier); Scopus but not SSCI indexed
  [~] Pakistan banking only; 2020 COVID data; cross-sectional
  [~] Innovation DVs (RI, II) ≠ individual productivity
  [~] H1 (HPWS→RI direct) not supported — partial confirmation
  [~] Erratum notices appended (ethics statement addendum only;
      does not affect findings)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Black-box mediation pattern across Block C:
  C05 (Stirpe2022): HRP satisfaction → engagement → performance
    (full mediation for extra-role)
  C13 (AlMamun2024): SRHRM → EGB → SP (full mediation; direct ns)
  C15 (Kakakhel2022): HPWS → SC → RI (full mediation; direct ns)
  Three papers, three countries, three different mediators
  (engagement; green behaviour; social capital) all confirm the
  same pattern: management systems (HPWS/SHRM) do not directly
  produce organisational performance — they do so via employee
  behavioural/social mediators. This is the most important
  convergence in Block C for the thesis mediation logic.

Convergence with C12 (VanBeurden2025) — social capital and alignment:
  C12 found that unused HPWPs (manager implements; employee does
  not experience) destroy commitment via trust erosion. C15 finds
  that HPWS builds social capital (β=0.736) which then enables
  innovation. Together: management systems must be both well-
  implemented (C12) and trust-building (C15) to generate the
  social capital that mediates performance outcomes.

AMO ability-dimension primacy confirmed again:
  Developmental practices (training/development) carry the
  highest outer weight (0.270) in the formative HPWS construct —
  consistent with C11 (T&D only significant HPWS moderator),
  C06 (training β=0.211), and C05 (training → engagement). Five
  papers now converge on training/development as the dominant
  AMO-A component.

Convergence with C11 (Zhai2025) — developing economy context:
  C11 found HPWS→OP stronger in low human capital countries;
  T&D critical in low-HC contexts. C15 provides country-level
  corroboration from Pakistan (a low-HC economy): HPWS is a
  significant driver of SC and innovation in a developing economy
  banking context, consistent with C11's meta-analytic finding.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - International Journal of Innovation Studies (IJIS): first
    IJIS paper in corpus; KeAi/Elsevier; Scopus only
  - Social capital as HPWS mediator: first paper in corpus to
    empirically test internal social capital as the mediating
    mechanism in the HPWS → performance black box
  - Radical vs. incremental innovation: first explicit
    radical/incremental innovation typology in corpus as DVs
  - Full mediation via SC: HPWS→RI fully mediated (direct ns);
    consistent with C13 full mediation pattern; third full
    mediation paper in Block C
  - Formative second-order HPWS construct: C10 used second-
    order HPWS (PLS); C15 explicitly uses disjoint two-stage
    approach with formative HOC — first formative HPWS in corpus
  - Pakistan context: first Pakistan study in corpus

APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Kakakhel, F. J., & Khalil, S. H. (2022). Deciphering the black box
of HPWS–innovation link: Modeling the mediatory role of internal
social capital. International Journal of Innovation Studies, 6(2),
78–91. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.ijis.2022.04.004
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C16
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C16
Cite key        : Wojtczuk-Turek2021
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HPWS operationalised via AMO:
                  skill-enhancing [recruitment, selection, training],
                  motivation-enhancing [performance appraisal,
                  compensation, promotion, career development],
                  opportunity-enhancing [job design, work teams,
                  employee involvement, information sharing];
                  affective commitment as AMO motivational mediator;
                  self-efficacy as moderator),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HPWS as
                  integrated HR system; evaluative vs. descriptive
                  HPWP perception; KIBS knowledge workers; Poland;
                  mediated-moderation design)
Title           : HPWSs and employee performance in KIBS companies:
                  a mediating–moderating analysis
Authors         : Wojtczuk-Turek, A., & Turek, D.
Year            : 2021 (received 24 April 2020; revised/accepted
                  15 February 2021; published Vol. 16, No. 3, 2021)
Journal         : Baltic Journal of Management (BJM; Emerald)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 16, No. 3, 2021, pp. 345–365
DOI             : [10.1108/BJM-03-2020-0081](https://doi.org/10.1108/BJM-03-2020-0081)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : Warsaw School of Economics, Poland (both authors)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; cross-sectional; CATI
                  (computer-assisted telephone interview); PROCESS
                  macro (Hayes 2018; ver. 3.3); CFA (AMOS v25);
                  mediated-moderation analysis; bootstrapping
                  (5,000 samples; 95% CI)
Aim             : To investigate how HPWS influences productive and
                  counterproductive work behaviours via affective
                  commitment (mediator) and self-efficacy (moderator)
                  in KIBS sector knowledge workers
Setting/context : Poland; Knowledge-Intensive Business Service (KIBS)
                  sector — IT/software (code 62), information services
                  (63), management consultants (70), architecture/
                  engineering (71), R&D (72), technical analysis (73),
                  other professional activities (74); March–June 2018
Sample          : n=563; CATI method; random sampling from KIBS firms;
                  medium and large companies only; 67% medium; 26%
                  large; 43% aged 26–35; 91% university educated;
                  88% >5 years experience; 53% female
IVs             : HPWS — 25-item evaluative perception scale (based
                  on Huselid 1995; Fu et al. 2015); 3 AMO bundles;
                  CFA: χ²=138.998(85), p=.026; RMSEA=.045; CFI=.974;
                  TLI=.955; SRMR=.049; α=0.87
Mediator        : Affective commitment — 6-item (Allen & Meyer, 1996);
                  α=0.83
Moderator       : Self-efficacy — 6-item (Bandura, 2006); α=0.75
DVs             : Productive work behaviours — 12-item; 3 dimensions
                  (innovative, proactive, pro-social); CFA: χ²=65.591
                  (48), p=.046; RMSEA=.026; CFI=.987; TLI=.982 ✓
                  Counterproductive work behaviours — 8-item
                  (Bennett & Robinson, 2000); α=0.79
Controls        : Education; sex; age; job seniority
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000; Jiang et al.,
frameworks        2012): HPWS → ability + motivation + opportunity →
                  commitment → productive/counterproductive behaviours
                  Bandura's self-efficacy theory: self-efficacy as
                  moderator of HPWS → performance; high SE = less
                  dependent on HPWS for productive behaviour;
                  high SE = more responsive to HPWS for reducing
                  counterproductive behaviour

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CMB             : Harman's single-factor test (no factor >50%) +
                  Common Latent Factor (CLF) test (ΔR²<0.20) — both
                  confirm CMB not problematic ✓
                  [Better CMB control than C13/C15; two methods used]
CFA             : Baseline five-factor model fits data well:
                  χ²=956.391(632), p=.075; RMSEA=.045; CFI=.994;
                  TLI=.971; SRMR=.020 — superior to all nested models
Mediation       : PROCESS Model 4 (Hayes, 2018); bootstrapped CI
                  (5,000 samples); indirect effects reported
Moderation      : Interaction terms HPWS × Self-efficacy; interaction
                  plots for both productive and counterproductive DVs
R²              : Productive behaviours: ΔR²=0.47 (large)
                  Counterproductive behaviours: ΔR²=0.08 (small)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY CORRELATIONS:
  HPWS ↔ Productive behaviours: r=0.45, p<.01
  HPWS ↔ Counterproductive behaviours: r=−0.21, p<.01
  HPWS ↔ Affective commitment: r=0.47, p<.01
  HPWS ↔ Self-efficacy: r=0.30, p<.01
  Affective commitment ↔ Productive: r=0.36, p<.01
  Affective commitment ↔ Counterproductive: r=−0.18, p<.01
  Self-efficacy ↔ Productive: r=0.49, p<.01
  Self-efficacy ↔ Counterproductive: r=−0.13, p<.01

H1a — HPWS → Productive work behaviours (direct):
  β=0.10, p<.01 ✓ SUPPORTED

H1b — HPWS → Counterproductive work behaviours (direct):
  β=−0.08, p<.01 ✓ SUPPORTED (negative; HPWS reduces CWB)

H2a — Affective commitment MEDIATES HPWS → Productive:
  Indirect: β=0.05; 95% CI [0.03, 0.06] — excludes zero
  ✓ SUPPORTED (partial mediation — direct also significant)

H2b — Affective commitment MEDIATES HPWS → Counterproductive:
  Indirect: β=−0.03; 95% CI [−0.05, −0.01] — excludes zero
  ✓ SUPPORTED (partial mediation)

H3a — Self-efficacy MODERATES HPWS → Productive:
  Interaction: β=−0.09, p<.01; CI [−0.14, −0.04] ✓ SUPPORTED
  KEY: Low SE employees show STRONGER HPWS→productive effect;
  High SE employees maintain performance regardless of HPWS
  [High SE = self-sufficient; HPWS supplements low-SE employees]

H3b — Self-efficacy MODERATES HPWS → Counterproductive:
  Interaction: β=−0.08, p<.05; CI [−0.15, −0.01] ✓ SUPPORTED
  KEY: High SE employees reduce CWB MORE in response to HPWS;
  Low SE employees do NOT reduce CWB even with strong HPWS
  [High SE = amplifies HPWS's protective effect against CWB]

MOST IMPORTANT FINDINGS FOR THESIS:
  (1) Affective commitment mediates HPWS → both productive AND
  counterproductive behaviours — partial mediation confirmed
  (2) Self-efficacy creates ASYMMETRIC moderation: high SE
  employees are self-sufficient for productive behaviour but
  HPWS-dependent for reducing counterproductive behaviour;
  low SE employees depend heavily on HPWS for productive
  behaviour but are immune to HPWS's CWB-reducing effect
  (3) HPWS is evaluated by employees (evaluative, not descriptive
  measure) — employee evaluation of management systems quality
  matters; positive evaluation → commitment → performance
  (4) KIBS finding: in knowledge-intensive service contexts,
  job design and empowerment may matter more than training
  for performance — different AMO dimension primacy from
  C11 (manufacturing/general; T&D dominant)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 AMO mediation-
               moderation"), C16 provides the most methodologically
               complete AMO mediation-moderation analysis in the
               corpus. Four thesis contributions:

               (1) Affective commitment as AMO motivational mediator:
               C16 confirms that HPWS → affective commitment →
               productive behaviour (partial mediation). For GGHN:
               management systems (OMS/MIS) that build employee
               commitment to the hybrid work model are the pathway
               to productivity — not direct HR system effects. This
               parallels the thesis hybrid work mediation argument.

               (2) Self-efficacy as boundary condition: high-SE
               employees are self-sufficient (HPWS adds less value
               for their productive behaviour) but HPWS is crucial
               for reducing their counterproductive behaviour. For
               GGHN: management systems matter differently across
               employees — experienced, high-confidence health
               workers may need HPWS less for task performance but
               benefit more from HPWS preventing disengagement.

               (3) Evaluative HPWP perception: C16 uses evaluative
               (not descriptive) HPWP measures — employee evaluation
               of whether management systems meet their expectations.
               This is precisely what the thesis argues: GGHN
               employees' subjective experience of OMS/MIS quality
               determines whether management systems enable or
               hinder productivity. Aligns with C12 (alignment gap)
               and C14 (perceived vs. received resources).

               (4) Service sector context: KIBS knowledge workers in
               Poland share structural characteristics with GGHN's
               knowledge-intensive NGO health professionals — both
               involve complex, non-routine, client-facing tasks where
               empowerment and autonomy may matter more than training.

Limitation   : Poland KIBS only (high power equality, collective work
               culture; different institutional context from Nigeria);
               single-source CATI; cross-sectional; Harman + CLF
               (better than Harman only but not as strong as multi-
               source); BJM is regionally focused Eastern European
               journal; counterproductive behaviours ΔR²=0.08 (weak);
               data collected 2018 (pre-COVID; before widespread hybrid
               work); KIBS culture may not transfer to Nigerian NGO.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Baltic Journal of Management (BJM; Emerald) — Scopus
      indexed; ESCI (Emerging Sources Citation Index); rigorous
      peer-reviewed management journal
  [+] n=563 — largest sample in Block C knowledge worker study
  [+] CATI method — structured interviews reduce social
      desirability vs. self-administered; professional context
  [+] Two-method CMB control (Harman + CLF) ✓
  [+] CFA with AMOS: excellent baseline model fit (CFI=.994;
      RMSEA=.045); five nested model comparisons ✓
  [+] PROCESS bootstrapping (5,000 samples; 95% CI) ✓
  [+] Both productive AND counterproductive DVs tested — broader
      outcome scope than most corpus papers
  [+] Self-efficacy moderator produces novel asymmetric findings
  [+] Evaluative HPWP perception explicitly distinguished from
      descriptive — theoretically important
  [+] AMO framework rigorously applied; three-bundle HPWS
      validated via CFA ✓
  [~] Poland KIBS only; pre-COVID; cross-sectional
  [~] BJM regionally focused; ABS 1 (lower prestige than
      core corpus journals)
  [~] CWB R²=0.08 — weak explanation; model better for
      productive than counterproductive behaviours
  [~] Single-source despite CATI method

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C05 (Stirpe2022) — commitment as mediator:
  C05 found satisfaction with HRPs → engagement (commitment
  proxy) → extra-role performance (Italy pharma; OLS). C16
  confirms: HPWS → affective commitment → productive behaviour
  (Poland KIBS; PROCESS). Two different countries, sectors,
  and methods converge on the commitment pathway as the key
  mediating mechanism between management systems and employee
  performance. This is the most important bilateral convergence
  for the thesis mediation argument.

Convergence with C12 (VanBeurden2025) — evaluative perception:
  C12 found that HPWP alignment (what managers implement vs.
  what employees experience) determines commitment and
  performance. C16 uses evaluative HPWP measures (do the
  practices meet employee expectations?) — both papers confirm
  that employee subjective evaluation of management systems
  quality matters more than objective presence of practices.

Convergence with C11 (Zhai2025) — KIBS context vs. manufacturing:
  C11 found T&D is the dominant HPWS moderator across all
  industries (meta-analysis). C16 notes that in KIBS, job design
  and empowerment may matter more than training for performance
  (Wojtczuk-Turek, 2017 cited in text). This divergence is
  sector-specific: in knowledge-intensive service work, the
  opportunity dimension of AMO (empowerment, autonomy, job
  design) may be more critical than the ability dimension.
  For GGHN (NGO health sector): an intermediate position —
  both T&D (for digital/hybrid work skills) and empowerment
  (for hybrid work autonomy) are likely important.

Self-efficacy as AMO boundary condition (new contribution):
  No prior corpus paper tests self-efficacy as a moderator of
  HPWS → performance. C16 adds the individual difference
  dimension to the Block C AMO framework: management systems
  effects are contingent on employee self-efficacy. For GGHN:
  design OMS/MIS to build employee confidence in hybrid work
  tools (raising self-efficacy) rather than relying on HPWS
  alone — this changes the design philosophy.

Full mediation accumulation (Block C):
  C05 (partial, via engagement), C13 (full, via EGB), C15
  (full, via SC), C16 (partial, via commitment) — four papers
  now confirm the HPWS → behavioural mediator → performance
  pattern across different mediators and contexts. The thesis
  can present this as a robust cross-study finding.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Baltic Journal of Management (BJM; Emerald): first BJM paper
    in corpus; Emerald; ESCI; regionally focused
  - Counterproductive work behaviours (CWB): first explicit
    CWB construct as DV in corpus; important negative
    performance dimension
  - Self-efficacy as moderator: first explicit moderation role
    for self-efficacy in corpus; distinct from prior uses as
    mediator
  - Mediated-moderation (conditional process analysis): first
    full mediated-moderation (PROCESS macro) design in corpus
    (vs. mediation-only in C05/C13/C15 or moderation-only)
  - CATI data collection: first CATI-administered survey in
    corpus; reduces social desirability vs. self-administered
  - Evaluative HPWP perception (explicitly labelled): first
    paper in corpus to formally distinguish evaluative from
    descriptive HPWP measures and use evaluative measures
  - KIBS sector: first knowledge-intensive business service
    sector study in corpus; relevant for GGHN knowledge work
  - Common Latent Factor (CLF) test: first use in corpus
    alongside Harman — more rigorous CMB testing

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Wojtczuk-Turek, A., & Turek, D. (2021). HPWSs and employee
performance in KIBS companies: a mediating–moderating analysis.
Baltic Journal of Management, 16(3), 345–365.
https://doi.org/10.1108/BJM-03-2020-0081
```
