```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C17
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C17
Cite key        : Hansen2025
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T1 (AMO theory — HPWPs classified via AMO: skill-
                  enhancing [training/development, recruiting/
                  selection], motivation-enhancing [compensation/
                  benefits, performance management, promotions],
                  empowerment-enhancing [job/work design, employee
                  relations, communication]; AMO framework as
                  theoretical foundation for HPWP → organisational
                  and individual outcomes; SME context),
                  T2 (organisational management systems — HPWP
                  taxonomy (Posthuma et al., 2013); HPWS composite
                  index vs. single practices; SME HR management;
                  resource-based view; human capital theory; global
                  evidence; 115 studies; 124 samples),
                  T4 (meta-analysis — k=115 studies; 124 samples;
                  three-level random-effects HLM; PRISMA 2020;
                  leave-one-out outlier analysis; organisational and
                  individual outcome levels)
Title           : A meta-analysis on the effects of high-performance
                  work practices in small and medium-sized enterprises:
                  An exploration of organizational- and individual-
                  level outcomes
Authors         : Hansen, C., Usanova, K., & Geraudel, M.
Year            : 2025 (received 27 June 2024; revised 24 July 2025;
                  accepted 7 September 2025; available online
                  19 September 2025)
Journal         : Journal of Business Venturing Insights (JBVI;
                  Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 24 (2025), Article e00572
DOI             : [10.1016/j.jbvi.2025.e00572](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbvi.2025.e00572)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : University of Luxembourg (Hansen, Geraudel);
                  UCD School of Business, Ireland (Usanova)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Meta-analysis; three-level random-effects model
                  (Konstantopoulos, 2011); R package metafor;
                  Hunter & Schmidt (1990, 2004) corrections; PRISMA
                  2020; leave-one-out outlier analysis (DFBETA;
                  Cook's distance); robustness checks (simple
                  averaging method)
Aim             : (1) Confirm HPWP → organisational performance in
                  SMEs; (2) explore HPWP → individual employee
                  outcomes in SMEs (first meta-analysis to do so)
Sample          : 115 primary studies; 124 unique samples; 2000–2022;
                  Africa, Asia, Europe, North America, Oceania;
                  Nigeria (2 samples), Pakistan (5), Spain (14),
                  UK (11), USA (17)
HPWP taxonomy   : Posthuma et al. (2013) — nine categories:
                  (i) compensation/benefits, (ii) job/work design,
                  (iii) training/development, (iv) recruiting/
                  selection, (v) employee relations, (vi) communication,
                  (vii) performance management/appraisal,
                  (viii) promotions, (ix) turnover/retention/exit;
                  also grouped into skill-, motivation-, and
                  empowerment-enhancing
Theoretical     : AMO theory (Appelbaum et al., 2000); human capital
frameworks        theory; resource-based view (Barney, 1991)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS — ORGANISATIONAL OUTCOMES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Financial performance:
  r=0.13** (k=100, s=38); HPWP index r=0.15**
  [Consistent with Rauch & Hatak (2016) r=0.103; Combs et al.
  (2006) r=0.13 for large firms]

Subjective performance:
  r=0.33** (k=166, s=54); HPWP index r=0.38**
  [~3× stronger than objective financial performance]

Growth:
  r=0.13** (k=42, s=15); HPWP index r=0.18**

Innovation & corporate entrepreneurship:
  r=0.39** (k=76, s=30); HPWP index r=0.45**
  [STRONGEST organisational outcome; HPWPs especially beneficial
  for SME innovation]

Turnover rates:
  r=−0.17** (k=28, s=12); HPWP index r=−0.24**

Absenteeism rates:
  r=−0.12** (k=12, s=3)

ORGANISATIONAL PATTERN: Innovation strongest (r=0.39/0.45);
subjective performance > objective; HPWS composite > single
practices for most outcomes; single practices often non-significant
or based on few observations

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS — INDIVIDUAL OUTCOMES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Employee engagement & motivation:
  r=0.35** (k=14, s=8); HPWP index r=0.44**

Employee creativity & innovative work behaviour:
  r=0.29** (k=15, s=8); HPWP index r=0.31**

Job satisfaction:
  r=0.47** (k=6, s=3); HPWP index r=0.61**
  [STRONGEST individual outcome]

Organisational commitment:
  r=0.32** (k=26, s=10); HPWP index r=0.34**

Turnover intention:
  r=−0.28** (k=5, s=3)

INDIVIDUAL PATTERN: Individual outcomes stronger than most
organisational outcomes; job satisfaction strongest (r=0.47/0.61);
HPWS composite index consistently larger than single practices;
individual outcomes play mediating role between HPWP and
organisational performance (not tested here but identified as key gap)

KEY SYNTHESIS: HPWPs are beneficial for SMEs at both organisational
and individual levels. HPWS composite (bundled) systems show
consistently stronger effects than single practices. Innovation
benefits most at organisational level; job satisfaction and
engagement benefit most at individual level. Individual outcomes
are the "missing link" in the HPWP → organisational performance
relationship in SMEs.

AMO DIMENSION PATTERN:
  Empowerment-enhancing: strongest for job satisfaction (r=0.51)
  and engagement (r=0.34)
  Skill-enhancing: strongest for organisational commitment (r=0.36)
  and creativity (r=0.35)
  Motivation-enhancing: broader positive effects across outcomes
  [NOTE: No single AMO dimension dominates; context-dependent]

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2/2.3 AMO meta-
               analysis"), C17 provides the most recent meta-analytic
               evidence for HPWP effects at both organisational and
               individual levels, including in developing economy SME
               contexts (Nigeria 2 samples, Pakistan 5 samples).
               Five thesis contributions:

               (1) Individual outcome mediation: C17 identifies
               individual outcomes (engagement, satisfaction,
               commitment) as the "missing link" between HPWPs and
               organisational performance in SMEs — precisely the
               thesis mediation logic. Management systems (OMS/MIS)
               produce productivity gains via their effects on
               employee attitudes and behaviours (hybrid work
               mediator), not directly.

               (2) HPWS bundling effect: composite HPWS consistently
               outperforms single practices (e.g., subjective
               performance: r=0.38 composite vs. single practice
               ranges of 0.26–0.27). For GGHN: a coherent,
               integrated OMS + MIS system will outperform
               individual HR interventions — the thesis should
               propose integrated system design, not standalone
               practices.

               (3) Innovation as strongest SME organisational
               outcome (r=0.39/0.45): While the thesis focuses on
               employee productivity (subjective performance r=0.33),
               innovation effects are stronger. For GGHN context:
               to the extent that GGHN values programme innovation
               and adaptive service delivery, HPWS provides
               particularly strong evidence.

               (4) Nigeria included (2 samples): C17 includes 2
               Nigerian samples in its meta-analytic database —
               the only meta-analysis in the corpus that explicitly
               includes Nigeria. This directly supports the thesis
               argument that HPWP effects are empirically confirmed
               in Nigerian organisational contexts.

               (5) Empowerment-enhancing practices for engagement/
               job satisfaction: empowerment dimension (including
               communication and job design) shows strongest effects
               for engagement (r=0.34) and job satisfaction (r=0.51).
               For GGHN hybrid work: OMS components that give
               employees autonomy and information (communication;
               job design) are the most critical for individual
               well-being outcomes.

Limitation   : SME focus only — GGHN is an NGO, not an SME;
               structural differences (ownership, mission, resources)
               may limit transferability; individual practice
               subcategory effects often based on very few samples
               (low statistical power for single-practice analyses);
               heterogeneity high (I²=82–100% for most analyses);
               mediation not tested (stated limitation); publication
               2000–2022 (pre-2023 gap for most recent studies);
               Nigeria only 2 samples (insufficient for subgroup
               analysis).

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Journal of Business Venturing Insights (JBVI; Elsevier) —
      Scopus indexed; entrepreneurship focused; rigorous outlet
  [+] 115 studies; 124 samples; global (5 continents); 2000–2022
  [+] Three-level random-effects model (Konstantopoulos, 2011)
      — methodologically superior to simple averaging; accounts
      for nested structure ✓
  [+] PRISMA 2020 chart; structured + Google Scholar + citation
      scanning search strategy ✓
  [+] Leave-one-out outlier analysis (DFBETA; Cook's distance) ✓
  [+] Robustness check (simple averaging) — results consistent ✓
  [+] First meta-analysis on HPWP → individual outcomes in SMEs
  [+] Two Nigerian samples included — unique in corpus
  [+] Nine-category HPWP taxonomy (Posthuma et al., 2013) —
      most comprehensive HPWP taxonomy in corpus
  [+] Bundling synergy confirmed — composite HPWS > single
  [+] Open access CC BY 4.0
  [~] SME focus — GGHN is NGO; structural differences
  [~] High heterogeneity (I²=82–100%); moderators not tested
  [~] Many single-practice subgroup results have very low k
      and s — caution required for single practice claims
  [~] Mediation not tested (stated limitation)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C11 (Zhai2025) — meta-analytic evidence:
  C11 found HPWS→OP r=0.259 (k=232; N=56,868; large firms/all
  sizes; 20 countries). C17 finds HPWS→subjective performance
  r=0.33 (k=166; SMEs only) and financial performance r=0.13
  (k=100). C17's subjective performance effect is larger than
  C11's general OP effect — consistent with C11's finding that
  subjective OP measures yield stronger effects. Both confirm
  positive HPWS → OP association as robust across contexts.

Convergence with C16 (Wojtczuk-Turek2021) — individual outcomes:
  C16 found HPWS → productive behaviours (β=0.10**) mediated by
  affective commitment (β=0.05). C17 finds HPWS → organisational
  commitment r=0.32** and job satisfaction r=0.47** at meta-
  analytic level (SMEs). The commitment pathway is confirmed
  across both individual study and meta-analytic levels.

Bundling synergy across Block C:
  C15 (full mediation via SC; HPWS composite effect), C16
  (integrated HPWS as system outperforms isolated practices),
  C17 (composite HPWS r=0.38 vs. single practice r=0.26–0.27
  for subjective performance) all converge: integrated HPWS
  bundles outperform single practices. This is the strongest
  evidence base for the thesis recommendation that GGHN should
  design an integrated OMS + MIS system rather than standalone
  HR interventions.

Innovation as strongest AMO outcome:
  C17 reports innovation as the strongest organisational outcome
  (r=0.39/0.45). C15 found HPWS → social capital → innovation
  in Pakistan banking. Together they confirm that AMO-structured
  management systems have particularly strong effects on
  innovation outcomes — highly relevant for GGHN's programme
  development and adaptive health service delivery context.

Nigeria inclusion:
  C17 is the only meta-analysis in the corpus that explicitly
  includes Nigerian samples (2 samples). Together with C03
  (Jakpa2025; Nigerian multi-sector SHRM), C11 (developing
  economy inference), and C04 (East African green HRM), C17
  provides the most direct meta-analytic validation of HPWP
  effects in a Nigerian organisational context.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Journal of Business Venturing Insights (JBVI): first JBVI
    paper in corpus; Elsevier; entrepreneurship/SME focused
  - SME focus: first SME-specific meta-analysis in corpus
    (C11 was general; C17 is SME-only)
  - Posthuma et al. (2013) nine-category HPWP taxonomy: first
    explicit nine-category taxonomy in corpus (C11 used 6
    categories; C17 uses most comprehensive classification)
  - Nigeria included in meta-analytic sample: first meta-
    analysis in corpus to explicitly include Nigerian samples
  - Three-level HLM meta-analysis: first three-level random-
    effects model in corpus for SME context (C11 also used
    three-level HLM but for large firms/general population)
  - Absenteeism rates as outcome: first explicit absenteeism
    outcome in corpus meta-analysis
  - Innovation as strongest HPWP outcome: first explicit
    quantification in corpus (r=0.39/0.45 composite)
  - Empowerment-enhancing > others for job satisfaction:
    first AMO dimension comparison for individual outcomes

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

sized enterprises: An exploration of organizational- and individual-
level outcomes. Journal of Business Venturing Insights, 24, Article
e00572. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.jbvi.2025.e00572
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C18
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C18
Cite key        : McMackin2021
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — agile HR
                  as management systems operational strategy; HR
                  operating model evolution; four-wave SHRM history;
                  RBV + TCE + sociotechnical systems; HR function
                  structure; management systems theory for HR
                  operations; OMS design principles)
Title           : Agile for HR: Fine in practice, but will it work
                  in theory?
Authors         : McMackin, J., & Heffernan, M.
Year            : 2021 (received 1 March 2020; revised 1 October 2020;
                  accepted 1 October 2020; available online 15 October
                  2020; print 2021)
Journal         : Human Resource Management Review (HRMR; Elsevier)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 31 (2021), Article 100791
DOI             : [10.1016/j.hrmr.2020.100791](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.hrmr.2020.100791)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY-NC-ND 4.0
Affiliations    : Dublin City University, Dublin, Ireland

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Conceptual / theoretical review paper with case
                  study illustrations (BBVA; SKY Group); no primary
                  empirical data; historical analysis of four waves
Aim             : To define agile HR as a HR operational strategy
                  (Agile for HR, not HR for Agile); to situate it
                  within SHRM theoretical evolution across four waves;
                  to propose a research agenda for agile HR in SHRM;
                  to address the research-practice gap in SHRM
Scope           : Four waves of SHRM evolution (1950s–2020):
                  Wave 1: Planning (1950s–70s); Wave 2: Positioning
                  (1970s–90s); Wave 3: Resource-based (1990s–2010);
                  Wave 4: Towards agile (2010–present)
Key distinction : "HR for Agile" (HR systems to support agile
                  implementation elsewhere in the org) vs. "Agile
                  for HR" (agile as an operational strategy for the
                  HR function itself) — this paper concerns ONLY
                  the latter

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY THEORETICAL CONTENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
DEFINITION — AGILE FOR HR (OPERATIONAL STRATEGY):
  "As an operational strategy, Agile HR seeks to minimise waste
  and optimise the flow of value to its customers by organising
  the HR function in multidisciplinary, empowered teams, that
  continuously align with changing business needs by sensing and
  adapting through open communication while operating in short
  cycles. Agile principles are reflected in all aspects of the
  HR operation including structures, roles, processes, and tools
  as well as skills and behaviours of HR management and HR
  employees."

THEORETICAL FOUNDATIONS:
  Resource-Based View (Barney, 1991): integrated agile HR
  operating strategy is VRIN (valuable, rare, inimitable,
  non-substitutable) — unlike individual agile tools (Kanban,
  sprints) which are highly imitable. Full system integration
  yields sustainable competitive advantage; partial adoption
  does not.
  Resource Orchestration (Sirmon et al., 2007, 2011): HR
  managers' actions in structuring, bundling, and leveraging
  HR resources to generate competitive advantage via agile
  operational strategy.
  Transaction Cost Economics (TCE; Williamson, 1971; Yin et al.,
  2019): under high human asset specificity and high performance
  ambiguity (both high in knowledge-intensive/complex roles),
  employee empowerment reduces transaction costs (monitoring,
  coordination, communication). This is the TCE justification
  for agile HR's empowered teams and self-management.
  Sociotechnical Systems Theory (Trist & Bamforth, 1951): agile
  requires both technical (tools, processes) AND social (culture,
  roles, behaviours) dimensions — neither alone sufficient.

FOUR WAVES OF SHRM EVOLUTION (HR operating model angle):
  Wave 1 (Planning; 1950s–70s): HR = administrative; cost
  minimisation; "file maintenance" stage
  Wave 2 (Positioning; 1970s–90s): HR seeks strategic partner
  role; Fombrun et al. (1984); Beer et al. (1984); credibility
  gap; HR operational strategy largely neglected
  Wave 3 (Resource-based; 1990s–2010): Ulrich (1997) HR
  operating model (business partners + centres of expertise +
  shared services); dominated practitioner thinking; limited
  SHRM integration; fragmentation criticisms ("Polo problem");
  partial success only
  Wave 4 (Towards agile; 2010–present): research-practice gap
  widening; environmental turbulence demands HR agility;
  agile HR as next evolutionary step in HR operational strategy

KEY ARGUMENT:
  SHRM researchers have progressively excluded HR operating
  models from their models — interpreting "internal fit" too
  narrowly (only as HPWP alignment) and neglecting the HR
  function's own structure, roles, and operating strategy as
  key variables in the SHRM causal chain. Agile HR as a HR
  operational strategy reinserts the HR function as an
  important actor in the HRM-performance relationship.

CASE STUDY EVIDENCE (illustrative, not empirical):
  BBVA (global bank; 130,000 employees; 2000+ T&C staff in
  10 countries): Full agile T&C transformation since 2017;
  four groups (Front/Business Partners, Disciplines/Expert
  teams, Solutions development scrums, Employee experience
  teams); reported gains: employee engagement, productivity,
  product quality, time to market.
  SKY Group (UK; 30,000+ employees): agile HR operating model
  since 2018; squads, Scrum Masters, Product Owners, Kanban,
  sprints + "swarms"; culture of collaboration, "test and learn",
  data-driven effectiveness assessment.

AGILE PRINCIPLES RELEVANT TO HR OPERATING MODEL:
  — Continuous improvement (minimise waste; optimise value flow)
  — Empowered, multidisciplinary teams (self-managing)
  — Short cycles (sprints; iterative delivery; rapid feedback)
  — Servant leadership (managers enable teams, not control)
  — Transparency of information (open communication)
  — Continuous learning (test and learn; adaptation)
  — Alignment with changing business needs (sensing and adapting)

RESEARCH GAP IDENTIFIED:
  HR operating strategy has been a central practitioner concern
  (McKinsey, 2015) but has virtually no presence in SHRM
  academic literature — a major science-practice gap. Agile HR
  specifically: 161,000 Google hits; 148 Google Scholar hits.
  No empirical research on Agile for HR as a HR operational
  strategy exists as of 2021.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 management systems
               theory"), C18 provides the management systems theory
               foundation for the thesis OMS argument — specifically
               the theoretical basis for how HR operational strategy
               (management systems) is designed and structured to
               enable performance outcomes. Four contributions:

               (1) Agile HR as OMS: The paper's definition of
               "Agile for HR" as an operational strategy closely
               parallels the thesis concept of OMS as a management
               system designed to structure, coordinate, and enable
               employee performance. For GGHN's hybrid work context:
               the OMS proposed in the thesis is precisely an HR
               operational strategy — it organises how GGHN manages,
               monitors, and supports employees across hybrid work
               arrangements. C18 provides the theoretical scaffolding
               for this argument.

               (2) RBV + TCE + sociotechnical systems integration:
               C18 integrates three theoretical frameworks that
               complement AMO and JD-R (used elsewhere in the corpus)
               — RBV (HPWS as ownership-specific advantage; VRIN),
               TCE (empowerment reduces monitoring costs in complex
               roles), and sociotechnical systems (technical + social
               change required). These three frameworks extend the
               thesis theoretical chapter beyond AMO/JD-R/SET/COR.

               (3) HR operating models and SHRM causal chain: C18
               argues that SHRM research has excluded the HR function's
               own structure (operating model) from models of the
               HRM-performance relationship. The thesis addresses this
               gap at the organisational level: GGHN's OMS design IS
               the HR operating strategy that links management intent
               to employee hybrid work behaviour.

               (4) TCE for hybrid work design: under high human asset
               specificity (GGHN health professionals' specialised
               skills) and high performance ambiguity (remote work
               makes monitoring difficult), empowerment practices
               (enabling employee self-management in hybrid work)
               reduce transaction costs. This is the TCE justification
               for outcome-based rather than surveillance-based OMS
               design — directly applicable to GGHN.

Limitation   : Conceptual paper only — no primary empirical data;
               case studies are illustrative (BBVA, SKY) rather than
               rigorous; Western European organisational context
               (Ireland, Spain, UK); agile HR literature is almost
               entirely practitioner-focused; no Nigeria/developing
               economy consideration; no AMO or employee productivity
               outcome data; research gap acknowledged (no empirical
               testing available); HRMR (ABS 4) — high-standing
               journal but paper is conceptual.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Human Resource Management Review (HRMR; Elsevier) — ABS 4;
      SSCI indexed; top-tier HRM theory journal
  [+] Comprehensive four-wave SHRM historical analysis — positions
      agile HR within the full SHRM intellectual tradition
  [+] Clear definition of Agile for HR as an operational strategy
      — first clear academic definition of this concept
  [+] Theoretically rigorous: RBV + resource orchestration + TCE +
      sociotechnical systems — multi-theory foundation
  [+] HR operating model neglect in SHRM literature explicitly
      documented — important gap that validates thesis OMS focus
  [+] Strong research agenda provided
  [+] Dublin City University — peer-reviewed conceptual paper
  [~] No primary empirical data; case studies are illustrative
  [~] Western European context only
  [~] Agile HR literature still primarily practitioner-based;
      academic evidence base limited
  [~] Published 2021 (pre-hybrid work normalisation post-COVID)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C12 (VanBeurden2025) — HR operating model gap:
  C12 showed that what employees experience (alignment) matters
  more than what management formally implements. C18 explains why:
  the HR operating model (how HR strategy is actually delivered)
  is the missing link. Unused HPWPs (C12) result from HR
  operating model failures — when the HR function cannot
  effectively communicate and deliver what it designs. C18
  provides the theoretical explanation for C12's empirical finding.

Convergence with C14 (Demerouti2025) — resource-based perspective:
  C14 frames JD-R theory in terms of job resources and demands.
  C18 uses RBV to frame HPWS (management systems) as ownership-
  specific VRIN advantages. Both converge on the same argument:
  management systems that create VRIN-like conditions (difficult
  to imitate, deeply embedded in employee behaviours and culture)
  are the source of sustainable competitive advantage. For GGHN:
  OMS + MIS that create a unique hybrid work culture are more
  valuable than any single HR practice.

TCE and empowerment in hybrid work (new for corpus):
  C18 introduces TCE explicitly: high human asset specificity +
  high performance ambiguity → empowerment reduces monitoring
  costs. This is the most direct theoretical justification in
  the corpus for outcome-based (vs. surveillance-based) hybrid
  work management systems. No other corpus paper applies TCE
  to HR management systems design. For GGHN: C18 provides the
  theoretical basis for recommending that GGHN's OMS rely on
  outcome transparency and employee self-management rather than
  attendance monitoring or micro-surveillance.

Convergence with C04 (Omolo2026) — green performance paradox:
  C04 found that monitoring → fatigue in green HRM contexts.
  C18's TCE argument provides the theoretical explanation:
  surveillance-based monitoring in high-ambiguity roles increases
  transaction costs AND triggers the conflicting outcomes
  pathway (C10). C18 + C04 + C10 form a triangle supporting
  outcome-based OMS design for GGHN.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Agile for HR (operational strategy): first paper in corpus
    to define and theorise HR agility as an operational strategy
  - Transaction Cost Economics (TCE): first explicit TCE
    application in corpus (Williamson 1971; Yin et al. 2019)
  - Resource Orchestration (Sirmon et al. 2007, 2011): first
    resource orchestration framework in corpus
  - Sociotechnical Systems Theory (Trist & Bamforth, 1951):
    first explicit sociotechnical systems reference in corpus
  - HR operating model as SHRM causal chain variable: first
    paper in corpus to explicitly identify HR operating model
    (vs. HPWP bundle) as a key missing variable in SHRM research
  - Ulrich HR operating model: first explicit treatment in corpus
  - Four-wave SHRM history: first systematic four-wave SHRM
    historical synthesis in corpus
  - Human asset specificity + performance ambiguity: first
    explicit TCE constructs in corpus
  - Dublin City University: first Irish institution in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
McMackin, J., & Heffernan, M. (2021). Agile for HR: Fine in practice,
but will it work in theory? Human Resource Management Review, 31,
Article 100791. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.hrmr.2020.100791
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      C19
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : C19
Cite key        : Parker2022
Block           : C — Management Systems and Theoretical Frameworks
Themes          : T2 (organisational management systems — work design
                  and sociotechnical systems perspective; digital
                  technologies and job demands/resources; hybrid and
                  remote work design; top-down vs. bottom-up
                  (job crafting) approaches; algorithmic management;
                  ICT and work quality; management systems as work
                  design),
                  T5 (productivity enablers and barriers — digital
                  work design effects on job autonomy, emotional
                  exhaustion, loneliness, performance; hybrid work
                  specific; surveillance → work-home interference →
                  exhaustion; support → performance; ICT-enabled
                  crafting → well-being)
Title           : More than 'more than ever': Revisiting a work
                  design and sociotechnical perspective on digital
                  technologies
Authors         : Parker, S. K., & Grote, G.
Year            : 2022 (received 14 August 2022; accepted 16 August
                  2022; print 2022)
Journal         : Applied Psychology: An International Review
                  (APIR; Wiley, IAAP)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 71, No. 4 (2022), pp. 1215–1223
DOI             : [10.1111/apps.12425](https://doi.org/10.1111/apps.12425)
                  [Confirmed from paper header]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY (Creative Commons Attribution)
Affiliations    : Centre for Transformative Work Design, Curtin
                  University, Australia (Parker);
                  ETH Zürich, Switzerland (Grote)
Funding         : Australian Research Council (FL160100033)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Invited article / theoretical commentary; response
                  to commentaries on Parker & Grote (2020); systematic
                  update of key arguments; no primary empirical data;
                  synthesis of recent research on work design and
                  digital technologies (2020–2022)
Aim             : To update and extend the sociotechnical work design
                  agenda for digital/hybrid work; respond to Demerouti
                  (2020) and Wiklund (2020) commentaries; identify
                  what has and has not changed in work design research
                  since 2020; provide specific agenda for hybrid work
                  design
Scope           : Four substantive areas:
                  (1) Digital technology effects on job demands/
                  resources (top-down work design)
                  (2) Job crafting as bottom-up response to digital
                  change
                  (3) Virtual/hybrid work design
                  (4) Proactive sociotechnical redesign agenda

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY THEORETICAL CONTENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CORE ARGUMENT (extending Parker & Grote 2020):
  Work design matters more than ever in a digital world; the
  pandemic accelerated digital transformation and hybrid work;
  top-down proactive work redesign remains critically neglected;
  sociotechnical perspective requires designing BOTH technical
  AND social systems together.

KEY FINDINGS FROM REVIEWED RESEARCH (2020–2022):

(1) Digital technology effects on job demands/resources:
  Algorithmic management (Parent-Rocheleau & Parker, 2022):
  when algorithms perform scheduling, monitoring, PM, goal-
  setting → work intensified; job autonomy reduced; worker
  voice reduced; demands increased. Transparency, human
  influence, and fairness in algorithm design can offset this.
  AI loan system (Strich et al., 2021): AI-based systems can
  upskill lower-level employees BUT simultaneously reduce higher-
  skilled employees' autonomy and role identity — asymmetric
  effects by job level.
  RPA/automation (Peeters & Plomp, 2022): robotic process
  automation → lower job autonomy and task variety → reduced
  engagement.

(2) Hybrid/remote work design (most relevant for thesis):
  Wang et al. (2021) — COVID remote work:
  + Social support → less procrastination, less loneliness,
    better performance, lower exhaustion ✓
  − High workload AND monitoring → work-home interference →
    greater exhaustion ✗
  + Job autonomy when WFH → lower loneliness → better well-being ✓
  Knight et al. (in press): overloaded, closely monitored, OR
  underloaded when WFH → greater psychological distress

  KEY SYNTHESIS for hybrid work design:
  "Rather than focusing on whether remote working is positive or
  negative for work design, a more fruitful question is HOW TO
  CREATE positive work designs when working remotely."
  Hybrid work: useful to DISAGGREGATE jobs — examine work design
  separately for WFH days vs. office days.
  Preliminary finding (Knight et al., 2022): lack of colleague
  support in the OFFICE (not at home) was the STRONGEST driver
  of loneliness in hybrid workers — face-to-face office support
  has unique value that remote support cannot replace.

(3) Job crafting in digital contexts:
  ICT-enabled crafting (Tarafdar & Saunders, 2022): remote/
  mobile blue-collar workers use ICT to increase job resources
  and manage demands → better well-being ✓
  Crafting as coping with AI change (Strich et al., 2021):
  workers adapted but at high cost; top-down proactive work
  design would have reduced the burden.
  Overall: bottom-up crafting has worked better than top-down
  redesign for adapting to digital change — but crafting is
  reactive; proactive top-down redesign is needed for sustainable
  solutions.

(4) Sociotechnical redesign agenda (proactive):
  — Design technology WITH employees (co-design), not just for
    them; include users in design and procurement
  — Build work design considerations into technology design
  — Macro-level change needed: education, policy, regulation
  — Upskill HR professionals and managers in work design/
    sociotechnical perspectives
  — More interdisciplinary research (psychologists + technologists)
  — Intervention studies (not just cross-sectional observation)
  — Consider individual differences in responses to digital work
    (neurodiversity; disability; age; technological self-efficacy;
    work-home segmentation preferences)

MONITORING FINDING (critical for GGHN hybrid work OMS):
  "Having a job with high work load and monitoring created
  work-home interference, which led to greater emotional
  exhaustion" (Wang et al., 2021 cited).
  "Being overloaded, monitored closely or underloaded when
  working from home all contributed to a greater likelihood of
  rising psychological distress" (Knight et al. cited).
  These findings are directly relevant to GGHN: surveillance-
  based OMS (monitoring-heavy) harms employee well-being and
  productivity in hybrid/remote work — consistent with C04,
  C10, and C18's arguments.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.2 use  : Per CSV (chapter_use: "Ch. 2.2 theoretical; work
               design"), C19 provides the most direct and current
               work design theory for hybrid/remote work — from
               Parker (one of the world's leading work design
               scholars) in direct response to the post-pandemic
               digital work context. Six thesis contributions:

               (1) Hybrid work design disaggregation principle: the
               proposal to examine work design SEPARATELY for WFH
               vs. office days is directly applicable to GGHN's
               hybrid work arrangements. For the thesis: GGHN's OMS
               should specify different support structures, autonomy
               provisions, and social connection mechanisms for
               remote days vs. on-site days — not a uniform policy.

               (2) Monitoring → exhaustion (surveillance harm):
               high monitoring + high workload → work-home
               interference → exhaustion. This is the strongest
               evidence-based argument against surveillance-heavy
               OMS in hybrid work. For GGHN: outcome-based OMS
               (C18's TCE argument) is not just theoretically
               preferable but empirically shown to avoid the
               monitoring → exhaustion → productivity loss pathway.

               (3) Social support in office > remote support:
               lack of office colleague support was the strongest
               driver of loneliness in hybrid workers. For GGHN:
               office days must prioritise face-to-face social
               connection and colleague support — the MIS alone
               (remote connection) cannot fully substitute for
               in-person interaction. This nuances the thesis
               MIS argument.

               (4) Sociotechnical systems + proactive top-down
               redesign: C19 reinforces C18's sociotechnical
               systems argument: designing BOTH technical (MIS)
               AND social (OMS/management practices) systems
               together is required. Reactive (crafting) is
               not enough; proactive organisational-level design
               is needed for sustainable hybrid work productivity.

               (5) ICT-enabled job crafting: Tarafdar & Saunders
               (2022) evidence that remote workers use ICT to
               increase resources and manage demands → well-being.
               For GGHN: MIS that enable employees to use ICT for
               self-directed resource management (crafting) are
               an additional pathway through which management
               systems → productivity.

               (6) Job autonomy → lower loneliness → better
               well-being in WFH: autonomy when working from home
               reduces loneliness. For GGHN: OMS that give
               employees autonomy over their hybrid work
               schedules (consistent with C14's employee-led
               flexibility = resource) reduce isolation risk —
               a key GGHN-specific concern.

Limitation   : Invited commentary/theoretical update (not primary
               empirical study); 9-page journal response format
               limits depth; largely synthesises others' work
               rather than producing original findings; mostly
               COVID/pandemic context (2020–2022); largely
               developed-world studies (China, Netherlands,
               US, Germany, Austria); no Nigeria/Africa
               consideration; ICT infrastructure assumed available
               (may not apply fully to all GGHN sites).

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Applied Psychology: An International Review (APIR; Wiley;
      IAAP) — ABS 3; SSCI; leading applied psychology journal
  [+] Invited article — peer-reviewed commentary from leading
      scholars; Sharon Parker is one of the world's top work
      design researchers (ARC Laureate Fellowship)
  [+] Directly addresses hybrid/remote work design — most
      current work design synthesis on this topic in corpus
  [+] Integrates sociotechnical systems, JD-R (Demerouti 2020
      cited), and job crafting perspectives — multi-framework
  [+] Monitoring → exhaustion empirical evidence summarised
      with specific citations (Wang et al. 2021; Knight et al.)
  [+] Hybrid work disaggregation principle — novel and directly
      applicable to GGHN
  [+] Co-design principle (employees + technology designers) —
      reinforces thesis implementation recommendation
  [+] Open access CC BY; Curtin University
  [~] Invited commentary format — 9 pages; synthesis not
      primary data
  [~] COVID/pandemic context (2020–2022); post-pandemic
      normalization of hybrid work may differ
  [~] Largely developed-world empirical base cited
  [~] No AMO framework explicitly; focused on work design
      (JD-R adjacent) rather than HPWP/HPWS

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with C14 (Demerouti2025) — JD-R and hybrid work:
  C14 (Demerouti herself) distinguishes employee-led flexibility
  (resource) from employer-led (demand). C19 provides the
  empirical evidence: job autonomy when WFH → lower loneliness
  → better well-being; monitoring → exhaustion. Together they
  form the theoretical (C14) + empirical (C19) foundation for
  the thesis argument that hybrid work autonomy is the key
  resource and surveillance is the key demand in GGHN's context.

Convergence with C18 (McMackin2021) — sociotechnical systems:
  C18 argued for sociotechnical systems as one of three
  theoretical foundations for agile HR (alongside RBV and TCE).
  C19 provides the most up-to-date empirical evidence that
  sociotechnical systems design — designing BOTH technical (MIS)
  AND social (OMS) dimensions together — is necessary for
  hybrid work quality. C18 + C19 = the complete sociotechnical
  systems argument for GGHN's OMS + MIS integrated design.

Convergence with C04 (Omolo2026) — green performance paradox:
  C04 found monitoring → fatigue in green HRM (developing
  economy). C19 confirms monitoring → work-home interference →
  exhaustion in hybrid work (developed economy). Cross-context
  convergence: surveillance-based management systems harm
  employee well-being regardless of whether the context is
  green HRM or hybrid work — consistent with both COR (C04)
  and JD-R (C19) theoretical frameworks.

Office support unique value (new convergence):
  C19's finding that lack of office support (not remote support)
  is the strongest driver of loneliness in hybrid workers
  directly responds to a potential weakness in the thesis MIS
  argument. MIS tools alone cannot substitute for face-to-face
  office connection. The thesis must therefore argue for BOTH:
  (a) MIS for remote connection days + (b) quality in-person
  support mechanisms for office days — a balanced hybrid OMS.

Full Block C convergence on surveillance harm:
  C04 (monitoring → fatigue; green paradox), C10 (HPWS must
  reduce demands not increase them), C18 (TCE: empowerment
  reduces monitoring costs), C19 (monitoring → work-home
  interference → exhaustion) — four papers across four different
  theoretical frameworks all converge on the same practical
  recommendation: GGHN's OMS must be outcome-based, not
  surveillance-based.

First-mention tracker updates (Block C):
  - Applied Psychology: An International Review (APIR; Wiley):
    first APIR paper in corpus; ABS 3; SSCI; IAAP journal
  - Work design (job design literature; Parker & Grote 2020):
    first explicit work design paper in corpus (as opposed to
    HPWP/HPWS or JD-R theory)
  - Hybrid work disaggregation principle: first explicit
    proposal in corpus to examine WFH vs. office days separately
    for work design — novel methodological insight
  - Office colleague support > remote support for loneliness:
    first empirical finding in corpus specifically about the
    unique value of face-to-face office support in hybrid work
  - Algorithmic management: first explicit treatment in corpus
    of algorithms performing HR functions (scheduling, PM, goal-
    setting, monitoring)
  - Job crafting in digital/ICT contexts: first empirical
    synthesis in corpus of ICT-enabled job crafting
  - Co-design principle (technology with users): first explicit
    co-design recommendation in corpus
  - Industry 5.0 / human-centred technology: first reference
    in corpus to the European Commission's Industry 5.0
    (technology serves people) framing
  - Sharon Parker (Centre for Transformative Work Design,
    Curtin): first Curtin University paper in corpus; first
    ARC Laureate Fellow author in corpus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Parker, S. K., & Grote, G. (2022). More than 'more than ever':
Revisiting a work design and sociotechnical perspective on digital
technologies. Applied Psychology: An International Review, 71(4),
1215–1223. https://doi.org/10.1111/apps.12425
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B03
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B03
Cite key        : Ayogoi2026
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — three-dimension model: work
                  arrangement, work flexibility, organisational support),
                  T6 (empirical: hybrid work → employee productivity
                  and wellbeing; Nigeria private sector),
                  T7 (Africa — Nigeria; Bayelsa State; telecoms)
Title           : Hybrid work models and their influence on employee
                  productivity and wellbeing: Evidence from MTN Nigeria,
                  Bayelsa State
Authors         : Ayogoi, Y. S.
Year            : 2026 (published April 2026)
Journal         : International Journal of Social Science Research
                  and Review
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 9, No. 4, pp. 19–27
DOI             : 10.47814/ijssrr.v9i4.3161
                  [Missing from shortlist CSV; confirmed from paper]
Open access     : Yes — CC BY 4.0
Affiliations    : University of Africa Toru-Orua, Nigeria

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Quantitative empirical; descriptive survey; multiple
                  regression; cross-sectional
Aim             : To examine how hybrid work components (work
                  arrangement, work flexibility, organisational support)
                  influence employee productivity and wellbeing at
                  MTN Nigeria, Bayelsa State
Setting/context : MTN Nigeria employees, Bayelsa State; Nigerian
                  telecommunications sector; post-pandemic hybrid work
Constructs      : IVs: Hybrid Work — three dimensions:
                    (1) Work Arrangement (structural/scheduling clarity)
                    (2) Work Flexibility (autonomy over time/space)
                    (3) Organisational Support (digital tools, training,
                        communication, psychological support)
                  DVs:
                    Y1: Employee Productivity
                    Y2: Employee Wellbeing
Theoretical lens: Job Demands–Resources (JD-R) Theory (Bakker &
                  Demerouti, 2007; 2017)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Descriptive survey; cross-sectional; quantitative
Sample          : 123 MTN Nigeria employees, Bayelsa State
Instrument      : Structured questionnaire; 5-point Likert scale
Reliability     : Cronbach's α ≥ 0.70 (threshold confirmed; specific
                  values not reported in tables [FLAG: α values absent])
Analysis        : Descriptive statistics (means, SD);
                  Multiple linear regression (separate models for
                  productivity and wellbeing)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Descriptive statistics (Table 1):
  Work Arrangement: mean = 3.92 (SD = 0.61)
  Work Flexibility: mean = 4.02 (SD = 0.58) [highest]
  Organisational Support: mean = 3.88 (SD = 0.64)
  Employee Productivity: mean = 4.10 (SD = 0.55) [highest]
  Employee Wellbeing: mean = 3.95 (SD = 0.59)

Regression → Employee Productivity (Table 2, all p<0.005):
  Work Flexibility:       β = 0.428, p = 0.000 (STRONGEST)
  Organisational Support: β = 0.365, p = 0.002
  Work Arrangement:       β = 0.312, p = 0.001

Regression → Employee Wellbeing (Table 3, all p<0.005):
  Organisational Support: β = 0.451, p = 0.000 (STRONGEST)
  Work Flexibility:       β = 0.402, p = 0.000
  Work Arrangement:       β = 0.298, p = 0.004

Key conclusions:
1. All three hybrid work dimensions significantly predict both
   productivity AND wellbeing
2. Work Flexibility is the strongest predictor of PRODUCTIVITY
3. Organisational Support is the strongest predictor of WELLBEING
4. Results support JD-R: job resources buffer job demands → enhanced
   outcomes
5. Organisational support mediates hybrid work demands-outcomes
   relationship — directly supports thesis mediation hypothesis

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Three-dimension hybrid work construct (work arrangement/
               flexibility/organisational support) provides a clean
               operationalisation of the thesis mediator. Organisational
               support dimension overlaps directly with the thesis's
               management systems IV.

Ch. 2.3 use  : Strongest empirical finding in Block B so far for
               hybrid work → productivity in Nigeria. Organisational
               support β=0.451 (wellbeing) and β=0.365 (productivity)
               show that management systems (as organisational support)
               drive outcomes MORE strongly than flexibility alone.
               This is the empirical basis for the thesis mediation
               argument: management systems (organisational support)
               mediates hybrid work → productivity.

Ch. 2.2 use  : JD-R theory second application in corpus (B02 was
               conceptual; B03 is first EMPIRICAL JD-R application);
               organisational support as job resource buffering demands.

Statement of Problem: Nigeria telecoms + Bayelsa State context —
               useful for situating the problem in a Nigeria-specific
               work environment where hybrid adoption is growing.

Limitation   : Single organisation (MTN Nigeria); single state
               (Bayelsa); single author; Cronbach's α values not
               reported numerically; no R² for regression models
               [FLAG: R² absent — explanatory power unknown]; private
               sector telecoms (not public sector/NGO);
               cross-sectional only.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Strong
Flags:
  [+] Empirical study; multiple regression; n=123; Nigeria context
  [+] All three hybrid work predictors significant for both DVs
  [+] β coefficients clearly reported; p-values confirmed
  [+] JD-R theory applied empirically — first empirical JD-R in corpus
  [+] Nigeria private sector; Bayelsa State — regional specificity
  [+] Organisational support β=0.451 → wellbeing: strongest single
      hybrid work → outcome coefficient in Block B so far
  [~] Cronbach's α threshold stated but individual values absent
  [~] R² not reported — explanatory power cannot be assessed
  [~] Single organisation; single state; single author
  [~] Private sector telecoms ≠ public sector/NGO context

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B02 (Usman2025):
  Both use JD-R framework; B02 conceptual in MDAs; B03 empirical
  in telecoms. Together: conceptual framework (B02) + empirical
  validation (B03) for JD-R in Nigerian hybrid work context.
  Organisational support emerges as critical in both.

Convergence with A07 (Alexandro2025) and A12 (Ihejirika2025):
  A07 and A12 found null direct effects for work flexibility →
  performance; B03 finds significant positive effects (β=0.428).
  Difference: B03 measures perceived productivity (self-report);
  A07/A12 used more objective or inferential criteria. Context
  matters: telecoms vs MSMEs vs hospitals.

KEY CORPUS FINDING — ORGANISATIONAL SUPPORT:
  B03 organisational support β=0.451 → wellbeing (strongest predictor)
  confirms that management systems provision (support infrastructure)
  outperforms pure flexibility as a driver of employee outcomes.
  This is the empirical basis for the thesis argument that management
  systems mediation is necessary and sufficient.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - JD-R first empirical application in corpus (B03);
    Bakker & Demerouti (2007; 2017)
  - Organisational support as strongest hybrid work predictor
    (β=0.451 → wellbeing; β=0.365 → productivity)
  - MTN Nigeria / Bayelsa State context: Nigeria private sector
    telecoms hybrid work evidence

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ayogoi, Y. S. (2026). Hybrid work models and their influence on
employee productivity and wellbeing: Evidence from MTN Nigeria, Bayelsa
State. International Journal of Social Science Research and Review,
9(4), 19–27. https://doi.org/10.47814/ijssrr.v9i4.3161
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B02
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B02
Cite key        : Usman2025
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (hybrid work — four-dimension model: flexible
                  scheduling, ICT infrastructure, virtual supervision,
                  work-life balance), T6 (conceptual: hybrid work
                  adoption → employee performance in Nigeria public
                  sector), T7 (Nigeria — Federal MDAs; public
                  administration context)
Title           : Hybrid work adoption and employee performance in
                  Nigerian federal ministries, departments and agencies
                  (MDAs): A conceptual review
Authors         : Usman, A. S., Joshua, S. S., & Akinloye, F. Z.
Year            : 2025 (published September 2025)
Journal         : International Journal of Management Technology
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 12, No. 5, pp. 19–32
DOI             : 10.37745/ijmt.2013/vol12n51932
                  [Missing from shortlist CSV; confirmed from paper]
Open access     : Yes (European Centre for Research Training and
                  Development, UK)
Publisher       : ECRTD-UK / eajournals.org
Affiliations    : Department of Business Administration and
                  Entrepreneurship, Nile University of Nigeria, Abuja

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Conceptual paper; no empirical data; theory-driven
                  literature review; narrative conceptualisation
Aim             : To conceptually explore the relationship between
                  hybrid work adoption and employee performance in
                  Nigerian Federal MDAs; to develop a conceptual
                  framework proposing ICT competence as moderator
Setting/context : Nigerian Federal Ministries, Departments and Agencies
                  (MDAs); post-pandemic public sector context;
                  sub-Saharan Africa developing country
Constructs      : IVs: Hybrid Work Adoption — four dimensions:
                    (1) Flexible Scheduling
                    (2) ICT Infrastructure
                    (3) Virtual Supervision
                    (4) Work-Life Balance
                  DV: Employee Performance — two axes:
                    (1) Task Performance
                    (2) Organisational Citizenship Behaviour (OCB)
                  Moderator: ICT Competence (individual digital skill)
Theoretical lens: Job Demands–Resources (JD-R) Model (Demerouti et al.,
                  2001; Bakker & Demerouti, 2017) — primary;
                  Technology Acceptance Model (TAM; Davis, 1989);
                  Sociotechnical Systems Theory (STS; Trist &
                  Bamforth, 1951); Social Exchange Theory (SET;
                  Blau, 1964); Goal-Setting Theory (Locke & Latham,
                  1990)

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Conceptual; theory-driven; narrative literature review
Data            : None — conceptual paper only
Literature      : Google Scholar, Scopus, ScienceDirect; 2010–2025
                  literature; post-2020 focus for hybrid work context
Hypotheses      : Proposed for future empirical testing (not tested):
  H₁: Flexible work scheduling → task performance (+)
  H₂: Virtual supervision → employee performance (+)
  H₃: Hybrid work adoption → performance outcomes (+)
  H₄: ICT competence moderates hybrid work → performance
Recommendation  : Future empirical validation via SEM or PLS-SEM

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY CONCEPTUAL CONTRIBUTIONS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
1. Four-dimension hybrid work model for Nigerian MDA context:
   flexible scheduling + ICT infrastructure + virtual supervision +
   work-life balance → task performance + OCB

2. ICT competence as moderator: where high — hybrid work benefits
   fully realised; where low — hybrid work becomes barrier;
   digital exclusion risk in Nigerian civil service

3. JD-R application to hybrid work: job resources (flexibility,
   digital tools, supervisory support) buffer job demands (digital
   stress, blurred boundaries, workload)

4. Performance paradox identified: Nigeria MDA performance problems
   are NOT due to hybrid work itself but misalignment between hybrid
   structures and existing performance management systems

5. Five complementary theoretical frameworks applied in combination:
   JD-R (primary), TAM, STS, SET, Goal-Setting Theory — richest
   multi-theory framework in Block B so far

6. Direct Nigeria public sector framing: infrastructure limitations,
   hierarchical cultures, uneven digital capacity — mirrors GGHN's
   operating environment

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.1 use  : Four-dimension hybrid work construct operationalisation
               (flexible scheduling, ICT infrastructure, virtual
               supervision, work-life balance) — the most explicitly
               operationalised hybrid work model in Block B specific
               to Nigeria. Directly usable for thesis Ch. 2.1
               mediator construct definition.

Ch. 2.2 use  : JD-R Model introduced as the primary theoretical lens
               for hybrid work → performance in the Nigerian public
               sector. TAM, STS, SET, Goal-Setting Theory add
               complementary dimensions. JD-R + SET combination
               directly compatible with the thesis's AMO/SET/COR
               framework — SET is already in the corpus (A11 Achief2025).

Ch. 2.3 use  : The "performance paradox" argument — that hybrid work
               problems in Nigerian MDAs stem from MISALIGNMENT
               between hybrid structures and performance management
               systems — is the conceptual equivalent of the thesis
               mediation hypothesis. This is the most explicit
               articulation in the corpus of WHY management systems
               mediation is necessary.

Statement of Problem: Infrastructure limitations, hierarchical
               cultures, and uneven digital capacity in Nigerian
               public sector — directly supports GGHN contextual
               framing. Calls for "strategic and integrated approach"
               combining digital infrastructure + supervisory systems +
               employee competence = management systems.

Limitation   : No empirical data; conceptual only; hypotheses
               untested; no statistical evidence; Western literature
               dominated despite Nigeria focus; no Cronbach's α.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Nigerian Federal MDA context — most contextually proximate
      public sector setting to GGHN in Block B
  [+] Four-dimension hybrid work construct explicitly operationalised
  [+] Five theoretical frameworks applied — JD-R primary; TAM, STS,
      SET, Goal-Setting complementary
  [+] "Performance paradox" argument directly supports mediation
      hypothesis — best conceptual support for thesis model in Block B
  [+] ICT competence moderator — adds moderating layer to thesis model
  [~] Conceptual paper only — no empirical evidence
  [~] Hypotheses proposed but untested; SEM validation called for
  [~] Literature sourced from 2010–2025 but Western-centric despite
      Nigeria focus

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with B01 (Ukpere2026):
  Both are Nigeria-context hybrid/remote work → performance papers.
  B01 = empirical (NGOs, n=50); B02 = conceptual (federal MDAs).
  Together: descriptive evidence (B01) + conceptual framework (B02)
  for Nigeria hybrid work → performance evidence base in Ch. 2.3.

Convergence with A07 (Alexandro2025) and A12 (Ihejirika2025):
  B02's "performance paradox" (misalignment between hybrid structures
  and PMS) = the conceptual explanation for A07's null H5/H6 and
  A12's null H3. Three papers converge on the same argument: direct
  hybrid work → productivity is insufficient without management
  systems alignment.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Job Demands–Resources (JD-R) Model: first explicit JD-R
    application in corpus; Demerouti et al. (2001); Bakker &
    Demerouti (2017); primary theory for hybrid work
  - Technology Acceptance Model (TAM): second mention in corpus
    (first: A06 Magboul2024); now applied to hybrid work context
  - Sociotechnical Systems Theory (STS): first in corpus; Trist &
    Bamforth (1951); social-technical interdependence in hybrid work
  - Four-dimension hybrid work construct (Nigerian MDA): flexible
    scheduling/ICT infrastructure/virtual supervision/WLB
  - Performance paradox (misalignment thesis): conceptual anchor
    for thesis mediation hypothesis

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Usman, A. S., Joshua, S. S., & Akinloye, F. Z. (2025). Hybrid work
adoption and employee performance in Nigerian federal ministries,
departments and agencies (MDAs): A conceptual review. International
Journal of Management Technology, 12(5), 19–32.
https://doi.org/10.37745/ijmt.2013/vol12n51932
```

```
EXTRACTION RECORD                                                      B01
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
BIBLIOGRAPHIC DETAILS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Paper ID        : B01
Cite key        : Ukpere2026
Block           : B — Hybrid Work and Employee Productivity
Themes          : T3 (remote/flexible work — WFH during COVID-19;
                  challenges and benefits in Nigeria NGO context),
                  T6 (empirical: remote work → employee performance,
                  perceived), T7 (Africa — Nigeria NGO sector, Abuja)
Title           : Exploring the effects of remote work on staff
                  performance: A study of selected NGOs in Abuja
Authors         : Ukpere, V. N., Iwara, A. I., & Ayobami, O. T.
Year            : 2026 (received April 2026; published May 2026)
Journal         : International Journal of Research and Innovation in
                  Social Science (IJRISS)
Volume/Issue    : Vol. 10, No. 4, pp. 2594–2616
DOI             : 10.47772/IJRISS.2026.100400199
                  [Missing from shortlist CSV; confirmed from paper]
Open access     : Yes (IJRISS open access)
Affiliations    : University of Gloucestershire; University of Calabar;
                  University of Ibadan

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
STUDY OVERVIEW
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Type            : Descriptive survey; exploratory; cross-sectional
Aim             : To examine the impact of remote work during COVID-19
                  on staff performance in selected NGOs in Abuja,
                  Nigeria
Setting/context : Three NGOs in Abuja, Nigeria (Centre for Integrated
                  Capacity and Resources Development; Community-based
                  Initiative for Growth and Sustainability;
                  Environmental Research and Sustainability Resource
                  Centre); COVID-19 pandemic context
Constructs      : IV: Remote work (WFH) — operationalised as:
                    Benefits (flexibility, family proximity, reduced
                    transport cost)
                    Challenges (operational cost, security risk,
                    reduced staff interaction)
                  DV: Employee performance (perceived/self-reported:
                    job performance, task completion)
Theoretical lens: No explicit management or organisational theory —
                  Theory of Planned Behaviour (Ajzen, 1985) referenced
                  in literature; no formal theoretical framework applied

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
METHODOLOGY
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Design          : Descriptive survey; exploratory; cross-sectional
Sample          : 50 employees across 3 NGOs; purposive + cluster
                  sampling; only employees with ≥1 year of service
Instrument      : 4-point Likert questionnaire (SA/A/D/SD); 4 sections
Reliability     : Cronbach's α ≥ 0.70 stated as threshold; specific
                  coefficient not reported [FLAG: α value absent]
Validity        : Content/face validity by management/research experts
Analysis        : Simple percentages and mean scores only; no
                  inferential statistics

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
KEY FINDINGS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Benefits of remote work (% agreeing):
  Reduces transportation costs: 96% (mean = 3.46, rank 1)
  Enables closeness to family: 86% (mean = 3.28, rank 2)
  Increases job performance: 84% (mean = 3.24, rank 3)
  Flexible / no fixed office hours: 83% (mean = 3.14, rank 4)

Challenges of remote work (% agreeing):
  Information security risk: 94% (mean = 3.34, rank 1)
  Affects staff interaction: 90% (mean = 3.32, rank 2)
  Increases operational cost (internet/electricity): 82% (mean = 3.28)

Preferred communication modes: Email (50%), phone calls (20%),
  WhatsApp (18%), Zoom (12%)

Solutions proposed by employees: Internet data provision (65%),
  flexible working hours (25%), working individually (10%)

Overall conclusion: Remote work has potential to positively impact
  performance IF proper infrastructure, organisational policies, and
  financial incentives are in place. In the Nigerian NGO context,
  infrastructural constraints (electricity, internet) significantly
  moderate this relationship.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
RELEVANCE TO THESIS
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ch. 2.3 use  : Provides Nigeria-specific NGO empirical context for
               remote work → employee performance. The 84% perceived
               performance improvement finding is usable as descriptive
               evidence, but must be cited with the qualification that
               it is self-reported perception (no objective measure).
               The moderation by infrastructure (electricity, internet)
               directly mirrors GGHN's operational constraints in
               Nigeria and supports the thesis argument that management
               systems must mediate the remote work → productivity
               relationship.

Ch. 3 use    : Challenges identified (internet cost, electricity
               disruption, reduced staff interaction) are directly
               applicable to GGHN's hybrid work context — usable in
               Statement of Problem and in contextualising the thesis
               setting.

Mediation argument: "Remote work has the potential of positively
               impacting employee performance PROVIDED there are proper
               infrastructure, organisational policies and incentives
               in place" — this conditional framing directly supports
               the thesis mediation hypothesis (management systems as
               enabling condition).

Limitation   : n=50 from 3 NGOs; descriptive only; no regression;
               no inferential statistics; self-reported performance;
               no Cronbach's α value; COVID-19 context (lockdown
               period) rather than voluntary hybrid work arrangement;
               no comparison group.

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
QUALITY ASSESSMENT
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Overall quality : Adequate
Flags:
  [+] Nigeria NGO sector — highest T7 contextual match to GGHN in
      Block B; directly analogous organisational setting
  [+] Infrastructure constraints framing is directly relevant to
      GGHN's operational context (electricity/internet in Nigeria)
  [+] Conditional performance finding supports mediation argument
  [~] n=50; 3 NGOs; Abuja only — not generalisable
  [~] No inferential statistics; descriptive/frequency only
  [~] Cronbach's α threshold stated but value not reported
  [~] Self-reported perceived performance — not objective measure
  [~] COVID-19 lockdown context (forced WFH ≠ voluntary hybrid work)
  [~] No theoretical framework explicitly applied
  [-] Very small sample; non-random (purposive); low rigour

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
CONVERGENCE NOTES
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Convergence with A07 (Alexandro2025) and A12 (Ihejirika2025):
  All three find conditional/null direct effects of flexible/remote
  work on performance. B01 qualifies: "potential if proper
  infrastructure in place" — exactly the mediation condition the
  thesis argues management systems provide. Three-paper Nigeria +
  Indonesia cross-national convergence for mediation hypothesis.

Convergence with A10 (Ishiwu2024) and A11 (Achief2025):
  Nigeria public/NGO sector cluster building: A10 (Enugu ministries),
  A11 (Nigeria healthcare SLR), B01 (Abuja NGOs). B01 adds the
  hybrid/remote work dimension to the Nigeria evidence base.

First-mention tracker updates (Block B):
  - Remote work → perceived performance in Nigeria NGO sector:
    first Nigeria-specific T6 evidence; Abuja; COVID-19 context
  - Infrastructure as moderator of remote work effectiveness:
    electricity/internet as constraints in Nigerian hybrid work
  - NGO sector context (Abuja): most contextually proximate to
    GGHN's development sector positioning

───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
APA 7 REFERENCE
───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Ukpere, V. N., Iwara, A. I., & Ayobami, O. T. (2026). Exploring the
effects of remote work on staff performance: A study of selected NGOs
in Abuja. International Journal of Research and Innovation in Social
Science, 10(4), 2594–2616.
https://doi.org/10.47772/IJRISS.2026.100400199
═══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
```

```
% ── C19 ─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
@article{Parker2022,
  author    = {Parker, Sharon K. and Grote, Gudela},
  title     = {More than `more than ever': {Revisiting} a work design
               and sociotechnical perspective on digital technologies},
  journal   = {Applied Psychology: An International Review},
  year      = {2022},
  volume    = {71},
  number    = {4},
  pages     = {1215--1223},
  doi       = {10.1111/apps.12425},
  url       = {https://doi.org/10.1111/apps.12425},
  note      = {Open access CC BY. Invited article; response to
               commentaries on Parker\&Grote (2020). Theoretical
               update on work design and sociotechnical systems for
               digital/hybrid work (2020--2022). Key findings:
               (1) Algorithmic management: monitoring/scheduling
               algorithms \(\rightarrow\) work intensification;
               reduced autonomy; reduced voice. (2) Hybrid/remote:
               Wang et al. (2021): social support \(\rightarrow\)
               better perf + lower exhaustion; monitoring + high
               workload \(\rightarrow\) work-home interference
               \(\rightarrow\) exhaustion; autonomy when WFH
               \(\rightarrow\) lower loneliness \(\rightarrow\)
               well-being. Knight et al. (in press): monitoring
               \(\rightarrow\) psychological distress. Knight et al.
               (2022): lack of OFFICE (not remote) colleague support
               = strongest driver of loneliness in hybrid workers.
               (3) ICT-enabled crafting (Tarafdar\&Saunders 2022):
               remote workers use ICT to increase resources and
               manage demands \(\rightarrow\) well-being. (4) Hybrid
               work disaggregation: examine WFH vs. office days
               separately. Sociotechnical agenda: proactive top-down
               redesign; co-design; macro policy; upskill HR/managers.
               ARC Laureate Fellowship FL160100033. Curtin Univ/ETH
               Zürich. APIR Vol.71(4) 2022.},
}

               four-wave SHRM history (Planning 1950s-70s; Positioning
               1970s-90s; Resource-based 1990s-2010; Towards Agile
               2010-present). Key distinction: "HR for Agile" (HR
               practices support agile elsewhere) vs. "Agile for HR"
               (agile as HR operational strategy) -- paper concerns
               only the latter. Definition of Agile for HR: minimise
               waste; optimise value flow; multidisciplinary empowered
               teams; short cycles; transparency; continuous learning.
               Theoretical foundations: RBV (Barney 1991; VRIN;
               full system integration required); Resource Orchestration
               (Sirmon et al. 2007, 2011); TCE (Williamson 1971;
               Yin et al. 2019: high human asset specificity + high
               performance ambiguity \(\rightarrow\) empowerment
               reduces monitoring/coordination costs); Sociotechnical
               Systems (Trist\&Bamforth 1951). Case studies: BBVA
               (130,000 employees; full agile T\&C since 2017);
               SKY Group (30,000+ employees; HR agile squads).
               HR operating model neglect in SHRM literature
               documented. Dublin City University, Ireland. HRMR
               Vol.31 2021.},
```
